The Ruling on Debating Sophists
Al-Jurjani explains the ruling and reasons for forbidding debate with sophists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the major scholars (muhaqqiqun) have forbidden debating with sophists.

Core Idea
Debate is useless when the opponent denies all known truths.

After explaining the etymology of sophists, Al-Jurjani turns to the practical ruling. The verifiers among the scholars have prohibited debate with sophists. Why? Because the purpose of debate is to reach unknown conclusions from known premises, but sophists do not accept any known truth as a starting point. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English. Notice the two conditions for debate that are mentioned.
2
Identify the two conditions for debate
The passage states that debate is for gaining the unknown through speculation, and that the unknown must be unknown (i.e., not self-evident) and the opponent must accept a known truth.
3
Understand why sophists fail these conditions
Sophists deny all knowledge, so they do not acknowledge any known truth. Also, necessary sciences are self-evident, not unknown. So both conditions are absent.
4
Conclusion about engaging
Therefore, engaging in answering their doubts is pointless. The last line seems to say it is an 'iltizam' (commitment) but is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: ليس يمكن أن يكون في العالم قوم عقلاء ينتحلون هذا المذهب، بل كل غالط سوفسطائي في موضع غلطة، فإن سوفا بلغة اليونانيين اسم للعلم، واسطاً اسم للغلط فسوفسطا معناه علم الغلط، كما أن فيلا بلغتهم اسم المحب، وفيلسوف معناه محب العلم، ثم عرب هذان اللفظان واشتق منهما السفسطة والفلسفة والمناظرة معهم أي مع السوفسطائية قد منعها المحققون من العلماء لأنها لإفادة المجهول المحتاج إلى النظر بالعلوم ولا يتصور في الضروريات كونها مجهولة أي محتاجة إلى النظر والخصم لا يعترف بمعلوم حتى يثبت به مجهول فانتفي القيدان المعتبران في المناظرة فالاشتغال به أي بجواب ما ذكروه من الشبه التزام
And it is said: It is not possible that there exist in the world rational people who adopt this doctrine. Rather, every person who makes an error is a sophist in the place of his error. For 'sufa' in the language of the Greeks is the name for knowledge, and 'asta' is the name for error, so 'sufasta' means the science of error. Just as 'fila' in their language is the name for lover, and 'faylasuf' means lover of wisdom. Then these two words were Arabized and derived from them 'safsata' and 'falsafa'. And the discussion (munazara) with them, i.e., with the sophists, has been forbidden by the verifiers among the scholars, because it is for the purpose of gaining the unknown that requires speculation, but the necessary sciences cannot be unknown, i.e., they are in need of speculation, and the opponent does not acknowledge any known thing by which the unknown could be established. So both conditions considered necessary for debate are absent. Therefore, engaging in it, i.e., in answering the doubts they mention, is an engagement...
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of sophists with two points. First, an etymology: the term 'sufasta'iyya' is derived from Greek 'sufa' (knowledge) and 'asta' (error), so it means 'science of error', parallel to 'falsafa' (love of wisdom). Some say that no rational person could actually adopt sophism; rather, every person who errs is a sophist in that specific error. Second, the major scholars (muhaqqiqun) have forbidden debating with sophists. The reason is that the purpose of debate is to gain unknown knowledge from known premises, but necessary knowledge cannot be unknown (since it is self-evident), and the sophist does not accept any known truth to start from. Therefore the two necessary conditions for debate are absent, so engaging with them is pointless.

Key Terms
المناظرة
المحققون
الضروريات
المجهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Debate is forbidden because sophists are evil or disbelievers.
Correction: The forbiddance is due to epistemological impossibility: no common ground for meaningful debate.

Checkpoint
Why do verifiers forbid debate with sophists?
A) Sophists are irrational.
B) Sophists are wicked.
C) Sophists do not accept any known truth.
D) Debate is always harmful.

Key Takeaway
Debate with sophists is forbidden because they deny all known truths, making productive discourse impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your life where you resist acknowledging a clear truth—about yourself, your Lord, or His creation—simply because accepting it would demand change? What is that truth, and what will you do about it today?
?
When you enter a disagreement, do you first check whether the other person shares any common ground with you, or do you argue without foundation? How can you avoid wasting your time in futile debate?
?
Have you ever persisted in a religious discussion that went nowhere, driven by pride or the need to be right? Can you commit now to walk away from such arguments and instead focus on what is productive?

Why Debate with Sophists Is Prohibited
Ruling prohibiting debate with Sophists and its justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verifying scholars prohibit debating the Sophists and the conditions that must be present for valid debate.

Core Idea
Debate is a tool for discovering truth, not a game with skeptics.

The Sophists deny all knowledge, making genuine debate impossible. In this passage, the verifying scholars explain that the very conditions for useful debate are absent when dealing with absolute skeptics. Engaging with them only serves their agenda.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the ruling and its reasoning.
Notice the use of 'المحققون' (the verifying scholars).
2
Identify the purpose of debate
The passage states that debate is for informing of the unknown that needs investigation. Understand this purpose.
3
Understand the two conditions
The passage mentions two conditions: necessary knowledge cannot be unknown, and the opponent must acknowledge known facts to prove an unknown.
These conditions are absent with Sophists.
4
Grasp the ruling and its implication
Conclude that debating Sophists is prohibited because it supports their doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والمناظرة معهم أي مع السوفسطائية قد منعها (المحققون من العلماء (لأنها لإفادة المجهول ) المحتاج إلى النظر ( بالعلوم ولا يتصور في الضروريات كونها مجهولة ) أي محتاجة إلى النظر ) والخصم لا يعترف بمعلوم حتى يثبت به مجهول ) فانتفي القيدان المعتبران في المناظرة ) فالاشتغال به أي بجواب ما ذكروه من الشبه ( التزام لمذهبهم ومحصل لغرضهم كما قرروه في قولهم إن أجبتم عنها إلخ .
And debate with them, i.e., with the Sophists, has been prohibited by the verifying scholars of the learned, because it is for the purpose of informing of the unknown that needs investigation through the sciences, and it is inconceivable for necessary things to be unknown, i.e., in need of investigation. And the opponent does not acknowledge any known thing by which to prove an unknown. So the two conditions considered in debate are negated. So engaging in it, i.e., in answering the doubts they mention, is adherence to their doctrine and attainment of their purpose, as they have established in their statement: 'If you answer them...' etc.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling of the verifying scholars that debating the Sophists is prohibited. It explains the purpose of debate: to inform of the unknown that needs investigation. Since the Sophists deny all necessary knowledge, the conditions for debate are absent—first, the matter cannot be unknown if it is necessary, and second, the opponent does not acknowledge any known fact to serve as a premise. Therefore, answering their doubts only reinforces their doctrine and achieves their goal. The passage thus provides a methodological justification for avoiding engagement with absolute skeptics.

Key Terms
المناظرة
السوفسطائية
المحققون
المجهول
الضروريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think debating Sophists is useful to refute their skepticism.
Correction: The verifying scholars prohibit it because the necessary preconditions for debate are missing: necessary knowledge cannot be unknown, and the opponent denies all known facts.

Checkpoint
Why do the verifying scholars prohibit debating the Sophists?
A) Because Sophists are ignorant.
B) Because the conditions for debate are absent: necessary knowledge cannot be unknown and the opponent denies all known facts.
C) Because debate is always forbidden.
D) Because Sophists are stronger debaters.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of debating Sophists is based on the absence of the necessary conditions for valid debate, as they deny all known facts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent discussion where you felt the other person was denying basic truths. Did you persist in debating? After reading this passage, do you see that persistence as a form of validating their approach? How will you handle similar situations in the future?
?
The scholars say debating Sophists is 'adherence to their doctrine.' Are there any conversations you engage in that, by your participation, give false ideas legitimacy even if you oppose them? What is one conversation you can choose to walk away from this week?
?
This lesson reveals that not every debate is fruitful; some are harmful. Is there an area of your life—in relationships, faith, or work—where you keep arguing without common ground? What would it cost you to stop, and what might you gain?

Proper Method to Refute Sophists
Prescribes sensory evidence and physical coercion to refute Sophists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold method for refuting Sophists: using undeniable sensory evidence and, if necessary, physical coercion to force admission or eliminate their harmful influence.

Core Idea
Sophists can be refuted by confronting them with undeniable sensations and, if they persist, by coercive measures.

This lesson presents a practical method from Iji to deal with Sophistical denial. Instead of abstract arguments, the approach relies on concrete sensory experiences and even physical force to expose stubbornness or neutralize the threat. The passage also addresses a subtle objection about the reliability of sensory perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two main parts: the method and the objection-answer.
The method has two sub-steps: enumeration and coercion.
2
Analyze the method's steps
Identify the first step: enumerating undeniable matters like pain vs. pleasure. Then the second step: beating and burning if denial continues.
The goal shifts to destruction if no admission.
3
Examine the objection and answer
Note the objection: Sophists may admit sensation but consider it possibly erroneous. Understand the answer: the aim is real admission or removal of their influence.
This clarifies the method's ultimate purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل الطريق معهم في إلزامهم ودفع إنكارهم أن تعد عليهم أمور لا بد لهم من الاعتراف بثبوتها والجزم فيها حتى يظهر عنادهم في إنكار الأشياء كلها مثل إنك هل تميز بين الألم واللذة، أو بين دخول النار والماء، أو بين مذهبك وما يناقضه، فإن أبوا إلا الإصرار على الإنكار أوجعوا ضرباً وأصلوا ناراً أو يعترفوا أي إلى أن يعترفوا بالألم وهو من الحسيات وبالفرق بينه وبين اللذة وهو من البديهيات. قوله : ( إلى أن يعترفوا أو يحترقوا حذف الثاني لظهوره . قوله أو يعترفوا بالألم وهو من الحسيات) قيل : الحق أنه ضعيف لأنهم يعترفون بإحساسهم الألم لكنهم يجوزون أن يكون خطأ كما في سائر الأغلاط الحسية، والجواب : أن المراد أو يعترفوا بالألم حقيقة فإذا لم يعترفوا بحقيقة الألم، وجوزوا أن يكون إحساسهم به خطأ، يتركون في النار فيحصل المقصود وهو اضمحلال تأثير فتنتهم باحتراقهم، وبالجملة ليس مقصودنا اعترافهم بخصوصه، بل إما اعترافهم بكون الألم مثلاً أمراً حقيقياً، أو احتراقهم فيحصل المقصود البتة .
Rather, the way with them in refuting them and repelling their denial is that you enumerate to them matters which they must acknowledge as established and be certain of, so that their stubbornness in denying all things becomes evident, such as: Do you distinguish between pain and pleasure, or between entering fire and water, or between your doctrine and what contradicts it? If they refuse except persistence in denial, beat them severely and burn them with fire, or they will admit—i.e., until they admit pain, which is from the sensory things, and the difference between it and pleasure, which is from the self-evident things. His saying: (until they admit or burn) the second is omitted due to its obviousness. His saying: 'or they admit pain which is from the sensory things' – it was said: the truth is that this is weak because they admit their sensation of pain but they consider it possible that it is an error like other sensory errors. The answer: that the meaning is that they admit pain as real; if they do not admit the reality of pain and consider their sensation of it an error, they are left in the fire, and the objective is achieved, namely the dissipation of the influence of their dissension by their burning. In short, our objective is not their admission specifically, but either their admission that pain, for example, is a real thing, or their burning, so the objective is certainly achieved.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage explains the proper method to refute Sophists: confront them with undeniable sensory experiences and self-evident distinctions, such as the difference between pain and pleasure, or between fire and water. If they persist in denial, physical coercion (beatings and burning) is applied until they admit the reality of pain or are burned. A clarification addresses an objection: Sophists may admit feeling pain but consider it possibly erroneous. The answer is that the aim is for them to admit pain as real; if they do not, burning them removes their harmful influence. Thus, the goal is either their genuine admission or their destruction, achieving the overall objective.

Key Terms
الطريق
إلزام
الإدراكات الحسية
البديهيات
الألم واللذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the method relies on the Sophist's admission, so if they falsely admit, the method fails.
Correction: The passage clarifies that if they do not admit genuinely, their destruction by burning still achieves the goal of neutralizing their harmful influence.

Checkpoint
What is the first step in the method against Sophists according to this passage?
A) Immediately beat them.
B) Burn them.
C) Enumerate undeniable matters like the difference between pain and pleasure.
D) Ignore them.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of Sophists combines undeniable sensory evidence with coercive measures to either force genuine admission or eliminate their harmful influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who stubbornly denies a clear truth from the Quran or Sunnah, do you rely on evidence alone, or are you prepared to take stronger measures? What limits would you set, and why?
?
The Sophists denied even the difference between pain and pleasure. In your own life, are there any truths you are in denial about, even though they are obvious to others? What would it take for you to admit them—would you need to feel the 'fire' first?
?
The author says the objective is achieved even if the Sophist burns, because their harmful influence is removed. How does this make you feel about the role of physical punishment in protecting the faith? Is there a part of your heart that resists this, and what does that resistance reveal about your attachment to mercy versus justice?

Should Sophistical Arguments Be Included in Theology Books?
Critique of including sophistical arguments in theology books.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the pedagogical debate over whether including sophistical arguments in theological textbooks helps or hinders students.

Core Idea
Exposing students to doubts can either mislead or strengthen their conviction.

This passage presents a brief but sharp exchange about a key pedagogical question: should books on religious principles begin by presenting sophistical arguments? The critic warns that doing so misleads seekers of truth, while a counterargument claims that encountering these doubts with their refutations builds firmness. This lesson will help you navigate this tension.

Guided Path
1
Read the critic’s warning
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the strong word 'tadhlīl' (misleading). The critic considers prefacing books with sophistical arguments to be harmful.
The critic is referred to as 'nāqid al-Muḥaṣṣal' – a commentator on al-Rāzī's al-Muḥaṣṣal.
2
Examine the counterargument
The phrase 'wa qad yuqāl' introduces an opposing view. Notice how it shifts the focus to potential benefit: acquaintance with doubts and their refutations fosters steadfastness (tathabbut).
3
Reflect on the pedagogical issue
Ask yourself: Under what conditions might including doubts be beneficial versus harmful? This passage does not resolve the debate but highlights its importance.
This connects to broader questions about teaching methodology in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ناقد المحصل : والحق أن تصدير كتب الأصول الدينية بمثل هذه الشبهات تضليل لطلاب الحق، وقد يقال : اطلاعهم على هذه الشبه ووجوه فسادها يفيدهم التثبت فيما يرومونه، كيلا يركنوا إلى شيء منها إذا لاح لهم في بادئ رأيهم .
The critic of al-Muhassal said: The truth is that prefacing books of religious principles with such sophistical doubts is misleading for seekers of truth. And it may be said: Their acquaintance with these doubts and the aspects of their corruption benefits them in being firm regarding what they seek, so that they do not incline to any of them if they appear to them at first glance.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a critique from the critic of al-Muhassal (likely a reference to a commentator on al-Muhassal of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi). He states that placing sophistical arguments at the beginning of books on religious principles misleads seekers of truth. However, a counterargument is mentioned: exposing students to these doubts and their refutations helps them become more steadfast in their beliefs and prevents them from being swayed by such arguments if they encounter them. The passage thus highlights a pedagogical debate about the wisdom of including skeptical arguments in theological education.

Key Terms
ناقد المحصل
كتب الأصول الدينية
الشبهات
التثبت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that exposing students to sophistical arguments is always beneficial because it prepares them to refute them.
Correction: The critic warns that this approach can actually mislead students, especially if they are not yet grounded in the truth.

Checkpoint
What is the critic's main concern about including sophistical arguments in books of religious principles?
A) They are too complex.
B) They mislead seekers of truth.
C) They are not necessary.
D) They are too long.

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of sophistical arguments in theological textbooks is a double-edged sword: it can either mislead or strengthen students, depending on how it is handled.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you first encountered a doubt about your faith, did you wish someone had shielded you from it? Or do you feel stronger now? What does this passage say about your own learning journey?
?
Are you more inclined to protect yourself from challenging questions or to face them head-on? How does that choice affect your faith?
?
If you were writing a book of aqeedah, would you include sophistical arguments? Why? What fear or hope drives your answer?

Forcing Skeptics through Sensory and Necessary Truths
Al-Jurjani presents a method to force skeptics to admit necessary truths through sensory experience and self-evident principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical theologians dealt with extreme skeptics by appealing to undeniable sensory and self-evident truths, and why physical coercion is part of the method.

Core Idea
Skeptics cannot deny the reality of pain without contradicting their own experience.

This lesson presents a unique method from al-Jurjani for confronting skeptics who deny all knowledge. Instead of lengthy rational arguments, the method forces them to acknowledge basic sensory distinctions like pain and pleasure. It shows that some truths are so immediate that denying them reveals pure stubbornness. The passage also addresses an important objection and offers a practical resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the confrontational tone and the specific examples used.
The passage includes a critical objection and response, so read to the end.
2
Identify the method's core strategy
Look for the phrase 'enumerate upon them matters which they must admit.' These are undeniable truths from sensory experience and self-evident knowledge.
Examples: distinction between pain and pleasure, between entering fire and water.
3
Understand the escalation clause
If the skeptic persists in denial, the method prescribes physical coercion ('beat them severely and burn them with fire') to force admission or eliminate their fitna.
This is not a rational argument but a practical compulsion.
4
Analyze the objection and response
A critic argues that skeptics may admit sensation but claim it could be erroneous. The response is that if they don't admit the reality of pain, burning them achieves the goal anyway.
The method aims to end the skeptic's influence, not just convince.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمذهبهم ومحصل لغرضهم كما قرروه في قولهم إن أجبتم عنها إلخ . ( بل الطريق ) معهم في إلزامهم ودفع إنكارهم ) أن تعد عليهم أمور لا بد لهم من الاعتراف بثبوتها ) والجزم فيها ( حتى يظهر عنادهم في إنكار الأشياء كلها (مثل إنك هل تميز بين الألم واللذة، أو بين دخول النار والماء، أو بين مذهبك وما يناقضه، فإن أبوا إلا الإصرار ) على الإنكار ( أوجعوا ضرباً وأصلوا ناراً أو يعترفوا) أي إلى أن يعترفوا بالألم وهو من الحسيات وبالفرق بينه وبين اللذة وهو من البديهيات ) قال ناقد المحصل : والحق أن تصدير كتب الأصول الدينية بمثل هذه الشبهات تضليل لطلاب الحق، وقد يقال : اطلاعهم على هذه الشبه ووجوه فسادها يفيدهم التثبت فيما يرومونه، كيلا يركنوا إلى شيء منها إذا لاح لهم في بادئ رأيهم . قوله : ( إلى أن يعترفوا أو يحترقوا حذف الثاني لظهوره . : قوله أو يعترفوا بالألم وهو من الحسيات) قيل : الحق أنه ضعيف لأنهم يعترفون بإحساسهم الألم لكنهم يجوزون أن يكون خطأ كما في سائر الأغلاط الحسية، والجواب : أن المراد أو يعترفوا بالألم حقيقة فإذا لم يعترفوا بحقيقة الألم، وجوزوا أن يكون إحساسهم به خطأ، يتركون في النار فيحصل المقصود وهو اضمحلال تأثير فتنتهم باحتراقهم، وبالجملة ليس مقصودنا اعترافهم بخصوصه، بل إما اعترافهم بكون الألم مثلاً أمراً حقيقياً، أو احتراقهم فيحصل المقصود البتة .
For their doctrine and the attainment of their purpose, as they stated in their statement 'If you answer them, etc.' (But the way) with them in obligating them and repelling their denial (is that you enumerate upon them matters which they must admit to be established) and be certain of them (so that their stubbornness in denying all things becomes apparent) (such as: Do you distinguish between pain and pleasure, or between entering fire and water, or between your doctrine and what contradicts it? If they refuse except persistence) upon denial (beat them severely and burn them with fire, or they admit) i.e. until they admit pain, which is among the sensibles, and the distinction between it and pleasure, which is among the self-evident truths.) The critic of al-Muhassal said: The truth is that placing such sophistries at the beginning of books on religious principles misleads seekers of truth. It may be said: Their acquaintance with these sophistries and the aspects of their corruption benefits them in being firm regarding what they seek, so that they do not rely on any of them if they appear to them at first sight. His statement: (until they admit or burn; the second is omitted due to its obviousness. His statement: 'or they admit pain, which is from the sensibles') It was said: The truth is that it is weak because they admit their sensation of pain but they consider it possible that it is an error, as in other sensory errors. The answer: That what is meant is that they admit pain as a reality; if they do not admit the reality of pain and consider it possible that their sensation of it is an error, they are left in the fire, and the goal is achieved, which is the destruction of the influence of their mischief by their burning. In short, our intention is not their admission specifically, but either their admission that pain, for example, is a real thing, or their burning, and thus the goal is absolutely achieved.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the method for dealing with extreme skeptics (sophists) who deny all knowledge. It is a proof from necessary sensory experiences. The author states that the way to obligate them and refute their denial is to present matters they cannot help but admit, such as distinguishing between pain and pleasure, or between entering fire and water. If they still refuse, they are to be beaten or burned until they admit the reality of pain. Then a critic objects that skeptics may admit sensation but consider it possibly erroneous. The answer is that if they do not admit the reality of pain, leaving them in the fire will still achieve the goal of eliminating their fitna. Thus the method relies on undeniable experiential truth, not rational argument alone.

Key Terms
العلوم الضرورية
البديهيات
الحسيات
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The method is a rational argument meant to convince skeptics logically.
Correction: The method relies on undeniable sensory and self-evident truths and physical coercion, not rational persuasion. Its aim is either admission of reality or elimination of the skeptic's influence.

Checkpoint
According to the method presented, what is the primary way to deal with a skeptic who denies all knowledge?
A) Present rational proofs for the existence of God
B) Force them to admit basic sensory distinctions like pain and pleasure
C) Debate them using syllogistic logic
D) Ignore them as hopeless cases

Key Takeaway
The method uses undeniable sensory and self-evident truths to compel skeptics to admit reality, with physical coercion as a final resort to achieve the goal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that some truths are so undeniable that denying them would lead to self-destruction. Is there a truth in your life—about your character, your beliefs, or your actions—that you have been stubbornly denying? What would it take for you to admit it, even if it costs you comfort?
?
Al-Jurjani uses the distinction between pain and pleasure as a self-evident truth. Do you ever ignore the difference between what truly brings you lasting peace (pleasure) and what only causes temporary satisfaction but leads to pain? Reflect on a decision you made recently—did you choose based on self-evident good or just immediate feeling?
?
The passage suggests that some people must be forced to admit reality through experience. Have you ever insisted on a falsehood until you were 'burned' by consequences? What did you learn from that experience, and are you still resisting a truth that is clear to you now?

Critique of Al-Amidi's Definition of Nazar
Critique of al-Amidi's definition of nazar and fikr as synonyms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique against al-Amidi's claim that nazar and fikr are synonymous and why it is considered a forced interpretation.

Core Idea
Stating synonymy in a definition with wording contrary to it is forced and unlikely.

This lesson examines al-Amidi's definition of nazar as synonymous with fikr. The author argues that the wording used by al-Amidi actually suggests a part-whole relationship rather than synonymy, making the interpretation strained. We will analyze the Arabic passage to see how the critique is built.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once to get the flow and identify the two parts: al-Amidi's claim and the critique.
2
Identify al-Amidi's claim
Notice that al-Amidi said 'النظر هو الفكر' to show unity of meaning, not as a definition part.
3
Analyze the critique
Focus on why the author calls it a forced interpretation: the wording naturally implies that 'thought' is a part of the definition, not a synonym.
Consider what phrasing would have been clearer for synonymy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الآمدي: لم يذكره جزاءً من التعريف بل قال: النظر هو الفكر بياناً لاتحاد مدلولهما، وما بعده الحد لهما وفيه تمحل لا يخفى لأن بيان الترادف واتحاد المدلول في مقام التحديد بعبارة ظاهرة في خلافه بعيد جداً، وإنما كانت ظاهرة في خلاف بيان الترادف، لأن المتبادر منها أن الفكر من أجزاء الحد، ولو أريد بيان ترادفهما لقيل من النظر والفكر.
Al-Amidi said: He did not mention it as a part of the definition, but rather said: 'Nazar is thought' as an explanation of the unity of their meanings, and what follows is a definition for both, but there is a forced interpretation in it that is not hidden, because explaining synonymy and unity of meaning in the context of definition with wording apparent to the contrary is very unlikely. And the wording was only apparent to the contrary of explaining synonymy, because what is understood from it is that thought is one of the parts of the definition, and if one wanted to explain their synonymy, it would have been said: 'from nazar and thought.'
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage criticizes al-Amidi's definition. Al-Amidi said that the definition of nazar is "الفكر" (thought) without separating, explaining that they have the same meaning. He did not include the rest as part of the definition but rather as a description. The author says this is a forced interpretation (تمحل) because stating that they are synonymous in the context of defining, with wording that suggests otherwise, is very far-fetched. The wording "النظر هو الفكر" naturally implies that thought is a part of the definition, not that they are synonyms. If they were synonyms, the phrasing would have been "النظر والفكر مترادفان" or similar.

تمحل

حد

Misconception: Al-Amidi's definition that nazar is thought is acceptable because he meant they are synonyms.
Correction: The author argues that the wording implies a part-whole relationship, making the interpretation forced; proper wording for synonymy would be different.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject al-Amidi's definition?
A) Because it is too broad
B) Because it implies synonymy which contradicts the wording
C) Because it adds the fasl
D) Because it is not comprehensive

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi's claim of synonymy between nazar and fikr is rejected because his wording suggests a different relationship, making his interpretation a forced one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a definition in a book of 'aqeedah, do you automatically assume the wording perfectly matches the intended meaning, or do you critically examine whether the phrasing could lead to a different understanding? This lesson shows how a forced interpretation can hide behind seemingly simple words. How can you develop the habit of questioning definitions you encounter?
Challenge yourself: choose one Islamic term you think you understand and write out a definition. Then ask: does the wording of that definition truly convey the intended meaning, or is it open to misinterpretation? Commit to clarifying definitions in your own study.
?
Al-Amidi's forced interpretation (tamahhul) was exposed because the wording naturally suggested a part-whole relationship, not synonymy. Think of a time when you accepted a teaching or explanation that 'felt right' but later realized the wording was inconsistent with what was meant. Perhaps it was a translation of a du'a or a hadith. How did that affect your understanding? Are you currently holding onto any interpretation of a religious text that may be a tamahhul? Be honest: what would it cost you to revisit that interpretation with fresh eyes?
?
This lesson is a small case study in the precision required for theological definitions. If you were to explain to a fellow Muslim why scholars dedicate time to critiquing phraseology, what would you say? More importantly, does this precision challenge your own practice? Do you see yourself as someone who 'tolerates ambiguity' in religious knowledge? What would it take for you to become a student who insists on clarity, even when it is uncomfortable? Where Does Clarity Belong? Reasoning or Proof?
Sharh analyzes whether clarity/obscurity are truly predicated of reasoning or of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why clarity and obscurity are attributes of the proof (dalil) rather than the act of reasoning (nazar), and how common expressions are interpreted metaphorically.

Core Idea
Clarity belongs to the proof, not the act of reasoning.

In Islamic logic and theology, we often say 'this reasoning is clear' or 'that reasoning is obscure.' But is clarity truly a property of the mental act of reasoning, or is it actually a property of the proof that reasoning uses? This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif unpacks that question, examining different interpretations and settling on the correct position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and the literal translation to get a sense of the discussion.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, rejection, alternative views, correct view.
2
Identify the main positions
Notice the different views presented: that clarity applies literally to reasoning as arranged matters, that it's metaphorical, that it refers to speed, and that it refers to premises.
Each interpretation has its own supporting reasoning.
3
Understand the correct position
Focus on the sentence starting 'والحق أن الجلاء والخفاء...' which states that clarity/obscurity are attributes of the thing known, not of reasoning.
This is the key doctrinal point.
4
See how common expressions are handled
Consider why saying 'this reasoning is clear' is common but metaphorical, according to the author.
This resolves the apparent conflict between everyday speech and technical accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فهو لا يعرض للنظر حقيقة) قيل قد ثبت بل اشتهر إطلاق النظر على نفس الأمور المرتبة فلا خفاء في صدقهما عليها حقيقة وأنت خبير بأن ذلك الإطلاق مجازي عند الجمهور، والكلام في اتصاف النظر الحقيقي بهما حقيقة، وقد يقال المراد بجلاء النظر وخفائه كونه مؤدياً أداء واضحاً سريعاً، أو أداء خفياً بطيئاً وإن كانا مستفادين من مادة البيان وصورته، وقيل أيضاً المراد بجلاء النظر كون مقدماته جلية، وهذا الكون المخصوص صفة النظر حقيقة، وكذا الكلام في الخفاء والنقل الصريح من أرباب هذه الصناعة بهذه الإرادة غير لازم لأن قولهم هذا النظر جلي وذا خفي شائع، والأصل في الكلام حقيقته فيحمل مرادهم على هذا والحق أن الجلاء والخفاء بالمعنى المتبادر منهما صفة للشيء باعتبار وتعلق العلم به فلا يوصف به النظر حقيقة لأن النظر ما يحصل به العلم لا ما يتعلق به ذلك، ويمكن أن يحمل قول الشارح على هذا وهذا بعيد فليتأمل .
He said: '(It [i.e., clarity and obscurity] does not apply to reasoning in reality).' It has been said, indeed it is well-known, that the term 'reasoning' is applied to the very arranged matters, so there is no doubt that they [clarity and obscurity] are truly predicated of them. But you know that this application is metaphorical according to the majority, and the discussion concerns the description of real reasoning with them in reality. And it may be said that the meaning of clarity of reasoning is that it leads clearly and quickly, or leads obscurely and slowly, even if both are derived from the matter and form of the demonstration. And it is also said that the meaning of clarity of reasoning is that its premises are clear, and this specific state is truly an attribute of reasoning, and similarly for obscurity. And explicit transmission from the masters of this craft with this intent is not necessary because their statement 'this reasoning is clear and that is obscure' is widespread, and the principle in speech is its literal meaning, so their intent is interpreted according to that. And the truth is that clarity and obscurity in their ordinary sense are attributes of the thing by virtue of the consideration and relation of knowledge to it, so reasoning is not described by them in reality because reasoning is that by which knowledge is obtained, not that to which it relates. And it is possible to interpret the commentator's statement according to this, but that is far-fetched, so let it be examined.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of whether the terms 'clear' (jali) and 'obscure' (khafi) are attributes of reasoning (nazar) itself or of the proof (dalil). The Sharh begins by noting that some have claimed that the word 'reasoning' is applied to the very ordered matters (i.e., the proof), so clarity and obscurity could be literally true of them. However, the Sharh points out that this usage is metaphorical (majazi) according to the majority, and the real question is whether true reasoning (the mental act) can be described as clear or obscure in reality. Several interpretations are considered: (1) clarity/obscurity refer to whether the reasoning leads quickly or slowly to the conclusion, which is derived from the matter and form of the demonstration; (2) they refer to the clarity or obscurity of the premises, which would make them attributes of the reasoning process itself. The Sharh then states the correct position (al-haqq): clarity and obscurity in their primary sense are attributes of the object of knowledge, not of the reasoning act, because reasoning is the means by which knowledge is obtained, not the object of knowledge. The common expressions 'this reasoning is clear' etc. are therefore metaphorical. The Sharh concludes by noting that the commentator's statement might be interpreted in this way, but that interpretation is far-fetched.

Key Terms
جلي
خفي
نظر
دليل
مجاز
بداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that when we say 'this reasoning is clear,' clarity is an actual property of the mental act of reasoning.
Correction: The correct view is that clarity and obscurity in their primary sense are attributes of the object of knowledge or the proof, not the act of reasoning; common expressions are metaphorical.

Checkpoint
According to the Sharh, in what sense can the statement 'this reasoning is clear' be considered true?
A) It is literally true because reasoning is a mental act.
B) It is true metaphorically, by applying the attribute of the proof to the reasoning.
C) It is false because reasoning can never be described as clear.
D) It is true only if the premises are self-evident.

Key Takeaway
Clarity and obscurity are attributes of the object of knowledge, not of the reasoning act; common usage is metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I see clearly now,' are you describing the truth or your own mental state? This lesson says clarity belongs to the object, not your reasoning. How often do you confuse your own confidence with actual clarity about Allah's signs?
?
You frequently speak of 'clear proofs' and 'obscure arguments' — but according to this lesson, you are speaking metaphorically. Are you willing to examine whether you truly know the truth, or merely have a well-arranged thought? This demands that you separate your love for your own reasoning from your love for the truth itself.
?
The masters of this craft carefully distinguish between reasoning and its object. How carefully do you guard your tongue and heart when discussing matters of faith? Are you attributing to your own mind what belongs only to the truth of revelation? This lesson calls you to humility: your reasoning is merely a tool; clarity is in the message.

Al-Razi's Objection to Proving Nazar by Nazar
Al-Razi argues that proving nazar by nazar leads to a logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's core objection that proving nazar by nazar entails a logical contradiction because it requires the thing to be known and not known simultaneously.

Core Idea
Self-referential proof leads to the thing being both known and unknown at once.

Imam al-Razi challenges the idea that one can prove the soundness of rational consideration (nazar) through consideration itself. He argues this would require consideration to be known before it is proven, which is impossible. This lesson unpacks his objection and the supposed contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's objection
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وأنكر عليه الإمام الرازي' up to 'إلا في دعوى الضرورة'. Then read its translation to grasp the argument.
Focus on the phrase 'إثبات الشيء بنفسه' (proving a thing by itself).
2
Identify the two aspects of the contradiction
Notice that al-Razi points out a double aspect: the thing is sought (so not yet known) and yet it is the instrument of seeking (so must be known). This yields a contradiction.
He writes: 'من حيث هو مطلوب يجب أن لا يكون حاصلاً حال الطلب، ومن حيث أنه آلة من الطلب يجب أن يكون حاصلاً في تلك الحال'.
3
Contrast with negating a thing by itself
Al-Razi contrasts this with negating a thing by itself, which is a clear contradiction because it combines affirmation and negation. He argues proving a thing by itself is not contradictory in that way but still has a different contradiction.
This sets up the debate about whether the issue is truly a logical impossibility.
4
Examine the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that the only escape is to claim necessity (al-darura), meaning the soundness of nazar must be known by intuition, not proof.
The text: 'فلا مخلص إلا في دعوى الضرورة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : النظر الصحيح عند الجمهور
وأنكر عليه الإمام الرازي) في النهاية ( فقال : إن إثبات الشيء بنفسه يقتضي أن يعلم به قبل نفسه ليمكن إثباته به ) وذلك يستلزم أن يعلم حين ما لا يعلم وتلخيصه أنه من وجه، كما أن : حيث هو مطلوب يجب أن لا يكون حاصلاً حال الطلب، ومن حيث أنه آلة من الطلب يجب أن يكون حاصلا في تلك الحال ( وهو تناقض ) قال : فبطل ما توهموه أن نفي الشيء بنفسه تناقض لاجتماع نفيه وإثباته معا بخلاف إثبات الشيء بنفسه، إذ لا تناقض فيه أصلاً ، فظهر أن إثبات كل النظر بالنظر يشتمل على تناقض من نفي كل النظر بالنظر متناقض من وجه آخر فلا مخلص إلا في دعوى الضرورة كما لخصناها ( والجواب أنه ( أي إمام الحرمين ( إنما يمنع كون إثبات كون النظر بالنظر إثباتاً للشيء بنفسه لا أنه يسلم ذلك ويمنع كونه تناقضاً) حتى يتجه عليه ذلك الإنكار ) وتحقيقه ) أي تحقيق ما ذكرناه من أن إثبات النظر بالنظر ليس إثباتا للشيء بنفسه وإن أوهمته العبارة ( أنا نثبت القضية الكلية القائلة كل نظر صحيح في القطعيات لا يعقبه ما ينافي العلم فإنه يفيده ) أو المهملة ) القائلة : النظر قد يفيد العلم ( على اختلاف التحريرين بمشخصة ) أي بقضية شخصية حكم فيها بقوله لاستلزامه كون الشيء معلوماً حين ما ليس معلوماً، وإن ما نقله عن الإمام إعادة لذلك فالصواب أن يقال : في شرح قوله تناقض كنفي الشيء بنفسه، ثم يحرر كلام إمام الحرمين بأنه لا تناقض في إثبات الشيء بنفسه لأنه إنما يقتضي ثبوت الشيء فقط بخلاف نفيه بنفسه، فإنه يستلزم انتفاء الشيء وثبوته معاً، وإنه تناقض ثم يورد عليه إنكار الإمام بأنه وإن لم يكن في إثبات الشي بنفسه التناقض الذي في نفي الشيء بنفسه، إلا أنه يستلزم تناقضاً آخر وهو أن يكون الشيء معلوماً، وأن لا يكون معلوماً في حالة واحدة . قوله: (وتلخيصه أنه إلخ) الحيثيات للتعليل لا للتقييد فلا يرد منع التناقض لاختلاف الحيثيتين.
قوله : ( على تناقض من وجه) وهو أن يكون معلوماً وأن لا يكون معلوماً في حالة واحدة .
قوله : ( من وجه آخر وهو أن يكون النظر ثابتاً ومنتفياً .
قوله : ( وإن أوهمته العبارة ) أعني قولنا إثبات النظر بالنظر . قوله : (أي بقضية شخصية أي وهي أن هذا النظر مفيد للعلم فإن قيل : إثبات الكلية والمهملة إذا كانت بنظر مخصوص كان الإثبات بنفس ذلك النظر الجزئي لأنه مفيد للعلم، قلت : إثباتها بذلك النظر متوقف على صحة مقدماته واستلزامه لها وهو معنى الإفادة، فيكون إثباتها موقوفاً على قولنا هذا النظر مفيد للعلم.
قوله: (وتلخيصه أنه من حيث هو مطلوب إلخ ) الحيثيتان المذكورتان للتعليل لا للتقييد فلا ينافيان التناقض .
The Fifth Observation - Third Objective: Sound Consideration (al-nazar al-sahih) according to the Majority. And Imam al-Razi objected to him in al-Nihaya saying: Surely proving a thing by itself requires that it be known by it before itself so that it can be proven by it, and that entails that it be known at the time when it is not known, and its summary: it is from one aspect, just as: where it is sought, it must not be obtained at the time of seeking, and from the aspect that it is an instrument of seeking, it must be obtained at that moment—and that is a contradiction. He said: so what they imagined—that negating a thing by itself is a contradiction due to the conjunction of its negation and affirmation together, unlike proving a thing by itself, for there is no contradiction in it at all—is false. So it became evident that proving all consideration by consideration includes a contradiction from one aspect; negating all consideration by consideration is contradictory from another aspect, so there is no escape except in claiming necessity (al-darura) as we summarized it. And the answer: He (i.e., Imam al-Haramayn) only prevents that proving consideration by consideration is proving a thing by itself, not that he concedes that and prevents its being a contradiction, so that that objection would apply to him. And its verification: (i.e., verification of what we mentioned, that proving consideration by consideration is not proving a thing by itself, even if the expression suggests it) is that we prove the universal proposition (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya) stating: every sound consideration (nazar sahih) in certainties (al-qat'iyyat) is not followed by what contradicts knowledge, for it yields it; or the particular indefinite proposition (al-muhmala) stating: consideration may yield knowledge, according to the difference of the two formulations, by a singular proposition (bi-mushakhkhasa), i.e., a particular proposition in which it is judged by his statement—due to its entailing that the thing be known when it is not known. And what he transmitted from al-Imam is a repetition of that, so the correct thing to say is: in explaining his statement 'contradiction like negating a thing by itself', then to refine the statement of Imam al-Haramayn: there is no contradiction in proving a thing by itself because it only entails the existence of the thing only, unlike negating it by itself, for it entails the negation and existence of the thing together, and that is contradiction. Then the objection of al-Imam is brought against him: even if there is not in proving a thing by itself the contradiction that is in negating a thing by itself, yet it entails another contradiction: that the thing be known and not be known in one state. His statement: (and its summary: it is, etc.): the two aspects (al-haythiyyat) are for explanation (al-ta'lil), not for restriction (al-taqyid), so the prevention of contradiction due to the difference of the two aspects does not apply. His statement: (upon a contradiction from one aspect): i.e., that it be known and not be known in one state. His statement: (from another aspect): i.e., that consideration be established and negated. His statement: (even if the expression suggests it): i.e., our statement 'proving consideration by consideration'. His statement: (i.e., by a singular proposition): i.e., it is that this consideration is productive of knowledge. For if it is said: The proof of the universal and the indefinite, if it is by a specific consideration, then the proof is by that particular consideration itself because it is productive of knowledge, I say: Its proof by that consideration depends on the validity of its premises and its entailment of them, and that is the meaning of production, so its proof is dependent on our statement 'this consideration is productive of knowledge'. His statement: (and its summary: it is from the aspect it is sought, etc.): the two mentioned aspects (al-haythiyyat) are for explanation, not for restriction, so they do not conflict with the contradiction.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer in the classical kalam debate about the soundness of rational consideration (nazar). The subheading identifies it as the third objective of the fifth observation: the nature of sound consideration according to the majority. Imam al-Razi objects to the claim that consideration can be proven by consideration itself, arguing that this leads to a contradiction: the thing (consideration) would need to be known before itself to serve as proof, which is impossible. He contrasts this with negating a thing by itself, which is a clear contradiction because it combines affirmation and negation; proving a thing by itself, however, involves no contradiction of that sort, yet it still entails another contradiction—the thing being known and not known simultaneously. The commentator then provides the answer: Imam al-Haramayn does not concede that proving consideration by consideration is proving a thing by itself; rather, he prevents that characterization. The proper account is that we prove a universal proposition about consideration (e.g., 'every sound consideration in certainties yields knowledge') not by the very act of consideration as a self-referential proof, but through a specific particular consideration. The commentary explains that the apparent contradiction is avoided because the two aspects (being sought and being an instrument) are reasons for the judgment, not restrictive conditions. The argument is refined through multiple layers: the distinction between proving a thing by itself (which is not actually intended here) and proving a universal by a particular instance, and the safeguarding against a regress.

Key Terms
النظر
إثبات الشيء بنفسه
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that proving a thing by itself is always logically valid or that it only involves a circularity, not a contradiction.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that proving a thing by itself leads to a concrete contradiction: the thing must be both known and unknown in the same state, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Razi identify as the core problem with proving nazar by nazar?
A) The proof is not sound.
B) It leads to a trivial circularity.
C) It entails that nazar is both known and unknown at the same time.
D) It requires an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Proving nazar by nazar is impossible because it creates a contradiction: nazar must be both known (as a tool) and not known (as an objective) in the same act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to prove a foundational belief using that same belief as evidence? In what area of your life are you using self-referential reasoning, and what contradiction might it be hiding?
?
Al-Razi shows that seeking knowledge while assuming the very tool you seek can create a contradiction. Where in your pursuit of truth are you assuming the method before proving it? Will you accept that some things must be known by necessity?
?
If rational consideration itself cannot be proven rationally, what does that mean for your own most certain beliefs? Are they based on necessity or on a fragile self-made proof that could collapse under scrutiny?

The Response: Reframing the Proof of Nazar
The commentator answers al-Razi by distinguishing proving a thing by itself from proving a universal through a particular instance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's response to al-Razi: that proving nazar by nazar is not a case of proving a thing by itself but rather proving a universal proposition about nazar through a particular instance.

Core Idea
The proof concerns a universal statement about nazar, not nazar itself.

Having presented al-Razi's objection, the commentator now offers a reply. He clarifies that Imam al-Haramayn does not concede that proving nazar by nazar is self-referential; rather, the proof concerns a universal proposition about all sound nazar, established via a specific particular nazar. This lesson unpacks this subtle distinction and the role of aspects (haythiyyat) in avoiding contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the response and its verification
Read from 'والجواب أنه' to the end of the passage. Notice the commentator's claim: Imam al-Haramayn prevents the characterization as 'proving a thing by itself'.
Key phrase: 'إنما يمنع كون إثبات النظر بالنظر إثباتاً للشيء بنفسه'.
2
Understand the two formulations: universal and indefinite
The commentator says we prove either a universal proposition (كل نظر صحيح) or an indefinite proposition (النظر قد يفيد العلم). These are not self-referential; they are claims about nazar in general.
The proof is done 'بمشخصة' (by a singular proposition), i.e., by observing a specific sound consideration.
3
Analyze the role of aspects (haythiyyat)
The text explains that the two aspects (being sought vs being an instrument) are for explanation, not restriction. They do not create a contradiction because they do not impose conditions on the same state.
This defends against al-Razi's charge of contradiction.
4
Synthesize the complete answer
Combine the pieces: the proof is not self-referential; it is a proof by a particular instance; and the aspects do not generate a real contradiction. Thus, the original argument stands.
The response successfully refutes al-Razi's objection without resorting to necessity.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : النظر الصحيح عند الجمهور
وأنكر عليه الإمام الرازي) في النهاية ( فقال : إن إثبات الشيء بنفسه يقتضي أن يعلم به قبل نفسه ليمكن إثباته به ) وذلك يستلزم أن يعلم حين ما لا يعلم وتلخيصه أنه من وجه، كما أن : حيث هو مطلوب يجب أن لا يكون حاصلاً حال الطلب، ومن حيث أنه آلة من الطلب يجب أن يكون حاصلا في تلك الحال ( وهو تناقض ) قال : فبطل ما توهموه أن نفي الشيء بنفسه تناقض لاجتماع نفيه وإثباته معا بخلاف إثبات الشيء بنفسه، إذ لا تناقض فيه أصلاً ، فظهر أن إثبات كل النظر بالنظر يشتمل على تناقض من نفي كل النظر بالنظر متناقض من وجه آخر فلا مخلص إلا في دعوى الضرورة كما لخصناها ( والجواب أنه ( أي إمام الحرمين ( إنما يمنع كون إثبات كون النظر بالنظر إثباتاً للشيء بنفسه لا أنه يسلم ذلك ويمنع كونه تناقضاً) حتى يتجه عليه ذلك الإنكار ) وتحقيقه ) أي تحقيق ما ذكرناه من أن إثبات النظر بالنظر ليس إثباتا للشيء بنفسه وإن أوهمته العبارة ( أنا نثبت القضية الكلية القائلة كل نظر صحيح في القطعيات لا يعقبه ما ينافي العلم فإنه يفيده ) أو المهملة ) القائلة : النظر قد يفيد العلم ( على اختلاف التحريرين بمشخصة ) أي بقضية شخصية حكم فيها بقوله لاستلزامه كون الشيء معلوماً حين ما ليس معلوماً، وإن ما نقله عن الإمام إعادة لذلك فالصواب أن يقال : في شرح قوله تناقض كنفي الشيء بنفسه، ثم يحرر كلام إمام الحرمين بأنه لا تناقض في إثبات الشيء بنفسه لأنه إنما يقتضي ثبوت الشيء فقط بخلاف نفيه بنفسه، فإنه يستلزم انتفاء الشيء وثبوته معاً، وإنه تناقض ثم يورد عليه إنكار الإمام بأنه وإن لم يكن في إثبات الشي بنفسه التناقض الذي في نفي الشيء بنفسه، إلا أنه يستلزم تناقضاً آخر وهو أن يكون الشيء معلوماً، وأن لا يكون معلوماً في حالة واحدة . قوله: (وتلخيصه أنه إلخ) الحيثيات للتعليل لا للتقييد فلا يرد منع التناقض لاختلاف الحيثيتين.
قوله : ( على تناقض من وجه) وهو أن يكون معلوماً وأن لا يكون معلوماً في حالة واحدة .
قوله : ( من وجه آخر وهو أن يكون النظر ثابتاً ومنتفياً .
قوله : ( وإن أوهمته العبارة ) أعني قولنا إثبات النظر بالنظر . قوله : (أي بقضية شخصية أي وهي أن هذا النظر مفيد للعلم فإن قيل : إثبات الكلية والمهملة إذا كانت بنظر مخصوص كان الإثبات بنفس ذلك النظر الجزئي لأنه مفيد للعلم، قلت : إثباتها بذلك النظر متوقف على صحة مقدماته واستلزامه لها وهو معنى الإفادة، فيكون إثباتها موقوفاً على قولنا هذا النظر مفيد للعلم.
قوله: (وتلخيصه أنه من حيث هو مطلوب إلخ ) الحيثيتان المذكورتان للتعليل لا للتقييد فلا ينافيان التناقض .
The Fifth Observation - Third Objective: Sound Consideration (al-nazar al-sahih) according to the Majority. And Imam al-Razi objected to him in al-Nihaya saying: Surely proving a thing by itself requires that it be known by it before itself so that it can be proven by it, and that entails that it be known at the time when it is not known, and its summary: it is from one aspect, just as: where it is sought, it must not be obtained at the time of seeking, and from the aspect that it is an instrument of seeking, it must be obtained at that moment—and that is a contradiction. He said: so what they imagined—that negating a thing by itself is a contradiction due to the conjunction of its negation and affirmation together, unlike proving a thing by itself, for there is no contradiction in it at all—is false. So it became evident that proving all consideration by consideration includes a contradiction from one aspect; negating all consideration by consideration is contradictory from another aspect, so there is no escape except in claiming necessity (al-darura) as we summarized it. And the answer: He (i.e., Imam al-Haramayn) only prevents that proving consideration by consideration is proving a thing by itself, not that he concedes that and prevents its being a contradiction, so that that objection would apply to him. And its verification: (i.e., verification of what we mentioned, that proving consideration by consideration is not proving a thing by itself, even if the expression suggests it) is that we prove the universal proposition (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya) stating: every sound consideration (nazar sahih) in certainties (al-qat'iyyat) is not followed by what contradicts knowledge, for it yields it; or the particular indefinite proposition (al-muhmala) stating: consideration may yield knowledge, according to the difference of the two formulations, by a singular proposition (bi-mushakhkhasa), i.e., a particular proposition in which it is judged by his statement—due to its entailing that the thing be known when it is not known. And what he transmitted from al-Imam is a repetition of that, so the correct thing to say is: in explaining his statement 'contradiction like negating a thing by itself', then to refine the statement of Imam al-Haramayn: there is no contradiction in proving a thing by itself because it only entails the existence of the thing only, unlike negating it by itself, for it entails the negation and existence of the thing together, and that is contradiction. Then the objection of al-Imam is brought against him: even if there is not in proving a thing by itself the contradiction that is in negating a thing by itself, yet it entails another contradiction: that the thing be known and not be known in one state. His statement: (and its summary: it is, etc.): the two aspects (al-haythiyyat) are for explanation (al-ta'lil), not for restriction (al-taqyid), so the prevention of contradiction due to the difference of the two aspects does not apply. His statement: (upon a contradiction from one aspect): i.e., that it be known and not be known in one state. His statement: (from another aspect): i.e., that consideration be established and negated. His statement: (even if the expression suggests it): i.e., our statement 'proving consideration by consideration'. His statement: (i.e., by a singular proposition): i.e., it is that this consideration is productive of knowledge. For if it is said: The proof of the universal and the indefinite, if it is by a specific consideration, then the proof is by that particular consideration itself because it is productive of knowledge, I say: Its proof by that consideration depends on the validity of its premises and its entailment of them, and that is the meaning of production, so its proof is dependent on our statement 'this consideration is productive of knowledge'. His statement: (and its summary: it is from the aspect it is sought, etc.): the two mentioned aspects (al-haythiyyat) are for explanation, not for restriction, so they do not conflict with the contradiction.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer in the classical kalam debate about the soundness of rational consideration (nazar). The subheading identifies it as the third objective of the fifth observation: the nature of sound consideration according to the majority. Imam al-Razi objects to the claim that consideration can be proven by consideration itself, arguing that this leads to a contradiction: the thing (consideration) would need to be known before itself to serve as proof, which is impossible. He contrasts this with negating a thing by itself, which is a clear contradiction because it combines affirmation and negation; proving a thing by itself, however, involves no contradiction of that sort, yet it still entails another contradiction—the thing being known and not known simultaneously. The commentator then provides the answer: Imam al-Haramayn does not concede that proving consideration by consideration is proving a thing by itself; rather, he prevents that characterization. The proper account is that we prove a universal proposition about consideration (e.g., 'every sound consideration in certainties yields knowledge') not by the very act of consideration as a self-referential proof, but through a specific particular consideration. The commentary explains that the apparent contradiction is avoided because the two aspects (being sought and being an instrument) are reasons for the judgment, not restrictive conditions. The argument is refined through multiple layers: the distinction between proving a thing by itself (which is not actually intended here) and proving a universal by a particular instance, and the safeguarding against a regress.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the commentator concedes to al-Razi and avoids the contradiction by a mere verbal trick.
Correction: The commentator provides a substantive distinction: the proof is not self-referential but rather a proof of a universal claim via a particular instance, which is logically sound.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is actually being proven when one 'proves nazar by nazar'?
A) The existence of nazar itself.
B) A universal or indefinite proposition about nazar, using a specific instance.
C) That nazar is self-evident.
D) That al-Razi's objection is invalid because it is based on a misunderstanding.

Key Takeaway
The proof of nazar's validity is not a self-referential proof but a justified generalization from a particular instance, and the aspects involved are explanatory, not restrictive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present arguments for your faith, do you ever rely on premises that assume the very conclusion you seek to prove? The commentator warns against such circularity. Where in your life might you be doing this unconsciously? Commit to examining one argument you often use and ensure its premises are independent.
?
The lesson teaches that 'aspects' are explanatory, not restrictive—they should not be used as an easy way to ignore real contradictions. Is there a tension in your beliefs that you have dismissed by saying 'different aspects' without seeking a deeper resolution? What step will you take this week to honestly address it?
?
You have just seen a refined logical defense of the foundation of reasoning (nazar). Are you willing to subject your own most foundational beliefs to the same kind of scrutiny? What holds you back from doing so? Write a concrete action you will take to investigate one foundational belief, even if it feels uncomfortable.

Establishing the Universal Claim: Sound Nazar Yields Knowledge
The commentator proves the universal proposition about sound nazar with two formulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator demonstrates that every sound rational consideration (nazar) in certainties necessarily produces knowledge through two formulations relying on self-evident premises.

Core Idea
Sound nazar yields knowledge: certain premises plus valid form guarantee a certain result.

This lesson introduces the foundational proof that every sound rational consideration in matters of certainty leads to knowledge. The commentator presents two equivalent formulations, both reducing to two self-evident propositions. Understanding this argument is essential for grasping the role of nazar in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the first formulation
Read from 'قطعاً، على جزئي معين' to 'وهذا معنى قولنا كل نظري قطعي المادة والصورة مفيد للعلم'. Notice how the result is said to be necessarily true because of certain premises and entailment.
The first formulation explicitly states the result in every syllogistic nazar is definitely true.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument reduces to a minor premise (sughra) and a major premise (kubra). Find them: the minor is that sound nazar includes certain premises and entailment; the major is that such a nazar yields knowledge.
3
Examine the second formulation
Read from 'وقد يقال بعبارة أخرى' to 'أفاد لنا العلم بأن كل نظر صحيح'. Note how it rephrases the same idea in terms of 'aspect of indication' and 'absence of obstacles'.
Both formulations end with the same summary about two self-evident propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطعاً، على جزئي معين من أفراد النظر، فنقول : النتيجة في كل نظر قياسي معلوم الصحة مادة وصورة لازمة لزوماً قطعياً لما قطعاً، هو حق وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حق فالنتيجة في كل قياس صحيح حقة قطعاً، وهذا معنى قولنا كل نظري قطعي المادة والصورة مفيد للعلم، أما الصغرى فإذ لا معنى للعلم بصحة المادة والصورة إلا القطع بحقية المقدمات وحقية استلزامها للنتيجة، وأما الكبرى فبديهية لا شبهة فيها، وقد يقال بعبارة أخرى : هكذا كل نظر صحيح في القطعيات لا يعقبه مناف للعلم يشتمل على ما يقتضي العلم مع عدم المانع وكل ما هو مشتمل على مقتضى العلم مع انتفاء المانع يفيد العلم ويستلزمه أما الصغرى فلأن النظر الصحيح ما ينطوي على جهة الدلالة أعني العلاقة العقلية الموجبة للانتقال إلى المطلوب، وقد اعتبرنا معه ارتفاع المانع، وأما الكبرى فلامتناع تخلف الشيء عن المقتضى مع ارتفاع المانع، وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان بديهيتان إذا نظرنا فيهما أفاد لنا العلم بأن كل نظر صحيح
قوله: (أما الصغرى إلخ) استدلال على حقيتها بأنها بديهية لأن تصور طرفيها كاف في الحكم، وكل بديهي فهو حق، وكذا قوله وأما الكبرى إلخ، وزاد قوله لا شبهة فيها إشارة إلى أنها بديهية لا خفاء فيها أصلاً باعتبار الحكم، ولا باعتبار الطرفين بخلاف الصغرى، فإن فيها خفاء باعتبار الطرفين وبما ذكرنا ظهر أن الاعتراض بأن الاستدلال على الصغرى والكبرى ينافي دعوى بداهتهما المستفادة من قوله، وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان إلخ والجواب بأن الاستدلال المذكور تنبيه أو تعليل لمي، والبديهي قد يكون نظرياً من حيث لميته كلام منشؤه عدم التدبر فتدبر. قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ ) مجمل الجواب أن هاهنا قضيتان بديهيتان بأي عبارة عبرنا بهما، إذا رتبناهما ترتيباً مخصوصاً يفيد ذلك الترتيب العلم بتلك القضية الكلية أو المهملة، فلا يكون إثبات الشيء بنفسه.
قوله : ( فنقول النتيجة في كل نظر قياسي إلخ) فإن قلت : معنى قولنا النظر يفيد العلم أنه يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة فمن ينكر استلزام النظر الصحيح للنتيجة كيف يسلم لزوم النتيجة؟ قلت : المنكر هو استلزام النظر للعلم بالنتيجة والمذكور هو استلزام المقدمات للنتيجة، والفرق ظاهر وبالجملة عنوان العلمية يلاحظ هاهنا في اللازم ولا عنوان النظر فقط في جانب الملزوم فلا إشكال فتأمل .
قوله : ( وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان (بدیهیتان قيل دعوى بداهتهما ينافي الصغرى والكبرى، اللهم إلا أن يقال: ما ذكر تنبيه فإن قلت قوله في التحرير الأول، وأما الكبرى فبديهية لا شبهة فيها يدل على نظرية الصغرى قلت بل أراد به الكبرى بديهية لا تحتاج إلى التنبيه كما دل عليه قوله : لا شبهة فيها، ويمكن أن يقال أيضاً البديهي قد يكون نظرياً نظراً إلى لميته كما صرح به في شرح المقاصد .
Definitely, upon a specific particular from the individuals of consideration (nazar), we say: The result in every syllogistic consideration (nazar qiyasi) known to be sound in matter and form is necessarily consequent upon what is definitely true, and everything that is thus is true, so the result in every sound syllogism is definitely true, and this is the meaning of our statement: every theoretical thing definite in matter and form is productive of knowledge. As for the minor premise (al-sughra), since there is no meaning to knowledge of the soundness of matter and form except the certainty of the truth of the premises and the certainty of their entailment of the result. And as for the major premise (al-kubra), it is self-evident (badihiyya), with no doubt in it. And it may be said in another formulation: thus, every sound consideration in certainties (qat'iyyat) is not followed by what contradicts knowledge; it comprises what necessitates knowledge along with the absence of an obstacle; and everything that comprises what necessitates knowledge along with the removal of an obstacle yields knowledge and entails it. As for the minor premise, because sound consideration is that which contains the aspect of indication (jiha al-dalala), i.e., the rational relation (al-'alaqa al-'aqliyya) that necessitates transition to the sought; and we have considered along with it the removal of the obstacle. And as for the major premise, because of the impossibility of the thing lagging behind the efficient cause (al-muqtada) when the obstacle is removed. In summary, here there are two self-evident propositions (qadiyatani badihiyyatani); when we consider them, they yield for us knowledge that every sound consideration... His statement: (As for the minor premise, etc.) is an inference to its truth by its being self-evident, because the conception of its two terms is sufficient for judgment, and every self-evident thing is true. Similarly his statement: 'And as for the major premise, etc.', and he added his statement 'no doubt in it' as an indication that it is self-evident with no obscurity at all in terms of judgment, nor in terms of the two terms, unlike the minor premise, for there is obscurity in it in terms of the two terms. And from what we mentioned, it appears that the objection that the inference of the minor and major contradicts the claim of their self-evidence, derived from his statement 'in summary, here are two propositions, etc.', and the answer is that the mentioned inference is a reminder (tanbih) or a causal explanation (ta'lil limi), and the self-evident may be theoretical from the perspective of its causal aspect (limiyya). All this originates from lack of reflection, so reflect. His statement: (And in summary, etc.) is the summary of the answer: here are two self-evident propositions; whichever expression we express them by, if we arrange them in a specific arrangement, that arrangement yields knowledge of that universal or indefinite proposition, so it is not proving a thing by itself. His statement: (So we say: the result in every syllogistic consideration, etc.): For if you say: the meaning of our statement 'consideration yields knowledge' is that it entails knowledge of the result; so whoever denies that sound consideration entails the result, how does he concede the entailment of the result? I say: the denial is of the entailment of consideration for knowledge of the result, while what is mentioned is the entailment of the premises for the result, and the difference is clear. In summary, the aspect of knowledge (al-'ilmiyya) is observed here in the necessary (al-lazim), and the aspect of consideration only is not in the side of the necessitating (al-malzum), so there is no problem, so reflect. His statement: (And in summary, here are two self-evident propositions): It is said that the claim of their self-evidence contradicts the minor and major, unless it is said: what was mentioned is a reminder. For if you say: his statement in the first formulation 'and as for the major premise, it is self-evident, no doubt in it' indicates that the minor premise is theoretical, I say: rather he meant by it that the major premise is self-evident, not needing a reminder, as indicated by his statement 'no doubt in it'. And it can also be said: the self-evident may be theoretical in terms of its causal aspect (limiyya), as was stated in Sharh al-Maqasid.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a detailed demonstration and defense of the claim that every sound rational consideration (nazar) in matters of certainty (qat'iyyat) yields knowledge ('ilm). The commentator begins by stating that the result (natija) in any syllogistic consideration that is sound in matter (content) and form is necessarily true, because if the premises are certainly true and the entailment is certain, the result is certainly true. This is the meaning of the phrase 'every theoretical matter definite in matter and form is productive of knowledge.' Two formulations of the proof are given: one focusing on the certainty of the premises and entailment, the other focusing on the presence of an indicator (jiha al-dalala) and the absence of obstacles. In both formulations, the argument reduces to two self-evident (badihiyya) premises: the minor premise (sughra)—that sound consideration includes the indicator or that the premises are certain—and the major premise (kubra)—that whatever necessitates knowledge in the absence of obstacles yields knowledge. The commentator then addresses an objection: that explaining these premises as self-evident contradicts the fact that they are being argued for. He responds that the demonstration is not a real proof but a reminder (tanbih) or a causal explanation (ta'lil limi), and that self-evident propositions can be theoretical from the perspective of their causal aspect (limiyya). Another potential confusion is that the entailment of the result is from the premises, not from the consideration itself, so no circularity arises. The passage concludes by noting that the two propositions are self-evident regardless of formulation, and their arrangement produces knowledge of the universal claim without proving a thing by itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof is circular because it relies on self-evident premises that are themselves argued for.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the premises are self-evident; the argument is a reminder (tanbih) or causal explanation, not a proof that the premises are true.

Checkpoint
According to the first formulation, what is the result in every syllogistic consideration that is sound in matter and form?
A) It is probably true.
B) It is definitely true.
C) It is unknown.
D) It is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The commentator establishes that sound nazar yields knowledge by reducing the argument to two self-evident propositions: the minor premise that sound nazar includes the necessary conditions, and the major premise that these conditions guarantee knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The second formulation emphasizes the absence of obstacles. What obstacles in your heart or mind (pride, laziness, attachment to a certain view) prevent you from gaining knowledge through sound reasoning? Will you commit to removing them starting today?

Defending the Self-Evidence of the Premises
The commentator defends the self-evidence of the two premises and refutes objections about circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the self-evidence claim and how the commentator defends it using the concepts of tanbih (reminder) and ta'lil limi (causal explanation).

Core Idea
Self-evident premises can be explained without circularity through causal reminders.

After presenting the proof, the commentator addresses potential objections. If the two premises are truly self-evident, why does he argue for them? And does the entailment come from nazar itself or from the premises? This lesson clarifies these points and deepens our understanding of the epistemological status of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read from 'قوله: (أما الصغرى إلخ)' to 'فلا يكون إثبات الشيء بنفسه'. Identify the objection that arguing for self-evident premises contradicts their self-evidence, and the answer that it is a reminder or causal explanation.
The commentator distinguishes between the judgment being self-evident and the causal explanation being theoretical.
2
Understand the distinction about entailment
Read from 'قوله : ( فنقول النتيجة في كل نظر قياسي إلخ)' to 'فلا إشكال فتأمل'. Notice the distinction: the entailment is from the premises, not from nazar itself, so no circularity.
The objector confuses two different relations.
3
Review the final clarifications
Read the last paragraph about the two self-evident propositions again. Reflect on how the two formulations are consistent and how the commentator resolves the apparent contradiction.
The commentator reaffirms that the self-evident can be explained causally without losing its self-evidence.
