قطعاً، على جزئي معين من أفراد النظر، فنقول : النتيجة في كل نظر قياسي معلوم الصحة مادة وصورة لازمة لزوماً قطعياً لما قطعاً، هو حق وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حق فالنتيجة في كل قياس صحيح حقة قطعاً، وهذا معنى قولنا كل نظري قطعي المادة والصورة مفيد للعلم، أما الصغرى فإذ لا معنى للعلم بصحة المادة والصورة إلا القطع بحقية المقدمات وحقية استلزامها للنتيجة، وأما الكبرى فبديهية لا شبهة فيها، وقد يقال بعبارة أخرى : هكذا كل نظر صحيح في القطعيات لا يعقبه مناف للعلم يشتمل على ما يقتضي العلم مع عدم المانع وكل ما هو مشتمل على مقتضى العلم مع انتفاء المانع يفيد العلم ويستلزمه أما الصغرى فلأن النظر الصحيح ما ينطوي على جهة الدلالة أعني العلاقة العقلية الموجبة للانتقال إلى المطلوب، وقد اعتبرنا معه ارتفاع المانع، وأما الكبرى فلامتناع تخلف الشيء عن المقتضى مع ارتفاع المانع، وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان بديهيتان إذا نظرنا فيهما أفاد لنا العلم بأن كل نظر صحيح
قوله: (أما الصغرى إلخ) استدلال على حقيتها بأنها بديهية لأن تصور طرفيها كاف في الحكم، وكل بديهي فهو حق، وكذا قوله وأما الكبرى إلخ، وزاد قوله لا شبهة فيها إشارة إلى أنها بديهية لا خفاء فيها أصلاً باعتبار الحكم، ولا باعتبار الطرفين بخلاف الصغرى، فإن فيها خفاء باعتبار الطرفين وبما ذكرنا ظهر أن الاعتراض بأن الاستدلال على الصغرى والكبرى ينافي دعوى بداهتهما المستفادة من قوله، وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان إلخ والجواب بأن الاستدلال المذكور تنبيه أو تعليل لمي، والبديهي قد يكون نظرياً من حيث لميته كلام منشؤه عدم التدبر فتدبر. قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ ) مجمل الجواب أن هاهنا قضيتان بديهيتان بأي عبارة عبرنا بهما، إذا رتبناهما ترتيباً مخصوصاً يفيد ذلك الترتيب العلم بتلك القضية الكلية أو المهملة، فلا يكون إثبات الشيء بنفسه.
قوله : ( فنقول النتيجة في كل نظر قياسي إلخ) فإن قلت : معنى قولنا النظر يفيد العلم أنه يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة فمن ينكر استلزام النظر الصحيح للنتيجة كيف يسلم لزوم النتيجة؟ قلت : المنكر هو استلزام النظر للعلم بالنتيجة والمذكور هو استلزام المقدمات للنتيجة، والفرق ظاهر وبالجملة عنوان العلمية يلاحظ هاهنا في اللازم ولا عنوان النظر فقط في جانب الملزوم فلا إشكال فتأمل .
قوله : ( وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان (بدیهیتان قيل دعوى بداهتهما ينافي الصغرى والكبرى، اللهم إلا أن يقال: ما ذكر تنبيه فإن قلت قوله في التحرير الأول، وأما الكبرى فبديهية لا شبهة فيها يدل على نظرية الصغرى قلت بل أراد به الكبرى بديهية لا تحتاج إلى التنبيه كما دل عليه قوله : لا شبهة فيها، ويمكن أن يقال أيضاً البديهي قد يكون نظرياً نظراً إلى لميته كما صرح به في شرح المقاصد .
Definitely, upon a specific particular from the individuals of consideration (nazar), we say: The result in every syllogistic consideration (nazar qiyasi) known to be sound in matter and form is necessarily consequent upon what is definitely true, and everything that is thus is true, so the result in every sound syllogism is definitely true, and this is the meaning of our statement: every theoretical thing definite in matter and form is productive of knowledge. As for the minor premise (al-sughra), since there is no meaning to knowledge of the soundness of matter and form except the certainty of the truth of the premises and the certainty of their entailment of the result. And as for the major premise (al-kubra), it is self-evident (badihiyya), with no doubt in it. And it may be said in another formulation: thus, every sound consideration in certainties (qat'iyyat) is not followed by what contradicts knowledge; it comprises what necessitates knowledge along with the absence of an obstacle; and everything that comprises what necessitates knowledge along with the removal of an obstacle yields knowledge and entails it. As for the minor premise, because sound consideration is that which contains the aspect of indication (jiha al-dalala), i.e., the rational relation (al-'alaqa al-'aqliyya) that necessitates transition to the sought; and we have considered along with it the removal of the obstacle. And as for the major premise, because of the impossibility of the thing lagging behind the efficient cause (al-muqtada) when the obstacle is removed. In summary, here there are two self-evident propositions (qadiyatani badihiyyatani); when we consider them, they yield for us knowledge that every sound consideration... His statement: (As for the minor premise, etc.) is an inference to its truth by its being self-evident, because the conception of its two terms is sufficient for judgment, and every self-evident thing is true. Similarly his statement: 'And as for the major premise, etc.', and he added his statement 'no doubt in it' as an indication that it is self-evident with no obscurity at all in terms of judgment, nor in terms of the two terms, unlike the minor premise, for there is obscurity in it in terms of the two terms. And from what we mentioned, it appears that the objection that the inference of the minor and major contradicts the claim of their self-evidence, derived from his statement 'in summary, here are two propositions, etc.', and the answer is that the mentioned inference is a reminder (tanbih) or a causal explanation (ta'lil limi), and the self-evident may be theoretical from the perspective of its causal aspect (limiyya). All this originates from lack of reflection, so reflect. His statement: (And in summary, etc.) is the summary of the answer: here are two self-evident propositions; whichever expression we express them by, if we arrange them in a specific arrangement, that arrangement yields knowledge of that universal or indefinite proposition, so it is not proving a thing by itself. His statement: (So we say: the result in every syllogistic consideration, etc.): For if you say: the meaning of our statement 'consideration yields knowledge' is that it entails knowledge of the result; so whoever denies that sound consideration entails the result, how does he concede the entailment of the result? I say: the denial is of the entailment of consideration for knowledge of the result, while what is mentioned is the entailment of the premises for the result, and the difference is clear. In summary, the aspect of knowledge (al-'ilmiyya) is observed here in the necessary (al-lazim), and the aspect of consideration only is not in the side of the necessitating (al-malzum), so there is no problem, so reflect. His statement: (And in summary, here are two self-evident propositions): It is said that the claim of their self-evidence contradicts the minor and major, unless it is said: what was mentioned is a reminder. For if you say: his statement in the first formulation 'and as for the major premise, it is self-evident, no doubt in it' indicates that the minor premise is theoretical, I say: rather he meant by it that the major premise is self-evident, not needing a reminder, as indicated by his statement 'no doubt in it'. And it can also be said: the self-evident may be theoretical in terms of its causal aspect (limiyya), as was stated in Sharh al-Maqasid.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a detailed demonstration and defense of the claim that every sound rational consideration (nazar) in matters of certainty (qat'iyyat) yields knowledge ('ilm). The commentator begins by stating that the result (natija) in any syllogistic consideration that is sound in matter (content) and form is necessarily true, because if the premises are certainly true and the entailment is certain, the result is certainly true. This is the meaning of the phrase 'every theoretical matter definite in matter and form is productive of knowledge.' Two formulations of the proof are given: one focusing on the certainty of the premises and entailment, the other focusing on the presence of an indicator (jiha al-dalala) and the absence of obstacles. In both formulations, the argument reduces to two self-evident (badihiyya) premises: the minor premise (sughra)—that sound consideration includes the indicator or that the premises are certain—and the major premise (kubra)—that whatever necessitates knowledge in the absence of obstacles yields knowledge. The commentator then addresses an objection: that explaining these premises as self-evident contradicts the fact that they are being argued for. He responds that the demonstration is not a real proof but a reminder (tanbih) or a causal explanation (ta'lil limi), and that self-evident propositions can be theoretical from the perspective of their causal aspect (limiyya). Another potential confusion is that the entailment of the result is from the premises, not from the consideration itself, so no circularity arises. The passage concludes by noting that the two propositions are self-evident regardless of formulation, and their arrangement produces knowledge of the universal claim without proving a thing by itself.

لمية
الملزوم
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that arguing for self-evident premises is circular or contradictory.
Correction: The commentator explains that such arguments can be reminders (tanbih) or causal explanations (ta'lil limi), which do not undermine self-evidence.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator answer the objection that inferring the minor and major premises contradicts their self-evidence?
A) He admits the premises are not self-evident.
B) He says the inference is a reminder (tanbih) or causal explanation (ta'lil limi).
C) He argues that self-evident propositions cannot be explained.
D) He changes the formulation of the premises.

Key Takeaway
The commentator defends the self-evidence of the two premises by clarifying that the inference is a reminder or causal explanation, not a proof, and that the entailment is from premises, not from nazar itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone challenges a belief that you hold as self-evident, do you immediately launch into a proof, or do you first clarify that the belief is foundational? How might the commentator's approach of 'reminder' change your defense of your own convictions?
?
Have you ever defended a basic tenet of your faith with arguments that inadvertently assumed the very point you were trying to prove? What would it take for you to distinguish between a genuine proof and a mere reminder?
?
Are there areas in your life where you confuse the act of thinking (nazar) with the content of what is known? How can you ensure that your reliance on reasoning does not lead you to circularity but rather to clearer understanding?

Classification of Deniers and Their Doubts
Commentary on the classification of deniers and their doubts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the doubts of deniers are not uniform; the earlier doubt was common to all deniers, but later doubts are specific to particular groups.

Core Idea
Not all deniers share the same doubt — each sect has its own specific challenge.

After presenting a doubt about the value of rational inquiry (nazar), the commentator clarifies a methodological point: that earlier doubt was intended for all deniers collectively, while the upcoming doubts target specific groups. This passage shows how the author organizes his refutations by recognizing that each sect's denial stems from a different premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice how the commentator distinguishes between the common doubt and the specific ones.
Focus on the phrase 'والمنكرون طوائف' as it sets up the classification.
2
Identify the two categories of doubts
The passage states that the previous doubt was one doubt for all deniers, but the coming doubts are specific. Understand why the commentator corrects the assumption that one doubt could be shared among all.
The correction is based on the fact that deniers have different positions, so a single doubt cannot apply to all.
3
Understand the reason for separation
The commentator explains that the answer to the previous doubt required precision and verification, so it was separated from the other doubts. This shows the author's careful methodology.
The previous doubt involved proving a thing by itself, which needed special treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والمنكرون طوائف) سياق كلامه مشعر بأن ما تقدم شبهة واحدة للمنكرين بأسرهم، وما سيأتي من الشبه مخصوصة بقوم دون قوم والصواب أن اشتراك شبهة واحدة فيما بينهم غير متصور، وأن ما سبق شبهة للمنكرين بالكلية أعني السمنية ألا ترى إلى قوله : فقيل قولكم لا شيء من النظر بمفيد وإلى أن هذه الشبهة في قوة أولى الشبه المنسوبة إليهم، فإن كون النظر مفيداً للعلم، وكون الاعتقاد الحاصل عقيبه علماً مؤداهما واحد ومدار الشبهتين على أن العلم بهما ليس ضرورياً، ولا نظرياً لكن لما كان الجواب عن لزوم إثبات الشيء بنفسه المذكور في الشبهة السابقة يشتمل على تدقيق وتحقيق أفرادها عن الشبه الأخر .
(And the deniers are groups) His expression indicates that what preceded was one doubt for all the deniers, and what will come of doubts are specific to one group, not another. And the correct view is that the sharing of one doubt among them is inconceivable, and that what preceded was a doubt for the deniers in general, I mean the Samaniyya. Do you not see his saying: 'For it was said: your statement that no sound reasoning yields knowledge' and that this doubt is in strength like the first of the doubts attributed to them. For that rational inquiry (nazar) is beneficial for knowledge, and that the belief occurring after it is knowledge, their implications are one, and the pivot of the two doubts is that knowledge of them is neither necessary nor theoretical. But because the answer to the necessity of proving a thing by itself mentioned in the previous doubt includes precision and verification, he separated it from the other doubts.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a methodological classification. The commentator observes that the author's wording indicates that the previously mentioned doubt (about the definition of nazar) was common to all deniers, while the upcoming doubts are specific to particular groups. He then corrects a possible misunderstanding: it is not possible for one single doubt to be shared among all deniers because their positions differ. The correct view is that the previous doubt was for the deniers in general, meaning the Samaniyya (or possibly others). He supports this by noting that the author said 'for it was said: your statement...' and that this doubt is equivalent in strength to the first doubt attributed to them. The commentator explains that both doubts revolve around the fact that the knowledge that nazar yields knowledge is neither necessary nor theoretical. However, because the answer to the previous doubt (which involved proving a thing by itself) was more precise and verified, the author separated it from the other doubts. The passage thus establishes the organizing principle of the subsequent discussion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all deniers of God share the same doubt or argument.
Correction: The commentator explains that the previous doubt was common only in a general sense (addressed to the Samaniyya), but in reality, each group of deniers has its own specific doubt, so a single doubt cannot apply uniformly to all.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why was the previous doubt separated from the other doubts?
A) Because it was refuted by the Samaniyya
B) Because it was common to all deniers and needed precise treatment
C) Because it was specific to the Samaniyya only
D) Because it was not a valid doubt at all

Key Takeaway
The doubt about nazar was common to the deniers in general (the Samaniyya), but later doubts are specific to particular groups, reflecting the author's careful methodological organization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who denies a truth of faith, do you assume their reason is the same as everyone else's? How does this lesson challenge you to listen carefully and understand the unique doubt each person holds before responding? Will you commit to doing that from now on?
?
Are you guilty of lumping all skeptics together, thinking they all have the same fundamental issue? How can you change that starting today, especially in your conversations with non-believers or those with doubts?
?
This lesson shows that even among deniers, there are distinct doubts. How does this affect your own heart? Are you sometimes dismissive of others' objections because you think you've already heard them all? What will you do to cultivate humility and genuine inquiry when faced with a challenge to your beliefs?

The Second Objection: Two Premises Cannot Coexist in the Mind and Its Refutation
Statement of second objection and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the claim that correct consideration yields knowledge, and how al-Sanusi refutes it by showing that two premises can coexist in the mind.

Core Idea
Two premises can coexist in the mind just as the two sides of a conditional must coexist for judgment.

This passage presents a key objection to the view that logical reasoning (nazar) can produce certain knowledge. The opponent argues that since the mind cannot attend to two judgments at once, two premises cannot coexist—so reasoning cannot yield knowledge. al-Sanusi refutes this by pointing to conditional statements, where both parts must be present in the mind simultaneously. Let's examine the argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second objection
Read the objection carefully: why does the opponent claim two premises cannot coexist in the mind? Identify the premise about attention and the conclusion that no consideration yields knowledge.
The objection relies on an intuitive observation about mental focus.
2
Examine the author's response
Notice how al-Sanusi denies the claim with a clear counterexample: the two sides of a conditional. Understand why they must coexist for a judgment of entailment or incompatibility.
This shows that coexistence of two premises in the mind is not only possible but necessary in some cases.
3
Reflect on the distinction between attention and knowledge
The author adds that direction (tawajjuh) is not the same as knowledge ('ilm). The impossibility of simultaneous attention does not imply impossibility of simultaneous knowledge.
This distinction is crucial for preserving the possibility of knowledge through reasoning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (المقدمتان إلخ) تقريرها لو كان النظر مفيداً للعلم لاجتمع المقدمتان اللتان وقع معلومية عدم المعارض، فحينئذ يمكن الجواب بأنه ينقطع التسلسل بانقطاع التوجه للتحصيل. الشبهة الثانية المقدمتان لا تجتمعان في الذهن معاً لأنا متى توجهنا إلى حكم مقصود امتنع منا في تلك الحالة التوجه إلى حكم (آخر بالوجدان)، وحينئذ لم يتحقق نظر مفيد للعلم إذ المقدمة الواحدة لا تنتج اتفاقا وهذه منقوضة بإفادة النظر للظن إذا كانت متفقا عليها بخلاف الشبهة الأولى والسابقة، فإن الظن الضروري قد يظهر خطؤه ، ويجوز اختلاف العقلاء فيه وتفاوته بالنسبة إلى ظن آخر ( قلنا : لا نسلم أنه لا يجتمع مقدمتان ) في الذهن بل قد يجتمعان ) وذلك كطرفي الشرطية فإنهما قضيتان يجب اجتماعهما في الذهن ( ولولا اجتماعهما فيه لامتنع الحكم بينهما بالتلازم ) أي اللزوم في المتصلات ) والعناد ) في المنفصلات ومنهم من فرق بأن طرفي الشرطية قضيتان بالقوة لا حكم بالفعل في شيء منهما بخلاف مقدمتي النظر ونحن نعلم بالضرورة أن الحكم في إحداهما لا يجامع الحكم في الأخرى دفعة، ثم أجاب فيهما النظر في الذهن والتالي باطل فالمقدم مثله، أما الملازمة فلأن الموصل مجموع المقدمتين دون إحداهما، وأما بطلان التالي فلان توجه النفس قصداً إلى حكمين في زمان واحد محال، وحاصل الجواب منع بطلان التالي مستنداً بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يجتمعا في الذهن؟ كاجتماع طرفي الشرطية، ولما كان منع المقدمة المدللة غير صحيح، أشار إلى أن منعها باعتبار أن دليلها غير مثبت لها فهي في الحقيقة غير مدللة، وذلك لأن التوجه غير العلم، ولا يلزم من امتناع اجتماع التوجهين امتناع اجتماع ،العلمين وهذا الطريق في المنع مذكور في شرح الطوالع للأصفهاني في مواضع كثيرة وما قيل : إن قوله قلنا : لا نسلم أنه لا يجتمع مقدمتان في جواب بطريق المعارضة حيث استدل على خلاف مدعى الخصم وقوله : والتوجه غير العلم إشارة إلى نقض مقدمة دليل الخصم وهي قوله : لأنا إذا توجهنا إلى آخره فبعيد لفظاً، لأن قوله : لا نسلم صريح في المنع ومعنى لأن الدليل أعني ،قوله، وذلك كطرفي الشرطية لا يثبت اجتماع المقدمتين، بل جواز الاجتماع. قوله : ( ومنهم من فرق بأن إلخ يعني أن السند المذكور لا يصلح للسندية لأن طرفي الذهن قوله : ( فإن الظن الضروري قد يظهر خطؤه إنما يتم القريب بهذا القول إذا كان مدعاهم ظنية هذا القول أعني كل نظر صحيح يفيد الظن وأما إذا كان المدعى قطعيته فلا تقريب له وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( ويجوز اختلاف العقلاء فيه على أن الاختلاف هاهنا ممنوع. :قوله قلنا لا نسلم أنه لا يجتمع إلخ) هذا جواب بطريق المعارضة حيث استدل على خلاف مدعى الخصم، وقوله : والتوجه إلخ إشارة إلى نقض مقدمة دليل الخصم، وهي قوله : لانا متى توجهنا إلخ . قوله: (ومنهم من فرق بأن إلخ) رد عليه بأن في طرفي الشرطية فرض الحكمين والتصديقين، وهو مستلزم لملاحظة الحكمين فيهما، فيجتمعان في العلم وإن لم يجتمعا في
His saying: ('The two premises, etc.') Its exposition: If consideration were productive of knowledge, then the two premises that are concerned with the known absence of an obstacle would have to coexist. Then it could be answered that the infinite regress is cut off by the cessation of attention for acquisition. The second objection: The two premises do not coexist together in the mind, because whenever we direct our attention to a particular judgment, we are prevented in that state from directing attention to another judgment, as found intuitively. Thus no consideration productive of knowledge is realized, since one premise does not produce a conclusion by agreement. And this is refuted by the fact that consideration produces opinion (ẓann) when the premises are agreed upon, unlike the first and previous objection, for necessary opinion may show its error, and rational individuals may differ in it and it varies relative to another opinion. We said: We do not concede that two premises cannot coexist in the mind; rather, they may coexist, like the two sides of a conditional, for they are two propositions that must coexist in the mind. Were they not to coexist, judgment between them of entailment (luzūm) in conjunctions and incompatibility (‘inād) in disjunctions would be impossible. And some differentiate that the two sides of a conditional are potential propositions with no actual judgment in either, unlike the two premises of consideration. And we know necessarily that the judgment in one does not coexist with the judgment in the other simultaneously. Then he answered concerning them: The consideration in the mind, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. As for the entailment, because what conveys is the sum of the two premises, not one of them. As for the falsity of the consequent, because the soul's intentional direction to two judgments at one time is impossible. The summary of the answer is denying the falsity of the consequent, based on: why is it not permissible for them to coexist in the mind, like the coexistence of the two sides of a conditional? And since denying the proven premise is not sound, he indicated that its denial is because its proof does not establish it, so it is in fact unproven. That is because direction (tawajjuh) is not knowledge, and from the impossibility of the coexistence of two directions, the impossibility of the coexistence of two knowledges does not follow. This method of denial is mentioned in Sharḥ al-Ṭawāli‘ by al-Iṣfahānī in many places. And what is said: That his saying 'We do not concede that two premises do not coexist' is an answer by way of opposition, where he adduced evidence contrary to the opponent's claim. And his saying: 'Direction is not knowledge' is an indication of refuting the premise of the opponent's evidence, namely his saying: 'For when we direct our attention, etc.' — but it is far-fetched in wording, because his saying 'We do not concede' is explicit in denial. And the meaning because the evidence, I mean his saying: 'like the two sides of a conditional', does not prove the coexistence of the two premises but rather the permissibility of coexistence. His saying: ('And some differentiate, etc.') means that the aforementioned support is not valid as support because the two sides of the mind... His saying: ('For necessary opinion may show its error') — this proximity is only completed with this statement if their claim is the opiniative nature of this statement, I mean 'every correct consideration yields opinion'. But if the claim is certain, then there is no proximity, and it is apparent. His saying: ('And rational individuals may differ in it') — but difference here is denied. His saying: 'We do not concede that two premises do not coexist, etc.') This is an answer by way of opposition, where he adduced evidence contrary to the opponent's claim. His saying: 'Direction, etc.' is an indication of refuting the premise of the opponent's evidence, namely his saying: 'For when we direct our attention, etc.' His saying: ('And some differentiate, etc.') is a response that in the two sides of a conditional there is supposition of the two judgments and the two acts of assent, which entails considering the two judgments in them, so they coexist in knowledge even if they do not coexist in...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (al-shubha al-thāniya) against the claim that correct consideration yields knowledge. The objection argues that two premises cannot coexist in the mind simultaneously because whenever one attends to one judgment, one cannot attend to another; thus a consideration cannot produce knowledge since one premise alone cannot produce a conclusion. The author responds by denying that two premises cannot coexist, using the example of the two sides of a conditional statement, which must coexist for a judgment of entailment or incompatibility to be made. He addresses a differentiation that the two sides of a conditional are only potentially judgments whereas premises are actual, but maintains that coexistence is possible in knowledge even if not in attention. The commentary includes multiple glosses clarifying that the opponent's claim about attention does not apply to knowledge itself, and that the argument is answered by opposing the premises or refuting the evidence. The discussion references al-Iṣfahānī's Sharḥ al-Ṭawāli‘.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the impossibility of focusing attention on two judgments simultaneously implies the impossibility of knowing them together.
Correction: The author explicitly distinguishes between attention (tawajjuh) and knowledge ('ilm). The inability to attend to two judgments at once does not entail the inability to know both, as knowledge does not require simultaneous active attention.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to argue that two premises can coexist in the mind?
A) Two sides of a conditional
B) Two separate mental acts
C) Memory and imagination
D) Perception and reasoning

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of dual attention does not entail the impossibility of dual knowledge; premises can coexist in the mind, as demonstrated by conditional logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are trying to understand a difficult truth, do you often feel overwhelmed by the need to hold multiple ideas at once? How does the author's example of a conditional statement change your view of your own mental capacity?
?
The author distinguishes between attention and knowledge. In your daily life, how often do you confuse being distracted from something with not knowing it? What would change if you truly believed you could know something even when not actively thinking about it?
?
This lesson shows that premises can coexist in the mind. What is one belief you hold about Allah that you know to be true even when you are not consciously thinking about it? How does that truth shape your actions when you do recall it?

The Sixth Objection: Prior Knowledge Is Conceptual, Not Assent
The author presents the sixth objection to nazar and replies that the required prior knowledge is conceptual (tasawwur) not assenting (tasdiq).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection to nazar and how the author refutes it by distinguishing conceptual knowledge from assent.

Core Idea
Prior knowledge needed for nazar is conceptual, not assenting.

The objector presses that depending on prior knowledge of the evidence's indication leads to a dilemma: either that prior knowledge is already known from the aspect sought (making the search impossible) or it is not known (making recognition of the conclusion impossible). The author resolves this by specifying that the required prior knowledge is merely conceptual—a grasp of the terms—not full assent. This lesson unpacks the objection and the author's reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and dilemma
Read the full passage once, then the translation. Notice how the objection presents two horns: either prior knowledge is known from the sought aspect or not.
The dilemma aims to show that nazar cannot produce new knowledge.
2
Identify the two horns
Focus on the first horn: if prior knowledge is known from the sought aspect, seeking it is impossible (obtaining the already obtained). Second horn: if unknown, you cannot recognize the conclusion when it arrives.
The objector claims both horns lead to absurdity.
3
See the author's choice and answer
The author chooses the second horn and denies its consequence: the conclusion can be recognized because the prior knowledge is conceptual (tasawwur), which distinguishes the sought object from others. When assent (tasdiq) is obtained, it is recognized as the sought conclusion.
The prior knowledge is a mental concept of the terms, not the judgment.
4
Understand the tolerance in defining dalaalah
The passage ends with a note that defining 'indication' as 'benefit of nazar' is a tolerance (tasamuh). The actual meaning is that the evidence leads to the conclusion. The elaboration is for clarity.
This clarifies the objector's language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة أن دلالة الدليل) أي إفادة النظر فيه العلم بالمدلول ( إن توقفت على العلم بدلالته عليه ) أي على ذلك المدلول ، فهو إما معلوم من الجهة التي يطلب بالنظر أو غير معلوم من تلك الجهة، والأول يستلزم امتناع أن يطلب بالنظر فضلا عن أن يفيده لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، والثاني يستلزم أن لا يعلم بعد الحصول أنه علم بالمطلوب، وبهذا ظهر أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : في إبطال الشق الثاني، فلا يطلب لامتناع التوجه إليه كما سبق في التصور . قوله : (هو معلوم إلخ ) جواب باختيار الشق الثاني ومنع قوله فإذا حصل لم يعرف أنه المطلوب لأنه معلوم من حيث التصور الذي به يمتاز عما عداه، وإذا حصل التصديق به علم أنه المطلوب، ولم يقل في الجواب أنه معلوم ظناً مطلوب يقيناً لعدم اطراده في جميع الصور. قوله : ( أي إفادة النظر فيه إلخ) لاخفاء في أن الدلالة صفة الدليل وإفادة النظر صفة النظر، يصح تعريف أحدهما بالآخر، والشارح في أمثال هذه العبارة يحمل الكلام على التسامح، فالمراد كون الدليل موصلاً إليه كما صرح به فيما بعد وإنما ارتكب التسامح بإقامة السبب مقام المسبب قطعاً، للإطناب في تقرير الشبهة فإنه لو حمل الدلالة على الإيصال يكون تقرير الشبهة هكذا، لو أفاد النظر في الدليل العلم لكان الدليل دالا عليه أي موصلا إليه، لأن إفادة النظر في الدليل للعلم يستلزم كونه موصلاً إليه، بخلاف ما إذا قيل لو أفاد النظر في الدليل العلم فإفادته إما أن تكون إلى آخره .
The sixth objection: that the indication of the evidence—i.e., the benefit of reflection (nazar) in it of knowledge of the indicated (al-madlool)—if it depends on knowledge of its indication of it, i.e., of that indicated, then it is either known from the aspect sought by reflection or not known from that aspect. The first entails the impossibility of seeking by reflection, let alone that it benefits, due to the impossibility of obtaining the already obtained; the second entails that after obtaining it, it would not be known that it is knowledge of the sought object. And by this it appears that it cannot be said in refutation of the second horn: 'It is not sought due to the impossibility of directing attention to it, as previously in conception (tasawwur).' His statement: 'It is known [conceptually], etc.' is an answer by choosing the second horn and denying his statement: 'Then if it is obtained, it would not be known that it is the sought object' because it is known from the aspect of conception (tasawwur) by which it is distinguished from others, and when the assent (tasdiq) to it is obtained, it is known that it is the sought object. And he did not say in the answer that it is known by opinion (zann) as the sought object with certainty, because that does not apply in all cases. His statement: 'i.e., the benefit of reflection in it, etc.' There is no obscurity that indication (dalaalah) is a quality of the evidence and the benefit of reflection is a quality of reflection; it is correct to define one by the other. And the commentator (al-sharih) in such expressions considers the speech as a tolerance (tasamuh). So the intended meaning is that the evidence leads to it, as he explained later. And he only committed this tolerance by substituting the cause for the effect absolutely, for the sake of elaboration in presenting the objection. Because if he had taken indication in the sense of leading, then the presentation of the objection would be thus: If reflection in the evidence benefits knowledge, then the evidence would be indicative of it, i.e., leading to it, because the benefit of reflection in the evidence for knowledge entails that it leads to it, unlike if it is said: 'If reflection in the evidence benefits knowledge, then its benefit is either ... to the end.'
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector argues that if the indication of the evidence depends on prior knowledge of its indication, we have a dilemma: either that prior knowledge is already known from the aspect sought (then seeking is impossible) or it is not known from that aspect (then we cannot recognize the conclusion when obtained). The author responds by choosing the second horn and denying that the conclusion would not be recognized, because the required prior knowledge is conceptual (tasawwur), not assenting (tasdiq). The seeker has a conceptual grasp of the two terms (subject and predicate) without affirming or denying the relation. When the assent is later obtained through nazar, it is recognized as the sought conclusion because it matches the conceptual framework. The author also explains why the answer was not that it is known by opinion (zann), because that does not cover all cases. Then the commentator clarifies that the definition of 'dalaalah' as 'benefit of nazar' is a tolerance (tasamuh) intended to elaborate the objection, and the actual intended meaning is that the evidence leads to the conclusion.

Misconception: Prior knowledge of the indication must be an assent (tasdiq) or full knowledge of the conclusion.
Correction: The author clarifies that the prior knowledge is conceptual (tasawwur)—a grasp of the terms and what is sought—which suffices to recognize the conclusion when assent is later obtained.

Checkpoint
In responding to the sixth objection, what type of prior knowledge does the author say suffices to recognize the sought conclusion?
A) Assent (tasdiq)
B) Conceptual knowledge (tasawwur)

Key Takeaway
The sixth objection is resolved by distinguishing conceptual knowledge from assent: prior knowledge of the indication is merely conceptual, not assenting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do I sometimes approach learning with a false sense of certainty, assuming I already know the answer before truly reflecting? How would my study change if I deliberately separated conceptual understanding from assent?
?
When I encounter a difficult argument, do I rush to assent or reject it without first clarifying my concepts? What would it cost me to patiently hold conceptual knowledge without judgment?
?
The objector's dilemma capitalizes on ambiguity in prior knowledge. In my own life, where might I be using an unclear 'prior knowledge' that prevents me from seeing the truth? Am I willing to name that ambiguity and seek conceptual clarity before demanding certainty?

The Circularity Objection Against Nazar
The author presents the objection of circularity and its two alternative formulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection raised against nazar and its two formulations.

Core Idea
Circularity arises if knowledge of the indication depends on knowledge of the indicated, and vice versa.

The objection of circularity (dawr) is a serious challenge to the validity of nazar. The opponent argues that the process of reasoning from evidence to conclusion involves a mutual dependence that makes the process invalid. This lesson presents the two formulations of this objection as stated by the author.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection text
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical structure of the objection.
2
Identify the two formulations
The objector presents two ways circularity arises. Find both: one based on mutual dependence, the other on the evidence becoming disconnected.
The first formulation is explicit, the second is introduced by 'wa illa'.
3
Understand the premise about relation
The objection relies on the idea that knowledge of a relation (idafah) requires knowledge of the two relata. Grasp why this leads to circularity.
This will be crucial for understanding the response.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لزم الدور) لأن العلم بدلالة الدليل على المدلول يتوقف على العلم بالمدلول ضرورة أن العلم بالإضافة مسبوق بالعلم بالمضافين، فيتوقف كل واحد من العلم بالمدلول، وإفادة النظر إياه على الآخر ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم تتوقف إفادة النظر على العلم بالدلالة لزم كون الدليل دليلاً وكون النظر فيه مفيداً للعلم بالمدلول، (وإن لم يعتبر) ولم يعلم (وجه دلالته عليه ) وأنه باطل ( لأن الدليل إذا لم يعتبر وجه دلالته على المدلول كان أجنبياً منقطع التعلق عنه فلا يكون النظر فيه مفيداً للعلم به (قلنا : لا تتوقف ) يفيده النظر العلم التصديقي به فلا دور وقيل : الظاهر أن مبنى لزوم الدور هو أن العلم بالشيء تحققه لأن العلم بوقوع شيء انتفى المطابقة بينهما لم يكن علماً بل جهلاً، وليس معناه أنه فرع لوقوعه وإلا لزم انتفاء العلم الفعلي ولم يكن الواجب من العلم عالماً بالأشياء قبل وقوعها. قوله : فيتوقف كل واحد إلخ توقف إفادة النظر على العلم بالمدلول ظاهر مما سبق، وأما توقف العلم بالمدلول على إفادة النظر، فلا إلا أن يقال العلم بالمدلول النظري موقوف على النظر في الواقع، وفيه أن المعلوم استلزام النظري إياه لا توقفه عليه فالأولى أن يقال : فيتقدم العلم بالمدلول على إفادة النظر المتقدم عليه فيلزم الدور أي تقدم الشيء على نفسه الذي هو لازمه . قوله : وكون النظر فيه إلخ عطف تفسيري بناء على التسامح الذي ارتكبه في تفسير الدلالة . قوله : ( وإن لم يعتبر ولم يعلم وجه دلالته مبناه إما عدم الفرق بين وجه الدلالة والدلالة كما يدل عليه التعرض لبيان الفرق بينهما في الجواب، وإما أن وجه الدلالة إنما يعتبر للعلم بالدلالة فإذا لم تتوقف الدلالة على العلم بها لم يكن لاعتبار وجه الدلالة وجه، فالتعرض لبيان الفرق فائدة زائدة على الجواب . قوله : ( لزم الدور) قيل : هذا الوجه أيضاً منقوض بإفادة الظن هذا، ثم الظاهر أن مبنى لزوم الدور هو أن العلم بالشيء فرع تحققه لأن العلم بوقوع شيء ظل لوقوعه في نفسه على ما صرح به من قبل، في دفع احتجاج القائلين بأن ما اعتقاده لازم للمكلف ضروري فيتوقف العلم بالدلالة حينئذ على نفس الدلالة فيدور، وأما ما ذكره التصديق بالدلالة متوقف على التصديق بالمدلول أيضاً، لأن الإضافة ملزوم للمضافين والتصديق بوجود الملزوم ملزوم للتصديق بلازمه وفيه أن اللازم المعلوم استلزام التصديق بوجود الملزوم التصديق بوجود لازمه بعد العلم بالملازمة لا توقفه عليه فتدبر.
Circularity necessarily follows) because knowledge of the evidence's indication of the indicated depends on knowledge of the indicated, necessarily because knowledge of a relation is preceded by knowledge of the two relata. Thus each of knowledge of the indicated and the benefit of reflection (nazar) of it depends on the other. (Otherwise) i.e., if the benefit of reflection does not depend on knowledge of the indication, then it necessarily follows that the evidence is an evidence and that reflection in it benefits knowledge of the indicated, (even if the aspect of its indication of it is not considered) and not known, and that is false (because if the aspect of its indication of the indicated is not considered, the evidence would be a foreign thing disconnected from it, so reflection in it would not benefit knowledge of it). (We say: It does not depend) reflection benefits assenting knowledge of it, so no circularity. And it is said: The apparent basis of the necessity of circularity is that knowledge of a thing is its realization, because knowledge of the occurrence of something is a shadow of its occurrence in itself. Thus knowledge of the indication depends on the indication itself, resulting in circularity. But this is nothing, because the meaning of knowledge being a shadow of its known is that it is a representation of it and that conformity is considered on its side, whether it precedes the known or comes after it, such that if conformity is absent between them, it would not be knowledge but ignorance. And it does not mean that it is a branch of its occurrence, otherwise actual knowledge would be negated and the one obligated would not be knowledgeable of things before their occurrence. His statement: 'Thus each depends, etc.' The dependence of the benefit of reflection on knowledge of the indicated is apparent from what preceded. As for the dependence of knowledge of the indicated on the benefit of reflection, no, unless it is said that knowledge of the theoretical indicated depends on reflection in reality. But in that, the known is that the theoretical implies it, not that it depends on it. So it is better to say: Knowledge of the indicated precedes the benefit of reflection that precedes it, thus circularity results, i.e., the precedence of a thing over itself, which is its necessary consequence. His statement: 'And that reflection in it, etc.' is an explanatory conjunction based on the tolerance he committed in interpreting 'indication'. His statement: '(And if it is not considered and not known the aspect of its indication)' is based either on the lack of difference between the aspect of indication and indication, as indicated by the attempt to clarify the difference between them in the answer, or that the aspect of indication is only considered for knowledge of the indication, so if indication does not depend on knowledge of it, there is no point in considering the aspect of indication. So the attempt to clarify the difference is an additional benefit to the answer. His statement: '(Circularity necessarily follows)' it is said: This aspect is also refuted by the benefit of opinion (zann). This, then the apparent basis of the necessity of circularity is that knowledge of a thing is a branch of its realization, because knowledge of the occurrence of something is a shadow of its occurrence in itself, as he stated before, in repelling the argument of those who say that what is believed is necessary for the obligated, so knowledge of the indication then depends on the indication itself, resulting in circularity. As for what the commentator mentioned, there is obvious discussion in it, because assenting to the indicated depends on the benefit, which depends on assenting to the indication, which depends on conceptualization of the indicated, because knowledge of a relation is preceded by conceptualization of the relata, not assent to them, so no circularity. And it may be answered that assenting to the indication also depends on assenting to the indicated, because the relation is a necessary concomitant of the relata, and assenting to the existence of the necessary concomitant is necessary for assenting to its necessary concomitant. But in that, the necessary concomitant known is that assenting to the existence of the necessary concomitant entails assenting to the existence of its necessary concomitant after knowledge of the concomitance, not dependence on it. So ponder.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage deals with the objection that nazar leads to circularity (dawr). The objection states: Knowledge of the indication of the evidence depends on knowledge of the indicated, because knowledge of a relation depends on knowledge of the two relata. Thus knowledge of the indicated and the benefit of nazar each depend on the other, leading to circularity. Alternatively, if we deny dependence, then the evidence is not recognized as a proof. The author responds: 'It does not depend.' The response is that the required prior knowledge is only conceptual (tasawwur) of the indicated, not assenting (tasdiq). Thus nazar can produce assenting knowledge without circularity. The commentator then discusses various interpretations, including the view that circularity is based on the idea that knowledge is a shadow of its object, but he rejects this, explaining that knowledge is a representation (hikaayah) and does not require priority. He also discusses the difference between conceptual and assenting knowledge in the relation, concluding that the apparent circularity is resolved by the fact that conceptual knowledge suffices for the indication, and assent comes later.

علم
دلالة
إضافة
مضافان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That nazar is automatically circular because it involves prior knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that the objection assumes mutual dependence, which will later be resolved. At this stage, it is just the objection.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does circularity occur?
A) Because knowledge of the indication requires knowledge of the indicated, and the benefit of nazar also requires knowledge of the indication.
B) Because evidence is self-evident.
C) Because nazar produces opinion, not certainty.
D) Because the objector is irrational.

Key Takeaway
The circularity objection claims that nazar involves a vicious circle because knowledge of the indication depends on knowledge of the indicated, and the benefit of nazar depends on knowledge of the indication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector argues that knowing the indication depends on knowing the indicated. In your pursuit of knowledge about God, have you ever assumed what you were trying to prove? When you reflect on the creation as evidence for the Creator, do you truly know the Creator independently, or is your reflection circular? What would it mean for your faith if this objection were valid? Are you willing to examine your own reasoning for hidden circularity today?
?
If you consider the evidence of God without understanding its aspect of indication, it becomes disconnected. In your daily life, do you sometimes look at the world without seeing it as a sign pointing to God? How can you ensure that your reflection on God's signs is truly connected to Him and not just a disconnected intellectual exercise? Commit today to reflecting on one sign with full awareness of its indication.

Resolving Circularity: Tasawwur vs Tasdiq
The author and commentator resolve the circularity objection by distinguishing conceptual from assenting knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the circularity objection by distinguishing between conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).

Core Idea
The required prior knowledge is only conceptual, not assenting, so no circularity occurs.

The author responds to the circularity objection by asserting that nazar does not depend on complete knowledge of the indicated. Instead, only a conceptual (tasawwur) knowledge is required, not assenting (tasdiq) knowledge. This distinction breaks the alleged circularity. The passage also discusses the nature of knowledge as a shadow and clarifies that it does not imply temporal priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the author's direct reply: 'We say: It does not depend.'
2
Note the key phrase
Identify the phrase 'yufiduhu al-nazar al-‘ilm al-tasdiqi bihi fala dawr' – reflection benefits assenting knowledge, so no circle. This is the core response.
Assenting knowledge (tasdiq) is the result, not the prerequisite.
3
Understand the commentator's explanation
Read the commentator's clarification that the prior knowledge needed is only conceptual (tasawwur) of the relata, not assent. This resolves the circularity.
The discussion of the shadow analogy is also addressed.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how distinguishing conceptual from assenting knowledge breaks the alleged mutual dependence.
This distinction is a key methodological tool in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لزم الدور) لأن العلم بدلالة الدليل على المدلول يتوقف على العلم بالمدلول ضرورة أن العلم بالإضافة مسبوق بالعلم بالمضافين، فيتوقف كل واحد من العلم بالمدلول، وإفادة النظر إياه على الآخر ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم تتوقف إفادة النظر على العلم بالدلالة لزم كون الدليل دليلاً وكون النظر فيه مفيداً للعلم بالمدلول، (وإن لم يعتبر) ولم يعلم (وجه دلالته عليه ) وأنه باطل ( لأن الدليل إذا لم يعتبر وجه دلالته على المدلول كان أجنبياً منقطع التعلق عنه فلا يكون النظر فيه مفيداً للعلم به (قلنا : لا تتوقف ) يفيده النظر العلم التصديقي به فلا دور وقيل : الظاهر أن مبنى لزوم الدور هو أن العلم بالشيء تحققه لأن العلم بوقوع شيء ظل لوقوعه في نفسه، فيتوقف العلم بالدلالة على نفس الدلالة، فيدور وليس بشيء لأن معنى كون العلم ظلا لمعلومه أنه حكاية عنه وأن المطابقة تعتبر جانبه سواء كان متقدماً على المعلوم أو متأخراً عنه، حتى لو انتفى المطابقة بينهما لم يكن علماً بل جهلاً، وليس معناه أنه فرع لوقوعه وإلا لزم انتفاء العلم الفعلي ولم يكن الواجب من العلم عالماً بالأشياء قبل وقوعها. قوله : فيتوقف كل واحد إلخ توقف إفادة النظر على العلم بالمدلول ظاهر مما سبق، وأما توقف العلم بالمدلول على إفادة النظر، فلا إلا أن يقال العلم بالمدلول النظري موقوف على النظر في الواقع، وفيه أن المعلوم استلزام النظري إياه لا توقفه عليه فالأولى أن يقال : فيتقدم العلم بالمدلول على إفادة النظر المتقدم عليه فيلزم الدور أي تقدم الشيء على نفسه الذي هو لازمه . قوله : وكون النظر فيه إلخ عطف تفسيري بناء على التسامح الذي ارتكبه في تفسير الدلالة . قوله : ( وإن لم يعتبر ولم يعلم وجه دلالته مبناه إما عدم الفرق بين وجه الدلالة والدلالة كما يدل عليه التعرض لبيان الفرق بينهما في الجواب، وإما أن وجه الدلالة إنما يعتبر للعلم بالدلالة فإذا لم تتوقف الدلالة على العلم بها لم يكن لاعتبار وجه الدلالة وجه، فالتعرض لبيان الفرق فائدة زائدة على الجواب . قوله : ( لزم الدور) قيل : هذا الوجه أيضاً منقوض بإفادة الظن هذا، ثم الظاهر أن مبنى لزوم الدور هو أن العلم بالشيء فرع تحققه لأن العلم بوقوع شيء ظل لوقوعه في نفسه على ما صرح به من قبل، في دفع احتجاج القائلين بأن ما اعتقاده لازم للمكلف ضروري فيتوقف العلم بالدلالة حينئذ على نفس الدلالة فيدور، وأما ما ذكره الشارح ففيه بحث ظاهر لأن التصديق بالمدلول موقوف على الإفادة وهي تتوقف على التصديق بالدلالة المتوقف على تصور المدلول، لأن العلم بالإضافة مسبوق بتصور المضافين لا التصديق بهما فلا دور وقد يجاب بأن التصديق بالدلالة متوقف على التصديق بالمدلول أيضاً، لأن الإضافة ملزوم للمضافين والتصديق بوجود الملزوم ملزوم للتصديق بلازمه وفيه أن اللازم المعلوم استلزام التصديق بوجود الملزوم التصديق بوجود لازمه بعد العلم بالملازمة لا توقفه عليه فتدبر.
Circularity necessarily follows) because knowledge of the evidence's indication of the indicated depends on knowledge of the indicated, necessarily because knowledge of a relation is preceded by knowledge of the two relata. Thus each of knowledge of the indicated and the benefit of reflection (nazar) of it depends on the other. (Otherwise) i.e., if the benefit of reflection does not depend on knowledge of the indication, then it necessarily follows that the evidence is an evidence and that reflection in it benefits knowledge of the indicated, (even if the aspect of its indication of it is not considered) and not known, and that is false (because if the aspect of its indication of the indicated is not considered, the evidence would be a foreign thing disconnected from it, so reflection in it would not benefit knowledge of it). (We say: It does not depend) reflection benefits assenting knowledge of it, so no circularity. And it is said: The apparent basis of the necessity of circularity is that knowledge of a thing is its realization, because knowledge of the occurrence of something is a shadow of its occurrence in itself. Thus knowledge of the indication depends on the indication itself, resulting in circularity. But this is nothing, because the meaning of knowledge being a shadow of its known is that it is a representation of it and that conformity is considered on its side, whether it precedes the known or comes after it, such that if conformity is absent between them, it would not be knowledge but ignorance. And it does not mean that it is a branch of its occurrence, otherwise actual knowledge would be negated and the one obligated would not be knowledgeable of things before their occurrence. His statement: 'Thus each depends, etc.' The dependence of the benefit of reflection on knowledge of the indicated is apparent from what preceded. As for the dependence of knowledge of the indicated on the benefit of reflection, no, unless it is said that knowledge of the theoretical indicated depends on reflection in reality. But in that, the known is that the theoretical implies it, not that it depends on it. So it is better to say: Knowledge of the indicated precedes the benefit of reflection that precedes it, thus circularity results, i.e., the precedence of a thing over itself, which is its necessary consequence. His statement: 'And that reflection in it, etc.' is an explanatory conjunction based on the tolerance he committed in interpreting 'indication'. His statement: '(And if it is not considered and not known the aspect of its indication)' is based either on the lack of difference between the aspect of indication and indication, as indicated by the attempt to clarify the difference between them in the answer, or that the aspect of indication is only considered for knowledge of the indication, so if indication does not depend on knowledge of it, there is no point in considering the aspect of indication. So the attempt to clarify the difference is an additional benefit to the answer. His statement: '(Circularity necessarily follows)' it is said: This aspect is also refuted by the benefit of opinion (zann). This, then the apparent basis of the necessity of circularity is that knowledge of a thing is a branch of its realization, because knowledge of the occurrence of something is a shadow of its occurrence in itself, as he stated before, in repelling the argument of those who say that what is believed is necessary for the obligated, so knowledge of the indication then depends on the indication itself, resulting in circularity. As for what the commentator mentioned, there is obvious discussion in it, because assenting to the indicated depends on the benefit, which depends on assenting to the indication, which depends on conceptualization of the indicated, because knowledge of a relation is preceded by conceptualization of the relata, not assent to them, so no circularity. And it may be answered that assenting to the indication also depends on assenting to the indicated, because the relation is a necessary concomitant of the relata, and assenting to the existence of the necessary concomitant is necessary for assenting to its necessary concomitant. But in that, the necessary concomitant known is that assenting to the existence of the necessary concomitant entails assenting to the existence of its necessary concomitant after knowledge of the concomitance, not dependence on it. So ponder.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage deals with the objection that nazar leads to circularity (dawr). The objection states: Knowledge of the indication of the evidence depends on knowledge of the indicated, because knowledge of a relation depends on knowledge of the two relata. Thus knowledge of the indicated and the benefit of nazar each depend on the other, leading to circularity. Alternatively, if we deny dependence, then the evidence is not recognized as a proof. The author responds: 'It does not depend.' The response is that the required prior knowledge is only conceptual (tasawwur) of the indicated, not assenting (tasdiq). Thus nazar can produce assenting knowledge without circularity. The commentator then discusses various interpretations, including the view that circularity is based on the idea that knowledge is a shadow of its object, but he rejects this, explaining that knowledge is a representation (hikaayah) and does not require priority. He also discusses the difference between conceptual and assenting knowledge in the relation, concluding that the apparent circularity is resolved by the fact that conceptual knowledge suffices for the indication, and assent comes later.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
دور
إضافة
ظل
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the response simply denies the objection without explanation.
Correction: The response is supported by a distinction between conceptual and assenting knowledge, which logically dissolves the circularity.

Checkpoint
What kind of prior knowledge of the indicated does the author say is needed for nazar to function without circularity?
A) Assenting knowledge.
B) Conceptual knowledge only.
C) Complete certainty.
D) No knowledge at all.

Key Takeaway
The key to refuting the circularity objection is that the prior knowledge needed for nazar is only conceptual, not assenting, allowing nazar to produce new assenting knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a new concept in your faith, do you rush to judge it as true or false before you've fully understood it? How can the discipline of separating tasawwur from tasdiq protect you from hasty conclusions that may harm your iman?
?
Think of a time you felt stuck in a circular argument about a religious matter. Could it be that you were demanding full assent (tasdiq) at the outset instead of being content with conceptual understanding (tasawwur) first? How will you change your approach to learning from now on?

The Eighth Doubt: Simultaneity of Nazar and Knowledge Refuted
Al-Sharh presents and refutes the eighth doubt about nazar yielding knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth doubt against nazar yielding knowledge and its refutation based on the condition of no opposite arising.

Core Idea
Nazar yields knowledge after it, but only if no opposite intervenes.

The eighth doubt attempts to show that knowledge cannot follow from nazar by arguing that they cannot be simultaneous nor sequential without interruption. The refutation clarifies that knowledge does follow, but only under the condition that no opposite of knowledge (like sleep or death) occurs in between. This lesson unpacks the doubt and the precise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and its two disjuncts
The doubt presents two possibilities: knowledge either comes with nazar or after it. Both are claimed invalid. Note the reasoning for each.
The first is rejected due to opposition, the second due to possible intervening opposites.
2
Study the refutation
The answer affirms that knowledge comes after nazar, but only if no opposite arises. This conditional preserves the causality of nazar.
This is a key nuance in kalam epistemology.
3
Consider the extension to probability
The commentary notes that this doubt does not apply to nazar yielding probability (zann), since probability can lag without issue.
This shows the doubt is specific to certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة (الثامنة لو أفاد) النظر (العلم فإما أن يكون ذلك معه أو بعده والأول باطل إذ لا يجتمعان ) لأن النظر مضاد للعلم بالمنظور فيه، ومشروط بعدمه ) وكذا الثاني ) باطل أيضاً ( لجواز طرو ضد العلم بعده ) أي بعد النظر بلا مهلة ( كنوم أو موت) أو غفلة، فلا يتصور حينئذ حصول العلم بعده ( قلنا : يفيد بعده بشرط عدم طرو الضد كما أومأنا إليه عند تحرير المبحث ) حيث قلنا كل نظر صحيح في القطعيات لا يعقبه ضد للعلم قوله : (فهذا الذي ذكرتموه إلخ لو بدل قوله فيقبح التكليف به بقولنا، فلا يقع التكليف به اندفع هذا الجواب . قوله : ( لو أفاد النظر العلم إلخ ولا تجري في إفادته الظن لأنا نختار الشق الثاني، ونقول : إنه يفيد الظن مع إمكان التخلف عنه . قوله : ( لو أفاد النظر إلخ تقريره أنه لو أفاد النظر في الدليل العلم، لكان النظر واقعاً في الدليل، وكلما كان واقعاً فيه فالدليل المنظور فيه ،موجبه، إما نفس المدلول أو العلم به إذا لا يوجب شيئاً وإلا لم يكن الدليل دليلاً ولا أمراً ثالثاً، إذ لا تعلق له بالدليل، لكن التالي أعني كون موجبه أحد الأمرين باطل لما بينه المقدم مثله ثم الترديد بين موجب الدليل مبني على أن الدليل المنظور فيه إما مغاير للنظر في الدليل، فيكون موجب أحدهما غير موجب الآخر أو عينه بناء على أن الموجب مجموع النظر والدليل والفرق بمجرد التعبير فيكون يجوز أن لا الحكم الضروري موجب له، يمتنع تخلفه عنه بخلاف النظري لا يفيد عدم مقدورية الأوليات مطلقاً، وأما ثالثاً فلان الباء في قول المصنف بالنظر إذ لم يجعل صلة للتكليف بل للسببية يمكن إرجاع كلام المصنف إلى هذا الجواب فليتأمل. قوله : ( إنما يلزم المعتزلة إلخ لا يذهب عليك أن التكليف بغير المقدور، وإن كان جائزاً عند الأشاعرة فالصحيح عندهم أنه غير واقع، فيمكن تقرير الشبهة بالنظر إلى وقوع التكليف بالنظري، وحينئذ يندفع هذا الوجه من الجواب لكن إنما أورده نظراً إلى التقرير السابق، حيث بني الكلام فيه على قبح التكليف، وقد يقال : تجويز التكليف بمثله ممنوع أيضاً، إنما المجوز المعنيان من الثلاثة على ما سيفصل في الإلهيات وهو غيرهما . هو قوله : ( الثامنة لو أفاد إلخ ) منقوض بإفادة الظن المتفق عليها .
The eighth doubt: If nazar (sound reasoning) yields knowledge, then that is either with it or after it. The first is invalid, because they (nazar and knowledge of the thing regarded) do not coincide, since nazar is opposed to knowledge of the thing regarded and is conditioned by its absence. The second is also invalid, because of the possibility of an opposite of knowledge arising after it (i.e., after nazar without delay), such as sleep or death or heedlessness, so knowledge is not conceivable after it. We say: It yields after it on condition that no opposite arises, as we indicated when defining the topic, where we said: every sound nazar concerning certainties is not followed by an opposite of knowledge. He said: 'This which you mentioned [about the ugliness of obligation]' – if he changed his statement 'so obligation with it is ugly' to our statement 'so obligation with it does not occur', then this answer would be repelled. He said: 'If nazar yields knowledge etc.' It does not apply to yielding probability, because we choose the second disjunct and say: It yields probability with the possibility of its failure. He said: 'If nazar yields knowledge etc.' Its explication: If nazar in the proof yields knowledge, then nazar would be occurring in the proof, and whenever it occurs in it, the proof regarded has as its muijib (efficient cause) either the very thing indicated or knowledge of it, since it does not necessitate anything else, else it would not be a proof, nor is there a third thing, because it has no relation to the proof. But the consequent, i.e., that its muijib is one of the two, is invalid for what the antecedent explained similarly. Then the disjunction concerning the muijib of the proof is based on whether the proof regarded is different from nazar in the proof, so the muijib of one is different from the muijib of the other, or it is identical, on the basis that the muijib is the totality of nazar and the proof and the difference is merely in expression. Then it may be that the necessary judgment does not have a muijib, so it is impossible for it to fail, unlike the theoretical, which does not yield the absolute impossibility of primary [knowledge]. Thirdly, because the ba' in the author's phrase 'by nazar' — if he did not make it relate to obligation but to causality — the author's words can be referred to this answer, so reflect. He said: 'This only applies to the Mu'tazila etc.' Do not be unaware that obligation with what is not in one's power, though permissible according to the Ash'aris, the correct view with them is that it does not occur. So the doubt could be presented with respect to the occurrence of obligation with the theoretical, and then this aspect of the answer is repelled. But he only brought it with regard to the previous presentation, which was built on the ugliness of obligation. And it may be said: Permission for obligation with the like of it is also denied; only the two meanings out of three are permissible, as will be detailed in the divine matters, and it is other than them. He said: 'The eighth [doubt]: If it yields etc.' is invalidated by the agreed-upon yielding of probability.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the eighth doubt (shubha) against the position that sound reasoning (nazar) yields knowledge. The doubt argues that if nazar yields knowledge, it must be either simultaneously with it or after it. The first option is invalid because nazar and knowledge are opposites, so they cannot coexist. The second is invalid because after nazar, without any delay, an opposite of knowledge (like sleep or death) could arise, preventing knowledge. The answer (qulna) clarifies: nazar yields knowledge after it, but only on the condition that no such opposite occurs. The commentary then discusses previous answers and emphasizes that the refutation stands firmly, noting that the doubt is also invalidated by the agreed fact that nazar yields probability (zann). The discussion includes nuances about obligation (taklif) and the Mu'tazilite vs. Ash'arite positions, but the core refutation centers on the conditional nature of knowledge after nazar.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
علم (knowledge)
ضد العلم (opposite of knowledge)
موجب (muijib)
ظن (probability)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if knowledge comes after nazar, it must come immediately and inevitably without any condition.
Correction: The text clarifies that knowledge follows only if no opposite (like sleep or death) arises; it is conditional, not unconditional.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the eighth doubt, when does nazar yield knowledge?
A) Simultaneously with nazar
B) After nazar, unconditionally
C) After nazar, on condition that no opposite of knowledge arises
D) It never yields knowledge

Key Takeaway
Nazar yields knowledge after it, but only if no opposite of knowledge intervenes; this is the correct Ash'arite position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you were absolutely certain of something (like a belief or understanding). Did you ever consider that your certainty could be interrupted by an 'opposite'—such as forgetfulness, distraction, or death? How does this lesson's condition of the absence of an opposite challenge your confidence in your own knowledge? Do you rely on your reasoning without acknowledging the obstacles that could prevent knowledge? How can you guard against those obstacles in your pursuit of certainty?
?
The lesson states that knowledge from nazar is conditional on no opposite arising. Are there 'opposites' in your own life—such as heedlessness (ghaflah) or sin—that block the knowledge that sound reasoning would normally produce? What would it mean to actively remove those obstacles? Can you commit to removing one this week?
?
The opponent argued that if knowledge comes after nazar, it could be prevented by sleep or death. How does this uncertainty about the future affect your urgency to seek knowledge now? If death could interrupt the process, how should that change your approach to learning and acting on truth today?

The Ninth Doubt: Nazar's Effect on the Proof's Entailment
Al-Sharh presents and refutes the ninth doubt about nazar and the proof's muijib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth doubt about nazar yielding knowledge and its refutation that distinguishes between the proof's role as entailer versus actual cause.

Core Idea
The proof's muijib is entailment, not causation — a key distinction in kalam.

This lesson tackles a subtle doubt: if nazar in a proof yields knowledge, then the proof must have a muijib. But what is that muijib? The doubt presents two possibilities, both apparently absurd. The refutation clarifies by distinguishing the proof considered in itself (as entailer) and with nazar (as cause of knowledge). This distinction is crucial for understanding how proofs work in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt presentation
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning until the statement of the doubt's two alternatives. Note the structure: the doubt claims that if nazar yields knowledge, it must be occurring in the proof, leading to two problematic options.
The doubt is introduced as 'الشبهة التاسعة'.
2
Understand the two alternatives and their absurdities
The doubt posits that the proof's muijib is either the existence of the Maker in reality or knowledge. Each leads to an absurdity: the first implies the Maker's existence depends on the proof; the second implies the proof ceases to be a proof without nazar.
Both are rejected as invalid (باطل).
3
Read the refutation's choice of both disjuncts
The answer (قلنا) chooses both disjuncts but clarifies the perspective: when considering the proof in itself, it entails the existence of the Maker; when considering the proof together with nazar, it yields knowledge. Thus, the muijib is not a single thing.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Grasp the distinction between actual cause and entailer
The commentator explains that 'muijib' can mean either actual cause (محصل) or entailer (مستلزم). The proof is an entailer, not an actual cause, so its non-existence does not negate the indicated in reality.
This distinction is the key to the refutation.
5
See how the doubt is repelled
Understand that the doubt's disjunction is ugly only regarding the muijib of nazar, not of the proof itself. Also, the doubt is further invalidated by the agreed fact that nazar yields probability (ظن).
The passage ends with this additional argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة (التاسعة لو أفاد النظر العلم لكان ذلك النظر واقعاً في الدليل، وهو باطل لأنا ( إذا ) نظرنا و استدللنا بدليل) كالعالم ( على وجود الصانع) مثلا ( فموجبه ) أي موجب ذلك الدليل الذي نظرنا فيه (إما ثبوت الصانع) في نفس الأمر أو العلم وكلاهما باطل، أما الأول فلأنه يلزم حينئذ من عدم ذلك الدليل أن لا يثبت الصانع في الواقع لأن انتفاء الموجب المفيد يستلزم انتفاء ظاهر البطلان فإنه تعالى يستحيل عليه العدم أوجد العالم أو لم يوجد ( وأما الثاني فلأنه يلزم ) حينئذ ( أن لا يبقى الدليل بتقدير عدم النظر فيه وإفادته للعلم دليلا ) إذ المفروض أن موجبه اللازم له هو العلم فإذا انتفي اللازم انتفي الملزوم وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن الأدلة أدلة في أنفسها سواء نظر فيها واستفيد العلم منها أم لا قلنا : إنه ) أي الدليل الذي نظر فيه واستدل به ( يوجب وجود الصانع أي يستلزمه ) من غير أن يكون محصلاً له في الواقع ) ولا يلزم من نفي (الملزوم الذي لا مدخل له في حصول لازمه ( نفي اللازم أو يوجب العلم به أي ) هو بحيث ) متى علم ) ونظر فيه (علم) موجبهما واحداً، وبما حررنا لك اندفع ما توهم من قبح الترديد في الموجب بعد اعتباره في المقدم إفادة النظر في الدليل العلم بالمدلول لأنه إنما يقبح ذلك الترديد في موجب النظر لا في موجب الدليل المنظور فيه ولأجل هذا زاد الشارح قوله لكان واقعاً في الدليل، وما يتوهم من أنه إذا كان موجب النظر العلم بالمدلول كيف يكون ذلك موجب الدليل أيضاً، فإنه يلزم توارد الموجبين على شيء واحد . (قوله : لأن انتفاء إلخ) قيد انتفاء الموجب بالمفيد والموجب بالمستفاد، لأن انتفاء الموجب الغير المفيد لا يستلزم انتفاء الموجب الغير المستفاد كالملزوم بالنسبة إلى اللازم الأعم. قوله : ( فإذا انتفى اللازم إلخ ) على تقدير عدم النظر انتفي الملزوم وهو كون الدليل دليلاً. قوله : ( قلنا إنه إلخ) أجاب باختيار الشقين ومبناه أن الدليل المنظور فيه، أن لوحظ ذاته مع قطع النظر عن النظر الواقع فيه فالمختار الشق الأول وإن لوحظ مع النظر فالمختار الشق الثاني . قوله : (من غير أن يكون محصلاً إلخ) فيه إشارة إلى أن الجواب بالترديد بأنكم إن أردتم بالموجب المحصل، فنختار أن الدليل لا موجب له بهذا المعنى وإن أردتم المستلزم فنختار الشق الأول، فإن الدليل متى وجد وجد المدلول من غير تخلف عنه، ولا يلزم من نفيه نفي العدم مدخليته في حصوله في نفس الأمر. اللازم قوله : (التاسعة لو أفاد إلخ) يمكن أن يقال فيه أيضاً : لو صح دليلكم لما أفاد النظر الظن مع أن هذه الإفادة متفق عليها كما مر .
The ninth doubt: If nazar yields knowledge, then that nazar would be occurring in the proof, and it is invalid, because when we reason and infer by a proof (like the world) for the existence of the Maker, for example, then its muijib, i.e., the muijib of that proof we regarded, is either the existence of the Maker in reality or knowledge, and both are invalid. As for the first, because then from the non-existence of that proof it would follow that the Maker does not exist in reality, since the non-existence of the effective muijib entails the non-existence of [the effect], which is clearly invalid, for He, exalted, cannot be non-existent whether the world is created or not. As for the second, because then it would follow that, assuming the absence of nazar in it and its yielding knowledge, the proof would not remain a proof, since the supposed muijib necessarily following it is knowledge, so if the necessary is negated, the necessitated is negated. That is also invalid, because proofs are proofs in themselves, whether one regards them and derives knowledge from them or not. We say: It (i.e., the proof that is regarded and inferred from) necessitates the existence of the Maker, i.e., entails it, without being an actual cause in reality, and the negation of the necessitated which has no role in the attainment of its necessary does not entail the negation of the necessary. Or it necessitates knowledge of it, i.e., it is such that whenever one knows it and regards it, one knows [the Maker]; their muijib is one. And with what we have clarified for you, what was suspected of the ugliness of disjunction concerning the muijib after considering in the premise that nazar in the proof yields knowledge of the indicated is repelled, because that disjunction is only ugly regarding the muijib of nazar, not the muijib of the proof regarded. For this reason the commentator added his statement 'it would be occurring in the proof'. And what is suspected that if the muijib of nazar is knowledge of the indicated, how can that be also the muijib of the proof? For it would entail two muijibs converging on one thing. (His statement: 'because the non-existence etc.') He restricted the non-existence of the muijib to the effective one and the muijib to the one from which benefit is derived, because the non-existence of the non-effective muijib does not entail the non-existence of the non-beneficial muijib, like the necessitated relative to the broader necessary. His statement: 'So if the necessary is negated etc.' – on the assumption of absence of nazar, the necessitated is negated, which is the proof being a proof. His statement: 'We say: It etc.' He answered by choosing both disjuncts, and its basis is that the proof regarded, if one considers its essence disregarding the nazar occurring in it, then the first disjunct is chosen; if one considers it together with nazar, then the second disjunct is chosen. His statement: 'without being an actual cause etc.' contains an indication that the answer is by disjunction: if you mean by muijib the actual cause, then we choose that the proof has no muijib in this sense; if you mean the entailer, then we choose the first disjunct, for whenever the proof exists, the indicated exists without failure, and its negation does not entail the negation of [the existence of the indicated] because of the proof's lack of causality in its attainment in reality. His statement: 'The ninth: If it yields etc.' It can also be said in it: If your proof were valid, nazar would not yield probability, but this yielding is agreed upon as mentioned.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the ninth doubt (shubha). The doubt argues: if nazar in a proof yields knowledge of the indicated (e.g., the existence of the Maker), then that nazar occurs in the proof. The proof's muijib must be either the very existence of the Maker in reality or knowledge of it. The first leads to the absurdity that if the proof did not exist, the Maker would not exist. The second leads to the absurdity that if nazar is absent, the proof would cease to be a proof. The answer (qulna) clarifies by distinguishing two aspects: when the proof is considered in itself, it entails the existence of the Maker (i.e., it is a logical entailment, not an actual cause). When considered together with nazar, it yields knowledge. Thus the doubt's disjunction is misguided. The commentator further explains that the 'muijib' can mean either 'actual cause' or 'entailer'; the proof is an entailer, not a cause. This resolves the suspicion of two muijibs converging. Finally, the doubt is also invalidated by the agreed fact that nazar yields probability (zann).

Key Terms
دليل (proof)
موجب (muijib)
مدلول (indicated)
محصل (actual cause)
مستلزم (entailer)
علم (knowledge)
ظن (probability)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof's muijib must be an actual cause that produces the indicated.
Correction: The proof's muijib is an entailer, not an actual cause; the proof entails the indicated without being a cause in reality.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation of the ninth doubt resolve the issue of the proof's muijib?
A) By denying that the proof has any muijib
B) By distinguishing between muijib as actual cause and muijib as entailer
C) By asserting that the proof's muijib is always the knowledge
D) By rejecting the validity of the proof altogether

Key Takeaway
The ninth doubt is resolved by distinguishing the proof as entailer (in itself) from the proof as yielding knowledge (through nazar); thus, the proof's muijib is not a single thing but depends on perspective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your reasoning about God falters, do you feel His existence becomes less certain? How does this lesson's distinction between entailment and actual causation challenge you to separate the proof from the reality it points to? Will you now remind yourself that proofs are only pointers, not causes?
?
Have you ever treated your intellectual arguments as if they create or sustain your faith? How would it change your reliance on Allah if you see proofs as mere signs that you must combine with nazar? Commit to reciting this lesson's key distinction when you next face doubt.
?
If proofs are proofs in themselves regardless of your understanding, does that give you confidence that Islam's truth stands independent of your grasp? How will you act on this truth when your own reasoning feels insufficient?
