The Ninth Shubha: Does Inquiry Necessitate Proof?
Objection and reply regarding nazar yielding knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if nazar yields knowledge, then proof would be contingent on inquiry, and the Ash'ari response distinguishing between the proof's inherent implication and its cognitive effect.

Core Idea
Proofs imply truth inherently, but our knowledge depends on inquiry.

The ninth objection challenges the very possibility of inference (nazar) yielding certain knowledge. If it did, the objector argues, then a proof would depend on our act of inquiry, which is problematic. Al-Isfahani's reply distinguishes between the proof's inherent implication and its cognitive effect, preserving both sound theology and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its two prongs
Read the passage from the start through the statement of both prongs. Notice that the objector claims that the proof's necessitator (mujib) must be either the existence of the Maker in reality or knowledge of Him.
The first prong is about God's existence becoming contingent on the proof; the second concerns the proof's status as proof.
2
Understand why both prongs seem problematic
Consider why the objector thinks each prong is false: the first would make God's existence contingent (absurd), the second would make a proof depend on our inquiry (also absurd).
3
Study the reply's two-part answer
Read the reply beginning with 'قلنا'. Note that it chooses both sides: the proof implies the Maker's existence without actualizing it, and it yields knowledge when inquired into. Understand the distinction between the proof in itself and the proof with inquiry.
The commentator explains that the alternation (tardid) is not objectionable because it applies to different aspects.
4
Note the commentator's clarification on alternation
Read the commentary section explaining why alternation is acceptable. The key is that the ugliness of alternation applies to the necessitator of the inquiry, not the necessitator of the proof.
5
Reflect on how this defends the reliability of rational inference
Consider how this reply preserves the objectivity of proofs while acknowledging that our knowledge comes through inquiry. Reflect on why this is important for theology.
This connects to the broader claim that nazar can yield certain knowledge.

التاسعة لو أفاد النظر العلم لكان ذلك النظر واقعاً في الدليل، وهو باطل لأنا ( إذا ) نظرنا و استدللنا بدليل) كالعالم ( على وجود الصانع) مثلا ( فموجبه ) أي موجب ذلك الدليل الذي نظرنا فيه (إما ثبوت الصانع) في نفس الأمر أو العلم وكلاهما باطل، أما الأول فلأنه يلزم حينئذ من عدم ذلك الدليل أن لا يثبت الصانع في الواقع لأن انتفاء الموجب المفيد يستلزم انتفاء ظاهر البطلان فإنه تعالى يستحيل عليه العدم أوجد العالم أو لم يوجد ( وأما الثاني فلأنه يلزم ) حينئذ ( أن لا يبقى الدليل بتقدير عدم النظر فيه وإفادته للعلم دليلا ) إذ المفروض أن موجبه اللازم له هو العلم فإذا انتفي اللازم انتفي الملزوم وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن الأدلة أدلة في أنفسها سواء نظر فيها واستفيد العلم منها أم لا قلنا : إنه ) أي الدليل الذي نظر فيه واستدل به ( يوجب وجود الصانع أي يستلزمه ) من غير أن يكون محصلاً له في الواقع ) ولا يلزم من نفي (الملزوم الذي لا مدخل له في حصول لازمه ( نفي اللازم أو يوجب العلم به أي ) هو بحيث ) متى علم ) ونظر فيه (علم) موجبهما واحداً، وبما حررنا لك اندفع ما توهم من قبح الترديد في الموجب بعد اعتباره في المقدم إفادة النظر في الدليل العلم بالمدلول لأنه إنما يقبح ذلك الترديد في موجب النظر لا في موجب الدليل المنظور فيه ولأجل هذا زاد الشارح قوله لكان واقعاً في الدليل، وما يتوهم من أنه إذا كان موجب النظر العلم بالمدلول كيف يكون ذلك موجب الدليل أيضاً، فإنه يلزم توارد الموجبين على شيء واحد . (قوله : لأن انتفاء إلخ) قيد انتفاء الموجب بالمفيد والموجب بالمستفاد، لأن انتفاء الموجب الغير المفيد لا يستلزم انتفاء الموجب الغير المستفاد كالملزوم بالنسبة إلى اللازم الأعم. قوله : ( فإذا انتفى اللازم إلخ ) على تقدير عدم النظر انتفي الملزوم وهو كون الدليل دليلاً. قوله : ( قلنا إنه إلخ) أجاب باختيار الشقين ومبناه أن الدليل المنظور فيه، أن لوحظ ذاته مع قطع النظر عن النظر الواقع فيه فالمختار الشق الأول وإن لوحظ مع النظر فالمختار الشق الثاني . قوله : (من غير أن يكون محصلاً إلخ) فيه إشارة إلى أن الجواب بالترديد بأنكم إن أردتم بالموجب المحصل، فنختار أن الدليل لا موجب له بهذا المعنى وإن أردتم المستلزم فنختار الشق الأول، فإن الدليل متى وجد وجد المدلول من غير تخلف عنه، ولا يلزم من نفيه نفي العدم مدخليته في حصوله في نفس الأمر. اللازم قوله : (التاسعة لو أفاد إلخ) يمكن أن يقال فيه أيضاً : لو صح دليلكم لما أفاد النظر الظن مع أن هذه الإفادة متفق عليها كما مر .
The ninth [objection]: If inquiry (nazar) were to yield knowledge, then that inquiry would be located in the proof (dalil). This is false, because when we inquire and use a proof, like the world for the existence of the Maker, its necessitating (mujib) is either the establishment of the Maker in reality or knowledge; both are false. As for the first, because it would then follow from the non-existence of that proof that the Maker is not established in reality, because the negation of the useful necessitator entails negation – obviously false, for He, exalted, cannot cease to exist whether He creates the world or not. As for the second, because it would then follow that the proof would not remain a proof on the supposition of no inquiry and its yielding knowledge, since it is assumed that its necessary concomitant (lazim) is knowledge; if the concomitant is negated, the concomitant (malzum) is negated – also false, because proofs are proofs in themselves whether inquiry is made and knowledge is obtained from them or not. We say: the proof which is inquired into and used necessitates the existence of the Maker, i.e., implies it (yastlzimuhu), without being its actualizer in reality, and the negation of the malzum which has no role in producing its lazim does not entail negation of the lazim; or it necessitates knowledge of Him, i.e., it is such that whenever it is known and inquired into, knowledge results. Their two necessitators are one. And by what we have written for you, what was imagined of the ugliness of alternation (tardid) in the necessitator after its consideration in the premise is refuted – the yielding of inquiry in the proof of knowledge of the denoted – because that ugliness of alternation is only in the necessitator of the inquiry, not in the necessitator of the proof inquired into. For this reason the commentator added his statement: 'it would be located in the proof.' And what is imagined – that if the necessitator of inquiry is knowledge of the denoted, how can that also be the necessitator of the proof? – would entail two necessitators converging on one thing. (His statement: 'because the negation etc.') He restricted the negation of the necessitator to the useful and the necessitator to the derived (mustafad), because the negation of a non-useful necessitator does not entail the negation of the non-derived, like the malzum relative to the more general lazim. His statement: 'then if the lazim is negated etc.': on the supposition of no inquiry, the malzum is negated, i.e., the proof being a proof. His statement: 'we say it etc.': he answered by choosing both sides, and its basis is that the proof inquired into: if its essence is considered irrespective of the inquiry occurring in it, then the first side is chosen; if considered with the inquiry, then the second side is chosen. His statement: 'without being an actualizer etc.': in it is an indication that the answer is by alternation: if you mean by 'necessitator' the actualizer (muhassil), then we choose that the proof has no necessitator in this sense; if you mean the implier (mustalzim), then we choose the first side, for whenever the proof exists, the denoted exists without lagging behind it, and its negation does not entail the negation of the non-existence of its role in its occurrence in reality. The lazim. His statement: 'the ninth: if it yields etc.': it can be said regarding it also: if your proof were valid, inquiry would not yield opinion (zann), whereas this yielding is agreed upon as previously.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth objection (shubha) against the view that rational inquiry (nazar) yields knowledge, followed by its refutation and extensive commentary. The objector argues: if inquiry yielded knowledge, that knowledge would be caused by the inquiry, but the inquiry occurs in the proof (dalil). The proof's 'necessitator' (mujib) must be either the existence of the Creator in reality or knowledge of Him. Both are problematic. The first would make God's existence contingent on the proof, which is absurd. The second would make the proof's status as proof dependent on our inquiry, which is also false because proofs are proofs in themselves. The answer (qulna) distinguishes: the proof necessitates the existence of the Maker in the sense of implying it, not actualizing it in reality. It also necessitates knowledge only when actually inquired into. The commentator then explains that the 'alternation' (tardid) between the two sides is not objectionable because it applies to different aspects: considering the proof in itself vs. considering it with inquiry. He also addresses a potential misunderstanding that the objector might conflate the two kinds of necessitation. The passage concludes with a further possible objection: if the argument were valid, inquiry would not even yield opinion (zann), but it does, so the argument fails.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
دليل (dalil)
موجب (mujib)
علم (ilm)
ظن (zann)
تلازم (talazum)
ترديد (tardid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes that if a proof implies something, it must also be the cause of that thing's existence or our knowledge of it.
Correction: The reply clarifies that a proof can imply (istilzam) without actualizing, and our knowledge depends on our engagement with the proof; the proof's implication is inherent and independent.

Checkpoint
What does the 'alternation' (tardid) in the answer refer to?
A) The proof necessitates either the Maker's existence or knowledge of Him, but not both.
B) The answer chooses both sides depending on whether the proof is considered in itself or with inquiry.
C) The proof is sometimes a cause of knowledge and sometimes not.
D) The objection itself is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Rational inference yields knowledge because proofs inherently imply their conclusions, even though our knowledge depends on engaging with them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your belief in Allah, do you find yourself relying on the inherent truth of proofs even when you do not actively reflect on them? How does that affect the stability of your faith?
?
This lesson shows that proofs imply their conclusions regardless of our inquiry. Yet, we often feel that our faith weakens when we neglect reflection. Where does this disconnect come from in your own experience?
?
If the proof's necessity does not depend on your inquiry, what does that say about the responsibility you have to engage with it? Are you satisfied with leaving proofs unattended?

The Tenth Shubha: Distinguishing Knowledge from Ignorance
Objection that certain belief may be knowledge or ignorance, and reply that for us the distinction is evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tenth objection against the reliability of nazar and the Ash'ari reply that distinguishes knowledge from ignorance based on the inquirer's necessary knowledge of true premises.

Core Idea
Certainty alone does not guarantee knowledge; true premises and proper reasoning yield necessary knowledge.

In this passage, we encounter a powerful objection: how can we be sure that our reasoning leads to knowledge and not ignorance? Both appear certain. The Ash'ari response appeals to the inquirer's direct awareness of the truth of premises, which makes the conclusion necessarily known. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the tenth shubha. Notice how it argues that certain belief can be either knowledge or ignorance, and that we cannot tell them apart because they share certainty and reliance on a supposed necessitator.
The objection is especially strong for those who claim ignorance is similar to knowledge.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila premise
The objection relies on the claim that ignorance is similar to knowledge (tamathul). Understand why this similarity makes distinction impossible: if two things are similar, they are indistinguishable.
3
Understand the Ash'ari reply
Focus on the reply: 'when the inquirer obtains knowledge of true certain premises and their arrangement leading to the desired conclusion, he knows by necessity that the result is knowledge.' This is the key point: the inquirer has direct awareness of the truth of the premises.
The reply only applies to those who do not affirm similarity (i.e., the Ash'aris).
4
Reflect on the necessity
Consider why the Ash'ari say this is a matter of badiha (self-evident). How does this address the objection? If the inquirer knows the premises are true and correctly arranged, the conclusion is necessarily known as knowledge, not ignorance.
This avoids the problem of distinguishing because the distinction is evident to the inquirer.

الشبهة ( العاشرة: الاعتقاد الجازم قد يكون علماً ) لكونه مطابقاً مستنداً لموجب ( وقد يكون جهلاً) لكونه غير مطابق مستنداً إلى شبهة أو تقليد ) ولا يمكن التمييز بينهما ) لوجود اشتراكهما في الجزم والاستناد إلى ما يجزم أنه موجب (سيما عند من يقول الجهل مماثل للعلم، فإذاً ماذا يؤمننا أن يكون الحاصل عقيب النظر جهلاً) مستنداً إلى شبهة ( لا علماً مستنداً إلى موجب حقيقي ( قلنا هذا الذي ذكرتم ( إنما يلزم المعتزلة ) القائلين بالتماثل بينهما، وأما نحن فنقول : إذا حصل للناظر العلم بالمقدمات الصادقة القطعية وبترتبها المفضي إلى المطلوب فإنه يعلم بالبديهة أن اللازم عنه علم لا جهل مخالف للعلم في الحقيقة ) ولا يمكنهم التخلص ) عن هذا الإشكال ( بتميز العلم ) عن الجهل ) بركون النفس إليه ) دون الجهل ( فإن ذلك التميز بالركون ( مع التماثل ) بينهما ( مشكل ) لأن حكم المتماثلين ،واحد ، فكيف يتصور الركون إلى أحدهما دون الآخر؟ ( وأيضاً فيلزمهم الكفرة المصرون ) على اعتقاداتهم الباطلة الراكنون إليها على سبيل الاطمئنان التام وقيل للمعتزلة أن يتخلصوا عنه بأن قوله : ( وهذه الحيثية لا تفارق إلخ ) فقولكم : يلزم أن لا يبقى الدليل بتقدير عدم النظر فيه دليلاً إن أردتم انتفاء دلالته بالفعل فمسلم ، وإن أردتم انتفاء دلالته بالقوة فممنوع. قوله : ( لموجب) اللام للتعليل متعلق بالكون وليس صلة لمطابقاً . قوله : ( لوجود إلخ) ولا فرق بينهما إلا باستناد العلم إلى موجب حقيقي، واستناد العلم إلى موجب اعتقادي وبعبارة أخرى لا فرق بينهما إلا بالمطابقة ،وعدمها، ولا شك أن الاطلاع على الموجب الحقيقي وعدمه أو المطابقة وعدمها في غاية الخفاء. الضدين. قوله: (سيما عند من يقول إلخ) أي بتماثلهما فإن الاشتباه في المتماثلين أكثر بخلاف قوله : (فإذاً ماذا يؤمننا إلخ) فلا يحصل العلم بأن ما أفاده النظر علم، فهذه الشبهة أيضاً تفيد نفي العلم يكون المفاد علماً لا إفادته العلم. قوله : ( إنما يلزم إلخ) لأن الاشتباه إنما يقع في الأمثال لافي الأضداد . (قوله: (وقيل للمعتزلة إلخ) يعني أن الفرق بينهما إنما هو بالمطابقة وعدمها، فإذا أفاد قوله: (وقيل للمعتزلة أن يتخلصوا إلخ ويمكن أيضاً أن يقولوا : الجزم بأن اللازم علم لاجهل بواسطة مقدمتين هما أن هذا حاصل عن قطع يقيني، وما هو كذلك فعلم إما بالنظر أو بالحدس، ولا تسلسل في النظر لانقطاعه عند انقطاع الالتفات كما مر .
The tenth objection: Certain belief (i'tiqad jazim) may be knowledge, being conformable (mutabiq) and based on a real necessitator (mujib haqiqi), or it may be ignorance (jahl), being non-conformable and based on a specious argument or imitation (taqlid). It is impossible to distinguish between them because they share certainty and reliance on something judged to be a necessitator, especially according to those who say ignorance is similar to knowledge. So how can we be sure that what results from inquiry is ignorance based on a specious argument, not knowledge based on a real necessitator? We say: this which you have mentioned only applies to the Mu'tazila who affirm similarity between them. As for us, we say: when the inquirer obtains knowledge of true certain premises and their arrangement leading to the desired conclusion, he knows by necessity (bi al-badiha) that what necessarily follows from it is knowledge, not ignorance opposed to knowledge in reality. They cannot escape this difficulty by distinguishing knowledge from ignorance through the soul's reliance (rukoon) on it, without the ignorance, because that distinction by reliance, given the similarity between them, is problematic, for the judgment of two similar things is one; so how can reliance on one without the other be conceived? Also, it would entail that the persistent disbelievers, who firmly rely on their false beliefs with complete assurance, would have knowledge. It was said that the Mu'tazila could extricate themselves by... His statement: 'And this aspect does not leave etc.': so your statement 'it would entail that the proof would not remain a proof on the supposition of no inquiry' if you mean the negation of its actual indication, it is granted; if you mean the negation of its potential indication, it is denied. His statement: 'for a necessitator': the lam is for causation, attached to 'being', not a preposition for 'conformable'. His statement: 'for the existence etc.': there is no difference between them except that knowledge is based on a real necessitator, and ignorance on a believed necessitator; in other words, the difference is only in conformity or its absence, and there is no doubt that ascertaining the real necessitator or its absence, or conformity or its absence, is extremely obscure. Opposites. His statement: 'especially according to those who say etc.': i.e., due to their similarity, confusion is greater in similar things, unlike... His statement: 'so how can we be sure etc.': so one does not obtain knowledge that what inquiry yields is knowledge; this objection also denies knowledge that the yielded is knowledge, not the yielding of knowledge. His statement: 'only applies etc.': because confusion occurs only in similars, not opposites. (His statement: 'and it was said to the Mu'tazila to extricate etc.') means that the difference between them is only by conformity or its absence. So if it yields... His statement: 'and it was said to the Mu'tazila to extricate etc.' and it is also possible for them to say: certainty that the concomitant is knowledge not ignorance via two premises: that this is obtained from certain conviction, and whatever is so is knowledge either by inquiry or intuition, and there is no infinite regress in inquiry because it is cut off when attention is cut off, as previously.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth objection against the reliability of nazar: certain belief can be either knowledge or ignorance, and since they are both certain and based on something believed to be a necessitator, we cannot tell which results from inquiry. The objection is especially strong for those who claim ignorance is similar to knowledge. The reply distinguishes: this only applies to the Mu'tazila who affirm similarity. For us (Ahl al-Sunna), when one knows the premises are true and correct, one knows by necessity that the result is knowledge, not ignorance. The Mu'tazila cannot escape by appealing to the soul's 'reliance' (rukoon) because if ignorance and knowledge are similar, reliance should apply equally. Also, that would grant knowledge to persistent disbelievers who are fully confident. The commentator adds that the distinction is really about conformity to reality (mutabaqa), which is hidden, and that the Mu'tazila might try to use a two-premise argument to justify certainty, but the reply still stands.

Key Terms
اعتقاد جازم (i'tiqad jazim)
علم (ilm)
جهل (jahl)
تقليد (taqlid)
تماثل (tamathul)
مطابقة (mutabaqa)

Checkpoint
According to the reply in this passage, how do we know that the result of inquiry is knowledge and not ignorance?
A) By the soul's reliance on the belief.
B) By a two-premise argument that avoids infinite regress.
C) By the inquirer's knowledge of true certain premises and their arrangement, leading to a necessary realization.
D) By checking the belief against the opinions of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the tenth shubha is that the inquirer's direct knowledge of the truth of premises makes the conclusion necessarily known as knowledge, bypassing the problem of indistinguishability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a firm conviction about something important—like your purpose in life—do you ever pause to check whether that certainty stems from true premises or just comfortable familiarity? How would you begin that examination today?
?
The passage warns that certainty alone is not enough; your belief must conform to reality and be based on a real necessitator. Think of one belief you hold that you have never critically examined: by what concrete standard would you verify that it is knowledge and not ignorance?
?
If you were to apply the Ash'ari standard—that self-evident knowledge arises only from true certain premises arranged correctly—to your own process of forming beliefs, what would you discover about the reliability of your most treasured convictions? Are you willing to test them?

The Engineers' Denial: Reflection Only Yields Knowledge in Mathematics
Presents the engineers' claim and first proof against reflection in theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the engineers' position that reflection yields knowledge only in mathematical sciences, not in divine matters, and their first proof based on the inconceivability of divine realities.

Core Idea
The engineers reduce rational theology to mere conjecture by denying that reflection can yield certainty about God.

In this passage, Imam al-Sanusi introduces the second group of deniers: the engineers (muhandisūn). These are thinkers who accept the validity of logical inference in mathematics but reject it in theology. Their argument is subtle: they claim that divine realities cannot be conceived either through intuition or reasoning, so reflection can only produce conjecture, not certainty. Understanding their position helps clarify what is at stake in the debate over the use of reason in theological matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the engineers' overall claim
Read the passage and note that the engineers say reflection yields knowledge in geometry and arithmetic, but not in divine matters.
They do not reject reflection entirely—only in theology.
2
Understand their reasons
They argue that mathematical sciences are close to understanding, consistent, and error-free, whereas divine matters are far from minds and produce only conjecture.
This distinction is key to their argument.
3
Examine their first proof
The first proof: divine realities cannot be conceived either necessarily or through reflection, so reflection cannot produce knowledge about them.
This is a radical claim—if true, theology would be purely conjectural.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what follows if the engineers are correct: all theological reasoning would be uncertain, and only conjecture or 'what is more fitting' would be possible.
This sets up the Ash'ari response later.

لأنه لا شيء من التصورات ذاته النظر الصحيح العلم بالمطابقة حصل التمييز بينهما من غير فرق بين القول بالتماثل، وعدمه بدخول المطابقة وعدمها في ماهيتهما وخروجهما عنهما . قوله قريبة من الأفهام أي تنساق لا يكاد يقع الغلط فيها من هذه الجهة، إذا يعني جعلت بعضها مبادي لبعض . قوله : (لا يقع فيها (غلط) لكون المبادئ الأول أولية ،الذات والمناسبة والمبادي الثواني قطعية الذات بديهية المناسبة مترتبة، وقد رتب ترتباً ضروري الاستلزام، فلا يقع الغلط فيها لا حيث المادة ولا من حيث الصورة . قوله : ( بعيدة عن الأذهان إلخ تنساق إليها بكلفة ومشقة لاحتياجها إلى غاية التجرد عما ألفه الحس والوهم . قوله : ( لا تتصور أي يمتنع تصورها بالكنه كما يرشد إليه الدليل والجواب فلا يرد أن الحكم بعدم التصور يستدعي التصور ففيه تناقض . قوله : ( الثانية المهندسون قيل مآل الخلاف بيننا وبينهم إلى وجود النظر في القطعيات الإلهيات عندنا، وعدمه عندهم وحمل إنكارهم على الاعتراف بوجوده في الإلهيات قطعاً مع تخلف العلم عنه فيها بعيد جداً. قوله : (لا تتصور لا بالضرورة هذا إما إلزامي أو حكم ظني عندهم، وإلا فقد أفاد النظر العلم في الإلهيات بعدم تصور الحقائق الإلهية، وفيه أن الحكم بعدم تصورها يستدعي تصورها، فيتناقض إلا أن يدعي كفاية التصور بالوجه في الظني دون اليقيني، كما سيجيء وأيضاً قوله : إما لأنه لا شيء من التصورات بنظري لو The second group of deniers: the engineers said that it (i.e., reflection) yields knowledge in geometry and arithmetic because they are sciences close to understanding, consistent, well-ordered, in which no error occurs, but not in divine matters, for they are very far from minds, and the utmost end in them is conjecture and accepting what is more fitting and more appropriate regarding His essence, attributes, and acts. And they argued for this with two proofs: The first: The divine realities from His attributes cannot be conceived either by necessity—which is apparent—nor by reflection, because none of the conceptions [are possible]... his essence. Correct reflection: knowledge of correspondence, the distinction between them has been obtained without difference between the statements of similarity and its opposite, by including correspondence and its absence in their essence and their exclusion from them. His statement: (close to understanding) i.e., they are drawn to it without difficulty because their first principles are primary in themselves and in their suitability for the conclusions. His statement: (consistent, well-ordered) in the dictionary: 'itasaqa' means became ordered, and 'naẓama al-lu'lu' means strung pearls, so they are well-ordered; those issues apparently harmonize with each other, rarely causing error in this respect, meaning some are made principles for others. His statement: (no error occurs in them) because the first principles are primary in essence and suitability, and the secondary principles are certain in essence, intuitive in suitability, arranged in a sequence of necessary implication, so no error occurs in them neither materially nor formally. His statement: (far from minds, etc.) they are drawn to it with difficulty and hardship because they require utmost abstraction from what sense and imagination are accustomed to. His statement: (cannot be conceived) i.e., it is impossible to conceive them in their essence, as the proof and answer indicate; so the objection that the judgment of non-conception requires conception, leading to contradiction, does not arise. His statement: (the second group: the engineers) it is said the point of disagreement between us and them is the existence of reflection in certain divine matters for us, and its non-existence for them; interpreting their denial as admitting its existence in divine matters absolutely while knowledge fails to follow from it is very far-fetched. His statement: (cannot be conceived either by necessity) this is either a necessary consequence or a conjectural judgment for them; otherwise, reflection would have produced knowledge in divine matters by [asserting] the inconceivability of divine realities, and there is a contradiction in that the judgment of their inconceivability requires their conception, unless one claims that conception in a certain aspect suffices for conjecture but not certainty, as will come. Also, his statement: (because none of the conceptions) by reflection, if complete, would indicate that reflection does not produce knowledge at all, especially in simple matters, while they claim it produces knowledge in other than those; unless it is said that they claim it produces knowledge in other than divine matters, so the proposition is indefinite, true in some materials where the conception of the terms is necessary, and its non-production in them means universal negation.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the second group of deniers of the efficacy of reflection in divine matters: the engineers (al-muhandisūn). They claim that reflection (naẓar) yields certain knowledge in geometry and arithmetic because these sciences are close to the understanding, consistent, well-ordered, and free from error, but not in divine matters (al-ilāhiyyāt), which are far from minds and can only produce conjecture and the adoption of what is most fitting regarding God's essence, attributes, and acts. They support this with two proofs, the first of which is that divine realities cannot be conceived (lā tataṣawwar) either necessarily or through reflection. The commentary elaborates on the characteristics of mathematical sciences: their first principles are primary and suitable for the conclusions; they are well-ordered and arranged so that error is rare both in matter and form. In contrast, divine matters require extreme abstraction from sense and imagination. Regarding the first proof that divine realities cannot be conceived, the commentator clarifies that this means they cannot be grasped in their essence; the judgment of inconceivability does not entail a contradiction because it does not require full conception. The commentary also notes the point of disagreement: the Ash'aris affirm that reflection in divine matters can yield certainty, while the engineers deny it. Interpreting their denial as admitting that reflection exists but knowledge does not follow is far-fetched. The commentator also discusses the logical issues: if the engineers claim that no conception is possible through reflection, that would imply that reflection never yields knowledge at all, but they make an exception for mathematics; they must therefore be making a universal negative claim only for divine matters.

Correction: They accept reflection in mathematics but deny it in theology.

Key Takeaway
The engineers' first proof against reflection in theology is that divine realities cannot be conceived either necessarily or through reflection, reducing theology to conjecture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do you ever settle for comfortable conjecture instead of seeking certain knowledge from revelation and sound reason? What is one attribute you have treated as 'too far' to understand, and how will you actively pursue a firmer understanding of it starting today?
?
The engineers claimed that divine realities are utterly inconceivable. Have you ever dismissed a theological question as 'impossible to know' without first exerting genuine intellectual effort? Identify one such question you gave up on and commit to a structured study of it over the next week.
?
If you were to honestly assess your own reflection on matters of faith, do you approach them with the same rigor and confidence as you would a mathematical problem? If not, what practical step will you take this week to treat your theology with the seriousness it demands?

The Engineers' Challenge: Does Reasoning Yield Certainty in Theology?
Engineers' objection and refutation on reasoning in theology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the engineers' objection that reasoning yields only opinion in theology, and the response that conception through accident suffices for certain assent.

Core Idea
Correct reasoning in theology can yield certainty, not just opinion.

The engineers, a group of deniers, argued that theological truths are too remote from the mind to be known with certainty through reasoning. They distinguished between mathematical sciences, where reasoning yields certain knowledge, and theology, where it yields only opinion. In this passage, we examine their argument and the refutation that shows even if we cannot conceive divine realities in their essence, conception through an accident is sufficient for certain assent.

Guided Path
1
Read the engineers' objection
Focus on their claim that reasoning gives only opinion in theology, unlike mathematics. Identify the two proofs they offer.
Notice they first argue that divine realities cannot be conceived at all, which prevents assent.
2
Analyze the first proof
Understand why they say conception is impossible: not by necessity, nor by reasoning (definition or description).
Definition requires composition, which is absent in divine realities; description does not yield inner reality.
3
Understand the response's direct denial
The refutation begins by rejecting the absolute impossibility of conception, offering two possibilities: direct creation by God or transfer via a necessary concomitant.
This shows that while not universal, the route to conception is not closed.
4
Analyze the concessive argument
Even if essence-conception is impossible, conception through an accident suffices for certainty. This is a key point.
The engineers would also lose opinion if they insist on essence-conception, creating inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the overall force
Consider how the response defends theology against the charge of yielding only opinion, and how it turns the tables.
The engineers' own standard would undermine their position on opinion as well.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : النظر الصحيح عند الجمهور
الطائفة ( الثانية ) من المنكرين ( المهندسون قالوا : إنه ) أي النظر ( يفيد العلم في الهندسيات) والحسابيات لأنها علوم قريبة من الأفهام متسقة منتظمة لا يقع فيها غلط ) دون الإلهيات ) فإنها بعيدة عن الأذهان جداً ) والغاية) القصوى (فيها الظن والأخذ بالأحرى والأخلق) بذاته تعالى وصفاته وأفعاله ) واحتجوا) على ذلك ) بوجهين الأول الحقائق الإلهية من وصفاته ( لا تتصور لا بالضرورة وهو ظاهر ولا بالنظر إما لأنه لا شيء من التصورات بنظري كما ذهب إليه جمع، وإما لأنه إما بالحد وهو مختص بالمركب، ولا تركيب في الحقائق الإلهية، أو بالرسم وأنه لا يفيد العلم بالكنه ) والتصديق بها فرع التصور ) فامتنع التصديق أيضاً ( قلنا : لا نسلم أنها لا تتصور بحقائقها قطعاً) لجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى فينا العلم بكنه حقيقته وحقائق صفاته ابتداء، أو يكون هناك لازم ينتقل الذهن منه إلى كنه حقائقها، فإنه غير ممتنع وإن لم يكن الانتقال من اللازم إلى كنه الملزوم أمراً كلياً ( وإن سلم أنها لا تتصور بالكنه أصلاً ( فيكفي) للتصديق اليقيني ( تصورها بعارض (ما وهو حاصل بلا شبهة ثم (هذا الذي ذكرتموه (يلزمكم في الظن ) لأنه أيضاً تصديق متفرع على التصور، فيجب أن لا يكون حاصلاً في الإلهيات
قوله: (ولا تركيب في الحقائق الإلهية بالإجماع والاتفاق سواء تم الدليل على انتفاء تركب حقائق صفاته أو لا .
قوله : (بكنه حقيقته وحقائق صفاته ابتداء، فاللازم حينئذ عدم جريان النظر في التصورات الإلهية لا في التصديقات الإلهية التي هي المقصد الأقصى.
قوله : (ثم هذا يلزمكم في الظن لهم أن يقولوا التصور بالوجه يكفي في الظن دون الجزم، والفارق ظاهر لأن الظن لضعفه يصلح أن يكون مبناه التصور بوجه بخلاف اليقين، نعم لا يلزم في الجزم أيضاً التصور بالكنه، لكن هذا هو الجواب التسليمي المذكور أولاً
The Fifth Observatory - The Third Objective: Correct Reasoning according to the Majority. The second group of deniers (the engineers said: that it, i.e., reasoning, yields knowledge in geometrical and mathematical matters because they are sciences near to minds, consistent, organized, in which error does not occur, but not in theology, for it is very far from minds, and the ultimate goal in it is opinion and taking the worthier and more fitting concerning His essence, attributes, and actions. And they argued for that with two proofs: first, the divine realities from His attributes cannot be conceived either necessarily, which is apparent, nor by reasoning, either because none of the conceptions are by reasoning, as a group held, or because it is either by definition, which is specific to the composite, and there is no composition in the divine realities, or by description, and it does not yield knowledge of the inner reality; and assent is dependent on conception, so assent also is impossible. We said: we do not concede that they absolutely cannot be conceived in their realities, because it is possible that God may create in us knowledge of the inner reality of His essence and the realities of His attributes initially, or there may be a necessary concomitant from which the mind transfers to the inner reality of their realities, for that is not impossible even if transfer from the necessary concomitant to the inner reality of the necessitated is not universal. And if it is conceded that they cannot be conceived in their inner reality at all, then their conception through some accident is sufficient for certain assent, and that is obtained without doubt. Then this which you have mentioned applies to you in opinion, because it also is assent dependent on conception, so it must not occur in theology. Commentary: 'nor is there composition in the divine realities' by consensus and agreement, whether the proof for the denial of composition of the realities of His attributes is complete or not. Commentary: 'through the inner reality of His essence and the realities of His attributes initially', then the necessary conclusion is the non-occurrence of reasoning in divine conceptions, not in divine assents which are the ultimate objective. Commentary: 'then this applies to you in opinion' — they could say that conception through a face suffices for opinion but not for certainty, and the difference is apparent because opinion, due to its weakness, can be based on conception through a face, unlike certainty. Yes, certainty also does not require conception through inner reality, but this is the concessive answer mentioned first.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by the second group of deniers — the engineers — against the claim that correct reasoning yields certain knowledge in theology, and then provides a refutation. The engineers assert that reasoning only yields knowledge in mathematical and geometrical sciences because those are well-ordered and error-free, but in theology it yields only opinion because theological truths are far from the mind. They give two proofs: first, the divine realities cannot be conceived at all — not necessarily, nor by reasoning, since reasoning either uses definition (which needs composition) or description (which does not give inner reality). Since assent depends on conception, assent is also impossible. The response (قلنا) denies that they cannot be conceived in their realities absolutely, because God could create knowledge of their inner reality directly, or there could be a necessary concomitant that leads to it. Even if we concede they cannot be conceived in their inner reality, conception through an accident is sufficient for certain assent, since that conception is available. Furthermore, the engineers' argument would also eliminate opinion in theology, since opinion also depends on conception. The glosses clarify that the lack of composition in divine realities is agreed upon, that the responders' point about direct creation concerns conceptions not assents, and that the final argument could be countered by distinguishing opinion (which only needs a face) from certainty.

Misconception: If we cannot conceive the essence of divine realities, we cannot have any certain knowledge about them.
Correction: Conception through an accident (a non-essential attribute) is sufficient for certain assent, as the passage argues.

Checkpoint

A) Certainty
B) Opinion

D) Ignorance

Key Takeaway
Even without full knowledge of the essence, certain theological knowledge is possible through indirect conception, such as through an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may never fully grasp the essence of Allah, but this lesson says that does not prevent you from having certain knowledge about Him. Are you living as if that is true? Or do you let the mystery turn into doubt? What would change if you fully believed that your limited understanding is sufficient for certainty?
?
The engineers argued that theology can only yield opinion, not certainty. Have you ever treated your beliefs about Allah as mere opinion, subject to change or uncertainty? Where in your life do you need to anchor your certainty more firmly?
?
The refutation shows that even without essence-conception, certain assent is possible. Is your faith built on such certain assent, or on shaky assumptions? What concrete step can you take today to strengthen your conviction in what you know about Allah?

Refutation of Objection on Quiddity Knowledge for Certainty
Commentator refutes objection that divine quiddities are unknowable, arguing accident suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the possibility of certain knowledge about divine realities even without full quiddity knowledge, using necessary concomitants and accidental conception.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge of God does not require knowing His full essence; accidental knowledge suffices.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a powerful objection: if certainty requires knowing the quiddity (essence) of a thing, and divine quiddities are inaccessible, then theology cannot yield certainty. The response reveals a subtle epistemology—either God may grant direct quiddity knowledge, or a necessary concomitant can lead to it, and failing that, an accidental conception is sufficient for certain assent. This lesson unpacks this layered argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand why the objector thinks divine quiddities are unknowable: definition is only for composites, and divine realities have no composition; description does not give quiddity knowledge. Therefore, certainty is impossible.
The objection is presented in the first lines of the passage.
2
Follow the first response
The commentator responds that we do not concede quiddity knowledge is impossible; God could create it directly, or a necessary concomitant (lāzim) could lead the mind to the quiddity.
This response allows for a possible path to quiddity knowledge.
3
Examine the second response
Even if quiddity knowledge is impossible, conceptualization by an accident (taṣawwur bi-ʻāriḍ) suffices for certain assent (taṣdīq yaqīnī).
This is the key move: accidental features that are uniquely associated with the thing can ground certainty.
4
Consider the counter-objection
The objector retorts that the same reasoning would block probable opinion (ẓann), but the commentator replies that the distinction lies in the strength of the evidence.
The conciliatory answer states that quiddity is not required even for certainty.
5
Reflect on the key principle
Certainty can be based on accidental attributes without needing the full essence. This principle is crucial for kalam epistemology.

بنظري كما ذهب إليه جمع، وإما لأنه إما بالحد وهو مختص بالمركب، ولا تركيب في الحقائق الإلهية، أو بالرسم وأنه لا يفيد العلم بالكنه ) والتصديق بها فرع التصور ) فامتنع التصديق أيضاً ( قلنا : لا نسلم أنها لا تتصور بحقائقها قطعاً) لجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى فينا العلم بكنه حقيقته وحقائق صفاته ابتداء، أو يكون هناك لازم ينتقل الذهن منه إلى كنه حقائقها، فإنه غير ممتنع وإن لم يكن الانتقال من اللازم إلى كنه الملزوم أمراً كلياً ( وإن سلم أنها لا تتصور بالكنه أصلاً ( فيكفي) للتصديق اليقيني ( تصورها بعارض (ما وهو حاصل بلا شبهة ثم (هذا الذي ذكرتموه (يلزمكم في الظن ) لأنه أيضاً تصديق متفرع على التصور، فيجب أن لا يكون حاصلاً في الإلهيات

التصديق .
قوله : ( أمراً كلياً ) أي جارياً في كل لازم وملزوم .
قوله: (فيكفي إلخ) يعني التصديق اليقيني منوط بتصور الطرفين على وجه هو مناط الحكم، ويجوز أن يكون ذلك أمراً عارضاً، فلا نسلم كون التصديق اليقيني فرع التصور بالكنه، وما توهم من أنه يجوز أن يكون في ذاته ما يمنع التصديق الحاصل من التصور بالوجه فمدفوع بعدم التنافي بين مقتضيات الماهية. قوله : ( لأنه أيضاً تصديق إلخ ) فإذا كان التصديق اليقيني متفرعاً على التصور بالكنه يكون

By my reflection as a group held, and because it is either by definition which is specific to composite, and there is no composition in divine realities, or by description and it does not yield knowledge of the quiddity. We say: we do not concede that they cannot be conceptualized in their quiddities at all, because it is possible that God Most High creates in us knowledge of the quiddity of His essence and the quiddities of His attributes initially, or that there is a necessary concomitant from which the mind transfers to the quiddity of their realities, for that is not impossible even if the transfer from the concomitant to the quiddity of the necessary is not a universal matter. Then this that you have mentioned would apply to you in the case of probable opinion, because it is also assent branching from conceptualization, so it must also not be obtained in divine matters. His statement: (And assent etc.) i.e., certain assent to their specific states individually is a branch of conceptualization by quiddity, for if it were not conceptualized by quiddity, it would be possible that in its essence there is something preventing the assent that occurred by virtue of conceptualization by aspect. And by what we have mentioned, what was said is refuted: that if certainty were a branch of conceptualization by quiddity, then the judgment about divine realities that they are not conceptualized certainly would not be a specific judgment. His statement: (so assent is also impossible) shows that their saying that correct reflection in divine matters does not yield knowledge is because knowledge of them is impossible, due to the impossibility of what it branches on, namely conceptualization by quiddity. So what was said—that their disagreement about yielding refers to disagreement about the realization of correct reflection in divine matters or not, and otherwise no rational person would say that while it is realized in them it does not yield knowledge—is nothing. His statement: (they cannot be conceptualized by their quiddities) i.e., they cannot be conceptualized as such, so your statement 'so assent is impossible' is invalid. His statement: (a universal matter) i.e., applying to every concomitant and necessary. His statement: (then suffice etc.) means certain assent is contingent on conceptualization of the two extremes in a way that is the basis of judgment, and it is possible that this is an accidental matter, so we do not concede that certain assent is a branch of conceptualization by quiddity. And what was imagined—that it is possible that in its essence there is something preventing the assent obtained from conceptualization by aspect—is repelled by the fact that there is no incompatibility between the requisites of the essence. His statement: (because it is also assent etc.) If certain assent is a branch of conceptualization by quiddity, then... His statement: (by the quiddity of His essence and the quiddities of His attributes initially), so the necessary consequence then is the non-occurrence of reflection in divine conceptualizations, not in divine assents which are the ultimate aim. His statement: (Then this applies to you in the case of probable opinion) They could say: conceptualization by aspect suffices for probable opinion but not for certainty, and the difference is apparent because probable opinion, due to its weakness, can be based on conceptualization by aspect unlike certainty. Yes, conceptualization by quiddity is not required for certainty either, but this is the conciliatory answer mentioned first.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an objection-response argument concerning the necessity of conceptualizing the quiddity (تصور بالكنه) for certain assent (تصديق يقيني) in divine matters. The objector first argues that correct reflection in divine matters cannot yield knowledge because divine realities cannot be conceptualized by their quiddities—either because definition is only for composites, and there is no composition in divine realities, or because description does not give knowledge of the quiddity. Since certain assent is a branch of conceptualization, it is impossible. The responder (قلنا) replies: we do not concede that they cannot be conceptualized by their quiddities at all, for God could directly create knowledge of the quiddity, or there could be a necessary concomitant (لازم) that leads the mind to the quiddity. Even if one concedes they cannot be conceptualized by quiddity at all, conceptualization by an accident (تصور بعارض) suffices for certain assent, and such conceptualization is undoubtedly obtained. Then the objector turns the argument: if that were the case, then the same would apply to probable assent (ظن), since it is also assent branching from conceptualization, so it should also not be obtained in divine matters. The commentary (قوله) then explains each part: the initial phrase clarifies that certain assent to specific states requires quiddity conceptualization, but the responder's point is that accidental conceptualization can suffice, and the imagined objection that the essence might contain something preventing the assent is refuted by the principle that there is no incompatibility between the requisites of the essence (عدم التنافي بين مقتضيات الماهية). The commentary also addresses a counter-argument: if certainty required quiddity conceptualization, then the judgment that divine realities are not conceptualized would itself be uncertain, but that is not a specific judgment. Finally, the commentary notes that the objector could respond that accidental conceptualization suffices for probability but not for certainty, but the responder's conciliatory answer (الجواب التسليمي) is that quiddity conceptualization is not required even for certainty.

Correction: Certainty can be attained through accidental attributes that are uniquely connected to the essence, without needing full quiddity knowledge.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
In kalam, certain assent about divine matters is possible through accidental conceptualization, not only through quiddity knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If accidental conception suffices for certain assent, then why do you sometimes waver in your faith when faced with doubts? What 'accident' are you relying on that might be shaken, and how can you anchor it to something firmer?
?
The lesson argues that you do not need to know God's quiddity to have certain faith. Does this challenge your tendency to demand philosophical explanations before believing? Will you now accept the 'accidental' knowledge He has given you as sufficient for certainty, even if mysteries remain?

The Second Proof: The Soul's Unknowability
Commentator argues the soul's unknowability proves reflection does not yield knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul's unknowability demonstrates that reflection does not yield certain knowledge in theological matters.

Core Idea
The closest thing to us—our own soul—is unknown in its essence, proving reflection falls short.

This lesson presents the second proof from the soul's unknowability. The commentator argues that even the most intimate object of knowledge, the self, cannot be fully known through reflection. This undermines the claim that reflection yields certainty about metaphysical realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from the soul's unknowability to the failure of reflection.
Pay attention to the distinction between assent to existence and conceptualization of quiddity.
2
Identify the three aspects of the soul discussed
The passage mentions: (1) assent to existence (intuitive), (2) conceptualization of quiddity (unknown), and (3) assent to states (also unknown). Note which are known and which are not.
3
Understand the argument from disagreement
The author argues: if reflection yielded knowledge, rational thinkers would not disagree about the soul. Since they do disagree, reflection does not yield knowledge.
This is the core logical step.
4
Extend the argument to probable assent
The author extends the principle: if certain knowledge requires conceptualization of quiddity, so does probable assent. Thus, the soul's unknowability blocks even probable judgments about it.
Note the alternative view that probable assent might require less.
5
Reflect on the implication for divine matters
If the soul—closest to us—is unknowable, then reflection on God and the unseen is even more uncertain. This supports the skeptic's position.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : النظر الصحيح عند الجمهور
الوجه الثاني أقرب الأشياء إلى الإنسان) ( وأولاها بأن يكون معلوماً له بحقيقته وأحواله ( هويته) التي يشير إليها بقوله : إنا ( وإنها غير معلومة لا من حيث التصديق بوجودها فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه، بل من حيث ) تصورها بكنهها، ومن حيث التصديق بأحوالها من كونها عرضاً أو جوهراً، مجرداً أو جسمانياً منقسماً أو غير منقسم إلى غير ذلك من صفاتها (إذ قد كثر الخلاف فيها كثرة لا يمكن معها مع تلك الكثرة ( الجزم بشيء من الأقوال المختلفة ) المتنافية ) التي ذكرت فيها في تلك الهوية (كما ستقف عليها ) على تلك الأقوال في مباحث النفس، فلو كان النظر يفيد العلم بتلك الهوية وصفاتها، لما اختار العقلاء الناظرون فيها أقوالاً متناقضة ) وإذا كان أقرب الأشياء إليه كذلك ) أي بحيث لا يفيد
التصديق الظني أيضاً كذلك، إذ لا فرق بينهما في أن كلاً منهما يستدعي تصور الطرفين على ما هو مناط الحكم، فإذا وجب التصور بالكنه في التصديق اليقيني لجواز أن يكون في ذاتهما ما ذلك التصديق، وجب في التصديق الظني أيضاً، لجواز أن يكون في ذاتهما ما يمنع التصديق، وقيل الظني لضعفه يجوز أن يكفي فيه التصور بالوجه الذي هو ضعيف بخلاف
التصديق اليقيني .
قوله : ( وأولاها إلخ) أي لكونها حاضرة عنده دائماً، والعلم ليس إلا حضور المدرك عند المدرك وفيه إشارة إلى أن المراد الأقرب إدراكاً لا ذاتاً .
قوله : ( فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه إذ كل أحد يعلم بأنه موجود حتى الصبيان والمجانين، وهذا التصديق ليس بالأحوال المخصوصة حتى يستدعي تصوره بالكنه، فلا يرد أنه إذا كان التصديق اليقيني فرع التصور بالكنه عندهم كيف يقولون بحصول هذا التصديق؟ مع عدم التصور بالكنه .
قوله : ( الثاني أقرب الأشياء إلخ ينبغي أن يقيدوا الأشياء بالغائبة عن الحواس، وعدم الاتساق والقرب من الأوهام كيلا ينقض دليلهم بالهندسيات والحسابيات والممكنات، ثم إنه إنما يتم على تقدير تسليم عدم معلومية النفس أن لو كانت أقربيتها في المدركية، وإذا لا يلزم من أقربيتها اتصالاً أقربيتها إدراكاً ألا يرى أن القوة الحاسة لا تدرك نفسها لم يلزم مدعاهم .
قوله : (لا) من حيث التصديق بوجودها فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه فيه بحث لأن التصديق عندهم يستدعي تصور المحكوم عليه بالكنه كما تبين من دليلهم الأول، وإذا لم تكن النفس معلومة من حيث التصور فكيف يقولون هي معلومة من حيث التصديق بالوجود بداهة؟ والحمل على بداهة التصديق الظني بوجودها بعيد اللهم إلا أن يبني الكلام على إرادة إلزام الخصوم بأنها غير معلومة عندكم، فلزمكم الاعتراف بما ذكرنا فمرادهم بقوله : فإنه بديهي لا خلاف فيه أنه بديهي عندكم لا خلاف فيه بينكم.
The Fifth Observatory - Third Objective: Correct Reflection According to the Majority. The second proof: The closest of things to man, and most worthy of being known to him in its quiddity and states, is his own self (هويته) which he indicates by his saying 'I'. Yet it is unknown—not from the aspect of assent to its existence, for that is intuitive and indisputable, but from the aspect of conceptualization of its quiddity, and from the aspect of assent to its states, such as whether it is an accident or a substance, abstract or corporeal, divisible or indivisible, and other attributes. For disagreement about it has become so widespread that with that multiplicity it is impossible to be certain of any of the conflicting statements that have been mentioned about that self, as you will encounter those statements in the investigations of the soul. Were reflection to yield knowledge of that self and its attributes, rational thinkers who reflect on it would not have chosen contradictory statements. And since the closest thing to him is like this—i.e., it does not yield even probable assent similarly, for there is no difference between the two in that each requires conceptualization of the two extremes in the way that is the basis of judgment. So if conceptualization by quiddity is required for certain assent due to the possibility that in their essence there is something that prevents that assent, then it is also required for probable assent due to the possibility that in their essence there is something that prevents assent. And it is said that for probable assent, due to its weakness, it is permissible that conceptualization by aspect, which is weak, suffices, unlike certain assent. His statement: (and most worthy etc.) i.e., because it is always present with him, and knowledge is nothing but the presence of the perceived thing to the perceiver, and it indicates that what is meant is the nearest in perception, not in essence. His statement: (for it is intuitive and indisputable) since everyone knows that he exists, even children and madmen. And this assent is not about specific states so as to require its conceptualization by quiddity, so the objection does not arise that if certain assent is a branch of conceptualization by quiddity according to them, how do they claim to have this assent without conceptualization by quiddity? His statement: (the second proof: the closest of things etc.) They should qualify 'things' as those absent from the senses, and lacking in consistency and proximity to illusions, so that their argument is not refuted by geometries, calculations, and contingents. Then it is valid only on the assumption of conceding the unknowability of the soul, if its proximity is in perceptibility. And if proximity in connection does not entail proximity in perception—do you not see that the sensitive faculty does not perceive itself?—their claim does not follow. His statement: (not from the aspect of assent to its existence, for it is intuitive and indisputable) contains a point of inquiry, because assent according to them requires conceptualization of the subject of judgment by quiddity, as shown in their first proof. If the soul is unknown from the aspect of conceptualization, how can they say it is known from the aspect of assent to existence by intuition? And interpreting it as the intuition of probable assent to its existence is far-fetched, unless the statement is based on intending to compel the opponents that it is unknown according to you, so you must concede what we mentioned. So what they mean by saying 'it is intuitive and indisputable' is that it is intuitive according to you, indisputable among you.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (الوجه الثاني) that correct reflection does not yield knowledge in divine matters. The proof is based on the soul (النفس), which is the closest thing to a person and thus most worthy of being known in its quiddity and states. Yet it is unknown: not in its existence (which is intuitive), but in its quiddity and in its specific attributes (such as whether it is substance or accident, abstract or corporeal). The commentator notes that disagreement about the soul is so extensive that no certain judgment can be made. If reflection yielded knowledge, rational thinkers would not have chosen contradictory views. The soul's unknowability then extends to divine matters, because the same principle applies: both certain and probable assent require conceptualization of the two extremes. If quiddity conceptualization is required for certainty, it is also required for probability—or at least it is argued that probability might be satisfied with weaker conceptualization. The commentary then clarifies that 'most worthy' means nearest in perception, not in essence. It also addresses a potential objection: if certain assent requires quiddity conceptualization, how can they say existence of the soul is known intuitively? The answer is that this existence is not a specific state requiring quiddity, or it is meant as a concession to the opponents. Finally, the commentator advises that 'closest of things' should be restricted to things absent from the senses and far from illusions, to avoid counterexamples from geometry and mathematics.

Key Terms
النفس
الهوية
التصور بالكنه
التصديق بالوجود
بديهي
مباحث النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the soul is known because we experience it directly, so reflection on the soul should yield certain knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that while we have intuitive knowledge of the soul's existence, its quiddity and specific attributes are unknown and disputed, proving reflection fails.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the soul's unknowability proves reflection does not yield knowledge?
A) Because the soul is simple and cannot be defined
B) Because rational thinkers disagree about the soul despite it being closest to us
C) Because the soul is not a physical object
D) Because reflection on the soul is forbidden

Key Takeaway
The soul's unknowability, despite being closest to us, shows that reflection cannot yield certainty, and this principle extends to all objects of reflection.

The Second Objection: Need for a Teacher
Atheists argue need for teachers; response distinguishes difficulty from impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the atheists' objection about needing teachers fails because it confuses difficulty with impossibility.

Core Idea
Need does not mean impossibility; difficulty is not a proof of dependence.

In this passage, a common atheist objection is raised: if even simple sciences require a teacher, how can profound sciences be understood without one? The response is a critical distinction between difficulty and impossibility. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows why the objection misses its mark.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the atheist argument: people need teachers for simple sciences like grammar, so they must need them for profound sciences like theology.
The objection seems plausible on the surface.
2
Identify the two senses of 'need'
The response distinguishes between need in terms of difficulty (عسر) and need in terms of impossibility (امتناع). Find these terms in the passage.
This is the key move in the argument.
3
Understand why the objection fails
The objection only shows that learning without a teacher is difficult, not that it's impossible. The response concedes the difficulty but denies the impossibility.
The conclusion does not follow from the premise.
4
Reflect on the wider significance
This distinction protects the possibility of rational knowledge of God. It shows that difficulty is not a barrier to certainty.

But in the sense of impossibility, we do not concede it, and your statement does not prove it.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection of the atheists and the response. The objection: people need teachers even in simple sciences (like grammar, morphology, prosody) that require minimal reflection, so surely they need teachers in the profound sciences (like theology) that are far from sense perception and ordinary nature, and where certainty is required. The response distinguishes two senses of 'need' (الاحتياج): (1) need in the sense of difficulty—it is difficult to attain knowledge without a teacher, but not impossible; this is conceded (مسلم). (2) need in the sense of impossibility—knowledge cannot be attained without a teacher at all; this is not conceded, and the objection does not prove it. Thus the objection fails because it only demonstrates difficulty, not impossibility.

Correction: The passage shows that this need is a matter of difficulty, not impossibility. Correct consideration can still attain certainty, though with effort.

The objection that teachers are needed fails because it only proves difficulty, not impossibility, of rational knowledge.

Distinguishing Recollection from Initial Investigation
Mu'tazila distinguish between recollection and initial investigation to avoid tahsil al-hasil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila distinguish between recollection and initial investigation to avoid the absurdity of tahsil al-hasil.

Core Idea
Recollection follows knowledge; initial investigation precedes it.

In this passage, the Mu'tazila offer a second response to the companions' analogy. They argue that recollection and initial investigation are fundamentally different: recollection occurs after knowledge has been attained, while initial investigation occurs before. This distinction allows them to reject the analogy without falling into logical absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the full translation. Notice the key terms and the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بالفرق قالوا' – they are making a distinction.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms 'التذكر', 'ابتداء النظر', and 'تحصيل الحاصل' in the text. Understand their meanings in context.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the temporal distinction
The Mu'tazila assert that recollection occurs after knowledge (بعد حصول العلم) while initial investigation occurs before it (قبله). This temporal difference is the basis of their argument.
Consider why this timing matters for the generation of knowledge.
4
Trace the logical consequence
Because recollection is after knowledge, generating knowledge from it would be 'tahsil al-hasil' (obtaining what is already obtained), which is absurd. Therefore, recollection does not generate knowledge. But initial investigation, being before knowledge, faces no such absurdity, so it may generate knowledge.
The Mu'tazila use this to break the analogy.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how this distinction specifically refutes the companions' analogy. The companions had equated recollection and initial investigation; the Mu'tazila show they are different in kind.
This is a classic example of 'farq' (distinction) in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً جواب آخر للمعتزلة عن قياس الأصحاب بالفرق قالوا: التذكر إنما يكون بعد حصول العلم وابتداء النظر قبله فلا يلزم من عدم توليد التذكر لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل عدم توليد ابتداء النظر الذي لا يلزمه هذا المحال.
And also another answer for the Mu'tazila against the analogy of the companions, by distinction: they said: recollection only occurs after the attainment of knowledge, while initial investigation is before it. So, the non-generation of recollection does not necessitate the non-generation of initial investigation, in order to avoid the absurdity of obtaining what is already obtained, which does not apply to initial investigation.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents a second response from the Mu'tazila to the companions' analogy. They argue by making a distinction (farq): recollection (tadhakkur) takes place after knowledge has already been obtained, whereas initial investigation (ibtida' al-nazar) occurs before knowledge. Therefore, the fact that recollection does not generate knowledge (because it would involve obtaining what is already obtained, tahsil al-hasil) does not imply that initial investigation also fails to generate knowledge. In initial investigation, there is no risk of tahsil al-hasil because knowledge is not yet present. Thus, the analogy between the two is invalid.

Key Terms
التذكر (tadhakkur)
ابتداء النظر (ibtida' al-nazar)
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil)
الفرق (farq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Recollection and initial investigation are analogous because both involve seeking knowledge.
Correction: The Mu'tazila emphasize that they differ in temporal order: recollection comes after knowledge, initial investigation before. Therefore, the logical implications differ.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila argue that recollection does not parallel initial investigation?
A) Because recollection is a type of knowledge.
B) Because recollection occurs after knowledge, avoiding tahsil al-hasil.
C) Because initial investigation is not possible.
D) Because they reject analogy altogether.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila refute the analogy by showing that recollection and initial investigation differ in temporal order, thus avoiding tahsil al-hasil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Core Argument: Why Reflection, Not Recollection, Generates Knowledge
Al-Iji presents the objection from tadhakkur and the mutakallimun's response based on timing and tahsil al-hasil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun argue that the non-generation of knowledge in recollection does not undermine the generative capacity of initial reflection.

Core Idea
Timing is everything: recollection follows knowledge, reflection precedes it.

The mutakallimun faced an objection: if recalling known information does not produce new knowledge, why should first-time reflection? Their response hinges on a crucial temporal distinction. In this lesson, we will read the passage presenting the objection and the key differentiation, and then unpack the argument from tahsil al-hasil.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Carefully read the Arabic passage that presents the objection from recollection and the mutakallimun's answer. Focus on the key phrase identifying the difference in timing.
Notice the words 'بعد حصول العلم' and 'قبله'.
2
Identify the core distinction
The mutakallimun argue that recollection occurs after knowledge, while initial reflection occurs before knowledge. This timing difference is why the non-generation in recollection does not imply non-generation in reflection.
This is the central point of the argument.
3
Understand the tahsil al-hasil argument
The mutakallimun add that if recollection were to generate knowledge, it would lead to 'obtaining the obtained' (tahsil al-hasil), which is impossible. This impossibility does not apply to initial reflection.
Tahsil al-hasil is a key concept in kalam logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الرابع : كيفية إفادة النظر الصحيح للعلم بين منع) وجود (الجامع) في الفرع ( ومنع) وجود (الحكم) في الأصل فإنه يقول : عدم التوليد في التذكر معلل عندي بعدم المقدورية، فإن صح هذا لم توجد العلة في الفرع الذي هو ابتداء النظر، وإن لم يصح عدم المقدورية في التذكر منعنا عدم توليده ( وأيضاً ) جواب آخر للمعتزلة عن قياس الأصحاب بالفرق قالوا : ( التذكر) إنما يكون ( بعد حصول العلم وابتداء النظر (قبله فلا يلزم من عدم توليد التذكر، لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل عدم توليد ابتداء النظر الذي لا يلزمه هذا المحال قوله : ( والخصم فيه بين إلخ) أي الخصم في الجواب دائر بين هذين الأمرين. قوله: (جواب آخر إلخ) في الجواب الأول منع لعلية علة المستدل أعني كونه نظراً صحيحاً بإبداء علة أخرى أعني عدم المقدورية، والثاني منع لعليتها استقلالاً بإبداء شرط هو لزوم تحصيل الحاصل وما قيل : أن لزوم تحصيل الحاصل إنما يظهر فيما إذا غفل عن النظر دون العلم بالمنظور فيه، وليس بشيء لأنه على تقدير الغفلة عن المنظور فيه اللازم تذكر العلم لا العلم، ولذا صرح الشارح في الإلهيات بأن المراد صورة الغفلة عن النظر والعلم بالمنظور فيه أيضاً . قوله : ( لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل قيل : هذا إنما يظهر فيما إذا غفل عن النظر دون العلم بالمنظور فيه والظاهر أن كلامهم عام بل قد صرح الشارح في الإلهيات بأن المراد صورة الغفلة عن النظر والعلم بالمنظور فيه أيضاً، والحق أن المنظور فيه إن كان معلوماً مشاهداً للنفس، فتذكر النظر لا يفيد العلم به، ولا تذكره للزوم تحصيل الحاصل، وإن كان معلوماً غير مشاهد فهو يفيده تذكره وإن صار نسياً منسياً، فهو يستلزم العلم به فتأمل
The Fifth Observation - Fourth Section: How correct reflection yields knowledge. Between preventing the existence of the comprehensive in the branch and preventing the existence of the judgment in the original, for he says: The absence of generation in recollection is explained by me as due to lack of capability. If this is correct, then the cause is not present in the branch which is the beginning of reflection; and if the lack of capability in recollection is not correct, we prevent its non-generation. And also another response to the Mu'tazila regarding the analogy of the companions by differentiation: they said that recollection only occurs after the acquisition of knowledge, and the beginning of reflection is before it, so the non-generation of recollection does not imply the non-generation of the beginning of reflection, in order to avoid the necessity of obtaining the obtained, which is impossible. His saying: (The opponent in it is between etc.) i.e., the opponent in the response revolves around these two matters. His saying: (Another response etc.) In the first response, there is prevention of the causality of the cause of the arguer, namely its being correct reflection, by adducing another cause, namely the lack of capability; and the second is prevention of its causality independently by adducing a condition, namely the necessity of obtaining the obtained. And it was said that the necessity of obtaining the obtained appears only when one is heedless of the reflection, not of the knowledge of the object of reflection, but this is nothing, because on the assumption of heedlessness of the object of reflection, what is necessary is the recollection of knowledge, not knowledge itself. Hence the commentator stated in Ilahiyyat that what is intended is the case of heedlessness of both the reflection and the knowledge of the object of reflection. His saying: (Lest the obtaining of the obtained be necessary. It was said: This appears only when one is heedless of the reflection, not of the knowledge of the object of reflection, and apparently their speech is general, but the commentator stated in Ilahiyyat that what is intended is also the case of heedlessness of both the reflection and the knowledge of the object of reflection. The truth is that if the object of reflection is known and witnessed by the soul, then recollecting the reflection does not yield knowledge of it, nor does recollecting it due to the necessity of obtaining the obtained; but if it is known but not witnessed, then recollecting it yields it even if it has become forgotten and obliterated, so it necessitates knowledge of it; so contemplate.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's argument about the relationship between correct reflection (al-nazar al-sahih) and the resulting knowledge. It begins by describing the three possibilities when a legal analogy (qiyas) is challenged: one may deny the existence of the common attribute (al-jami') in the branch, or deny the existence of the judgment (al-hukm) in the original, or deny the causal link. The text then focuses on an objection: if recollection (al-tadhakkur) does not generate knowledge because the person already knows, then why should initial reflection (ibtida' al-nazar) generate knowledge? The mutakallimun respond by differentiating: recollection comes after knowledge whereas initial reflection is before knowledge, so the non-generation of recollection does not imply the non-generation of initial reflection, otherwise it would lead to the absurdity of obtaining what is already obtained (tahsil al-hasil). The marginal commentary explains that the opponent's response can be either preventing the causality by adducing another cause (lack of capability) or preventing its independence by adducing a condition (necessity of obtaining the obtained). The commentary then discusses when the problem of tahsil al-hasil arises, concluding that if the object of reflection is already mentally present, recollection does not produce new knowledge, but if it is forgotten, recollection does produce knowledge.

Key Terms
النظر
التذكر
التوليد
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if recollection does not generate knowledge, then no form of reflection can generate knowledge.
Correction: The mutakallimun clarify that recollection is after knowledge, while initial reflection is before knowledge, so they are not analogous.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, why does the non-generation in recollection not imply non-generation in initial reflection?
A) Because recollection is a different type of mental act.
B) Because recollection occurs after knowledge, while initial reflection occurs before knowledge.
C) Because the object of recollection is always unknown.
D) Because the cause of generation is absent in both cases.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun defend the generative power of initial reflection by distinguishing it from recollection based on timing relative to knowledge, thereby avoiding the absurdity of tahsil al-hasil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In your personal study, are you merely recollecting and rearranging what you already know, or are you engaging in genuine initial reflection? How can you tell the difference, and what one practical step will you take today to move from tadhakkur to ibtida' al-nazar?
