Refining the Objection: Two Responses and the Condition of Tahsil al-Hasil
Al-Iji explains the two responses to the objection and the condition under which tahsil al-hasil applies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct ways the mutakallimun can respond to the objection from recollection and when the problem of tahsil al-hasil actually applies.

Core Idea
The objection is deflected either by denying the cause (capability) or by adding a condition (tahsil al-hasil).

After the initial response, the commentary elaborates two strategies to block the analogy between recollection and initial reflection. The first prevents the causality of reflection by citing lack of capability, while the second prevents its independence by invoking the condition of tahsil al-hasil. This lesson delves into these responses and the subtle case where recollection might still generate knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two responses
Read the Arabic passage that discusses the two ways the opponent can respond to the mutakallimun's original argument. Note the distinction between preventing causality and preventing independence.
Look for the phrases 'منع لعلية' and 'منع لعليتها استقلالاً'.
2
Contrast the two responses
The first response denies that reflection is the cause of knowledge, offering an alternative cause (lack of capability). The second response allows reflection as a cause but adds a condition (that knowledge be not already present) to prevent independence.
The second response focuses on tahsil al-hasil as a blocking condition.
3
Examine when tahsil al-hasil applies
The passage concludes by clarifying that tahsil al-hasil only arises when the object of reflection is already mentally present. If it is forgotten, recollection does produce knowledge, so the objection no longer stands.
This nuance is important for the completeness of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الرابع : كيفية إفادة النظر الصحيح للعلم بين منع) وجود (الجامع) في الفرع ( ومنع) وجود (الحكم) في الأصل فإنه يقول : عدم التوليد في التذكر معلل عندي بعدم المقدورية، فإن صح هذا لم توجد العلة في الفرع الذي هو ابتداء النظر، وإن لم يصح عدم المقدورية في التذكر منعنا عدم توليده ( وأيضاً ) جواب آخر للمعتزلة عن قياس الأصحاب بالفرق قالوا : ( التذكر) إنما يكون ( بعد حصول العلم وابتداء النظر (قبله فلا يلزم من عدم توليد التذكر، لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل عدم توليد ابتداء النظر الذي لا يلزمه هذا المحال قوله : ( والخصم فيه بين إلخ) أي الخصم في الجواب دائر بين هذين الأمرين. قوله: (جواب آخر إلخ) في الجواب الأول منع لعلية علة المستدل أعني كونه نظراً صحيحاً بإبداء علة أخرى أعني عدم المقدورية، والثاني منع لعليتها استقلالاً بإبداء شرط هو لزوم تحصيل الحاصل وما قيل : أن لزوم تحصيل الحاصل إنما يظهر فيما إذا غفل عن النظر دون العلم بالمنظور فيه، وليس بشيء لأنه على تقدير الغفلة عن المنظور فيه اللازم تذكر العلم لا العلم، ولذا صرح الشارح في الإلهيات بأن المراد صورة الغفلة عن النظر والعلم بالمنظور فيه أيضاً . قوله : ( لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل قيل : هذا إنما يظهر فيما إذا غفل عن النظر دون العلم بالمنظور فيه والظاهر أن كلامهم عام بل قد صرح الشارح في الإلهيات بأن المراد صورة الغفلة عن النظر والعلم بالمنظور فيه أيضاً، والحق أن المنظور فيه إن كان معلوماً مشاهداً للنفس، فتذكر النظر لا يفيد العلم به، ولا تذكره للزوم تحصيل الحاصل، وإن كان معلوماً غير مشاهد فهو يفيده تذكره وإن صار نسياً منسياً، فهو يستلزم العلم به فتأمل
The Fifth Observation - Fourth Section: How correct reflection yields knowledge. Between preventing the existence of the comprehensive in the branch and preventing the existence of the judgment in the original, for he says: The absence of generation in recollection is explained by me as due to lack of capability. If this is correct, then the cause is not present in the branch which is the beginning of reflection; and if the lack of capability in recollection is not correct, we prevent its non-generation. And also another response to the Mu'tazila regarding the analogy of the companions by differentiation: they said that recollection only occurs after the acquisition of knowledge, and the beginning of reflection is before it, so the non-generation of recollection does not imply the non-generation of the beginning of reflection, in order to avoid the necessity of obtaining the obtained, which is impossible. His saying: (The opponent in it is between etc.) i.e., the opponent in the response revolves around these two matters. His saying: (Another response etc.) In the first response, there is prevention of the causality of the cause of the arguer, namely its being correct reflection, by adducing another cause, namely the lack of capability; and the second is prevention of its causality independently by adducing a condition, namely the necessity of obtaining the obtained. And it was said that the necessity of obtaining the obtained appears only when one is heedless of the reflection, not of the knowledge of the object of reflection, but this is nothing, because on the assumption of heedlessness of the object of reflection, what is necessary is the recollection of knowledge, not knowledge itself. Hence the commentator stated in Ilahiyyat that what is intended is the case of heedlessness of both the reflection and the knowledge of the object of reflection. His saying: (Lest the obtaining of the obtained be necessary. It was said: This appears only when one is heedless of the reflection, not of the knowledge of the object of reflection, and apparently their speech is general, but the commentator stated in Ilahiyyat that what is intended is also the case of heedlessness of both the reflection and the knowledge of the object of reflection. The truth is that if the object of reflection is known and witnessed by the soul, then recollecting the reflection does not yield knowledge of it, nor does recollecting it due to the necessity of obtaining the obtained; but if it is known but not witnessed, then recollecting it yields it even if it has become forgotten and obliterated, so it necessitates knowledge of it; so contemplate.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's argument about the relationship between correct reflection (al-nazar al-sahih) and the resulting knowledge. It begins by describing the three possibilities when a legal analogy (qiyas) is challenged: one may deny the existence of the common attribute (al-jami') in the branch, or deny the existence of the judgment (al-hukm) in the original, or deny the causal link. The text then focuses on an objection: if recollection (al-tadhakkur) does not generate knowledge because the person already knows, then why should initial reflection (ibtida' al-nazar) generate knowledge? The mutakallimun respond by differentiating: recollection comes after knowledge whereas initial reflection is before knowledge, so the non-generation of recollection does not imply the non-generation of initial reflection, otherwise it would lead to the absurdity of obtaining what is already obtained (tahsil al-hasil). The marginal commentary explains that the opponent's response can be either preventing the causality by adducing another cause (lack of capability) or preventing its independence by adducing a condition (necessity of obtaining the obtained). The commentary then discusses when the problem of tahsil al-hasil arises, concluding that if the object of reflection is already mentally present, recollection does not produce new knowledge, but if it is forgotten, recollection does produce knowledge.

الاستقلال
الشرط
تحصيل الحاصل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the problem of tahsil al-hasil always applies whenever one recollects something, regardless of whether the object is mentally present.
Correction: The passage clarifies that tahsil al-hasil only applies if the object of reflection is already present to the soul; if it is forgotten, recollection does generate knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the problem of tahsil al-hasil arise in recollection?
A) Whenever one recollects anything.
B) Only when the object of reflection is known and witnessed by the soul.
C) Only when the object of reflection is unknown.
D) Only when one is heedless of the reflection.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's argument is refined by showing that the objection can be met by either denying the cause or conditioning it, and that tahsil al-hasil only blocks knowledge when the object is already present to the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Is there an area of your faith where you assume you already 'witness' the truth (like the object being present), and thus you neglect deep reflection? How might this assumption be blocking you from a more profound realization this lesson demands?
?
The passage distinguishes between knowledge that is witnessed and knowledge that is forgotten. What forgotten truths about Allah, your own soul, or the akhirah have you neglected to recollect through sincere reflection? What specific step will you take this week to bring that knowledge back to life in your heart?

The Two Absolute Conditions for Reasoning
Al-Jurjani enumerates the two absolute conditions for reasoning and explains their opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two absolute conditions required for any act of reasoning (nazar) and the types of opposites that prevent it.

Core Idea
Reasoning requires both an active intellect and the absence of any obstacle.

Al-Jurjani lays down the foundational prerequisites for speculative reasoning, known as nazar. He explains that beyond being alive, two conditions must be met: one positive (possessing intellect) and one negative (absence of anything that opposes reasoning). He then categorizes the opposing factors, showing not just what blocks reasoning but why. This passage is crucial for understanding when reasoning is even possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then read the translation to get the overall structure.
Notice the concise style and the use of wujūdī and 'adamī.
2
Identify the two conditions
The first condition is positive: existence of intellect (al-'aql). The second is privative: absence of the opposite of nazar.
Intellect is the ground of obligation (manāṭ al-taklīf).
3
Distinguish the types of opposites
Opposites are either general (like sleep, heedlessness, fainting) which block all perception, or specific (knowledge of the sought or compound ignorance) which block nazar specifically.
General opposites oppose perception absolutely; specific ones oppose reasoning in particular.
4
Understand why they block reasoning
Knowledge of the sought makes seeking impossible. Compound ignorance (certainty contrary to truth) prevents nazar because the person believes they already know, and it eliminates the doubt required by some scholars.
Al-Jurjani compares compound ignorance's blocking effect to fullness preventing eating.
5
Reflect on the role of doubt
Note the reference to Abū Hāshim's view that doubt (shakk) is a condition for reasoning. Compound ignorance contradicts doubt.
This connects to broader debates in uṣūl al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرط النظر إما مطلقا سواء كان صحيحاً أو فاسداً فبعد الحياة أمران الأول وجودي وهو وجود العقل الذي هو مناط التكليف وسيأتي تفسيره الثاني عدمي وهو عدم ضده أي ضد النظر وهو ما ينافيه فمنه ما هو عام يضاد النظر وغيره وهو كل ما هو ضد الإدراك مطلقاً من النوم والغفلة والغشية فإنه يضاد النظر لاستلزامه الإدراك ومنه ما هو خاص يضاد النظر بخصوصه وهو العلم بالمطلوب من حيث هو مطلوب وأما العلم به من وجه فلا بد منه ليمكن طلبه والجهل المركب به أعني الجزم به على خلاف ما هو عليه إذ صاحبهما لا يتمكن من النظر فيه أما صاحب الأول فلامتناع طلب العلم مع حصوله وأما صاحب الثاني فلانه جازم بكونه عالما وذلك يمنعه من الإقدام على النظر إما لأنه صارف عنه كالامتلاء عن الأكل وإما لأنه مناف للشك الذي هو شرط النظر عند أبي هاشم
The condition of reasoning (nazar), either absolutely (whether correct or corrupt), so after life are two things: the first is positive (wujūdī) and it is the existence of the intellect (al-‘aql) which is the ground of obligation (manāṭ al-taklīf) and its explanation will come; the second is privative (‘adamī) and it is the absence of its opposite, i.e., the opposite of reasoning, which is what contradicts it. Among these opposites is what is general (ʻāmm) and contradicts reasoning and other things, and it is everything that is opposed to perception (idrāk) absolutely, such as sleep, heedlessness (ghafla), and fainting (ghashya), for it contradicts reasoning because it necessitates perception. And among them is what is specific (khāṣṣ) and contradicts reasoning in particular, and it is knowledge of the sought (al-maṭlūb) insofar as it is sought. As for knowledge of it in some respect, it is necessary to make seeking it possible. And compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) of it, i.e., certainty of it contrary to what it is. For the possessor of either of them cannot engage in reasoning therein. As for the possessor of the first, because seeking knowledge is impossible when it is already obtained. And as for the possessor of the second, because he is certain that he is knowledgeable, and that prevents him from embarking on reasoning, either because it turns him away from it like fullness prevents eating, or because it contradicts the doubt (shakk) which is a condition of reasoning according to Abū Hāshim.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines the two absolute conditions for speculative reasoning (nazar), whether the reasoning is valid or invalid. After the condition of life, al-Jurjani states that the conditions are two: a positive condition (wujūdī) – the existence of the intellect ('aql), which is the faculty that grounds religious obligation; and a privative condition ('adamī) – the absence of whatever opposes nazar. He then explains the types of that opposite: a general opposite (ḍidd 'āmm) which opposes all perception, such as sleep, heedlessness, and fainting, since these prevent any intellectual activity. And a specific opposite (ḍidd khāṣṣ) which particularly opposes nazar, namely: (1) knowledge of the very thing being sought (al-maṭlūb) qua sought, because seeking what is already known is impossible; (2) compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) – that is, a firm belief contrary to the true state of affairs. The possessor of either cannot engage in reasoning: the one who already knows the sought cannot seek it, and the one with compound ignorance is prevented either because his certainty acts as a deterrent (like fullness preventing eating) or because it eliminates the doubt (shakk) that Abū Hāshim considers necessary for reasoning. The passage thus carefully distinguishes the types of opposites and explains why they block the act of reasoning.

Key Terms

العقل
الضد العام
الضد الخاص
العلم بالمطلوب
الجهل المركب
الشك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any type of ignorance about the sought object prevents reasoning.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies that only compound ignorance (firm belief contrary to truth) prevents reasoning, not simple ignorance or lack of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, which specific opposite of nazar involves being certain of something contrary to its true reality?
A) General opposite (al-ḍidd al-ʻāmm)
B) Sleep and heedlessness
C) Compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab)
D) Knowledge of the sought (al-ʻilm bi-al-maṭlūb)

Key Takeaway
The two absolute conditions for reasoning are the possession of intellect and the absence of any general or specific obstacle that opposes it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Jurjani identifies compound ignorance as a specific opposite to reasoning. Are there beliefs you hold with absolute certainty that you have never honestly questioned? What would it take for you to re-examine one of them today?
?
He mentions sleep, heedlessness, and fainting as general opposites. Which of these states most often prevents you from truly thinking about your faith? What practical step can you take to become more awake and present in your learning?
?
If doubt is a necessary condition for reasoning (according to Abū Hāshim), do you ever allow yourself to feel genuine uncertainty about your religious knowledge? Or do you assume you already know? How might embracing humble uncertainty lead to deeper understanding?

Answering the Objection: Seeking a Second Proof for the Known
Al-Jurjani answers the objection that seeking a second proof for the known would contradict the condition of ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the apparent contradiction of seeking a second proof for something already known by distinguishing the object of reasoning.

Core Idea
Seeking a second proof is for understanding how it indicates, not for finding anew.

After establishing that knowledge prevents nazar, an objection arises: how can one who already knows something seek a second proof? Al-Jurjani answers by clarifying the true aim of the second reasoning. This passage defends theological inquiry against the charge of redundancy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objection that knowledge would prohibit further reasoning because it would require seeking what is already obtained.
The objector assumes the second nazar aims at the result itself.
2
Identify Al-Jurjani's key distinction
Notice that he distinguishes between seeking the result (al-natīja) and seeking the manner of indication (wajh al-dalāla) of the second proof. The latter is unknown, so seeking it is valid.
3
Grasp the benefit of the second proof
Recognize that the purpose is increased reassurance (iṭmiʼnān) through multiple proofs, not obtaining new knowledge of the result.
4
Understand the condition for nazar
Al-Jurjani clarifies that lack of knowledge is a condition only for nazar aimed at the result, not for nazar on the manner of indication. Also, lack of opinion is not a condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : إن كان العلم بالمطلوب مضاداً للنظر منافياً له فماذا تقول : فيمن يعلم شيئاً بدليل ثم ينظر فيه ثانياً ويطلب دليلاً آخر إذ يلزم حينئذ اجتماع المتنافيين قلت : النظر هاهنا في وجه دلالة الدليل الثاني يعني أن المقصود بالنظر هنا ليس هو العلم بالمنظور فيه الذي هو النتيجة بل العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل الثاني عليه وهو أي هذا الوجه غير معلوم فلا يلزم هاهنا طلب الحاصل بخلاف ما إذا قصد به العلم بالمنظور فيه الذي هو النتيجة فإنه يستلزم طلبه مع كونه حاصلاً والفائدة في طلب العلم بوجه الدلالة في الدليل الثاني زيادة الاطمئنان بتعاضد الأدلة فعدم العلم بالمنظور فيه شرط للنظر الذي يطلب به العلم بالمنظور فيه وأما عدم الظن به على ما هو عليه أو على خلافه فليس شرطاً له
If you say: If knowledge of the sought is opposed to and incompatible with reasoning, then what do you say about someone who knows a thing by one proof, then reflects on it a second time and seeks another proof? For then the two mutually exclusive things would coincide. I say: The reasoning here is concerning the manner of indication (wajh dalāla) of the second proof. That is, the intended object of reasoning here is not knowledge of the thing reflected upon (al-manẓūr fīh), which is the result, but rather knowledge of the manner of indication of the second proof upon it. And this—i.e., this manner—is unknown, so seeking what is already obtained does not arise here, unlike the case where he intends by it knowledge of the thing reflected upon which is the result, for that entails seeking it while it is already obtained. The benefit of seeking knowledge of the manner of indication in the second proof is increased reassurance (iṭmiʼnān) through mutual reinforcement of proofs. Thus, lack of knowledge of the thing reflected upon is a condition for the reasoning by which knowledge of the thing reflected upon is sought. As for the lack of opinion (ẓann) about it as it is or contrary to it, it is not a condition for it.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues: if knowledge of the sought (al-maṭlūb) prevents nazar, how can a person who already knows a thing from one proof later seek a second proof for the same thing? This would require both the presence of knowledge (contradicting the condition) and the act of seeking (which requires its absence). Al-Jurjani answers by distinguishing the intended object of the second nazar. The second reasoning is not aimed at the result (al-natīja) itself, which is already known, but at the manner of indication (wajh al-dalāla) of the second proof—i.e., understanding how that proof leads to the known result. Since this manner is unknown, it can be sought without contradiction. The benefit of such an inquiry is increased reassurance (iṭmiʼnān) through the corroboration of multiple proofs. Al-Jurjani then clarifies that lack of knowledge of the sought is a condition only for nazar that aims to obtain that knowledge; for nazar aimed at the manner of indication, it is not required. He also notes that lack of opinion (ẓann) about the sought, whether correct or incorrect, is not a condition for nazar. Thus, the objection is resolved by specifying the precise object of the second act of reasoning.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that the second proof aims at understanding its manner of indication, not the result itself, providing reassurance through multiple proofs.

Checkpoint

B) The opinion (al-ẓann) about the result
C) The manner of indication (wajh al-dalāla) of the second proof

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani resolves the paradox of seeking a second proof for the known by showing that the reasoning targets the manner of indication, not the known result itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Would you actively seek additional proofs for it even though you are already certain? If not, is it because you fear doubt, or because you see no need? Challenge yourself to pursue deeper understanding—what proof could you explore today?

The Two Conditions for Correct Reasoning
Al-Isfahani states the two conditions for correct reasoning and explains their interdependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two essential conditions for valid theological reasoning (النظر) and why the second condition implies the first.

Core Idea
Valid reasoning must be on the proof and from its indicating aspect.

Al-Isfahani establishes the foundational rules for any sound theological argument. Before we can evaluate arguments, we need to know what counts as valid reasoning. This passage lays out two critical conditions that every student of kalam must grasp.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the passage and find the two conditions stated by Al-Isfahani. Note how they are introduced with 'الأول' and 'الثاني'.
The conditions are about the object of consideration and the aspect of consideration.
2
Understand the distinction between proof and doubt
The first condition distinguishes between genuine proof (دليل) and specious argument (شبهة). Reflect on why this distinction is crucial for sound reasoning.
3
Grasp the concept of indication
The second condition requires considering the proof from the aspect of its indication (دلالة). Understand that the proof has a specific attribute that leads to the conclusion.
Example: temporality of the world indicates a creator.
4
Examine the relationship between the two conditions
The commentary explains that the second condition renders the first redundant. Consider why focusing on indication automatically ensures one is dealing with a genuine proof.
This shows the priority of the second condition.
5
Apply the conditions to an example
Take a common theological proof, such as the argument from contingency, and see how it satisfies both conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول أن يكون النظر في الدليل دون الشبهة وهي التي تشبه الدليل وليست به الثاني أن يكون النظر في الدليل من جهة دلالته على المدلول وهي أمر ثابت للدليل ينتقل الذهن بملاحظته من الدليل إلى المدلول كالحدوث أو الإمكان للعالم فإن النظر في الدليل لا من جهة دلالته لا ينفع ولا يوصل إلى المطلوب، لأنه بهذا الاعتبار أجنبي منقطع التعلق عنه، كما إذا نظر في العالم باعتبار صغره أو كبره وطوله أو قصره. قوله: فليس شرطاً له أي العلم بالمنظور فيه بل هو شرط للنظر الذي يطلب به الظن بالمنظور فيه على اختلاف درجاته. قوله : ( الأول إلخ) الشرط الثاني مغن عن الأول، إلا أنه حاول التفصيل فلهذا، اعتبر كل واحد شرطاً برأسه . قوله : ( الثاني أن يكون النظر في الدليل قيل : اشتراط هذا الأمر الثاني يغني عن اشتراط الأول لاستلزامه إياه وأمره هين.
First: that the consideration (النظر) be of the proof (الدليل) and not of the doubt (الشبهة), which resembles the proof but is not it. Second: that the consideration be of the proof from the aspect of its indication (جهة دلالته) upon the indicated (المدلول), which is an attribute established for the proof by which the mind transfers from the proof to the indicated, like temporality (الحدوث) or contingency (الإمكان) for the world. For consideration of the proof not from the aspect of its indication does not benefit nor lead to the desired, because from that perspective it is extraneous, severed from connection to it, as if one considers the world from the aspect of its smallness or bigness, its tallness or shortness. His statement: 'So it is not a condition for it' means knowledge of the intended (المنظور فيه) is not a condition for it; rather it is a condition for the consideration that seeks opinion (الظن) of the intended in its various degrees. His statement: 'The first etc.' the second condition suffices for the first, except that he pursued detail, so he considered each one as a condition on its own. His statement: 'Second: that the consideration be of the proof' it is said: the stipulation of this second condition suffices for stipulating the first because it implies it, and its matter is easy.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage defines the two necessary conditions for correct reasoning (النظر) in theology. The first condition is that reasoning must be applied to the actual proof (الدليل) and not to specious arguments that only resemble proofs (الشبهة). The second condition is that the proof must be considered from the specific aspect of its indication (جهة دلالته) that leads to the conclusion, such as the temporality or contingency of the world that indicate the necessity of a creator. Considering the proof from unrelated aspects does not yield knowledge of the desired conclusion, just as considering the world's size or shape does not lead to knowledge of the creator. The commentary then clarifies that the absence of knowledge of the intended conclusion is not a condition for this type of reasoning that aims at opinion; rather it is a condition for reasoning that aims at certain knowledge. The commentator notes that the second condition is sufficient for the first, but the author mentioned both for the sake of detail.

الدليل
الشبهة
الدلالة

المطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That considering a proof from any aspect is sufficient; but it must be from the aspect of its indication.
Correction: The proof must be considered specifically from the aspect that indicates the conclusion, otherwise it is extraneous and does not lead to the desired knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the second condition for correct reasoning?
A) Reasoning must be based on doubt (الشبهة)
B) Reasoning must consider the proof from the aspect of its indication (جهة دلالته)
C) Reasoning must be based on the world's physical properties
D) Reasoning must avoid all opinions

Key Takeaway
The two conditions for valid reasoning are: (1) it must be applied to a genuine proof, not a doubt, and (2) it must consider the proof from the specific aspect that indicates the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine a belief you hold about Allah or the unseen, are you honestly certain that you are looking at a genuine proof (دليل) and not at a doubt that merely resembles one? Pause and list one conviction of yours and trace it back to its source — is that source a true proof or a doubt?
?
Can you recall a time when you accepted an argument because it 'sounded right' without checking whether it actually indicated the conclusion from the proper aspect? How will you change your approach to hearing Islamic arguments after this lesson?
?
The second condition says that even if you have the right proof, you must look at it from the right angle. Is there an area of your deen where you have been considering a proof (like the Quran or creation) from a wrong angle — focusing on its beauty or complexity instead of its clear indication of the Creator? What step will you take today to realign your consideration?

Compound Ignorance in Reasoning: Objection and Answer
The author addresses the objection about compound ignorance and explains its condition in reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise condition regarding compound ignorance in reasoning and how objections to it are resolved.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance only matters when it's about the very conclusion you're seeking.

This passage addresses a subtle point in the methodology of reasoning: the role of compound ignorance. An opponent objects that even someone with compound ignorance could reason correctly if he uses correct premises. The author clarifies that the condition applies specifically to ignorance of the sought conclusion, not the aspect of indication. We'll follow the objection and its careful resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Let the argument flow naturally.
Notice the structure: objection, answer, then an extra rebuttal.
2
Identify the objection and the author's main answer
Find where the objector raises the problem about compound ignorance, and then see how the author distinguishes between ignorance of the conclusion and ignorance of the aspect of indication.
The key phrase is 'ignorance related to what is sought by this very reasoning'.
3
Consider the additional rebuttal and its dismissal
A further objection is raised about someone accidentally reaching certainty. See how the author dismisses it by referring to what preceded.
The author ends with 'let it be pondered', implying the point is already settled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والجهل المركب به ) فإن قلت : إذا جاز النظر في الدليل الثاني بمعرفة وجه دلالته جاز أن يطلب الجاهل جهلاً مركباً معرفة وجه دلالة مقدمات يقينية مخزونة عنده، فيحصل اليقين فما معنى اشتراط عدم الجهل المركب قلت الجهل المركب الذي يشترط عدمه في النظر هو الجهل المتعلق بما طلب بهذا النظر بالذات، ولا جهل مركباً فيما ذكر بالنسبة إلى وجه الدلالة، حتى يلزم المحذور قيل ويرد عليه أن الجاهل ربما تصرف في مقدمات حاصلة عنده أو ملقاة إليه، ورتبها غافلاً عن خصوصية ما تؤدي إليه، فأدته إلى اليقين بخلاف اعتقاده، فيزول عنه جهله المركب، وقد تحققت اندفاعه مما سبق فليتأمل .
His statement: 'and compound ignorance of it.' If you say: if reasoning in the second proof is permissible by knowing the aspect of its indication, then it is permissible for the one with compound ignorance to seek knowledge of the aspect of indication of certain premises stored with him, so he obtains certainty. So what is the meaning of stipulating the absence of compound ignorance? I say: compound ignorance whose absence is stipulated in reasoning is ignorance related to what is sought by this very reasoning, and there is no compound ignorance in what was mentioned relative to the aspect of indication, such that the difficulty arises. It was said: and there is rebutted against him that the ignorant person may perhaps manipulate premises present with him or presented to him, and arrange them, heedless of the specificity of what they lead to, and they lead him to certainty contrary to his belief, so his compound ignorance is removed. And its rebuttal has been verified from what preceded, so let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage deals with the condition that the reasoner must not have compound ignorance regarding the desired conclusion. The objection raises that someone with compound ignorance could, by considering the indication of certain premises, obtain certainty and thus overcome his ignorance. The answer clarifies that compound ignorance that is a barrier for reasoning is only that which pertains directly to the conclusion sought by that very reasoning. In the case described, the person is not ignorant of the aspect of indication; he is ignorant of the conclusion itself. So the condition does not apply. The additional rebuttal suggests that even if he were ignorant, he might accidentally arrange premises correctly and obtain certainty, but the author implies that this is not the typical case, and the condition remains valid.

Key Terms
الجهل المركب
وجه الدلالة
مقدمات
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition of not having compound ignorance applies to any ignorance.
Correction: It specifically applies to ignorance of the sought conclusion in that particular reasoning, not to ignorance of the aspect of indication or premises.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of compound ignorance is a barrier to correct reasoning?
A) Ignorance about the aspect of indication of the proof
B) Ignorance about the conclusion sought by this reasoning
C) Ignorance about the world's properties
D) Ignorance about the proof itself

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is only a barrier to reasoning when it pertains to the very conclusion being sought, not to the aspect of indication or premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one area in your life where you are absolutely certain—yet you have never seriously examined the evidence? Are you willing to question that certainty today?

?

The Obligation of Reasoning and Its Controversy
The author affirms the obligation of reasoning for knowing Allah and notes the disagreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obligation of reasoning (النظر) to know Allah is agreed upon by consensus between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila, but they differ on whether this obligation is grounded in revelation (السمع) or reason (العقل).

Core Idea
Reasoning is obligatory, but whose proof makes it binding? Revelation or reason?

In this passage, the author establishes the sixth objective: the obligation of using rational consideration (النظر) to attain knowledge of Allah. He highlights a critical point of agreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila — both affirm this obligation — but they fundamentally disagree on its ground: Is it revealed law or pure reason that demands such reasoning? This disagreement sets up a foundational debate in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the complete Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the author's flow.
The passage includes both the matn and a short commentary.
2
Identify the two consensuses
Notice that the author says reasoning for knowledge of Allah is obligatory by consensus of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila, and knowledge of Allah is obligatory by consensus of the entire Ummah.
These are separate claims.
3
Pinpoint the disagreement
After affirming consensus, the author states that they differed on the 'way of its establishment' — i.e., the source of the obligation. For Ash'aris it is revelation, for Mu'tazila it is reason.
This is the core debate.
4
Understand the objection and answer
The passage answers an objection: the Mu'tazila said all knowledge is necessary (ضرورية), so how can they agree that reasoning is obligatory? The answer clarifies they meant compelled (اضطرارية), not independent of reasoning.
This preserves the consensus.
5
Note the incomplete mention of 'two paths'
The passage ends by saying Ash'aris have two paths (مسلكان) to prove the obligation, but the text cuts off. This indicates a deeper argument to be explored later.
We take the passage as given.

قوله : ( أما أصحابنا فلهم (مسلكان فإن قلت : لما سلف أن النظر واجب بالإجماع منا ومن المعتزلة كان التمسك بهذا الإجماع كافياً في إثبات وجوبه الشرعي، فلا حاجة إلى هذه
[The Sixth Objective: On Knowledge of Allah] Consideration (النظر) for knowledge of Allah, i.e., to attain it, is obligatory by consensus (إجماعاً) from us (Ash'aris) and from the Mu'tazila. As for knowledge of Him, it is obligatory by consensus of the Ummah. And they differed on the way of its establishment, i.e., the establishment of the obligation of consideration in knowledge. So it, meaning the way of establishment, according to our companions (Ash'aris) is revelation (السمع), and according to the Mu'tazila is reason (العقل). As for our companions, they have for establishing the obligation of consideration leading to knowledge.

His statement: 'And they differed on the way of its establishment' because the consensus from us and the Mu'tazila is not a proof for others, and consensus must have a basis. He mentioned the argument of each group for the claim, so it is not objected that after the establishment of consensus on the obligation of consideration, there is no need to cling to another proof.

His statement: 'Obligatory by consensus from us and the Mu'tazila etc.' If you say: the eight (groups) of the Mu'tazila said that all knowledge is necessary (ضرورية), so how can you judge that consensus from them is realized in the obligation of consideration for knowledge of Allah? I say: they meant by 'necessary' the meaning of being compelled (الاضطرارية), meaning that knowledge is not a directly volitional act of the servant. For this reason he said: knowledge is generated from consideration, as will come in the conclusion of the book.

His statement: 'As for our companions, they have two paths' If you say: since it was mentioned that consideration is obligatory by consensus from us and the Mu'tazila, then clinging to this consensus is sufficient to establish its legal obligation, so there is no need for these...
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth objective: the obligation of reasoning (النظر) for the purpose of attaining knowledge of Allah. The author states that this obligation is agreed upon by both Ash'aris and Mu'tazila, but they differ on the source of that obligation: Ash'aris rely on revelation (السمع), while Mu'tazila rely on reason (العقل). The commentary explains that the consensus itself is not an independent proof for others, so each group must present its own argument. An objection is raised regarding the Mu'tazila who claimed that all knowledge is necessary (ضرورية); the answer clarifies that they meant necessary in the sense of being compelled (اضطرارية), i.e., not directly chosen by the servant, but still attained through reasoning. The text then mentions that Ash'aris have two paths (مسلكان) to prove the obligation, though the passage cuts off.

Correction: Ash'aris hold that the obligation is known through revelation, not reason. The passage also clarifies that even Mu'tazila who claim knowledge is 'necessary' still affirm the need for reasoning, as 'necessary' means compelled, not independent.

Reasoning to know Allah is obligatory by consensus, but whether this obligation is derived from revelation or reason is a key point of divergence between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.

The Two Conditions for Valid Nazar
Al-Jurjani defines the two necessary conditions for valid nazar with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two necessary conditions for valid nazar (speculative reasoning) and why they are essential for sound theological inference.

Core Idea
Valid nazar requires both the right object (dalil) and the right perspective (dalala).

Before we can reason correctly about God, we need to know what makes reasoning valid. Al-Jurjani gives two crisp conditions that guard against two common errors: reasoning from false premises and reasoning that doesn't actually lead to the intended conclusion. This lesson unpacks those conditions with a practical example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Pay attention to how al-Jurjani introduces each condition sequentially.
The passage is dense but highly structured.
2
Identify the two conditions
Find the two explicit conditions. Notice that the first is about the object of nazar (dalil vs. shubha), and the second is about the aspect of nazar (dalala).
3
Understand the example
Study the example of the world: reflecting on its size does not lead to God, but reflecting on its contingency (imkan) does. This clarifies the second condition.
The example contrasts irrelevant attributes with indicative attributes.
4
Connect to broader theology
Ask yourself why these conditions matter. They ensure that nazar leads to certain knowledge and avoids sophistry.
This lesson is foundational for all later theological arguments.
5
Test your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to confirm you can identify valid nazar according to these conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الأول: أن يكون النظر في الدليل دون الشبهة، وهي التي تشبه الدليل وليست به. والشرط الثاني: أن يكون النظر في الدليل من جهة دلالته على المدلول، وهي أمر ثابت للدليل ينتقل الذهن بملاحظته من الدليل إلى المدلول كالحدوث أو الإمكان للعالم. فإن النظر في الدليل لا من جهة دلالته لا ينفع ولا يوصل إلى المطلوب، لأنه بهذا الاعتبار أجنبي منقطع التعلق عنه، كما إذا نظر في العالم باعتبار صغره أو كبره وطوله أو قصره.
The first condition: that nazar (speculative reasoning) be in the evidence (dalīl), without the shubha (fallacious resemblance), which is that which resembles the evidence but is not it. And the second condition: that nazar be in the evidence from the aspect of its indication (dalāla) upon the indicated (madlūl), which is a property established for the evidence by the mind's noting of it, moving from the evidence to the indicated, such as originatedness (ḥudūth) or contingency (imkān) of the world. For indeed nazar in the evidence not from the aspect of its indication neither benefits nor leads to the sought, because in that respect it is extrinsic and its connection is cut off, like when one considers the world in terms of its smallness or largeness, its length or shortness.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines the two necessary conditions for valid nazar. The first condition requires that nazar be directed at the actual evidence (dalil) and not at a shubha, which resembles evidence but is false. The second condition specifies that nazar must consider the evidence from the aspect of its dalala, i.e., the property that allows the mind to infer the conclusion. Examples of such properties are huduth (originatedness) and imkan (contingency) of the world, which indicate a creator. The author stresses that nazar that does not consider the evidence from this indicative aspect is useless, just as reflecting on the world's size or shape does not lead to knowledge of God.

Key Terms
النظر (nazar)
الدليل (dalil)
الشبهة (shubha)
الدلالة (dalala)
المدلول (madlul)
الحدوث (huduth)
الإمكان (imkan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any reflection on the world counts as valid nazar leading to knowledge of God.
Correction: Only reflection that considers the evidence from the aspect of its indication (dalala) leads to the intended conclusion; irrelevant attributes like size do not.

Checkpoint
What are the two conditions for valid nazar according to this passage?
A) Nazar must be in the evidence and from the aspect of its indication.
B) Nazar must be in the evidence and from any aspect.
C) Nazar must be in the shubha and from the aspect of indication.
D) Nazar must be in the madlul and from the aspect of its evidence.

Key Takeaway
Valid nazar must be directed at the true evidence (dalil) and must examine it from the aspect of its indication (dalala) to reach the intended conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the world around you, do you ever stop to ask whether your thoughts are truly directed at the evidence (dalil) or are you distracted by shubha—fallacious appearances? In your daily life, what 'shubhat' (false resemblances) do you find most tempting to rely on instead of genuine evidence? How can you train yourself to focus on the true indication of God's existence?
?
Consider the example given: thinking about the size or shape of the world does not lead to God. Have you ever found yourself focusing on irrelevant aspects of creation (like its beauty or complexity) without properly connecting them to their Creator? What would it mean for you to shift your reflection from mere admiration to genuine indication that requires a Creator?
?
The second condition insists that nazar must be from the aspect of dalala—that property which moves the mind from evidence to indicated. In your own search for certainty about God, are you consistently looking for that 'indicative property' in the world? Or do you sometimes assume that any reflection automatically counts? How can you ensure your reasoning is valid?

Consensus and Disagreement on the Obligation of Nazar
Al-Jurjani states consensus on nazar's obligation and the disagreement on its proof between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while both Ash'aris and Mu'tazila agree that nazar is obligatory, they disagree on whether this obligation is proven by scripture or reason.

Core Idea
Consensus on obligation, but disagreement on its proof.

This passage introduces a foundational point of agreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila: the obligation of nazar for attaining knowledge of God. However, immediately after stating the consensus, it highlights the key methodological disagreement: does this obligation stem from scripture or reason? Understanding this sets the stage for the proofs that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two consensuses
Read the passage carefully. Notice that two consensuses are mentioned: (a) that nazar is obligatory between our school and the Mu'tazila, and (b) that knowledge of God is obligatory by consensus of the whole Umma.
2
Understand the disagreement
The passage then says that they differed on the way of proving the obligation of nazar. Find the two positions: Ash'aris say scripture (sam'), Mu'tazila say reason ('aql).
This is a fundamental methodological split.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does this disagreement matter? It determines whether the proof for nazar comes from revelation or from rational inference alone.
This will affect how each school argues for the existence and attributes of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر في معرفة الله تعالى واجب إجماعاً منا ومن المعتزلة. وأما معرفته تعالى فواجبة إجماعاً من الأمة. واختلف في طريق ثبوته، أي ثبوت وجوب النظر في المعرفة، فهو يعني طريق الثبوت عند أصحابنا السمع وعند المعتزلة العقل.
Speculative reasoning (nazar) concerning knowledge of God (ma‘rifat Allāh), i.e., for the sake of attaining it, is obligatory by consensus (ijmā‘) of us and the Mu‘tazila. As for knowledge of Him, Exalted, it is obligatory by consensus of the Umma. And they differed on the way of proving it, i.e., proving the obligation of nazar in knowledge. Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage establishes that there is consensus (ijma') among both Ash'aris and Mu'tazila that nazar is obligatory for attaining knowledge of God. Moreover, knowledge of God itself is obligatory by consensus of the entire Muslim community. However, the two schools disagree on how to prove that nazar is obligatory: the Ash'aris rely on scriptural evidence (sam'), while the Mu'tazila rely on rational proof ('aql). This sets the stage for the Ash'ari proofs that follow.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the obligation of nazar is derived from reason alone; but the passage shows it is by consensus, and the proof is scriptural for Ash'aris.
Correction: The passage teaches that the obligation itself is agreed upon, but its proof is disputed. The Ash'ari position uses scripture, not reason, to prove the obligation.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of nazar is agreed upon by consensus; the disagreement is whether the proof for this obligation is scriptural (Ash'aris) or rational (Mu'tazila).

First Ash'ari Proof: Scriptural Evidence
Al-Jurjani presents the first Ash'ari proof: scriptural evidence for obligation of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Ash'ari proof for the obligation of nazar based on scriptural evidence, and why it is considered only probable (dhanni) rather than certain (qat'i).

Core Idea
Scriptural commands and threats imply obligation, but their interpretation remains probable.

Al-Jurjani presents the first of two Ash'ari proofs for the obligation of nazar: inference from the apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of Quranic verses and hadith. He cites specific verses that command 'looking' and a hadith that threatens those who do not reflect, then evaluates the certainty of this proof. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from presenting the proof to acknowledging its limitation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two scriptural proofs
Find the two Quranic verses and the hadith. Note how each is used to argue for the obligation of nazar.
The verses use the imperative 'look'; the hadith uses a threat.
3
Understand the logical steps
The command implies obligation (by default). The threat implies that the act is obligatory, because threats only apply to leaving an obligation. Follow the reasoning.
This is a standard Ash'ari move.
4
Recognize the proof's limitation
The author states this proof is only probable because the imperative could mean recommendation and the hadith is a solitary report. Understand why this reduces certainty.
This connects to the broader theme of epistemic certainty in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أصحابنا فلهم في إثبات وجوب النظر المؤدي إلى المعرفة مسلكان: الأول الاستدلال بالظواهر من الآيات والأحاديث الدالة على وجوب النظر في المعرفة نحو قوله تعالى: {قل انظروا ماذا في السموات والأرض} وقوله تعالى: {فانظر إلى آثار رحمة الله كيف يحيي الأرض بعد موتها} فقد أمر بالنظر في دليل الصانع وصفاته والأمر للوجوب كما هو الظاهر المتبادر منه. ولما نزل {إن في خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار لآيات لأولي الألباب} قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: ويل لمن لاكها بين لحييه ولم يتفكر فيها فقد أوعد بترك التفكر في دلائل المعرفة فهو واجب إذ لا وعيد على ترك غير الواجب. وهذا المسلك لا يخرج عن كونه ظنياً غير قطعي الدلالة لاحتمال الأمر غير الوجوب وكون الخبر المنقول من قبيل الآحاد.
And our companions have two approaches in proving the obligation of nazar leading to knowledge: the first is inference from the apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of the verses and hadiths that indicate the obligation of nazar in knowledge, such as His statement, Exalted: 'Say: Look at what is in the heavens and the earth' [Yūnus: 101], and His statement: 'Then look at the effects of God's mercy, how He revives the earth after its death' [al-Rūm: 50]; for He commanded looking (nazar) at the evidence of the Maker and His attributes, and the command is for obligation, as is the apparent and understood meaning. And when was revealed: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of night and day are signs for those of understanding' [Āl ‘Imrān: 190], he, peace be upon him, said: 'Woe to the one who chews it between his jaws and does not reflect upon it'—so he threatened with leaving reflection (tafakkur) on the evidences of knowledge. So it (nazar) is obligatory, since there is no threat for leaving that which is not obligatory. And this approach does not go beyond being probable (ẓannī), not definitive (qaṭ‘ī) in indication, due to the possibility that the command means something other than obligation, and that the transmitted report is of the type of solitary reports (āḥād).
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the first Ash'ari proof for the obligation of nazar. The author cites two Quranic verses that use the imperative 'look' and a hadith in which the Prophet threatened those who do not reflect on the signs of creation. The imperative form is taken as indicating obligation, and the threat implies that the act is obligatory. However, the author notes that this proof is only probable (dhanni), not certain (qat'i), because the imperative could be interpreted as a recommendation rather than an obligation, and the hadith may be a solitary report (khabar ahad) which does not yield certainty. Thus, while the proof is valid, it is not decisive.

Key Terms
الظواهر (zawahir)
الأمر (amr)
الوجوب (wujub)
الظن (dhann)
القطع (qat')
الآحاد (ahad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That scriptural commands automatically establish obligation with certainty.
Correction: The Ash'ari author explicitly states this proof is only probable because commands can be interpreted as recommendations and the hadith may be a solitary report.

Checkpoint
Why is the first Ash'ari proof considered only probable (dhanni)?
A) Because the verses are obscure.
B) Because the imperative could mean recommendation and the hadith is a solitary report.
C) Because the hadith is weak.
D) Because the Quran does not command nazar.

Key Takeaway
The first Ash'ari proof uses scriptural commands and threats to argue for the obligation of nazar, but is only probable due to interpretative possibilities and reliance on solitary reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic command 'Look at what is in the heavens and the earth,' do you immediately assume it obligates you to investigate? How many times have you genuinely pondered creation as a result? If you were held accountable today for neglecting this command, would you be guilty? Will you commit to setting aside time this week to reflect on one sign of God?
?
The author shows that even scriptural commands can be open to interpretation. In your own life, have you ever assumed something was obligatory based on a single verse without considering other evidence? How can you train yourself to be more careful? What step will you take today to verify your understanding before acting?
?
The hadith threatens those who 'chew' the verses without reflection. Are you someone who recites or reads Quran without pausing to think? If the threat is real, what does it say about your current practice? Will you start a habit of daily reflection on at least one verse, asking 'What does this teach me about my Lord?'

Second Ash'ari Proof: Consensus and Nazar
al-Jurjani presents the second Ash'ari proof for the obligation of nazar via consensus and critiques the probabilistic verse proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Ash'ari proof for the obligation of nazar, which is based on consensus and why its Quranic support is probabilistic.

Core Idea
Consensus that knowing Allah is obligatory makes nazar obligatory as its means.

Al-Jurjani now presents the relied-upon Ash'ari proof. He argues that since there is consensus among Muslims that knowledge of Allah is obligatory, and since nazar is the only proper means to attain that knowledge, nazar must also be obligatory. He also mentions a verse that is often adduced but notes it does not provide certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the author calls this the 'relied-upon' approach.
2
Understand the consensus argument
The core argument: Knowledge of Allah is obligatory by consensus. Since nazar is the means to that knowledge, it follows that nazar is obligatory.
This is a logical inference called 'muqaddimat al-wujūb'.
3
Examine why the verse is probabilistic
The author points out two reasons: the imperative form may not indicate obligation, and 'ilm may mean predominant opinion which could be attained through imitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسلك الثاني وهو المعتمد في إثبات وجوب النظر أن معرفة الله تعالى واجبة إجماعاً من المسلمين كافة، وقد يتمسك في ذلك بقوله تعالى: {فاعلم أنه لا إله إلا الله} لكنه ظني لما عرفت من احتمال صيغة الأمر غير الوجوب ولأن العلم قد يطلق لغة على الظن الغالب وذلك قد يحصل.
And the second approach, which is the relied-upon one in proving the obligation of nazar, is that knowledge of God, Exalted, is obligatory by consensus of all Muslims. One may also adduce in this regard His statement: 'Know that there is no god but God' [Muḥammad: 19]. But it (this proof) is probable (ẓannī) because of what you know about the possibility that the imperative form means something other than obligation, and because ‘ilm (knowledge) may be used linguistically to mean predominant opinion (al-ẓann al-ghālib), and that may be attained (through imitation).
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the second Ash'ari proof, which the author identifies as the relied-upon (mu'tamad) proof. The argument is that there is consensus (ijma') that knowledge of God (ma'rifat Allah) is obligatory. Since nazar is the means to attain that knowledge, it follows that nazar is also obligatory. The author also mentions that the verse 'Know that there is no god but God' can be used to support this, but this proof remains probable (dhanni) because the imperative could be interpreted as something other than obligation, and the term 'ilm can mean strong opinion (dhann ghalib) which might be attained through imitation (taqlid) rather than nazar. Therefore, the proof is not absolutely certain.

Key Terms
الإجماع (ijma')
العلم ('ilm)
الظن (dhann)
التقليد (taqlid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah' provides certainty for the obligation of nazar.
Correction: The verse is probabilistic because the imperative may not imply obligation and 'ilm can mean predominant opinion, which may be attained without nazar.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what makes nazar obligatory?
A) The Quranic verse explicitly commands nazar.
B) Consensus that ma'rifa is obligatory and nazar is its means.
C) The hadith threat.
D) Reason alone.

Key Takeaway
The second Ash'ari proof for nazar relies on consensus that knowing Allah is obligatory, making nazar obligatory as its means; the supporting verse is probabilistic due to linguistic possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that knowledge of Allah is obligatory by consensus, and that nazar is the necessary means to fulfill this obligation. If you were to be honest with yourself, do you invest any real intellectual effort in contemplating the proofs for Allah's existence, or have you simply inherited your belief without personal verification? What would it change in your life if you took this obligation seriously starting today?
?
The verse 'Know that there is no god but God' is not a definitive proof for nazar because the word 'know' could mean merely predominant opinion. Are you content with a probable or inherited knowledge of Allah, or do you yearn for certainty? What practical step can you take this week to move from imitation to personal conviction?
?
Consider the possibility that your current belief in Allah might be based on taqlid (imitation) rather than personal nazar. If that were true, would you be fulfilling the obligation that the consensus says falls on every Muslim? What would it cost you to actually investigate the evidence for yourself?

The First Path: Scriptural Evidence for the Obligation of Reflection
Al-Iji presents the first path based on Qur'an and Hadith and explains its probabilistic indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji uses Quranic verses and hadith to argue for the obligation of reflection (nazar) and why he considers this evidence probabilistic rather than decisive.

Core Idea
Scriptural commands for reflection are strong but not decisive; they require rational support.

Al-Iji begins his proof for the obligation of reflection by turning to the Quran and Sunnah. He cites direct commands to look and ponder, and a warning against neglect. However, he immediately qualifies this evidence as probabilistic (zanni) rather than certain. This lesson examines his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Iji uses very concise language.
2
Identify the scriptural evidence
Find the two Quranic verses and the hadith. Notice how each is used as a command or warning.
The verses are from Yunus 101 and Ar-Rum 50.
3
Understand the argument from command and threat
The command (amr) is taken to imply obligation; the threat (wa'id) implies that leaving reflection is sinful.
4
Grasp why the evidence is probabilistic
Al-Iji notes two reasons: the command could indicate recommendation rather than obligation, and the hadiths are isolated (ahad), which yield only probable knowledge.
This is a key methodological point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلكان الأول الاستدلال بالظواهر من الآيات والأحاديث الدالة على وجوب النظر في المعرفة (نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿قل انظروا ماذا في السموات والأرض ﴾ [يونس : ۱۰۱] ، وقوله تعالى : فانظر إلى آثار رحمة الله كيف يحيي الأرض بعد موتها [الروم : ٥٠ ] ، فقد أمر بالنظر في دليل الصانع وصفاته ) والأمر للوجوب ) كما هو الظاهر المتبادر منه ( ولما نزل أن في خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار لآيات لأولي الألباب قال عليه الصلاة والسلام : ويل لمن لاكها أي مضغها ( بين لحييه ) أي جانبي فمه (ولم يتفكر فيها ) فقد أوعد بترك التفكر في دلائل المعرفة ( فهو (واجب) إذ لا وعيد على ترك غير الواجب (وهذا المسلك ( لا يخرج عن كونه ظنياً) غير قطعي الدلالة لاحتمال الأمر غير الوجوب، وكون الخبر المنقول من قبيل الآحاد
The first way is deducing from the apparent meanings of the verses and hadiths that indicate the obligation of reflection (nazar) in knowledge: such as His saying, 'Say: Look at what is in the heavens and the earth' [Yunus: 101], and His saying, 'Then look at the effects of Allah's mercy, how He gives life to the earth after its death' [Ar-Rum: 50]. For He commanded reflection on the proof of the Maker and His attributes, and the command is for obligation as is the apparent and immediate understanding from it. And when was revealed 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of the night and day are signs for those of understanding', he (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'Woe to the one who chews them' meaning 'grinds them between his jaws' meaning the sides of his mouth 'and does not ponder over them.' So he warned against leaving reflection on the proofs of knowledge. So it is obligatory, for there is no threat for leaving a non-obligatory act. And this way does not go beyond being probabilistic (zanni), not decisive in indication, due to the possibility that the command indicates other than obligation, and because the transmitted report is of the type of isolated reports (ahad).
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two methodological paths for proving the obligation of reflection (nazar) in knowing Allah. It begins by stating that this first path relies on the apparent meanings (zawahir) of verses and hadiths that command reflection. Two Quranic verses are cited: 'Say: Look at what is in the heavens and the earth' and 'Then look at the effects of Allah's mercy...' These are commands, and the default in commands is obligation (wujub). Additionally, a hadith is mentioned: when the verse about signs in creation was revealed, the Prophet warned those who recite but do not ponder. This threat indicates that leaving reflection is forbidden, which implies that reflection is obligatory. However, the passage then notes that this path is only probabilistic (zanni) and not decisive (qat'i) in its indication. The reasons are: the command could be interpreted as recommending rather than obligating, and the transmitted reports (hadiths) are of the isolated (ahad) type, which yield only probable knowledge. Thus, while this path provides evidence, it is not absolutely certain.

Key Terms
الظواهر
الوجوب
الظني
الآحاد
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Iji consider the first path (based on scriptural evidence) to be probabilistic (zanni) rather than decisive (qat'i)?
A) Because the verses are abrogated
B) Because the command may indicate something other than obligation and the hadiths are ahad
C) Because the verses are ambiguous in wording
D) Because the scholars disagree on the meaning

Key Takeaway
Scriptural evidence supports the obligation of reflection but remains probabilistic due to interpretive possibilities and transmission type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you assume every command is binding without considering scholarly nuance? How will you change your approach to interpreting Allah's words after learning that even scriptural commands can be probabilistic?
?
The Prophet warned those who 'chew' the verses but do not ponder. Are you guilty of reciting without reflecting? What specific action will you take today to move from recitation to deep thought?
?
This passage shows that even strong scriptural evidence is not always decisive. Does this uncertainty weaken your trust in Islam or challenge you to seek deeper knowledge? How will you respond to the fact that some religious knowledge requires rational effort beyond scripture?

The Second Path: Consensus and the Principle of Indispensable Means
Al-Iji presents the relied-upon argument: knowledge of Allah is obligatory by consensus, and reflection is its indispensable means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second path's argument for the obligation of reflection (nazar), which relies on the consensus of Muslims and the logical principle that the means to an obligation are obligatory.

Core Idea
The obligation of reflection is proven by consensus and the necessity of means.

The author now presents the second, and most relied-upon, argument for why reflection (nazar) is obligatory. This argument does not rely on a single verse, but on the consensus of all Muslims that knowledge of Allah is obligatory, combined with a logical principle: whatever is indispensable for fulfilling an obligation is itself obligatory. Let us see how this argument is constructed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: consensus, verse, principle, conclusion.
The passage is compact but each part is essential.
2
Identify the two components of the argument
The argument has two main pillars: the consensus (ijma') that knowledge of Allah is obligatory, and the principle 'Ma la yatimmu al-wajib illa bihi fahuwa wajib'.
These are the foundation.
3
Understand why the verse alone is not enough
The verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah' is mentioned but set aside because it is probabilistic (zanni) - the command form could imply recommendation and 'ilm could mean probable opinion.
This shows the need for a stronger proof.
4
Grasp the application of the principle
Knowledge of Allah is an absolute obligation. It is only completed through reflection. Therefore, reflection becomes obligatory with the same type of obligation (individual or communal).
This is the logical punchline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسلك الثاني وهو المعتمد في إثبات وجوب النظر أن معرفة الله تعالى واجبة إجماعاً من المسلمين كافة، وقد يتمسك في ذلك بقوله تعالى : فاعلم أنه لا إله إلا الله ﴾ [محمد : ١٩] لكنه ظني لما عرفت من احتمال صيغة الأمر غير الوجوب ولأن العلم قد يطلق لغة على الظن الغالب، وذلك قد يحصل بالتقليد من غير نظر، كما ذكره الإمام الرازي ( وهي لا تتم إلا بالنظر وما لا يتم الواجب ( المطلق إلا به فهو واجب كوجوبه
The second way, and it is the relied-upon one in establishing the obligation of reflection, is that knowledge of Allah the Exalted is obligatory by consensus of all Muslims. One may also adduce in that His saying: 'Know that there is no god but Allah' [Muhammad:19], but it is probabilistic due to what you have learned of the possibility of the command form indicating other than obligation, and because 'knowledge' (al-'ilm) is sometimes used linguistically for predominant opinion (al-zann al-ghalib), and that may be acquired by blind acceptance (taqlid) without reflection, as al-Imam al-Razi mentioned. And it (knowledge) is not completed except by reflection, and whatever the absolute obligation is not completed without it, it is obligatory like its obligation.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the second, and relied-upon, methodological path for proving the obligation of reflection (nazar). It begins by stating that knowledge of Allah is obligatory by consensus (ijma') of all Muslims. This is the premise. Then it mentions that one could also use the verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah' as a proof, but quickly notes that this verse is also probabilistic (zanni) because the command form could be interpreted as recommendation, and the term 'ilm in Arabic can refer to probable opinion (zann), which can be attained through blind acceptance (taqlid) without reflection, as al-Razi pointed out. Therefore, the verse is not decisive. The core argument is then given: knowledge of Allah is only completed through reflection, and that without which an absolute obligation cannot be fulfilled is itself obligatory with the same degree of obligation (i.e., individual or communal). This is the famous principle: 'Ma la yatimmu al-wajib illa bihi fahuwa wajib.' Thus, reflection is obligatory.

Key Terms
المعتمد
الإجماع
الظني
التقليد
ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse 'Know that...' alone suffices to prove the obligation of reflection.
Correction: The passage shows the verse is probabilistic; the real proof is the consensus and the logical principle.

Checkpoint
According to the second path, what is the primary basis for the obligation of reflection (nazar)?
A) The verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah'
B) The consensus of Muslims that knowledge of Allah is obligatory
C) The hadith warning against those who do not ponder
D) The principle that reflection leads to certainty

Key Takeaway
The second path proves the obligation of reflection through consensus and the logical necessity of means.

Third Objection: The Impossibility of Ijma' on Obligation
The third objection disputes the occurrence of ijma' on the obligation of knowing God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the occurrence of ijma' on the obligation of knowing God and the response that consensus is possible when a common cause unites the community.

Core Idea
Consensus is possible when a common cause unites, not impossible in all matters.

This lesson presents the third objection raised against the claim that the Muslim community has consensus (ijma') on the obligation of knowing God. The objector argues that such consensus is impossible customarily, comparing it to everyone agreeing to eat the same food or utter the same word at the same time. The response shows that consensus is possible when there is a common cause (amr jami') that unites the community, such as abundant motives to submit to the shari'a and clear evidence on the matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Notice the structure: objection and response.
The passage uses parentheses to denote dialectical steps.
2
Identify the objection's comparison
The objector claims that consensus on the obligation of knowing God is as impossible as everyone agreeing to eat one specific food or say one word at the same time. Understand why this is impossible customarily.
3
Understand the concept of 'common cause' (amr jami')
The response introduces a key concept: consensus is possible when there is a common cause that unites people. For the obligation of knowing God, the common causes are abundant motives (tawaffur al-dawa'i) and clear evidence (qiyam al-dalil).
Contrast this with the food example, where no such common cause exists.
4
Examine the examples given
Notice that the response explicitly distinguishes between matters with a common cause (like religious obligations) and matters without (like food preferences based on individual temperaments).
5
Summarize the logical move
The objection fails because it compares two different categories: one with a unifying cause and one without. The response successfully defends the possibility of ijma' on the obligation of knowing God.
This reinforces the overall argument for the obligation of knowing God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث ) سلمنا إمكان وجوب المعرفة شرعاً لكن لا نسلم وقوعه ( قولكم : أجمعت الأمة على ذلك قلنا : لا يمكن الإجماع منهم على وجوبها ( عادة كعلى أي كالإجماع منهم على أكل طعام) واحد (و) على ( كلمة ) واحدة ( في آن واحد ( قلنا يجوز ) يجوز الإجماع منهم ( فيما يوجد ) فيه ( أمر جامع) لهم ( عليه ) كوجوب المعرفة مثلا، ثم بين الجامع بقوله ( من توفر الدواعي ) إلى انقياد الشريعة ومعرفة أحكامها ( وقيام الدليل) الظاهر على ذلك المجمع عليه ( وما ذكرتم من الإجماع على طعام واحد أو كلمة واحدة ( لا جامع ) لهم عليه ( بل شهواتهم بحسب أمزجتهم، وحالاتهم متخالفة داعية إلى عدم الاتفاق فيه
(The third: we concede the possibility of obligation of knowledge legally, but we do not concede its occurrence. Your statement: 'the community has consensus on that.' We say: their consensus on its obligation is not possible customarily, like their consensus on eating one food and one word at one time. We say: their consensus is permissible in what there exists a common cause (jāmi') for them upon it, like the obligation of knowledge, for example. Then he explained the common cause by his statement: 'from the abundance of motives' to submitting to the shari'a and knowing its rulings, 'and the existence of clear evidence' on that which is agreed upon. And what you mentioned of consensus on one food or one word has no common cause for them upon it, rather their appetites are according to their temperaments and their states are diverse, calling to disagreement in it.)
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: even if a legal obligation of knowing God is possible, it does not actually occur (wuqū'). The objector challenges the claim that the Muslim community (umma) has consensus (ijmā') that such knowledge is obligatory. The objection argues that such consensus is not possible customarily ('āda), comparing it to consensus on eating a specific food or uttering a specific word at the same time—which is impossible due to diverse inclinations. The response affirms that consensus is possible when there is a common cause (amr jāmi') that unites them, such as the obligation of knowledge, because there are abundant motives (tawaffur al-dawā'ī) to submit to the shari'a and clear evidence (qiyām al-dalīl) on the matter. The difference from the food/word example is that those lack a common cause; people's appetites differ based on temperaments, leading to disagreement. Thus, consensus on the obligation of knowing God is possible and indeed occurs.

Key Terms
إجماع
وقوع
عادة
أمر جامع
توفر الدواعي
قيام الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that consensus (ijma') is impossible in all matters because people naturally disagree.
Correction: Consensus is possible when there is a common cause ('amr jami') that unites people, such as abundant motives and clear evidence for religious obligations. It is not impossible in all matters; only in matters lacking such a unifying cause, like personal preferences.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why can the community have consensus on the obligation of knowing God but not on eating a specific food?
A) Because eating is a physical act, while knowledge is spiritual.
B) Because consensus on food requires unanimous agreement, which is impossible.
C) Because there is a common cause (jami') for the obligation of knowledge, namely abundant motives and clear evidence.
D) Because the community is more likely to agree on religious matters than on worldly matters.

Key Takeaway
Consensus in religious obligations like knowing God is possible due to a common cause that unites the community, unlike matters of personal preference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you feel a personal urgency to know God, or do you rely on the consensus of others without internalizing the motives and evidence that unite believers? What specific step will you take this week to deepen your own knowledge of God?
?
The passage mentions 'abundant motives' that drive people to submit to the shari'a. What is blocking your own motives from moving you from passive acknowledgment to active pursuit of Islamic knowledge?
?
Clear evidence (qiyām al-dalīl) is available for the obligation of knowing God. When you encounter such evidence in your life, do you respond with commitment or hesitation? What would change if you truly believed that the evidence is compelling enough for the entire community to agree?
