The Fourth Objection: Impossibility of Transmitting Ijma'
A succinct statement of the fourth objection against ijmā'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against using consensus (ijmā') as a proof for knowing Allah, which argues that even if consensus occurs, its transmission to later generations is impossible.

Core Idea
Even if consensus happens, its chain of transmission is impossible to verify.

The fourth objection targets not the possibility of consensus itself, but the reliability of its transmission across generations. The objector concedes that consensus could theoretically occur among the early community, but argues that we have no way to verify that such a consensus actually happened or what it determined. This challenges the entire basis of using transmitted consensus as evidence in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection acknowledges consensus might be possible in itself but denies its transmission.
Focus on the contrast between 'in itself' and 'transmission to us'.
2
Distinguish two claims
The objection distinguishes between consensus being established (thabata) and its transmission (naql). Understand that the objector is not denying the possibility of consensus entirely.
This distinction is key to understanding the objection's force.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this objection would undermine reliance on consensus as a proof. If we cannot access the consensus, how can we use it as evidence?
This prepares you for how a response might defend transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الرابع : الإجماع إن ثبت ) في نفسه ( امتنع نقله إلينا فلا يصح أن يتمسك به
(The fourth: consensus, even if established in itself, is impossible to transmit to us, so it cannot be adhered to.)
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This brief passage presents the fourth objection against using consensus (ijmā') as proof for the obligation of knowing God. The objector concedes that consensus might be possible in principle (thabata fī nafsihi), but argues that its transmission (naql) to later generations is impossible. Since we do not have access to the actual consensus of the early community, we cannot rely on it as a proof (tamassuk). This objection challenges the validity of relying on transmitted consensus as evidence. The text does not provide an explicit answer here; it is a succinct statement of the objection.

Key Terms
إجماع
نقل
تمسك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies that consensus can ever occur.
Correction: The objection only denies the possibility of transmitting consensus, not consensus itself.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth objection regarding consensus?
A) Consensus is not a valid proof in religious matters.
B) Consensus, even if it occurs, cannot be transmitted to later generations, so it cannot be used as evidence.
C) Consensus is impossible because the community never agrees.
D) Consensus requires unanimous agreement, which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection challenges the use of consensus as a theological proof by arguing that even if consensus occurs, its transmission across generations is unreliable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold because 'everyone says so'. According to this objection, how do you know that consensus has actually been transmitted to you with certainty? What would it take for you to be sure?
?
If you cannot rely on consensus to know Allah, then what is the foundation of your knowledge of Him? Does this challenge your certainty or strengthen it?
?
Consider the chains of transmission for the Islamic teachings you follow. Are there any that you accept without verifying their continuity? What would it mean for your faith if those chains were broken?

Knowledge Without a Teacher and the Conditions of Obligation
Three glosses on the possibility of knowledge without a teacher and the condition of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced debate on whether knowledge of God requires a teacher and the conditions for being obligated to seek it.

Core Idea
The obligation to know God does not require independence from a teacher.

Sanusi's glosses address critical issues in the theology of obligation: can genuine knowledge of God be acquired without a teacher? He clarifies that the discussion assumes reflection can be independent, but also that reflection combined with teaching is valid. The condition for obligation is understanding, not affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the three separate glosses.
2
Identify the three glosses and their targets
The passage contains three glosses (ta'līqāt). Notice that each gloss begins with 'قوله' (his statement). Identify what each gloss is commenting on and what point it makes.
The first gloss discusses 'without a teacher', the second clarifies 'heedless', the third raises a doubt about the claim.
3
Analyze the third gloss's doubt and the overall context
The third gloss argues that knowledge can be obtained through reflection combined with teaching, so the claim of independence is not valid. It then shows from the overall discourse that the obligation is for knowledge either with or without teaching.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that 'without a teacher' means teaching is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وفيها بلا (معلم لأن الكلام على تقدير كون إمكانها باعتبار استفادتها من النظر استقلالاً، كيلا يكون التكليف بالمعرفة تكليفاً، بما لا يطاق، وإذا كان إمكانها باعتبار استفادتها من النظر بمعونة التعليم يكون حصولها موقوفاً على فعل الغير، فلا يكون اختيارياً. قوله : (إذ شرط إلخ) فإن أريد بالغافل من لا يفهم الخطاب، ولا يتصوره فلا نسلم قوله إنه تكليف للغافل وإن أريد به من لا يصدقه فلا نسلم قوله وإنه باطل. قوله : ( وفيها بلا معلم فيه تأمل لجواز المعرفة بإفادة النظر المجامع للتعليم، فتفرع إمكانها على إفادة النظر العلم في الإلهيات بلا معلم ممنوع لا يقال : المدعى أن المعرفة بلا معلم واجب، فحينئذ يظهر التوقف لأنا نقول سياق الكلام يأباه، أما أولاً فلانه ادعي الإجماع على هذا الوجوب، ومن يعتبر إيمان المقلد لا يقول بالوجوب بدون التعليم، إذ عدم إيجاب التعليم ليس إيجاباً لعدمه، وأما ثانياً فلان قوله في جواب الإشكال السابع، قلنا: كل ذلك يحتاج إلى معرفة النظر يدل على أن المدعى وجوب المعرفة بالتعليم أو بدونه
His statement: 'and in it without a teacher' because the discussion is assuming that its possibility is by virtue of its being derived from reflection independently, so that the obligation of knowledge is not an obligation of what is unbearable. And if its possibility is by virtue of its being derived from reflection with the assistance of teaching, then its occurrence is dependent on the action of another, so it would not be voluntary. His statement: 'for the condition, etc.' For if by the heedless is meant one who does not understand the address and does not conceptualize it, then we do not concede his statement that it is an obligation on the heedless; and if by it is meant one who does not affirm it, then we do not concede his statement that it is invalid. His statement: 'and in it without a teacher' requires consideration because knowledge is possible through reflection combined with teaching; so making its possibility dependent on reflection producing knowledge in divine matters without a teacher is unacceptable. It cannot be said: the claim is that knowledge without a teacher is obligatory; then hesitation appears. But we say: the context of the discourse rejects it. First, because consensus has been claimed on this obligation, and whoever considers the faith of the imitator valid does not say that obligation exists without teaching, since not requiring teaching is not requiring its absence. Second, because his statement in answer to the seventh objection, 'we say: all of that needs knowledge of reflection' indicates that the claim is the obligation of knowledge either with teaching or without it.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage consists of three glosses (ta'līqāt) commenting on earlier arguments. The first gloss explains the statement 'in it without a teacher' (fīhā bilā mu'allim): the discussion assumes that the possibility of knowledge is derived from reflection independently (istiqlālan), so that the obligation is not unbearable. If it were dependent on teaching, its occurrence would rely on another's action, making it non-voluntary (ikhtiyārī). The second gloss clarifies the term 'heedless' (ghāfil) in the earlier answer to the second objection: if heedless means one who does not understand the address, then the objection's claim that it is an obligation on the heedless is rejected; if it means one who does not affirm (yuṣaddiq) the address, then the claim that it is invalid is rejected—the condition for obligation is understanding, not affirmation. The third gloss raises a doubt (ta'ammul) about the claim 'knowledge without a teacher': it is possible through reflection combined with teaching, so it is not valid to insist on independent reflection. The context of the overall discourse, especially the answer to the seventh objection, indicates that the claim is that knowledge is obligatory either with or without teaching—not exclusively without a teacher.

Key Terms
بلا معلم
استقلال
تعليم
غافل
فهم
تصديق
إجماع
مقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'knowledge without a teacher' implies that teaching is unnecessary and the obligation is only for independent reflection.
Correction: The third gloss clarifies that reflection combined with teaching is valid, and the overall claim is that knowledge is obligatory either with or without teaching.

Checkpoint
According to the third gloss, why is the claim that knowledge must be obtained without a teacher questionable?
A) Because it is impossible to gain knowledge without a teacher.
B) Because knowledge can be obtained through reflection combined with teaching, and the overall claim is that knowledge can be with or without teaching.
C) Because the consensus of the community is that a teacher is necessary.
D) Because the obligation of knowledge applies only to those who have a teacher.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to know God can be fulfilled through reflection with or without a teacher.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to understand a religious matter, do you honestly seek out a teacher, or do you hide behind the excuse that you should figure it out alone? How does this lesson expose that excuse?
?
If your knowledge of God came only through a teacher, do you sometimes feel it is less authentic or less valuable? Are you willing to accept that the obligation can be fulfilled through learning from others, or does your pride demand independence?
?
The condition for obligation is understanding, not full affirmation. Is your reaction to God's commands mere intellectual assent, or does your understanding move you to genuine submission? What would change if you took this condition seriously?

The Third Objection: Possibility of Consensus on Obligation
The author refutes the claim that consensus cannot occur on the obligation of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of consensus on the obligation of knowledge by invoking a unifying factor and clear evidence.

Core Idea
Consensus is possible when motives and evidence unite.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection: even if obligation is possible, has it actually occurred through consensus? The objector argues that consensus on such a matter is as unlikely as everyone agreeing on a single food. The author counters by distinguishing consensus driven by a unifying factor and compelling evidence from arbitrary agreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the objection's claim that consensus is not possible ordinarily.
2
Identify the unifying factor
Look for the phrase 'amr jāmiʿ' and how the author explains it with 'abundance of motives' and 'clear proof'.
3
Contrast the examples
Compare the objector's example (consensus on one food/word) with the author's example (consensus on obligation). Why is one impossible and the other possible?
The key is the presence of a unifying factor.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument defends the occurrence of obligation and why it matters for kalam.
This shows the author's method of responding to objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث ) سلمنا إمكان وجوب المعرفة شرعاً لكن لا نسلم وقوعه ( قولكم : أجمعت الأمة على ذلك قلنا : لا يمكن الإجماع منهم على وجوبها ( عادة كعلى أي كالإجماع منهم على أكل طعام) واحد (و) على ( كلمة ) واحدة ( في آن واحد ( قلنا يجوز ) يجوز الإجماع منهم ( فيما يوجد ) فيه ( أمر جامع) لهم ( عليه ) كوجوب المعرفة مثلا، ثم بين الجامع بقوله ( من توفر الدواعي ) إلى انقياد الشريعة ومعرفة أحكامها ( وقيام الدليل) الظاهر على ذلك المجمع عليه ( وما ذكرتم من الإجماع على طعام واحد أو كلمة واحدة ( لا جامع ) لهم عليه ( بل شهواتهم بحسب أمزجتهم، وحالاتهم متخالفة داعية إلى عدم الاتفاق فيه
The third ([objection]): We grant the possibility of the obligation of knowledge by revelation, but we do not grant its occurrence. Your statement: 'The community has consensus on that.' We say: Consensus from them on its obligation is not possible ordinarily, like their consensus on eating one food and on one word at one moment. We say: It is permissible for them to have consensus on something in which there is a unifying factor (amr jāmiʿ) for them, such as the obligation of knowledge, for example. Then he explained the unifying factor by his saying: 'due to the abundance of motives to submit to the Sharia and know its rulings, and the existence of the clear proof on that agreed-upon matter.' What you mentioned of consensus on one food or one word has no unifying factor for them; rather their desires are according to their temperaments and their states are diverse, leading to disagreement on it.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its response. The objector concedes the possibility of obligation but denies its actual occurrence, claiming that the ummah does not have consensus on it. The author counters that consensus is not impossible ordinarily; the objector's analogy with consensus on eating one food or saying one word is flawed because those lack a unifying factor. Instead, consensus on the obligation of knowledge is possible because there is a strong motive (dawāʿī) to submit to Sharia and clear evidence supports it. Thus the occurrence of obligation is not refuted by the impossibility of such arbitrary consensus.

Key Terms
وقوع الوجوب
الإجماع
الأمر الجامع
توفر الدواعي
قيام الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus is impossible on any matter because people's tastes and opinions differ.
Correction: Consensus is possible when there is a strong common motive and clear evidence, as in the obligation of knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is necessary for a consensus to be possible?
A) That all people agree spontaneously.
B) That there is a unifying factor (amr jāmiʿ) and clear evidence.
C) That the matter is about food or speech.
D) That the obligation is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Consensus on the obligation of knowledge is possible due to a unifying factor: abundant motives to follow Sharia and clear evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear obligation from the Sharia that you are reluctant to act upon, is it because your motives to obey are weak or because you are ignoring the evidence? What will you do today to strengthen both?
?
The passage says consensus is possible when there is a unifying factor. In your own life, what unifies you with other believers in seeking knowledge? Are you actively contributing to that unity, or are you letting personal desires create division?
?
The objector used a flawed analogy to deny an obligation. Have you ever rationalized your way out of a duty by comparing it to something irrelevant? What obligation is your heart resisting right now, and will you surrender to the truth despite your desires?

The Fourth Objection: Impossibility of Transmitting Consensus
Sanusi presents the fourth objection against consensus with four sub-objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four reasons given to argue that consensus cannot be reliably transmitted to later generations.

Core Idea
Four hypothetical impossibilities challenge the transmission of consensus.

The objectors raise a series of practical obstacles that would prevent any certain knowledge of consensus among early mujtahids. They argue that even if consensus occurred, we could never be sure of it. This lesson examines each of these obstacles in detail before turning to the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the fourth objection carefully. Note the four separate sub-objections listed.
The objection is structured as a series of possibilities that undermine certainty.
2
Identify the four sub-objections
Find and list the four reasons: 1) dispersal of mujtahids, 2) possibility of being hidden, 3) possibility of lying, 4) possibility of retraction.
Each is introduced with 'وجواز' or similar.
3
Understand each sub-objection
For each, ask: why does this make transmission impossible? The commentary explains each point.
4
Reflect on the cumulative force
Consider how these objections together seem to block any claim to certain knowledge of consensus.
This sets up the need for a strong response in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الإجماع إن ثبت ) في نفسه ( امتنع نقله إلينا فلا يصح أن يتمسك به ( لانتشار (المجتهدين) في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها فلا يعرفون بأعيانهم، فكيف تعرف أقوالهم ) وجواز خفاء واحد ) أما لخموله أو لوقوعه في بلاد الكفار أسيراً (و) جواز (كذبه) في قوله : إن الحكم عندي كذا بناء على احترازه من المخالفة المفضية إلى المفسدة، ولا شك أن المعتبر اعتقاده لا مجرد قول يفوه به (و) جواز (رجوعه) عن حكم أفتي به لتغير اجتهاده قبل فتوى الآخر ... (قلنا) ما ذكرتموه منقوض بما علم الإجماع عليه بطريق التواتر ( كالأركان ) الإسلامية من وجوب الصلوات الخمس وصوم رمضان وغيرهما ( وتقديم الدليل القاطع على الظني قوله : (امتنع نقله إلخ ) لعدم العلم للناقل بثبوته . قوله : ( وجواز كذبه) لعدم عصمته وإذا جاز كذبه لم يحصل للناقل العلم بصدقه، وإن صدق فيما قال فلا يحصل العلم بثبوت الإجماع وإن كان ثابتا . قوله : ( وجواز رجوعه إلخ) يعني لا يمكن سماع أقوال المجتهدين وإن صدقوا في زمان واحد، بل في زمان متطاول فربما يتغير اجتهاد بعض، فرجع عن ذلك الرأي قبل قول الآخر، وإذا جاز الرجوع لم يحصل العلم بثبوت الإجماع للناقل، وإن كان ثابتاً لعدم الرجوع فتدبر فإنه مماخفي على الناظرين. قوله : (قلنا ما ذكرتموه إلخ يعني أن ما ذكرتم تشكيك في مقابلة الضرورة، فإنا نعلم الصحابة والتابعين الإجماع في مسائل كثيرة، وما ذلك إلا بثبوته وبنقله إلينا، فانتقض قطعاً الدليلان باستلزامهما المحال .
The fourth: If consensus is established in itself, its transmission to us is impossible, so it cannot be used as evidence. Due to the dispersal of the mujtahids in the East and West of the earth, so they are not known individually, so how can their statements be known? And the possibility of one being hidden, either due to obscurity or being a prisoner in the land of the disbelievers; and the possibility of his lying in his statement: 'The ruling with me is such and such' based on his avoidance of opposition that leads to corruption, and no doubt that what is considered is his belief, not mere words he utters; and the possibility of his retraction from a ruling he gave due to change of his ijtihad before the fatwa of another. We said: What you have mentioned is refuted by what is known by consensus through tawatur, like the Islamic pillars: the obligation of the five prayers, fasting Ramadan, and others; and precedence of the decisive proof over the conjectural. His statement: 'its transmission is impossible etc.' because the transmitter does not have knowledge of its establishment. His statement: 'and the possibility of his lying' because he is not infallible; if his lying is possible, the transmitter does not obtain knowledge of his truthfulness, and even if he is truthful in what he said, he does not obtain knowledge of the establishment of consensus even if it is established. His statement: 'and the possibility of his retraction etc.' means that the statements of the mujtahids cannot be heard even if they are truthful at one time, but over an extended period, the ijtihad of some may change, so he retracts that opinion before the statement of another. If retraction is possible, the transmitter does not obtain knowledge of the establishment of consensus, even if it is established due to lack of retraction. So reflect, for it is something hidden from the observers. His statement: 'We said: What you have mentioned etc.' means that what you have mentioned is doubt against the obvious (ḍarūra). For we know that the Companions and Followers had consensus on many issues, and that is only due to its establishment and its transmission to us. So both proofs are conclusively refuted because they entail the impossible.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage treats the fourth objection against using consensus as proof: even if consensus occurs, it cannot be transmitted to later generations due to the wide dispersal of mujtahids, the possibility of some being unknown, lying, or retracting. The response is that this is a skeptical argument contradicted by the fact that we do know certain consensuses through tawatur (mass transmission), such as the five daily prayers and fasting. The commentary explains each point: the impossibility of transmission is due to lack of knowledge of its establishment, the possibility of lying and retraction prevent certainty, but the response cuts off the doubt by appealing to necessary knowledge that such consensuses are reliably transmitted.

Key Terms
نقل الإجماع
المجتهدون
جواز الكذب
جواز الرجوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus transmission is inherently impossible because of these hypothetical possibilities.
Correction: The objections are hypothetical possibilities; they do not prove actual impossibility. The response shows that many consensuses are known by tawatur.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the sub-objections listed against transmitting consensus?
A) Dispersal of mujtahids
B) Possibility of a mujtahid being hidden
C) Possibility of a mujtahid lying
D) Possibility of a mujtahid forgetting

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection raises four hypothetical obstacles that would render consensus untransmittable, but these are answered by the existence of tawatur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider that among the hypothetical obstacles is the possibility that a scholar might lie about his ruling to avoid conflict. Is your own commitment to truth strong enough that you would speak the truth even when it creates friction with those around you? What would you do differently starting today to ensure your words align with your inner conviction?
?
The fourth objection raises the possibility that a scholar might change his ruling before others follow, making consensus elusive. When you learn a new Islamic ruling, do you actively check whether it is based on a stable, widely-accepted consensus or on a single, possibly temporary opinion? How will you change your approach to seeking religious knowledge after this lesson?

The Response: Consensus Known by Tawatur
Sanusi refutes the objection by invoking consensuses known through tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection to consensus transmission is refuted by pointing to consensuses known through mass transmission (tawatur).

Core Idea
Known consensuses like the pillars of Islam defeat the skeptical objection.

The previous lesson introduced objections that consensus cannot be transmitted. Here, Sanusi offers a powerful response: the very fact that we do know certain consensuses with certainty—such as the five daily prayers—proves that transmission is possible. This lesson unpacks the response and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic text starting from 'قلنا'. Notice how the author directly counters the objection.
The response is concise but decisive.
2
Identify the counterexample
Find the examples given: the Islamic pillars. These are matters of consensus known by tawatur.
The phrase is 'كالأركان الإسلامية'.
3
Understand the argument structure
The response argues that the objection leads to a contradiction: if consensus were intransmittable, we would not know these pillars, but we do. Therefore, the objection is invalid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Consider the commentary on each part
The commentary explains that the objection is 'doubt against the obvious' and that the apparent transmission of consensus in major issues disproves the possibility of the obstacles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الإجماع إن ثبت ) في نفسه ( امتنع نقله إلينا فلا يصح أن يتمسك به ( لانتشار (المجتهدين) في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها فلا يعرفون بأعيانهم، فكيف تعرف أقوالهم ) وجواز خفاء واحد ) أما لخموله أو لوقوعه في بلاد الكفار أسيراً (و) جواز (كذبه) في قوله : إن الحكم عندي كذا بناء على احترازه من المخالفة المفضية إلى المفسدة، ولا شك أن المعتبر اعتقاده لا مجرد قول يفوه به (و) جواز (رجوعه) عن حكم أفتي به لتغير اجتهاده قبل فتوى الآخر ... (قلنا) ما ذكرتموه منقوض بما علم الإجماع عليه بطريق التواتر ( كالأركان ) الإسلامية من وجوب الصلوات الخمس وصوم رمضان وغيرهما ( وتقديم الدليل القاطع على الظني قوله : (امتنع نقله إلخ ) لعدم العلم للناقل بثبوته . قوله : ( وجواز كذبه) لعدم عصمته وإذا جاز كذبه لم يحصل للناقل العلم بصدقه، وإن صدق فيما قال فلا يحصل العلم بثبوت الإجماع وإن كان ثابتا . قوله : ( وجواز رجوعه إلخ) يعني لا يمكن سماع أقوال المجتهدين وإن صدقوا في زمان واحد، بل في زمان متطاول فربما يتغير اجتهاد بعض، فرجع عن ذلك الرأي قبل قول الآخر، وإذا جاز الرجوع لم يحصل العلم بثبوت الإجماع للناقل، وإن كان ثابتاً لعدم الرجوع فتدبر فإنه مماخفي على الناظرين. قوله : (قلنا ما ذكرتموه إلخ يعني أن ما ذكرتم تشكيك في مقابلة الضرورة، فإنا نعلم الصحابة والتابعين الإجماع في مسائل كثيرة، وما ذلك إلا بثبوته وبنقله إلينا، فانتقض قطعاً الدليلان باستلزامهما المحال .
The fourth: If consensus is established in itself, its transmission to us is impossible, so it cannot be used as evidence. Due to the dispersal of the mujtahids in the East and West of the earth, so they are not known individually, so how can their statements be known? And the possibility of one being hidden, either due to obscurity or being a prisoner in the land of the disbelievers; and the possibility of his lying in his statement: 'The ruling with me is such and such' based on his avoidance of opposition that leads to corruption, and no doubt that what is considered is his belief, not mere words he utters; and the possibility of his retraction from a ruling he gave due to change of his ijtihad before the fatwa of another. We said: What you have mentioned is refuted by what is known by consensus through tawatur, like the Islamic pillars: the obligation of the five prayers, fasting Ramadan, and others; and precedence of the decisive proof over the conjectural. His statement: 'its transmission is impossible etc.' because the transmitter does not have knowledge of its establishment. His statement: 'and the possibility of his lying' because he is not infallible; if his lying is possible, the transmitter does not obtain knowledge of his truthfulness, and even if he is truthful in what he said, he does not obtain knowledge of the establishment of consensus even if it is established. His statement: 'and the possibility of his retraction etc.' means that the statements of the mujtahids cannot be heard even if they are truthful at one time, but over an extended period, the ijtihad of some may change, so he retracts that opinion before the statement of another. If retraction is possible, the transmitter does not obtain knowledge of the establishment of consensus, even if it is established due to lack of retraction. So reflect, for it is something hidden from the observers. His statement: 'We said: What you have mentioned etc.' means that what you have mentioned is doubt against the obvious (ḍarūra). For we know that the Companions and Followers had consensus on many issues, and that is only due to its establishment and its transmission to us. So both proofs are conclusively refuted because they entail the impossible.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage treats the fourth objection against using consensus as proof: even if consensus occurs, it cannot be transmitted to later generations due to the wide dispersal of mujtahids, the possibility of some being unknown, lying, or retracting. The response is that this is a skeptical argument contradicted by the fact that we do know certain consensuses through tawatur (mass transmission), such as the five daily prayers and fasting. The commentary explains each point: the impossibility of transmission is due to lack of knowledge of its establishment, the possibility of lying and retraction prevent certainty, but the response cuts off the doubt by appealing to necessary knowledge that such consensuses are reliably transmitted.

Key Terms
قلنا
التواتر
الأركان الإسلامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The transmission of consensus is always impossible due to practical obstacles.
Correction: Transmission is possible and actually occurs via tawatur, as seen in the case of the Islamic pillars.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that consensus cannot be transmitted?
A) By denying that consensus exists
B) By pointing to consensuses known through tawatur, like the pillars of Islam
C) By proving that all mujtahids are known
D) By saying that the objection is invalid due to lack of evidence

Key Takeaway
The existence of consensuses known by tawatur (e.g., the pillars of Islam) proves that transmission of consensus is possible and refutes the skeptical objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you personally rely on the consensus of the Companions for your practice of the pillars? How certain are you that your prayer is the same as theirs, and what would change if you doubted that?
?
The objector raised doubts about transmission. Have you ever allowed theoretical possibilities (like error or change) to undermine your confidence in some religious knowledge? How does this lesson challenge you to trust what is mass-transmitted?
?
Sanusi calls the objection 'doubt against the obvious'. What 'obvious' truths of Islam have you allowed skepticism to weaken in your heart, and what step will you take today to reaffirm your certainty in them?

The Possibility of Error and the Validity of Consensus
Author argues against fifth objection using distinction between individual and collective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of error in individual scholars does not necessarily undermine the categorical authority of consensus.

Core Idea
Individual fallibility does not equal collective error.

This lesson addresses a critical objection against consensus as a source of knowledge: that since each mujtahid can err, the entire consensus could be wrong. The response introduces a key distinction between the individuals considered separately and the collective whole, which has a social form that can yield certainty. We will explore how the author defends consensus by showing that the conjunction of errors is not inevitable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the objection and the beginning of the response.
The passage includes both the objection and several layers of response.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that even if consensus is transmitted, it is not a proof because error is possible on each mujtahid. Understand why this would seem to invalidate consensus.
3
Focus on the key distinction
The response makes a distinction between each individual (kull wāḥid) and the collective whole (al-kull al-majmūʿī) that has a social form (al-hayʾa al-ijtimāʿiyya). This is the core of the author's argument.
The collective whole is not simply the sum of individuals; it has an additional quality that can lead to certainty.
4
Understand the conjunction of errors
The author argues that the conjunction of errors is not necessary; rather, correct opinions can join together and outweigh errors. This counters the assumption that individual errors accumulate.
5
Recognize the nature of the response
The response is described as a verification (taḥqīqī) rather than an obligatory reply (ilzāmī). This means it is a positive argument, not just a rebuttal.
The counterexample is not valid because the reply addresses the real basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس وإن سلم نقله بعد تسليم إمكانه وإمكان نقله ( فليس بحجة لجواز الخطأ على كل أي كل واحد من المجتهدين ( فكذا) يجوز الخطأ ( على الكل من حيث هو كل فلا يكون قولهم حجة قطعية (ولأن انضمام الخطأ) قوله : ( لجواز الخطأ إلخ) مبنى هذا عدم الفرق بين كل واحد والكل المجموعي الذي لم يعتبر فيه الهيئة الاجتماعية. قوله : ( ولأن انضمام الخطأ إلخ) مبنى هذا انضمام الصواب إلى الصواب، كما يرجح قوله: (وجواز كذبه جواز الكذب وكذا جواز الرجوع إلخ معتبر بالنسبة إلى الناقل، أي معتبر ثبوته عنده ثم جواز الرجوع مثلاً غير جواز الرجوع بالفعل، فلا ينافي المفروض أعني ثبوت الإجماع في نفسه كما ظن. قوله : (منقوض بما علم إلخ) هذا جواب عن رد الإمكان أيضاً، ثم إنه جواب تحقيقي لا إلزامي فلا يرد أن يقال : صورة النقض غير مسلم عند المانع كما ظن. قوله : ( ولأن انضمام الخطأ أكثر النسخ بالواو فالفرق بينه وبين التعليل الأول أعني قوله
The fifth: Even if its transmission is granted after granting its possibility and the possibility of its transmission, it is not a proof due to the possibility of error upon each, i.e., each individual mujtahid. Likewise, error is possible upon the whole as a whole, so their statement is not a categorical proof (ḥujja qaṭʿiyya). And because the conjunction of error... His statement: 'due to the possibility of error etc.' is based on the lack of distinction between each individual and the collective whole (al-kull al-majmūʿī) in which the social form (al-hayʾa al-ijtimāʿiyya) is not considered. His statement: 'And because the conjunction of error etc.' is based on the conjunction of correctness to correctness, as it gives preponderance. His statement: 'and the possibility of his lying, the possibility of lying, and likewise the possibility of retraction etc. are considered relative to the transmitter, i.e., their establishment for him is considered; then the possibility of retraction, for example, is not the actual retraction, so it does not contradict the assumed, namely the establishment of consensus in itself, as thought. His statement: 'refuted by what is known etc.' is a response to the refutation of possibility as well, and it is a verifications reply not an obliging one, so it cannot be said that the counterexample is not granted by the objector. His statement: 'and because the conjunction of error' in most copies with 'and' (wāw), so the difference between it and the first cause, i.e., his saying...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage covers the fifth objection: even if consensus is established and transmitted, it is not a definitive proof because each mujtahid is fallible, so error can occur in each, and by extension in the entire group. The response makes a distinction between a collection of individuals considered separately and the collective whole that has a social aspect. The conjunction of errors is not necessary; rather the conjunction of correct opinions can outweigh errors. The commentary further clarifies that the possibility of lying or retraction is considered from the transmitter's perspective and does not negate the actual existence of consensus. The author emphasizes that the response is a verification (taḥqīqī) not merely obligatory (ilzāmī), meaning it addresses the real basis rather than forcing the opponent.

Key Terms
جواز الخطأ
الكل المجموعي
الهيئة الاجتماعية
انضمام الخطأ
حجة قطعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the possibility of error in each individual necessarily implies error in the collective consensus.
Correction: The collective whole has a social form that can yield certainty, and errors do not necessarily combine; correct opinions can outweigh errors.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in response to the argument that error on each mujtahid invalidates consensus?
A) That mujtahids are infallible.
B) That the collective whole is different from the sum of individuals, and conjunction of errors is not necessary.
C) That consensus is only about non-essential matters.
D) That error is impossible in matters of religion.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of error in individual scholars does not automatically lead to error in the collective consensus, because the collective whole has a social form that can yield certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you follow a fatwa or rely on a scholarly opinion, do you truly trust that the collective consensus of qualified scholars has produced certainty, or do you secretly doubt because you know each scholar could be mistaken? How does this lesson challenge your personal hesitation?
?
Consider a time you dismissed a collective ruling because you saw flaws in individual scholars. Did you conflate the individual and the collective? What would it take for you to submit to a consensual ruling even when your own reasoning disagrees?
?
The author says the reply is 'verification' (تحقيقي), not merely 'obliging' (الزامي). In your own life, are you more concerned with being right in argument or arriving at the truth? Which approach does this lesson demand from you?

Objections to Transmitting Ijmā' and the First Refutation
Objector lists five reasons against transmitting ijmā'; author counters with known tawātur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical objections raised against the transmission of ijmā' and how the author refutes them by pointing to consensuses known through tawātur.

Core Idea
Known consensuses by tawātur refute claims that ijmā' is untransmittable.

The objector argues that transmitting the consensus of all mujtahids across the globe is practically impossible. How can we know what every scholar in every era has agreed upon? The author acknowledges the difficulty but points to well-known cases—like the five daily prayers—whose consensus has been transmitted via tawātur. This refutes the general claim of impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's five reasons against transmitting ijmā'. Notice each practical difficulty.
2
Identify each reason
List the five points: geographic spread, hidden individuals, lying, retraction, impossibility of tawātur.
These are cumulative.
3
Read the author's refutation
The author counters by pointing to known consensuses transmitted via tawātur, like obligatory prayers.
4
Reflect on the refutation's power
Ask yourself: Does the existence of such consensuses undermine the general impossibility claim?
The author concludes with a principle: decisive proof over conjectural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما امتنع نقله لانتشار المجتهدين في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها فلا يعرفون بأعيانهم، فكيف تعرف أقوالهم؟ وجواز خفاء واحد، أما لخموله أو لوقوعه في بلاد الكفار أسيراً، وجواز كذبه في قوله: إن الحكم عندي كذا بناء على احترازه من المخالفة المفضية إلى المفسدة، ولا شك أن المعتبر اعتقاده لا مجرد قول يفوه به، وجواز رجوعه عن حكم أفتى به لتغير اجتهاده قبل فتوى الآخر، وأيضاً نقل الإجماع بطريق التواتر ممتنع عادة وبطريق الآحاد لا يفيد في القطعيات. قلنا: ما ذكرتموه منقوض بما علم الإجماع عليه بطريق التواتر كالأركان الإسلامية من وجوب الصلوات الخمس وصوم رمضان وغيرهما. وتقديم الدليل القاطع على الظني. الخامس: وإن سلم نقله بعد تسليم إمكانه وإمكان نقله فليس بحجة لجواز الخطأ على كل واحد من المجتهدين، فكذا يجوز الخطأ على الكل من حيث هو كل فلا يكون قولهم حجة قطعية، ولأن انضمام الخطأ الصادر من أحدهم على انفراده إلى الخطأ الصادر من واحد آخر وهكذا إلى أن يشملهم الخطأ بأسرهم لا يوجب الصواب بل يوجب كون الكل على الخطأ. قلنا: كون الإجماع حجة قطعية معلوم بالضرورة من الدين، فيكون التشكيك فيه بالاستدلال في مقابلة الضرورة سفسطة لا يلتفت إليها. ولا يلزم من جواز الخطأ على كل واحد جواز الخطأ على الكل المجموعي لتغايرهما وتغاير حكميهما، فإن كل واحد من الإنسان تسعه هذه الدار ولا تسع كلهم. وأما احتمال انضمام الخطأ إلى الخطأ حتى يعم الكل فمدفوع بما علم من الدين ضرورة، وبما ثبت بالأدلة من عصمة الأمة.
And it is impossible to transmit it because of the spread of the mujtahids in the east and west of the earth, so they are not known in their persons, so how can their statements be known? And the possibility of one being hidden, either due to obscurity or his falling captive in the land of the unbelievers, and the possibility of his lying in his saying: 'The ruling according to me is such' due to his caution from opposition leading to corruption, and there is no doubt that what is considered is his belief, not a mere utterance; and the possibility of his retracting from a ruling he gave due to a change in his ijtihad before the fatwa of another; also transmitting ijmā' by the way of tawātur is impossible customarily, and by the way of āḥād does not benefit in certainties. We said: What you have mentioned is refuted by what is known of ijmā' by the way of tawātur, such as the Islamic pillars: the obligation of the five prayers, fasting Ramadan, and others. And presenting the decisive proof over the conjectural. The fifth: And if its transmission is granted after granting its possibility and the possibility of its transmission, it is not a proof due to the possibility of error on every one of the mujtahids; likewise, error is possible on the whole collectively, so their statement is not a decisive proof; and because the joining of error proceeding from one of them individually to error proceeding from another, and so on until error encompasses all of them, does not necessitate correctness, rather necessitates that all are in error. We said: That ijmā' is a decisive proof is known necessarily from religion, so doubting it by reasoning in opposition to necessity is sophistry not to be paid attention to. And it does not follow from the possibility of error on each individual the possibility of error on the whole collective, due to their difference and the difference of their rulings; for each individual human is accommodated by this house, but it does not accommodate all of them. As for the possibility of error joining to error until it covers all, it is repelled by what is known from religion necessarily, and by what is established by proofs of the infallibility of the community.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector raises several reasons why ijmā' (consensus) cannot be reliably transmitted: (1) the mujtahids are scattered worldwide and unknown individually, (2) some may be hidden or captive, (3) some may lie about their ruling to avoid conflict, (4) some may retract a ruling, (5) tawātur transmission is customarily impossible, and āḥād transmission does not yield certainty. The author refutes by pointing out that many consensuses are known by tawātur (e.g., pillars of Islam), so the objection is undermined. Then the objector presents a fifth point: even if transmission is granted, error is possible on each individual, and accumulated errors would lead to the whole being in error, so ijmā' is not decisive. The author responds that ijmā' as a decisive proof is known necessarily from religion; disputing it is sophistry. Furthermore, the possibility of error on individuals does not imply error on the whole collective, as they are different in kind. The possibility of cumulative error is refuted by the necessary knowledge from religion and by the established proofs of the community's infallibility.

Key Terms
إجماع
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ijmā' cannot be known because scholars are scattered and hidden.
Correction: Many consensuses are known by tawātur, so transmission is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what refutes the objector's claim that ijmā' cannot be transmitted?
A) The infallibility of the community
B) The existence of consensuses known by tawātur
C) The impossibility of error on the collective
D) The use of analogical reasoning

Key Takeaway
The objector's practical difficulties are overcome by the existence of consensuses transmitted through tawātur, such as the mandatory prayers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector feared that a mujtahid might lie about his ruling to avoid causing corruption. Have you ever twisted the truth or remained silent out of fear of conflict or personal harm? In what specific situation did you prioritize worldly safety over conveying the truth? What would it cost you to speak with integrity starting today?
?
The author points to the consensuses on the five prayers and fasting as undeniable examples of transmitted ijmā'. When have you doubted a clear teaching of Islam because it felt inconvenient or because you focused on practical difficulties? Will you submit to these known truths even when your lower self resists?

The Second Objection to Ijmā' and its Refutation: Error and Infallibility
Second objection: error possibility on individuals; refutation: collective is distinct and infallible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that even if transmitted, ijmā' could be erroneous, and the author's response that ijmā' is known necessarily and the collective is distinct from individuals.

Core Idea
Error on individuals does not entail error on the collective; ijmā' is decisive by necessity.

The objector concedes transmission but argues that since each mujtahid can err, the whole group could err cumulatively. The author counters with a powerful distinction: the collective as a whole is different from the sum of its parts. He invokes the necessary knowledge from religion and the established infallibility of the community, making the objection sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
The objector presents a fifth point: even if transmitted, ijmā' is not decisive because any individual can err, and errors can accumulate.
Notice the logical structure: possibility of error on each leads to possibility of error on all.
2
Understand the author's refutation
The author gives two replies. First, ijmā' as a whole is known necessarily from religion, so any argument against it is sophistry. Second, the possibility of error on each does not imply possibility of error on the collective, just as a house accommodating individuals does not accommodate all simultaneously.
The house analogy illustrates the distinction.
3
Reflect on the infallibility of the community
The author ends by stating that the possibility of cumulative error is repelled by necessary religious knowledge and proofs of the community's infallibility.
This reinforces the authority of ijmā'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is impossible to transmit it because of the spread of the mujtahids in the east and west of the earth, so they are not known in their persons, so how can their statements be known? We said: What you have mentioned is refuted by what is known of ijmā' by the way of tawātur, such as the Islamic pillars: the obligation of the five prayers, fasting Ramadan, and others. And presenting the decisive proof over the conjectural. The fifth: And if its transmission is granted after granting its possibility and the possibility of its transmission, it is not a proof due to the possibility of error on every one of the mujtahids; likewise, error is possible on the whole collectively, so their statement is not a decisive proof; and because the joining of error proceeding from one of them individually to error proceeding from another, and so on until error encompasses all of them, does not necessitate correctness, rather necessitates that all are in error. We said: That ijmā' is a decisive proof is known necessarily from religion, so doubting it by reasoning in opposition to necessity is sophistry not to be paid attention to. And it does not follow from the possibility of error on each individual the possibility of error on the whole collective, due to their difference and the difference of their rulings; for each individual human is accommodated by this house, but it does not accommodate all of them. As for the possibility of error joining to error until it covers all, it is repelled by what is known from religion necessarily, and by what is established by proofs of the infallibility of the community.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector raises several reasons why ijmā' (consensus) cannot be reliably transmitted: (1) the mujtahids are scattered worldwide and unknown individually, (2) some may be hidden or captive, (3) some may lie about their ruling to avoid conflict, (4) some may retract a ruling, (5) tawātur transmission is customarily impossible, and āḥād transmission does not yield certainty. The author refutes by pointing out that many consensuses are known by tawātur (e.g., pillars of Islam), so the objection is undermined. Then the objector presents a fifth point: even if transmission is granted, error is possible on each individual, and accumulated errors would lead to the whole being in error, so ijmā' is not decisive. The author responds that ijmā' as a decisive proof is known necessarily from religion; disputing it is sophistry. Furthermore, the possibility of error on individuals does not imply error on the whole collective, as they are different in kind. The possibility of cumulative error is refuted by the necessary knowledge from religion and by the established proofs of the community's infallibility.

Key Terms
خطأ
عصمة
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If each individual scholar can be mistaken, then the entire consensus of scholars can be mistaken.
Correction: The collective is distinct from the sum of individuals; error on each does not entail error on all, and ijmā' is protected from error by divine decree.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the status of ijmā' as a proof?
A) It is probable but not certain
B) It is known necessarily from religion
C) It is valid only for non-doctrinal matters
D) It is not a proof

Key Takeaway
The collective consensus is not reducible to individual opinions; it is known necessarily from religion and protected from error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed the consensus of scholars (ijmā') because you thought you saw a flaw in individual opinions? What does this lesson reveal about the arrogance of that assumption?
?
If ijmā' is known by necessity from religion, what does your hesitation to accept it say about your trust in Allah's preservation of this community?
?
When you approach a matter on which there is clear consensus, do you submit your reasoning to it, or do you secretly think your own intellect could override it? What will you change today?

The Obligation of Knowledge and the Faith of Common People
Al-Jurjani defends obligatory knowledge against an objection from common people's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani reconciles the obligation of theological knowledge with the faith of common people who lack detailed proofs.

Core Idea
Common people possess summary knowledge which suffices for their individual obligation.

Al-Jurjani addresses a powerful objection: if knowledge of Allah is obligatory for every Muslim, how can the Prophet and the community have accepted the faith of common people who only imitate? In this passage, he distinguishes between summary and detailed knowledge, showing that common people have an implicit awareness of the proofs, and that the detailed knowledge required for defending the faith is a communal, not individual, obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector denies consensus on the obligation of knowledge, pointing to the acceptance of common people's faith without requiring proofs.
Notice the strong claim: 'consensus is actually against it.'
2
Examine the answer (qulnā)
Al-Jurjani responds by asserting that common people possess summary knowledge (ʿilm ijmalī) of the proofs, illustrated by the Bedouin's analogy.
The Bedouin's statement shows innate reasoning from creation to Creator.
3
Understand the two types of knowledge
The author explains that knowledge (maʿrifa) is of two kinds: individual obligation (summary) and communal obligation (detailed). Common people fulfill the individual obligation through summary knowledge.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(السادس منع وقوع ( الإجماع عليه ) على وجوب المعرفة (بل الإجماع واقع على خلافه وذلك لتقرير النبي ، والصحابة وأهل سائر الأعصار إلى عصرنا هذا ( العوام) على إيمانهم ) وهم الأكثرون (في كل عصر (مع عدم الاستفسار عن الدلائل الدالة على الصانع وصفاته بل مع العلم بأنهم لا يعلمونها (قطعاً إذ غاية مجهودهم الإقرار باللسان والتقليد المحض الذي لا يقين معه، ولو كانت المعرفة واجبة لما جاز ذلك التقرير والحكم بإيمانهم (قلنا : كانوا يعلمون أنهم يعلمون الأدلة إجمالاً كما قال الأعرابي : البعرة تدل على البعير وأثر الأقدام على المسير أفسماء ذات أبراج وأرض ذات فجاج وبحر ذات أمواج لا تدل على اللطيف الخبير. غايته ) أي غاية ما في الباب ( أنهم قصروا عن التحرير والتوضيح للمقاصد العرفانية ) والتقرير والتفصيل للدلائل الدالة عليها ) وذلك ) القصور ( لا بضر) فإن المعرفة الواجبة أعم من الإجمالية التي لا يقتدر معها على التحرير والتقرير ودفع الشبه والشكوك والتفصيلية التي يقتدر معها على ذلك ( أو ندعي أنه ) أي العرفان التفصيلي واجب لكنه ) فرض كفاية فإن الوجوب ) الذي ادعيناه ( أعم من ذلك ) أي من فرض الكفاية، وفرض العين أيضاً، والحاصل أن المعرفة على وجهين أحدهما فرض عين، وهو حاصل للعوام الذين قرروا على أيمانهم والآخر فرض كفاية، وهو حاصل لعلماء الأعصار
The sixth: denial of the occurrence of consensus on the obligation of knowledge. Rather, consensus is actually against it, because the Prophet, the Companions, and the people of all ages up to our time have affirmed the faith of the common people, who are the majority in every age, without asking them about the proofs indicating the Maker and His attributes, but rather with the knowledge that they do not know them at all. For the utmost of their effort is verbal acknowledgment and pure imitation which yields no certainty. If knowledge were obligatory, such affirmation and judgment of their faith would not be permissible. We said: They knew that they know the proofs summarily, as the Bedouin said: 'The camel dung indicates the camel, and the footprints indicate the traveler. A sky with constellations, an earth with wide roads, and a sea with waves — do they not indicate the All-Subtle, the All-Aware?' The utmost of the matter is that they fell short in expression and clarification of the gnostic purposes, and in elaboration and detail of the proofs indicating them. And that shortcoming does no harm, because the obligatory knowledge is more general than the summary knowledge, which does not enable one to elaborate, clarify, and repel doubts and skepticism, and the detailed knowledge which enables one to do that. Or we claim that detailed gnosis is obligatory but it is a communal obligation, because the obligation we claim is more general than that — i.e., than communal obligation and individual obligation. The upshot is that knowledge is of two kinds: one is an individual obligation, which is attained by the common people whose faith is affirmed; the other is a communal obligation, which is attained by the scholars of each age.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection and its answer. The objector denies that there is consensus on the obligation of knowledge (maʿrifa), arguing that the Prophet and the community throughout the ages have accepted the faith of the common people without demanding proofs, which would be impermissible if knowledge were obligatory. The answer (qulnā) asserts that the common people possess summary knowledge (ʿilm ijmalī) of the proofs, as illustrated by the Bedouin's statement. They know the proofs implicitly, even if they cannot articulate them in detail. The author then clarifies that obligatory knowledge is more general: summary knowledge suffices for individual obligation (farḍ ʿayn), while detailed knowledge (tafṣīlī) is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya) incumbent upon scholars. Thus, there is no contradiction.

Misconception: One might think that the common people have no knowledge at all, only blind imitation.
Correction: The author argues that common people possess summary knowledge (ʿilm ijmalī) of the proofs, which suffices for their individual obligation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what kind of knowledge is obligatory for the common people?
A) Detailed knowledge of theological proofs.
B) Summary knowledge of the proofs.
C) Both summary and detailed knowledge.
D) Neither, because they rely on imitation.

Key Takeaway
Common people fulfill their obligation of knowledge through summary knowledge of the proofs, while detailed knowledge is a communal obligation for scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly possess the summary knowledge of God's proofs that the Bedouin had, or are you relying on inherited belief? Spend silent time listing the natural signs that point to the Creator, and ask yourself whether your faith rests on that foundation.
?
If you were asked to explain the proofs for God's existence to a skeptic, could you do so with clarity? The passage says detailed knowledge is a communal obligation—are you fulfilling your share, or are you content with summary knowledge while leaving the burden to others?
?
The Bedouin saw the camel dung and footprints as evidence. When did you last look at the world around you as a sign of its Maker? Convince yourself today by finding one sign in your immediate environment and verbally acknowledging the 'All-Subtle, All-Aware'.

Objection: Knowledge Without Rational Inquiry
Al-Jurjani presents an objection that knowledge can be attained without rational inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the necessity of rational inquiry for knowledge of God and the three alternative means proposed: inspiration, education, and purification.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God is claimed to be attainable without rational inquiry through inspiration, education, or purification.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani presents the seventh objection against the consensus that knowledge of God requires rational inquiry. The objector grants the obligation of knowledge but denies that rational inquiry is the exclusive method, offering three alternatives from different groups: inspiration from Indian sages, education from atheists, and purification from Sufis. This objection sets the stage for a critical discussion on the sources of theological knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the objection grants the consensus on obligation but challenges the exclusive means.
The passage ends abruptly; the answer is not included here.
2
Identify the three alternative methods
Find the three ways the objector claims knowledge can be attained: inspiration (ilhām) with complete concentration, education (taʿlīm), and purification (tasfiya).
Each is attributed to a different group: Indian sages, atheists, and Sufis.
3
Understand the context
Consider why this objection is important: it challenges the foundational role of rational inquiry in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the objection's strength
Think about how a theologian might respond. This objection is not the final word; it is presented to be refuted.
The answer likely follows in the original text.

Optimal Ordering of Objections in Kalam
Al-Sharh proposes a better ordering of two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the order of objections in kalam discourse matters, specifically that placing the ninth objection before the eighth aligns with the logical flow of concession and acceptance.

Core Idea
The order of objections should follow the flow of concession and acceptance.

In kalam, the arrangement of arguments is not arbitrary; it follows a logical flow. Here, al-Sharh notes that the ninth objection fits better before the eighth because the preceding discussion operates on concession and acceptance (tanzil wa taslim). This lesson explores that methodological insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's suggestion about reordering objections.
2
Understand the flow of concession and acceptance
Recognize that the discussion assumes certain premises are granted (tanzil wa taslim). The objection order should mirror this concessive flow.
Concession (taslim) means temporarily accepting a premise for the sake of argument.
3
Apply the principle
See how the reordering works: first concede that knowledge requires consideration, then deny absolute obligatoriness, then deny universality. This is a logical progression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قدم الإشكال التاسع على الثامن لكان أنسب بمساق الكلام. قوله : ( و لو قدم الإشكال التاسع إلخ لأن المساق على التنزيل والتسليم، فمقتضاه أن يقول : سلمنا أن المعرفة لا تتم إلا بالنظر لكن لا نسلم أن ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب مطلقاً، ولو سلم الإطلاق فلا نسلم الكلية التي عليها مدار الاستدلال لانتقاضها بعدم المعرفة والشك .
And if the ninth objection were advanced before the eighth, it would be more appropriate to the flow of the discourse. His statement: (And if the ninth objection were advanced before the eighth, etc.) because the flow is based on concession and acceptance, so its implication is that he says: we concede that knowledge is only attained through consideration, but we do not concede that that without which the obligatory is not complete is absolutely obligatory. And if the absoluteness is conceded, we do not concede the universality on which the proof turns because it is broken by the absence of knowledge and doubt.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note about the arrangement of objections. The author observes that if the ninth objection were placed before the eighth, it would align better with the flow of the discourse. The commentary explains that the preceding discussion is based on concession and acceptance (tanzil wa taslim). The proper order would be: first concede that knowledge requires consideration, then deny that what the obligatory depends on is absolutely obligatory, then deny the universality of the proof due to counterexamples like absence of knowledge and doubt. This ordering makes the argument more coherent.

Key Terms
مساق الكلام
التنزيل والتسليم
الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the ninth is stronger than the eighth.
B) Because it fits the flow of concession and acceptance in the discussion.
C) Because the eighth objection is invalid.
D) Because the tenth objection should come later.

Key Takeaway
The order of objections should respect the logical flow of concession and acceptance in kalam discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about faith, do you consciously structure your objections to follow the logical flow of what the other person has already conceded? What would it take for you to adopt the method of 'tanzil wa taslim' in your next conversation?
?

?
You have just read that the universality of a proof can be 'broken' by counterexamples. In your own beliefs, are there any claims you hold as universal that might require re-examination in light of a concession? Will you honestly examine one such claim this week?

The Logical Order of Objections in Kalam
Explanation of the logical order of objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper sequence of objections in theological argumentation—prevention, counterexample, and counter-argument—and how this order is applied in the text.

Core Idea
The order of objections follows a logical sequence: prevention, counterexample, counter-argument.

In theological debates, not all objections are equivalent. Scholars have established a specific order for presenting objections to ensure clarity and logical progression. This passage explains why the tenth objection is preceded by the eighth and ninth, revealing the underlying methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Notice the technical terms: منع, نقض, معارضة.
The passage is dense with methodological jargon.
2
Identify the three objection types
Find the three terms: prevention (منع), counterexample (نقض), and counter-argument (معارضة). Understand their definitions from the context.
The eighth is interpreted as prevention, ninth as counterexample, tenth as counter-argument.
3
Understand the ordering principle
The passage states the order of investigation: first prevention, then counterexample, then counter-argument. This explains why the tenth comes after the eighth and ninth.
Also note the textual discrepancy about the eighth being prevention versus what the text says.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله بمساق الكلام أي بسابقه ولاحقه فإن قيل : الثامن كلها منوع، وكذا التاسع فيذكر والثامن نقض إجمالي كما عرفت فيؤخر عن التاسع، ويقدم على العاشر لكونه معارضة، لأن ترتيب البحث أن يذكر المنع ثم النقض، ثم المعارضة وحمل الثامن على منع كلية الكبرى، وهم لا يساعده عبارة المتن حيث قال : الدليل منقوض .
His statement: 'By the context of the speech' meaning by its antecedent and subsequent. For if it is said: the eighth objection entirely is a prevention (man‘), and likewise the ninth is mentioned, and the eighth is a general counterexample (naqḍ ijmalī) as you know, so it is postponed after the ninth, and advanced before the tenth because it is a counter-argument (mu‘āraḍa). For the order of investigation is that prevention is mentioned, then the counterexample, then the counter-argument. And interpreting the eighth as a prevention of the whole major premise is not supported by the wording of the text where it says: 'the proof is invalidated (manqūḍ).'
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note explaining the placement of the tenth objection relative to the eighth and ninth. The phrase 'بمساق الكلام' refers to the context of the speech, i.e., what comes before and after. The commentator explains that the eighth objection is a 'منع' (prevention) of the whole, the ninth is a 'نقض إجمالي' (general counterexample), and the tenth is a 'معارضة' (counter-argument). The proper logical order in investigation is to mention prevention first, then counterexample, then counter-argument. Therefore, the eighth is postponed after the ninth and advanced before the tenth because it is a counter-argument. However, interpreting the eighth as a prevention of the entire major premise is not supported by the text's wording, which says 'the proof is invalidated' (منقوض), implying a counterexample, not a prevention.

Key Terms
منع
نقض
معارضة
ترتيب البحث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of objections are equivalent and can be presented in any order.
Correction: The passage establishes a specific logical order: prevention first, then counterexample, then counter-argument.

Checkpoint
What is the proper order of theological objections according to this passage?
A) Prevention, counter-argument, counterexample.
B) Counterexample, prevention, counter-argument.
C) Prevention, counterexample, counter-argument.
D) Counter-argument, prevention, counterexample.

Key Takeaway
The correct order of objections in theological argumentation is prevention, counterexample, and counter-argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in religious discussions, do you deliberately follow the order of prevention, counterexample, and counter-argument, or do you often skip to countering without first examining the validity of the other's premises? How can you commit to a more disciplined approach?
?
Why are you quick to present counter-arguments without first considering whether the original claim is actually invalidated? What does this reveal about your desire to 'win' debates rather than seek truth?
?
Are you willing to admit when your own arguments are 'invalidated' (manqūḍ) by a counterexample? How does this lesson challenge your intellectual humility and honesty before Allah?

The Companions and the Good Innovation of Kalam
Defense of kalam as a good innovation via analogy with fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Companions' non-systematization of kalam does not make it a blameworthy innovation, and how it parallels the later development of fiqh.

Core Idea
Necessity can make a later practice a good innovation even if early Muslims did not do it.

The objection that kalam is an unlawful innovation often rests on the fact that the Prophet and his Companions did not practice it. In this passage, the author dismantles that objection by showing that circumstances change: what was unnecessary in their time became essential in later times. He reinforces his point by comparing kalam to fiqh, which also underwent later systematization without being deemed blameworthy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's rhetorical strategy: he first concedes the premise (they did not do it) then explains why.
The Arabic contains editorial glosses in parentheses; treat them as integral.
2
Identify the two sets of reasons
The author gives internal reasons (purity, intellect, revelation) and external reasons (few opponents, few doubts) why Companions did not need kalam.
Contrast with our time where doubts accumulated.
3
Examine the fiqh analogy
Just as fiqh was later systematized into quarters, chapters, and technical terms without being blamed, so too kalam.
Note the list of usul al-fiqh terms: naqd, qalb, jam', etc.
4
Understand 'good innovation'
The author concludes 'some innovation is good' (bid'ah hasanah). This is the key theological defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم إنهم) يعني الصحابة (لم يدونوه ) أي علم الكلام كما دوناه ( ولم يشتغلوا الاصطلاحات وتقرير المذاهب وتبويب المسائل وتفصيل الدلائل وتلخيص السؤال والجواب ) كما اشتغلنا نحن بهذه الأمور ( ولم يبالغوا في تطويل الذيول والأذناب ( كما بالغنا فيه ( وذلك ) أعني ترك التدوين والاشتغال والمبالغة ( لاختصاصهم بصفاء النفوس وقوة الأذهان وحدة القرائح ( ومشاهدة الوحي) المقتضية لفيضان الأنوار على قلوبهم الزكية ) والتمكن من مراجعة من يفيدهم ) ويدفع عنهم ما يعرض لهم من شك أو شبهة ( كل حين) من الأحيان (مع) متعلق بالاختصاص أي اختصوا بما ذكر مع قلة المعاندين) المشككين لهم ( ولم تكثر الشبهات ) معطوف على ما قبله بحسب المعنى كأنه قيل : مع أنه قل المعاندون ولم تكثر الشبهات في زمانهم ) كثرتها في زماننا بما حدث من الشبه ( في كل حين) من الأحيان السالفة ( فاجتمع لنا بالتدريج كل ما حدث في الأعصار الماضية، فاحتيج في زماننا إلى تدوين الكلام لحفظ العقائد ودفع الشبه دون زمانهم ( وذلك ) أي عدم تدوينهم الكلام (كما لم يدونوا الفقه ولم يميزوا أقسامه أرباعاً) العبادات والمبايعات والمناكحات والجنايات ( وأبواباً وفصولاً كما ميزناها كذلك (ولم يتكلموا فيها) أي في أقسامه ومسائله ( بالاصطلاح المتعارف ( في زماننا ) من النقض ) وهو تخلف الحكم عما جعله علة في القياس (والقلب) وهو تعليق ما ينافي الحكم بعلته (والجمع) وهو أن يجمع بين الأصل والفرع بعلة مشتركة بينهما فيصح القياس ) والفرق ) وهو أن يفرق بينهما بما يختص بأحدهما فلا يصح ) وتنقيح المناط ) وهو إسقاط ما لا مدخل له في العلية ( وتخريجه) وهو تعيين العلة بمجرد إبداء المناسبة إلى غير ذلك من اصطلاحات الفقهاء فكما لم يلزم مما ذكرناه قدح في الفقه لم يلزم أيضاً قدح في الكلام ) وبالجملة فمن البدعة ما هي حسنة
Yes, they (meaning the Companions) did not write it down (i.e., the science of kalam as we wrote it), and they did not occupy themselves with technical terms, establishing doctrines, arranging chapters, detailing proofs, and summarizing questions and answers, as we occupied ourselves with these matters, and they did not exaggerate in lengthening tails and appendices as we exaggerated. That (i.e., their leaving off writing, occupying themselves, and exaggerating) was due to their being distinguished by purity of souls, strength of minds, sharpness of intellects, and witnessing of revelation, which necessitated the flow of lights upon their pure hearts, and the ability to consult someone who would benefit them and repel from them any doubt or suspicion that occurred to them at any time, along with, (the word 'with' relates to 'distinguished': they were distinguished by what was mentioned along with the fewness of opponents who cast doubt upon them, and that doubts were not numerous) in their time, unlike their abundance in our time due to doubts that arose in every past era. So there accumulated for us gradually everything that occurred in past ages, and thus in our time it became necessary to write down kalam to preserve beliefs and repel doubts, unlike their time. And that (i.e., their not writing down kalam) is just as they did not write down fiqh and did not distinguish its divisions into quarters (acts of worship, transactions, marriages, and crimes) and chapters and sections as we distinguished them, and they did not speak about them (i.e., its divisions and issues) with the conventional terminology in our time, such as al-naqd (which is the failure of the ruling to follow what is made the cause in analogy), al-qalb (which is attaching something contrary to the ruling to its cause), al-jam' (which is bringing together the original and the branch under a common cause so that the analogy is valid), al-farq (which is distinguishing between them by something specific to one so that the analogy is invalid), tanqih al-manat (which is removing what has no bearing on the causality), and takhrijuhu (which is identifying the cause merely by showing suitability), and so on from the terminologies of the jurists. So just as no objection follows from what we have mentioned against fiqh, no objection follows against kalam. In summary, some innovation is good.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the science of kalam against the charge that it is a blameworthy innovation (bid'ah). The author acknowledges that the Prophet and Companions did not engage in kalam as it came to be practiced later—they did not write it down, use technical terminology, systematize doctrines, elaborate proofs, or engage in extended dialectical argumentation. However, the author argues that this was due to their unique circumstances: they had pure souls, strong intellects, sharp minds, direct access to revelation, and the ability to consult the Prophet whenever doubts arose. Moreover, they faced few opponents and few doubts. In later times, by contrast, many doubts accumulated from past eras, making it necessary to formulate and write down kalam to preserve correct beliefs. The author draws an analogy with fiqh: the Companions did not systematically codify fiqh into quarters, chapters, sections, or technical terms like al-naqd, al-qalb, al-jam', al-farq, tanqih al-manat, and takhrij. Yet no one considers later fiqh scholarship blameworthy. Similarly, the later development of kalam is not blameworthy. The author concludes that some innovations are good (bid'ah hasanah).

Key Terms
بدعة حسنة
الاصطلاحات
النقض
القلب
الجمع
الفرق
تنقيح المناط
تخريج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since the Companions did not practice kalam, it must be a forbidden innovation.
Correction: The author argues that their non-practice was due to unique circumstances; later necessity justifies it as a good innovation, just as with fiqh.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the Companions did not 'write down' kalam?
A) Because they lacked the intellectual ability.
B) Because it was forbidden by the Prophet.
C) Because their circumstances—purity, access to revelation, few opponents—made it unnecessary.
D) Because they were too busy with other sciences.

Key Takeaway
Even though the Companions did not engage in systematic kalam, later developments are justified by necessity and are a good innovation, just like the later codification of fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a beneficial Islamic practice or discipline (like systematic theology) as an 'innovation' without considering whether necessity made it praiseworthy? How will you evaluate such practices moving forward based on this lesson's criteria?
?
This passage argues that changed circumstances justify new approaches to preserve belief. What doubts or challenges in your own time require you to learn and apply kalam—and are you actively doing so, or are you neglecting it under the excuse that the early Muslims didn't do it?
?
The author uses the analogy with fiqh to show that later developments can be good. Think of an area in your own religious life where you resist change or new methods. Is that resistance truly rooted in preserving the sunnah, or is it perhaps an excuse to avoid the effort of learning? What would it cost you to embrace a good innovation that protects your creed?
