The Nature and Prohibition of Blameworthy Jadal
Defines blameworthy jadal and cites Quranic prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defining characteristics of blameworthy jadal and why it is unequivocally prohibited.

Core Idea
Blameworthy jadal is deceitful argumentation aimed at promoting falsehood and destroying truth.

Before we can appreciate the value of good argumentation, we must first recognize its corrupt counterpart. This lesson defines blameworthy jadal as a form of obstinate, deceptive reasoning that serves falsehood, and anchors its condemnation in explicit Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of blameworthy jadal
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how jadal is described as 'ta'annut' and 'lijaj' — obstinacy and stubbornness.
The definition includes the means: fabrication of corrupt doubts and misrepresentation.
2
Identify the three Quranic proofs
Find the three verses cited (Ghafir 5, Az-Zukhruf 58, Al-Hajj 3). Reflect on how each condemns argumentation without knowledge or with false motives.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes unequivocally that such jadal is prohibited. No difference of opinion exists on this.
This sets the stage for the contrast with praiseworthy jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان الجدل (تعنتاً ولجاجاً) بتلفيق الشبهات الفاسدة لترويج الآراء الباطلة، ودفع العقائد الحقة وإراءة الباطل في صورة الحق بالتلبيس والتدليس، كما قال تعالى: {وجادلوا بالباطل ليدحضوا به الحق} [غافر: ٥]، وقال تعالى: {بل هم قوم خصمون} [الزخرف: ٥٨]، وقال: {ومن الناس من يجادل في الله بغير علم} [الحج: ٣]، ومثل هذا الجدال لا نزاع في كونه منهياً عنه. وأما الجدال بالحق لإظهاره وإبطال الباطل فمأمور به، قال الله تعالى: {وجادلهم بالتي هي أحسن} [النحل: ١٢٥]، وقال تعالى: {ولا تجادلوا أهل الكتاب إلا بالتي هي أحسن} [العنكبوت: ٤٦]. ومجادلة الرسول ﷺ لابن الزبعرى وعلي للقدري مشهورة. روى أنه لما نزل قوله تعالى: {إنكم وما تعبدون من دون الله حصب جهنم} [الأنبياء: ٩٨] قال عبد الله بن الزبعرى: قد عبدت الملائكة والمسيح، أفتراهم يعذبون؟ فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: ما أجهلك بلغة قومك، أما علمت أن "ما" لما لا يعقل. وروي أيضاً أن شخصاً قال: إني أملك حركاتي وسكناتي وطلاق زوجتي وعتق أمتي، فقال علي رضي الله عنه: أتملكها دون الله أو مع الله؟ فإن قلت: أملكها دون الله فقد أثبت دون الله مالكاً، وإن قلت: أملكها مع الله فقد أثبت له شريكاً.
Jadal was [defined as] obstinacy and stubbornness by fabricating corrupt doubts to promote false opinions, repudiate true beliefs, and show falsehood in the form of truth through misrepresentation and deceit, as Allah said: {And they argued with falsehood to refute the truth thereby} [Ghafir: 5], and He said: {Nay, they are a contentious people} [Az-Zukhruf: 58], and He said: {And among the people is he who argues about Allah without knowledge} [Al-Hajj: 3]. Such jadal is undoubtedly prohibited. As for arguing with truth to manifest it and refute falsehood, it is commanded. Allah said: {And argue with them in a way that is best} [An-Nahl: 125], and He said: {And do not argue with the People of the Scripture except in a way that is best} [Al-Ankabut: 46]. The argumentation of the Messenger ﷺ with Ibn al-Ziba'ra and of Ali with al-Qadari are well-known. It is reported that when the verse {Indeed, you and what you worship besides Allah are fuel for Hell} [Al-Anbiya': 98] was revealed, Abdullah ibn al-Ziba'ra said: 'I have worshipped the angels and the Messiah, are they then to be punished?' He said: 'How ignorant you are of the language of your people! Do you not know that "ma" is for that which does not reason?' And it is also reported that a person said: 'I own my movements and my stillness, the divorce of my wife, and the emancipation of my slave-girl.' Ali said: 'Do you own them independently of Allah or with Allah? If you say, 'I own them independently of Allah,' you have affirmed an owner besides Allah. And if you say, 'I own them with Allah,' you have affirmed a partner for Him.'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two types of jadal (argumentation). It begins by characterizing blameworthy jadal as obstinacy and stubbornness (ta'annut and lijāj), involving the fabrication of corrupt doubts to promote falsehood and repudiate truth through deception. Three Quranic verses are cited to support its prohibition: Ghafir 5, Az-Zukhruf 58, and Al-Hajj 3, all condemning arguing without knowledge or with falsehood. The passage then affirms that argumentation for the sake of truth (jadal bil-haqq) is commanded, citing An-Nahl 125 and Al-Ankabut 46, which enjoin arguing in the best manner. Two prophetic examples illustrate this: the Prophet ﷺ refuting Ibn al-Ziba'ra's objection to the verse about idols being fuel for Hell by pointing out a linguistic nuance (that the word 'ma' refers to non-rational beings). The second example is Ali's response to a man claiming independent ownership of his actions, forcing him to avoid both shirk and dualism by showing that such claims imply either a false independent owner or a partner with Allah. The overall argument is that jadal is not inherently evil; its moral value depends on its purpose and method.

Key Terms
الجدل
التعنت
اللجاج
الشبهات الفاسدة
التلبيس
التدليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of argumentation are prohibited in Islam.
Correction: This passage specifies that blameworthy jadal is prohibited due to its deceitful and obstinate nature; argumentation for truth is later commended.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the defining features of blameworthy jadal?
A) Arguing with knowledge and good manners
B) Obstinacy, fabrication of doubts, and misrepresentation
C) Seeking truth and refuting falsehood
D) Debating with people of the Book

Key Takeaway
Blameworthy jadal is deceitful, obstinate argumentation that promotes falsehood and is firmly prohibited by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about religion, do you ever catch yourself arguing more to win than to seek truth? What would it take for you to abandon obstinacy and embrace humility, even if it means admitting you were wrong?
?
The passage says blameworthy jadal uses 'corrupt doubts' and 'misrepresentation.' Can you recall a time when you twisted facts or exaggerated to make your point? How might you repent from that and commit to honesty in future conversations?
?
Allah condemns arguing without knowledge. Is there a topic in religion you speak confidently about but haven't actually studied properly? Will you commit to learning from reliable sources before speaking again?

The Command to Argue with Truth
Defines praiseworthy jadal and cites Quranic commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that arguing for truth is not only permitted but commanded, provided it is done in the best manner.

Core Idea
Arguing for truth is a commanded act, done in the best way.

After learning about the condemned form of argumentation, we now turn to the praiseworthy counterpart. This lesson establishes that jadal is not inherently evil; when directed toward truth and conducted with excellence, it becomes an obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of praiseworthy jadal
Read the Arabic: 'wa amma al-jadal bil-haqq li-izharihi wa ibtal al-batil fa ma'murun bih'. Note the purpose: to manifest truth and refute falsehood.
The phrase 'fa ma'murun bih' indicates a command, not just permission.
2
Identify the two Quranic commands
Find the verses from An-Nahl 125 and Al-Ankabut 46. Notice the phrase 'billati hiya ahsan' — in the best manner.
This shows that the manner of argument is as important as the intent.
3
Reflect on the contrast with the previous lesson
Compare this with the blameworthy jadal. The key differences are purpose (truth vs falsehood) and method (best vs deceitful).

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in arguments about religion, do you consciously ensure you are arguing with truth and in the best way as commanded in this lesson? ?

?
The Quran commands arguing with truth even with the People of the Book. But how often do you avoid calling people to Islam because you fear argument or dislike confrontation? Is your avoidance a form of disobedience to this clear command? What is one step you will take this week to overcome that fear?

Prophetic Examples of Praiseworthy Argumentation
Provides two historical examples of praiseworthy argumentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the Prophet ﷺ and Imam Ali used sound reasoning and linguistic precision in debates to defend truth and correct misunderstandings.

Core Idea
The Prophet and Ali exemplify praiseworthy argumentation with clarity and wisdom.

The previous lesson established the principle of arguing for truth in the best manner. Now we encounter two vivid examples: the Prophet's response to a linguistic objection from Ibn al-Ziba'ra, and Ali's logical refutation of a man claiming independent ownership of his actions. These stories demonstrate how to argue correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the first story: Prophet and Ibn al-Ziba'ra
Read the Arabic and translation. Understand the objection: Ibn al-Ziba'ra thought the verse implied that angels and Jesus would be in Hell because they were worshipped.
The Prophet corrected him by pointing out that the word 'ma' refers to non-rational beings, so it doesn't include them.
2
Read the second story: Ali and the man
A man claimed to own his actions independently. Ali presented a logical dilemma: either independent of Allah (shirk) or with Allah (partnership). Both are false, showing the man's statement is invalid.
3
Extract the key method
Both arguments use precise reasoning: linguistic clarification in the first, logical disjunction in the second. Notice how they refute without aggression, using the opponent's own premise.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان الجدل (تعنتاً ولجاجاً) بتلفيق الشبهات الفاسدة لترويج الآراء الباطلة، ودفع العقائد الحقة وإراءة الباطل في صورة الحق بالتلبيس والتدليس، كما قال تعالى: {وجادلوا بالباطل ليدحضوا به الحق} [غافر: ٥]، وقال تعالى: {بل هم قوم خصمون} [الزخرف: ٥٨]، وقال: {ومن الناس من يجادل في الله بغير علم} [الحج: ٣]، ومثل هذا الجدال لا نزاع في كونه منهياً عنه. وأما الجدال بالحق لإظهاره وإبطال الباطل فمأمور به، قال الله تعالى: {وجادلهم بالتي هي أحسن} [النحل: ١٢٥]، وقال تعالى: {ولا تجادلوا أهل الكتاب إلا بالتي هي أحسن} [العنكبوت: ٤٦]. ومجادلة الرسول ﷺ لابن الزبعرى وعلي للقدري مشهورة. روى أنه لما نزل قوله تعالى: {إنكم وما تعبدون من دون الله حصب جهنم} [الأنبياء: ٩٨] قال عبد الله بن الزبعرى: قد عبدت الملائكة والمسيح، أفتراهم يعذبون؟ فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: ما أجهلك بلغة قومك، أما علمت أن "ما" لما لا يعقل. وروي أيضاً أن شخصاً قال: إني أملك حركاتي وسكناتي وطلاق زوجتي وعتق أمتي، فقال علي رضي الله عنه: أتملكها دون الله أو مع الله؟ فإن قلت: أملكها دون الله فقد أثبت دون الله مالكاً، وإن قلت: أملكها مع الله فقد أثبت له شريكاً.
Jadal was [defined as] obstinacy and stubbornness by fabricating corrupt doubts to promote false opinions, repudiate true beliefs, and show falsehood in the form of truth through misrepresentation and deceit, as Allah said: {And they argued with falsehood to refute the truth thereby} [Ghafir: 5], and He said: {Nay, they are a contentious people} [Az-Zukhruf: 58], and He said: {And among the people is he who argues about Allah without knowledge} [Al-Hajj: 3]. Such jadal is undoubtedly prohibited. As for arguing with truth to manifest it and refute falsehood, it is commanded. Allah said: {And argue with them in a way that is best} [An-Nahl: 125], and He said: {And do not argue with the People of the Scripture except in a way that is best} [Al-Ankabut: 46]. The argumentation of the Messenger ﷺ with Ibn al-Ziba'ra and of Ali with al-Qadari are well-known. It is reported that when the verse {Indeed, you and what you worship besides Allah are fuel for Hell} [Al-Anbiya': 98] was revealed, Abdullah ibn al-Ziba'ra said: 'I have worshipped the angels and the Messiah, are they then to be punished?' He said: 'How ignorant you are of the language of your people! Do you not know that "ma" is for that which does not reason?' And it is also reported that a person said: 'I own my movements and my stillness, the divorce of my wife, and the emancipation of my slave-girl.' Ali said: 'Do you own them independently of Allah or with Allah? If you say, 'I own them independently of Allah,' you have affirmed an owner besides Allah. And if you say, 'I own them with Allah,' you have affirmed a partner for Him.'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two types of jadal (argumentation). It begins by characterizing blameworthy jadal as obstinacy and stubbornness (ta'annut and lijāj), involving the fabrication of corrupt doubts to promote falsehood and repudiate truth through deception. Three Quranic verses are cited to support its prohibition: Ghafir 5, Az-Zukhruf 58, and Al-Hajj 3, all condemning arguing without knowledge or with falsehood. The passage then affirms that argumentation for the sake of truth (jadal bil-haqq) is commanded, citing An-Nahl 125 and Al-Ankabut 46, which enjoin arguing in the best manner. Two prophetic examples illustrate this: the Prophet ﷺ refuting Ibn al-Ziba'ra's objection to the verse about idols being fuel for Hell by pointing out a linguistic nuance (that the word 'ma' refers to non-rational beings). The second example is Ali's response to a man claiming independent ownership of his actions, forcing him to avoid both shirk and dualism by showing that such claims imply either a false independent owner or a partner with Allah. The overall argument is that jadal is not inherently evil; its moral value depends on its purpose and method.

المجادلة
اللجاج
التعنت

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet and Ali simply silenced their opponents without reasoning.
Correction: Both examples show careful reasoning: linguistic analysis in the first, logical disjunction in the second.

Checkpoint

C) By telling him he was ignorant of theology
D) By threatening him

Key Takeaway
Praiseworthy argumentation relies on precise language and logical reasoning, as shown by the Prophet and Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to 'own' something in your life—your time, your body, your decisions—do you truly believe it is independent of Allah? How does Ali's argument expose that as shirk?
?
The Prophet corrected Ibn al-Ziba'ra with precise language. In your next discussion about Islam, will you commit to being equally precise, or will you rely on vague arguments?
?
Reflect on a recent conversation where you defended your faith. Did you resort to emotional rhetoric or did you use clear, logical reasoning? What one change can you make today to argue more wisely?

Refuting the Hadith of the Old Women
Refutes objection from hadith of old women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from the hadith of old women is refuted by questioning its authenticity, reinterpreting its meaning, and noting its status as khabar ahad.

Core Idea
A weak hadith cannot override the rational obligation of nazar.

This passage presents the third objection against the obligation of nazar: the Prophet's command to follow the religion of old women, which seems to recommend blind taqlid. The response demonstrates three powerful lines of refutation — challenging authenticity, reinterpreting intent, and appealing to hadith methodology. This lesson will unpack each response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: the Prophet said 'Hold fast to the religion of old women.' Since old women cannot do nazar, their religion must be based on taqlid, so nazar should be abandoned. Identify the key claim.
Notice the assumption that nazar is impossible for them.
2
Examine the first refutation
The first response questions the authenticity of the hadith. It is not found in the authentic collections and may be the words of Sufyan al-Thawri. Reflect on how this undermines the objection.
This is a isnad-based critique.
3
Study the second and third refutations
Even if authentic, the hadith means tafwid (reliance on Allah) and submission, not blind taqlid. Moreover, it is a khabar ahad and cannot contradict definitive proofs. Consider how these arguments preserve the obligation of nazar.
The third refutation invokes the principle that khabar ahad cannot oppose qati' proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: "عليكم بدين العجائز" ولا شك أن دينهن بطريق التقليد، ومجرد الاعتقاد إذ لا قدرة لهن على النظر فيجب علينا الكف عنه. قلنا: إن صح الحديث أي لا نسلم صحته إذ لم يوجد في الكتب الصحاح، بل قيل: إنه من كلام سفيان الثوري فإنه روي أن عمرو بن عبيد من رؤساء المعتزلة قال: إن بين الكفر والإيمان منزلة بين المنزلتين فقالت عجوز: قال الله تعالى: {هو الذي خلقكم فمنكم كافر ومنكم مؤمن} [التغابن: ٢] فلم يجعل الله من عباده إلا الكافر والمؤمن، فبطل قولك فسمع سفيان كلامها فقال: عليكم بدين العجائز. وإن سلمنا صحته فالمراد به التفويض إلى الله سبحانه فيما قضاه وأمضاه والانقياد له فيما أمر به ونهى عنه لا النظر والاقتصار على مجرد التقليد. ثم إنه خبر آحاد لا يعارض القواطع.
And the third [objection] is his saying ﷺ: 'Hold fast to the religion of old women.' There is no doubt that their religion is by way of taqlid and mere belief, for they have no capability for nazar, so we must refrain from it. We said: If the hadith is authentic — meaning we do not concede its authenticity, for it is not found in the authentic books. Rather, it is said that it is from the speech of Sufyan al-Thawri, for it is reported that Amr ibn Ubayd, one of the heads of the Mu'tazila, said: 'Between disbelief and faith there is a station between the two stations.' An old woman said: 'Allah said: {He it is Who created you, and among you is the disbeliever and among you is the believer} [At-Taghabun: 2]. Thus Allah has placed among His servants only the disbeliever and the believer, so your statement is false.' Sufyan heard her words and said: 'Hold fast to the religion of old women.' If we grant its authenticity, the intended meaning is tafwid (reliance) to Allah regarding what He has decreed and executed, and submission to Him in what He commands and forbids — not nazar and restricting oneself to mere taqlid. Moreover, it is a khabar ahad that does not oppose definitive proofs.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the third objection against nazar, based on the hadith 'Hold fast to the religion of old women' (alaykum bi dini al-'aja'iz). The objection argues that old women can only practice taqlid (imitation) due to their inability to reason, and since the Prophet commands following their religion, nazar should be abandoned. The response unfolds in three steps. First, the authenticity of the hadith is questioned: it is not found in the Sahih collections, and the story suggests it originated from Sufyan al-Thawri commenting on an old woman's correct response to the Mu'tazilite Amr ibn Ubayd. Second, even if authentic, its meaning is tafwid (reliance on Allah) and submission, not the rejection of nazar or promotion of blind taqlid. Third, even if taken as a command to follow, it is a khabar ahad (solitary report) and cannot oppose definitive (qati') proofs that establish the obligation of nazar. The refutation protects the rational foundation of Islamic theology from a weak textual objection.

Key Terms
العجائز
التقليد
التفويض
خبر آحاد
القواطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'Hold fast to the religion of old women' commands blind imitation and prohibits using reason (nazar).
Correction: The hadith is weak in authenticity, its intended meaning is reliance on Allah (tafwid) and submission, and as a solitary report it cannot overrule definitive rational proofs.

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason given in the passage for rejecting the hadith of the old women as evidence against nazar?
A) It is a fabricated hadith
B) It is a solitary report (khabar ahad) that cannot oppose definitive proofs
C) It was abrogated by later revelations
D) It was spoken only in a specific historical context

Key Takeaway
The hadith of old women cannot be used to reject nazar because it is not authentic, its intended meaning is submission to Allah's decrees, and as a solitary report it cannot overrule definitive rational proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to examine the authenticity of every hadith you rely on, even if it supports your current beliefs? What would it mean to hold yourself accountable to the standard that only strong proofs should guide your faith?
?
When you face a difficult question about your religion, do you seek genuine understanding through reason and evidence, or do you take comfort in a 'religion of old women' that asks nothing of your intellect? How would your relationship with Allah change if you took up the burden of nazar?
?
You have been told to follow the religion of the old women. But are you ready to answer to Allah for every belief you hold without having examined it? If you have never truly investigated the rational foundations of your faith, what is holding you back?

Objection and Two Interpretations of Innate Syllogism
Commentator presents an objection based on innate syllogism and gives two interpretations to resolve a textual contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that reflection's obligation could be an innate syllogism and how the commentator resolves the apparent contradiction with two interpretations.

Core Idea
The objection claims reflection's obligation is an innate syllogism, but its conflict with needing premises leads to two interpretations.

The commentator begins by presenting an objection: the obligation of reflection might be known through an innate syllogism, requiring only a reminder. However, this creates a paradox because calling it 'innate' yet needing premises seems contradictory. In this lesson, we examine the objection and the two interpretive approaches that resolve this tension.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is framed and the problem identified.
The passage is dense; focus on the flow from objection to the two interpretations.
2
Identify the objection and its components
The objector suggests the obligation of reflection could be an innate syllogism. Find what that means and why it is problematic.
Innate syllogism (fitri al-qiyas) means a proposition whose proof is already present in the mind.
3
Understand the two interpretations
The commentator offers two ways to resolve the contradiction: the first modifies what the premises give, the second modifies the nature of the innate syllogism. Compare both.
Interpretation 1: premises give only conceptualisation. Interpretation 2: the judgment is like an innate syllogism after premises.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on why this distinction matters for the overall discussion on reflection's obligation. It sets up the solutions to come.
This lesson prepares for the next lesson on the actual solutions.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد السادس : في معرفة الله تعالى
أنظر فيتوقف كل واحد من وجود النظر مطلقاً، ووجوبه على الآخر. لا يقال : قد يكون وجوب النظر فطري القياس أي من القضايا التي قياساتها معها فيضع النبي للمكلف مقدمات ينساق ذهنه إليها بلا تكلف وتفيده العلم بذلك ضرورة فيكون الحكم بوجوب النظر ضرورياً محتاجاً إلى تنبيه على تلك المقدمات، أو نظرياً قريباً من الضروري محتاجاً إلى أدنى التفات يحصل طرفيه مع تلك المقدمات.
قوله : ( ما لم يجب) أي على عقلاً فإن ما ليس بواجب على عقلاً لا أقدم عليه.
قوله : ( لا يقال إلخ) منع لقوله ولا يجب ما لم أنظر، وهذا المنع وارد على تقدير كون الوجوب شرعياً أيضاً، إذ يمكن أن يقال : لا نسلم قوله ولا يثبت الشرع عندي ما لم أنظر لجواز كون ثبوت الشرع فطري القياس فيضع النبي مقدمات تفيد العلم بذلك ضرورة .

قوله : (ضرورة) أي قطعاً .
قوله: (فيكون الحكم بوجوب النظر إلخ) اعلم أن في المتن إشكالاً إذ كون الحكم بوجوب النظر فطري القياس ينافي إفادة المقدمات له فلا بد من صرفه عن الظاهر إما في إفادة المقدمات له، أو في كونه فطري القياس فالتوجيه الأول تصرف في الإفادة، بأن المراد بإفادته إياه أن المقدمات الموضوعة تفيد تصور طرفيه على وجه هو ملزوم للقياس الذي يحضر عند تصورهما، فكونه فطري القياس على ظاهره والثاني أعني قوله أو نظرياً قريباً من الضروري تصرف في كونه فطري القياس بأن المراد أنه كفطري القياس في أنه بعد إلقاء المقدمات المرتبة الموضوعة يحصل بأدنى التفات من غير احتياج إلى الفكر، لا فرق بينهما إلا بأن في فطري القياس لازم لتصور الطرفين وهاهنا مستفاد من خارج فافهم، فإنه قد خفي على أقوام .
قوله : (مع تلك المقدمات متعلق بتنبيه أي إلى تنبيه يحصل مع تلك المقدمات ولم يقل بتلك المقدمات لئلا يوهم اكتساب التصور من القياس.
قوله : (أو نظرياً) بالاستفادة من المقدمات الموضوعة معطوف على ضرورياً .
قوله : ( أي من القضايا التي قياساتها معها هذه القضايا محتاجة إلى تصور الطرفين على مناط الجزم بلا شبهة لتحصيل قياساتها معها، فقيل التصور على ذلك الوجه قد يحتاج إلى وضع مقدمات ينساق ذهن المكلف إليها، ولذلك قال : فيضع النبي عليه السلام إلخ، فلا يرد أن بمجرد التكلم بالمدعى يحصل قياسه معه، فأي حاجة إلى وضع المقدمات بل المفهوم من قول الشارح مع تلك المقدمات، أن المقدمات أيضاً قد تحتاج إلى التنبيه .

لأنا نقول كونه فطري القياس مع توقفه على ما ذكرتموه من المقدمات الدقيقة الأنظار باطل قطعاً، وعلى تقدير صحته بأن يكون هناك دليل آخر (له) للمكلف أن لا يستمع إليه أي إلى النبي، وكلامه الذي أراد به تنبيهه ولا يأثم بتركه أي بترك النظر أو الاستماع إذ لم يثبت بعد وجوب شيء أصلاً، فلا تمكن الدعوة وإثبات النبوة وهو المراد بالإفحام.
الوجه الثاني الحل وهو أن قولك لا يجب النظر على ما لم يثبت الشرع عندي قلنا : هذا إنما يصح لو كان عليه بحسب نفس الأمر موقوفاً على العلم بالوجوب المستفاد من العلم بثبوت الشرع لكنه لا يتوقف الوجوب في نفس الأمر على العلم به إذ العلم بالوجوب موقوف على الوجوب لأن العلم بثبوت شيء فرع لثبوته في نفسه، فإنه إذا لم يثبت في نفسه كان اعتقاد ثبوته جهلاً لا علماً فلو توقف الوجوب على العلم بالوجوب

قوله : (ولا يأثم بتركه قد يمنع ذلك بأن النظر وجوب النظر في المعجزة من الواجب العقلي أيضاً لدفع الخوف، وفيه تأمل.
قوله : (الوجه الثاني الحل إلخ لو فرض أن يقول المكلف حينئذ لا أنظر ما لم أصدق بوجوب النظر، على ولا أصدق بوجوبه ما لم يثبت الشرع، وثبوته إنما هو بالنظر فيتوقف كل منهما على الآخر لم يتجه هذا الحل بل الحل حينئذ أن قوله لا أنظر ما لم أصدق باطل.
قوله : ( لكنه لا يتوقف الوجوب في نفس الأمر على العلم به لا يقال : لو لم يتوقف الوجوب على العلم به لزم تكليف الناس بما لا يعلمونه لأنا نقول : اللازم ملتزم فكم من واجب لا يعذر فيه بالجهل، وإنما مدار الوجوب الإمكان القريب للعلم به وقد يقال : عدم التوقف مسلم لكن لا يتم إلزام النظر حينئذ، لأنه يقول حينئذ سلمت أن الوجوب لا يتوقف على العلم بالوجوب، إلا أني لا أنظر ما لم أعلم الوجوب لأن ترك الواجب بدون العلم لا يوجب الإثم، والقول بأن الجهل ليس بعذر إنما هو لكون الدار دار التكليف وشيوع أحكام الشرع فيها وهو لم يثبت بعد
The Fifth Observatory - Sixth Purpose: On Knowledge of God the Exalted. Consider: each of the existence of reflection absolutely and its obligation depends on the other. One cannot say: Perhaps the obligation of reflection is an innate syllogism (fitri al-qiyas), i.e., of the propositions whose syllogisms are with them. So the prophet places premises for the responsible person, his mind flowing towards them without effort, and they give him knowledge of that (i.e., the obligation of reflection) necessarily. So the judgment of the obligation of reflection is necessary, needing a reminder of those premises, or theoretical close to the necessary, needing the slightest attention, its two sides being obtained with those premises. His statement: (what is not obligatory) i.e., rationally, because what is not rationally obligatory I do not undertake. His statement: (one cannot say, etc.) is a denial of his statement 'and it is not obligatory as long as I have not reflected.' And this denial applies even if the obligation is considered legal, because it can be said: we do not grant his statement 'and the law is not established for me as long as I have not reflected' due to the possibility that the establishment of the law is an innate syllogism, so the prophet places premises that give knowledge of that necessarily. His statement: (his mind flows towards them without effort) because they are close to necessities. His statement: (necessarily) i.e., certainly. His statement: (so the judgment of the obligation of reflection, etc.) Know that in the text there is a problem: the judgment of the obligation of reflection being an innate syllogism contradicts the premises giving it. So it must be diverted from the apparent meaning: either regarding the premises giving it, or regarding it being an innate syllogism. So the first interpretation is a modification in the giving: that what is meant by its giving him is that the placed premises give a conceptualization of its two sides in a way that is the necessary condition for the syllogism that is present when they are conceptualized, so being an innate syllogism is taken literally. The second, i.e., his statement 'or theoretical close to the necessary', is a modification in its being an innate syllogism, meaning that it is like an innate syllogism in that after the ordered premises are presented, it is obtained with the slightest attention without needing thought. There is no difference between them except that in the innate syllogism it is a necessary consequence of conceptualizing the two sides, while here it is obtained from outside. Understand, for it has been hidden from many. His statement: (with those premises) is related to 'a reminder', i.e., to a reminder that occurs with those premises, and he did not say 'by those premises' to avoid suggesting that the conceptualization is acquired from the syllogism. His statement: (or theoretical) i.e., by deriving benefit from the placed premises, is coordinated with 'necessary'. His statement: (i.e., of the propositions whose syllogisms are with them) These propositions need conceptualization of the two sides based on the basis of certainty without doubt to obtain their syllogisms with them. So it is said: conceptualization in that way may need the placement of premises that the responsible person's mind flows towards. Therefore he said: 'So the prophet places, peace be upon him, etc.' So it is not objected that merely by uttering the claim, its syllogism is obtained with it, so what need is there to place premises? Rather, the understanding from the commentator's statement 'with those premises' is that the premises may also need reminding. His statement: (or theoretical close to the necessary) if it is coordinated with 'necessary' as apparent, it is an indication that the innate syllogism is theoretical according to some, or that the mentioned theoretical is more general, whether it is real or judgmental. And if it is coordinated with his statement 'innate syllogism' as it is more appropriate, then the matter is clearer. For we say: it being an innate syllogism while depending on the subtle premises you mentioned is absolutely false. And assuming its correctness, that there is another proof, he (the responsible person) has the right not to listen to him (i.e., the prophet) and his speech by which he intended to remind him, and he does not sin by abandoning it (i.e., by abandoning reflection or listening), since no obligation has been established at all. So the call and proof of prophethood are not possible, and that is what is meant by silencing (ifham). The second aspect, the solution, is that your statement 'reflection is not obligatory as long as the law has not been established for me' — we say: this is only valid if it were, in itself, dependent on knowledge of the obligation derived from knowledge of the establishment of the law. But the obligation in itself does not depend on knowledge of it, because knowledge of the obligation depends on the obligation, since knowledge of something's existence is a branch of its existence in itself. For if it is not established in itself, then believing it to be established is ignorance, not knowledge. So if obligation depended on knowledge of obligation... etc. (marginal commentary continues).
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The commentator first states an objection: if the obligation of reflection (nazar) for knowing God were an innate syllogism (fitri al-qiyas) — meaning a proposition whose demonstration is always already present in the mind — then the prophet would only need to place premises that are immediately grasped. But then the judgment of obligation would be either necessary or close to necessary, requiring only a slight reminder. The commentator explains each phrase of the objection, noting that the denial (man') applies even if obligation is considered legal. He then highlights a problem: the text seems contradictory because calling it an innate syllogism yet needing premises for its acquisition is inconsistent. He resolves this with two interpretations: the first treats the premises as giving only conceptualization, not the judgment itself; the second treats the judgment as 'like' an innate syllogism after premises are presented. The first solution (al-man') then argues that the objection's premise is false: such an innate syllogism with subtle premises is impossible, and even if it existed, it would not compel action before obligation is established, thus undermining the prophet's mission. The second solution (al-hall) directly addresses the circularity: the objector claims reflection cannot be obligatory until its obligation is known, but this confuses the dependency. Obligation in itself does not depend on knowledge of it; rather, knowledge of obligation depends on the obligation itself. The marginal commentary elaborates, refuting a counter-argument that ignorance would excuse inaction by pointing out that many obligations are known only after reflection, and the lack of blame for ignorance depends on the context of revelation, which is not yet established.

Key Terms
فطري القياس
تناقض
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Innate syllogism cannot involve any premises; it is entirely self-evident.
Correction: The commentator shows that 'innate syllogism' can still require premises for conceptualization of terms, preserving its innate character.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation of the innate syllogism, what do the placed premises provide?
A) The judgment of obligation itself
B) Only the conceptualization of the two sides of the judgment
C) The proof of the prophet's truthfulness
D) The necessity of reflection

Key Takeaway
The two interpretations show how 'innate syllogism' can still require premises for conceptualization, preserving the necessity of reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a seeming contradiction between your innate sense of God's existence and the need for deliberate reflection, do you tend to ignore the tension or, like the commentator, dig deeper to find a resolution? What does your response reveal about your commitment to true understanding?
?
The passage shows that even 'innate' knowledge can require effort to conceptualize. Have you ever assumed your faith was effortless, only to find it challenged? What premises have you neglected to think through? Will you today identify one premise about Allah that you have accepted without reflection and actively seek to understand it?
?
The two interpretations both affirm the necessity of premises given by the prophet. Where in your own life have you dismissed the need for prophetic guidance, thinking your own intellect sufficient? How does this lesson call you to humbly receive the 'premises' of revelation?

Two Solutions: Denial and Direct Argument
Commentator presents two solutions to the objection: denial of innate syllogism's efficacy and direct resolution of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two solutions the commentator offers to counter the objection: denial of the innate syllogism's effectiveness and the direct argument that obligation does not depend on knowledge of it.

Core Idea
The obligation of reflection does not require prior knowledge of its obligation; it is the other way around.

After addressing the textual issue, the commentator turns to the substantive objection. Two solutions are presented: the first denies that the innate syllogism can work given its subtle premises, and the second resolves the circularity by clarifying the dependency between obligation and knowledge of obligation. This lesson unpacks both.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the two solutions
Read from 'For we say: it being an innate syllogism...' to the end. Identify the two distinct responses.
The first solution is labeled 'al-man'' (denial) and the second 'al-hall' (solution).
2
Analyze the first solution (man')
The commentator argues that an innate syllogism depending on subtle premises is impossible. Even if it were possible, it would not compel action, undermining the prophet's mission.
This solution attacks the feasibility of the objection's proposed mechanism.
3
Analyze the second solution (hall)
The objector claims obligation depends on knowledge of it, leading to circularity. The commentator refutes this: obligation in itself does not depend on knowledge; rather, knowledge depends on obligation.
This is a logical move that breaks the cycle.
4
Examine the counter-argument and its refutation
A counter-argument says ignorance would excuse inaction. The commentator responds that many obligations are known only after reflection, and the lack of blame depends on the context of revelation.
This reinforces the need for reflection despite initial ignorance.

The Fifth Observatory - Sixth Purpose: On Knowledge of God the Exalted. Consider: each of the existence of reflection absolutely and its obligation depends on the other. One cannot say: Perhaps the obligation of reflection is an innate syllogism (fitri al-qiyas), i.e., of the propositions whose syllogisms are with them. So the prophet places premises for the responsible person, his mind flowing towards them without effort, and they give him knowledge of that (i.e., the obligation of reflection) necessarily. So the judgment of the obligation of reflection is necessary, needing a reminder of those premises, or theoretical close to the necessary, needing the slightest attention, its two sides being obtained with those premises. His statement: (what is not obligatory) i.e., rationally, because what is not rationally obligatory I do not undertake. His statement: (one cannot say, etc.) is a denial of his statement 'and it is not obligatory as long as I have not reflected.' And this denial applies even if the obligation is considered legal, because it can be said: we do not grant his statement 'and the law is not established for me as long as I have not reflected' due to the possibility that the establishment of the law is an innate syllogism, so the prophet places premises that give knowledge of that necessarily. His statement: (his mind flows towards them without effort) because they are close to necessities. His statement: (necessarily) i.e., certainly. His statement: (so the judgment of the obligation of reflection, etc.) Know that in the text there is a problem: the judgment of the obligation of reflection being an innate syllogism contradicts the premises giving it. So it must be diverted from the apparent meaning: either regarding the premises giving it, or regarding it being an innate syllogism. So the first interpretation is a modification in the giving: that what is meant by its giving him is that the placed premises give a conceptualization of its two sides in a way that is the necessary condition for the syllogism that is present when they are conceptualized, so being an innate syllogism is taken literally. The second, i.e., his statement 'or theoretical close to the necessary', is a modification in its being an innate syllogism, meaning that it is like an innate syllogism in that after the ordered premises are presented, it is obtained with the slightest attention without needing thought. There is no difference between them except that in the innate syllogism it is a necessary consequence of conceptualizing the two sides, while here it is obtained from outside. Understand, for it has been hidden from many. His statement: (with those premises) is related to 'a reminder', i.e., to a reminder that occurs with those premises, and he did not say 'by those premises' to avoid suggesting that the conceptualization is acquired from the syllogism. His statement: (or theoretical) i.e., by deriving benefit from the placed premises, is coordinated with 'necessary'. His statement: (i.e., of the propositions whose syllogisms are with them) These propositions need conceptualization of the two sides based on the basis of certainty without doubt to obtain their syllogisms with them. So it is said: conceptualization in that way may need the placement of premises that the responsible person's mind flows towards. Therefore he said: 'So the prophet places, peace be upon him, etc.' So it is not objected that merely by uttering the claim, its syllogism is obtained with it, so what need is there to place premises? Rather, the understanding from the commentator's statement 'with those premises' is that the premises may also need reminding. His statement: (or theoretical close to the necessary) if it is coordinated with 'necessary' as apparent, it is an indication that the innate syllogism is theoretical according to some, or that the mentioned theoretical is more general, whether it is real or judgmental. And if it is coordinated with his statement 'innate syllogism' as it is more appropriate, then the matter is clearer. For we say: it being an innate syllogism while depending on the subtle premises you mentioned is absolutely false. And assuming its correctness, that there is another proof, he (the responsible person) has the right not to listen to him (i.e., the prophet) and his speech by which he intended to remind him, and he does not sin by abandoning it (i.e., by abandoning reflection or listening), since no obligation has been established at all. So the call and proof of prophethood are not possible, and that is what is meant by silencing (ifham). The second aspect, the solution, is that your statement 'reflection is not obligatory as long as the law has not been established for me' — we say: this is only valid if it were, in itself, dependent on knowledge of the obligation derived from knowledge of the establishment of the law. But the obligation in itself does not depend on knowledge of it, because knowledge of the obligation depends on the obligation, since knowledge of something's existence is a branch of its existence in itself. For if it is not established in itself, then believing it to be established is ignorance, not knowledge. So if obligation depended on knowledge of obligation... etc. (marginal commentary continues).
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The commentator first states an objection: if the obligation of reflection (nazar) for knowing God were an innate syllogism (fitri al-qiyas) — meaning a proposition whose demonstration is always already present in the mind — then the prophet would only need to place premises that are immediately grasped. But then the judgment of obligation would be either necessary or close to necessary, requiring only a slight reminder. The commentator explains each phrase of the objection, noting that the denial (man') applies even if obligation is considered legal. He then highlights a problem: the text seems contradictory because calling it an innate syllogism yet needing premises for its acquisition is inconsistent. He resolves this with two interpretations: the first treats the premises as giving only conceptualization, not the judgment itself; the second treats the judgment as 'like' an innate syllogism after premises are presented. The first solution (al-man') then argues that the objection's premise is false: such an innate syllogism with subtle premises is impossible, and even if it existed, it would not compel action before obligation is established, thus undermining the prophet's mission. The second solution (al-hall) directly addresses the circularity: the objector claims reflection cannot be obligatory until its obligation is known, but this confuses the dependency. Obligation in itself does not depend on knowledge of it; rather, knowledge of obligation depends on the obligation itself. The marginal commentary elaborates, refuting a counter-argument that ignorance would excuse inaction by pointing out that many obligations are known only after reflection, and the lack of blame for ignorance depends on the context of revelation, which is not yet established.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Obligation Does Not Depend on Knowledge
Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the second solution to the objection about reflection's obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation of reflection does not depend on the mukallaf's prior knowledge, and how this resolves objections about circularity and the disbeliever's case.

Core Idea
Obligation is grounded in reality, not in the mukallaf's awareness of it.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a key objection: how can reflection be obligatory before the law is known? The answer lies in distinguishing between obligation in itself and knowledge of it. By showing that obligation depends on the existence of the law in the divine decree, not on human awareness, the argument avoids circularity and explains why even the heedless can be accountable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by stating the objection and then refutes it.
2
Identify the core distinction
The key move is distinguishing between 'obligation in reality' (wujūb fī nafs al-amr) and 'knowledge of obligation'. Find where the author makes this distinction.
This distinction breaks the circularity.
3
Understand the circularity argument
If obligation depended on knowledge, then one would need knowledge of obligation before obligation exists, creating a vicious circle. Also, nothing would be obligatory for a disbeliever who lacks this knowledge.
The author uses the term 'dawr' (circularity).
4
Apply the solution to both cases
The same solution works for the original objector (who doubts the law) and for the Mu'tazila (who claim reflection is not obligatory before reflecting). The condition is ability to know, not actual knowledge.
The text says: 'sharṭ al-taklīf huwa al-tamakkun min al-ilm bihi lā al-ilm bihi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثاني) الحل وهو (أن قولك لا يجب ) النظر ( على ما لم يثبت الشرع) عندي ( قلنا : هذا إنما يصح لو كان عليه ) بحسب نفس الأمر ( موقوفاً على العلم بالوجوب ) المستفاد من العلم بثبوت الشرع ( لكنه لا يتوقف ) الوجوب في نفس الأمر على العلم به ( إذ العلم بالوجوب موقوف على الوجوب ) لزم الدور ولزم أيضاً أن لا يجب شيء على الكافر، بل نقول : الوجوب في نفس الأمر يتوقف على ثبوت الشرع في نفس الأمر والشرع ثابت في نفس الأمر علم المكلف ثبوته أو لم يعلم نظر فيه أو لم ينظر وكذلك الوجوب، وليس يلزم هذا تكليف من الغافل، لأن الغافل من لم يتصور التكليف لا من لم يصدق به كما مر، وهذا معنى قيل أن شرط التكليف هو التمكن من العلم به لا العلم به، وبهذا الحل أيضاً يندفع الإشكال عن المعتزلة، فيقال : قولك لا يجب النظر على ما لم أنظر باطل، لأن الوجوب ثابت بالعقل في نفس الأمر ولا يتوقف على علم المكلف بالوجوب والنظر فيه .
The second aspect is the solution: that your statement 'reflection is not obligatory' (based on the fact that the law has not been established according to me) – we say: this is only valid if it (i.e., obligation in reality) were dependent on knowledge of obligation derived from knowledge of the establishment of the law. But obligation in reality does not depend on knowledge of it, because knowledge of obligation depends on obligation. Thus circularity would follow, and also that nothing would be obligatory upon the disbeliever. Rather we say: obligation in reality depends on the establishment of the law in reality, and the law is established in reality whether the accountable person (al-mukallaf) knows its establishment or does not know, whether he reflects on it or does not reflect, and similarly with obligation. And this does not entail imposing obligation on the heedless (al-ghafil), because the heedless is one who does not conceive of obligation, not one who does not believe in it, as previously mentioned. This is the meaning of the saying that the condition of obligation is the ability to know it (al-tamakkun min al-ilm bihi), not actual knowledge. With this solution, the problem for the Mu'tazila is also repelled, so it is said: your statement 'reflection is not obligatory as long as I have not reflected' is false, because obligation is established by reason in reality and does not depend on the accountable person's knowledge of obligation and reflection upon it.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents the second solution to a common objection in kalām about the obligation of reflection (naẓar). The objector says that reflection is not obligatory before the law is established, because obligation itself depends on knowledge of the law. The commentator responds by distinguishing between obligation in itself (wujūb fī nafs al-amr) and knowledge of it. He argues that if obligation depended on knowledge, then knowledge would depend on obligation, leading to circularity (dawr). Moreover, obligation would never apply to a disbeliever who does not know the law. In reality, obligation is grounded in the existence of the law in itself, regardless of the mukallaf's awareness. The condition for obligation is not actual knowledge but the ability to acquire it (al-tamakkun min al-ilm). This solution also refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that reflection is not obligatory before the mukallaf has reflected, by affirming that obligation is fixed by reason and does not require the mukallaf's prior knowledge or reflection.

Key Terms
الوجوب
العلم بالوجوب
النظر
الشرع
الدور
التكليف
الغافل
التمكن من العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that obligation requires the mukallaf's actual knowledge.
Correction: The author shows that only the ability to know is required, not actual knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the second solution, what is the condition for a wājib to be binding on the mukallaf?
A) Actual knowledge of the obligation
B) Ability to know the obligation
C) Having already performed reflection
D) Acceptance of the law

Key Takeaway
Obligation is fixed by the reality of the law, not by the mukallaf's knowledge; the only condition is the ability to know the obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have been using ignorance as an excuse to delay certain duties. But the passage teaches that obligation is real whether you know it or not. What is one specific Islamic duty you have been avoiding by claiming you didn't know, and what step will you take today to remove that ignorance?
?
The author says the condition is not actual knowledge but the ability to know. Is there any area of your faith where you have the ability to find out the truth but you choose to remain unaware? What is keeping you from seeking knowledge, and what will you commit to doing about it this week?
?
The heedless is defined as one who does not even conceive of the obligation, not one who rejects it. Look at your daily life: Are there obligations of faith you haven't even considered? For example, are you neglecting to learn about God's commands simply because you don't think about them? What will you do to make yourself aware?

Disagreement on the First Obligation of the Mukallaf
Sharh al-Mawaqif enumerates four opinions on the first obligation of the mukallaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four major opinions on the first obligation of the mukallaf and the rationale behind each.

Core Idea
The first duty of a responsible person is debated—knowledge, reflection, its first part, or intention.

In this passage, the author of Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the classical disagreement on what constitutes the very first obligation (awwal wājib) upon every accountable person (mukallaf). This debate is foundational for understanding how one begins the path to knowing God. The passage outlines four distinct positions held by major theological schools, each with its own reasoning. By studying these, you'll see how different scholars prioritize the initial step in the quest for divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text once, then its literal translation. Let the author's own wording introduce you to the debate.
Notice the use of parentheses in the Arabic—they enclose explanatory additions.
2
Identify the four opinions
Scan the passage and list the four different views on the first obligation. Mark which scholars or groups are associated with each.
The opinions are introduced with 'فالأكثر', 'وقيل', 'وقيل', 'وقال القاضي'.
3
Understand the reasoning for each
For each opinion, note the argument given. Ask yourself: Why does each group think their candidate is the first duty?
The reasons range from foundational importance to logical entailment.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this debate affects one's understanding of the path to knowledge of God. Does it change how you view the role of reflection and intention?
This connects to the larger question of human responsibility and the means to acquire religious knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع : اختلف في أول واجب على المكلف ( قد اختلف في أول واجب على المكلف ( أنه ماذا ( فالأكثر) ومنهم الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري ) على أنه معرفة الله تعالى إذ هو أصل المعارف ) والعقائد ( الدينية وعليه يتفرع وجوب كل واجب ) من الواجبات الشرعية (وقيل: هو النظر فيها ) أي في معرفة الله سبحانه ( لأنه واجب ( اتفاقاً كما مر ( وهو قبلها ) وهذا مذهب جمهور المعتزلة والأستاذ أبي إسحاق الإسفرائيني ( وقيل : ( هو ( أول جزء من النظر) لأن وجوب الكل يستلزم وجوب أجزائه، فأول جزء من النظر واجب، وهو متقدم على النظر المتقدم على المعرفة ) وقال القاضي : واختاره ابن فورك) وإمام الحرمين أنه [القصد] .
The seventh treatise: They disagreed on the first obligation upon the accountable person (al-mukallaf) – what is it? The majority, including the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, hold that it is knowledge of God Almighty, because it is the foundation of religious knowledge and beliefs, and upon it branches the obligation of every obligatory act from the legal obligations. And it was said: it is reflection (al-naẓar) concerning it – i.e., concerning knowledge of God – because it is obligatory by consensus, as previously mentioned, and it precedes it. This is the doctrine of the majority of the Mu'tazila and al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini. And it was said: it is the first part of reflection, because the obligation of the whole entails the obligation of its parts, so the first part of reflection is obligatory, and it precedes reflection that precedes knowledge. And al-Qadi, followed by Ibn Furak and Imam al-Haramayn, said that it is the intention (al-qaṣd) [to reflect].
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage introduces the seventh treatise (al-maqṣad al-sābiʿ) on the first obligation (awwal wājib) upon the mukallaf. It presents four opinions without deciding among them. First, the majority, including al-Ash'ari, hold that the first obligation is knowledge of God (maʿrifat Allāh), because it is the foundation of all religious knowledge and obligations. Second, the majority of the Mu'tazila and Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini hold that it is reflection (naẓar) directed towards attaining knowledge of God, since reflection is obligatory by consensus and precedes knowledge. Third, some hold that it is the very first part of reflection (awwal juzʾ min al-naẓar), arguing that the obligation of the whole entails the obligation of its parts. Fourth, al-Qadi, Ibn Furak, and Imam al-Haramayn hold that it is the intention (qaṣd) to reflect, which is a precondition for the act of reflection itself. The passage thus maps the key positions in the classical debate on the initial duty of the mukallaf in acquiring knowledge of God.

Key Terms
أول واجب
معرفة الله
النظر
أول جزء من النظر
القصد
المكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the first obligation is simply to believe without reflection, ignoring the role of intellectual effort.
Correction: The passage shows that many scholars prescribe reflection or its intention as the first duty, emphasizing that knowledge of God requires a preceding act of thought or intention.

Checkpoint
Which group is reported to hold that the first obligation upon the mukallaf is the intention (qaṣd) to reflect?
A) The majority, including al-Ash'ari
B) The Mu'tazila and Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini
C) Al-Qadi, Ibn Furak, and Imam al-Haramayn
D) Those who hold it is the first part of reflection

Key Takeaway
The first obligation of the mukallaf is a debated issue with at least four major positions: knowledge, reflection, its first part, or intention, each supported by distinct reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you wake up each day, do you consciously remind yourself that knowing God is your foundational duty—or do you treat other obligations as more urgent? What would change if you prioritized that knowledge first?
?
The scholars debated whether the first duty is knowledge, reflection, or intention. Which of these do you find yourself neglecting most? What is one concrete step you can take today to act on that duty?
?
If even the first part of reflection is an obligation, does that mean your very first thought about God this morning was an act of worship—or have you missed it? How can you cultivate that first thought intentionally?

Imam al-Razi's Statement and the Verbal Nature of the Disagreement
Commentator clarifies al-Razi's view and shows disagreement is verbal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dispute about the first obligation is considered verbal (lafẓī) and how Imam al-Razi's analysis resolves it.

Core Idea
The disagreement about the first obligation is about definitions, not substance.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a statement by Imam al-Razi that reframes the entire debate about the first obligation. Rather than a substantive disagreement, al-Razi shows that the three schools differ only in their understanding of what it means for an act to be 'within one's power' (maqdūr) and what is meant by 'first intention' (al-qaṣd al-awwal). This insight transforms the debate into a verbal dispute.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the dispute
The three schools debate what the first obligation is. This passage begins by showing that al-Razi's comment reveals the dispute is merely verbal, not substantive.
Notice the commentator's opening statement.
2
Examine 'intended by the first intention'
Learn what it means for something to be intended primarily (by first intention) versus secondarily. Understand how this applies to inquiry and knowledge.
3
Compare the two views on knowledge being within power
One group (Mu'tazila) considers knowledge within power via mediation; another (Ash'ariyya) defines 'within power' strictly without mediation, excluding knowledge.
This shows how differing definitions lead to apparent disagreement.
4
See al-Razi's conclusion
Al-Razi shows that intention to inquire is intended secondarily, and since will attaches to it, it must be within power. Thus the disagreement is about definitions.
This clinches the verbal nature of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قال الإمام الرازي إلخ) بيان لكون النزاع بين المذاهب الثلاثة لفظياً، كون الواجب غير مقدور وتزييف لما ذكره المصنف من كون القصد غير مقدور . قوله : (المقصودة بالقصد (الأول) أي لا يكون مقصوداً بالتبع سواء كان وسيلة إلى واجب آخر كالنظر أولا كالمعرفة . قوله : ( عند من يجعلها مقدورة) لأن المقدور عنده ما يتمكن من فعله وتركه بلا واسطة أو بواسطة .. قوله : (عند من لا يجعل إلخ) لأن المقدور عنده ما يتمكن من فعله وتركه بلا واسطة، والعلم ليس كذلك فإنه قبل النظر ممتنع الحصول وبعده واجب الحصول . قوله : ( كيف كانت سواء كانت مقصودة بالذات أو بالتبع، فجعل الإمام القصد إلى النظر مقصوداً بالتبع، فعلم أنه مقدور إذ غير المقدور لا تتعلق به الإرادة .
His statement: 'Imam al-Razi said, etc.' is an explanation that the dispute among the three schools is verbal, that the obligatory act is not within power (ghayr maqdūr), and a critique of what the author mentioned that intention (al-qaṣd) is not within power. His statement: 'Intended by the first intention' means it is not intended secondarily, whether it is a means to another obligation like inquiry or the like, or knowledge. His statement: 'According to the one who deems it within power' because what is within power according to him is what one can do or leave without mediation or with mediation. His statement: 'According to the one who does not deem it within power, etc.' because what is within power according to him is what one can do or leave without mediation, and knowledge is not like that, for it is impossible to obtain before inquiry and necessarily obtained after it. His statement: 'Howsoever it may be, whether intended essentially or secondarily, so Imam [Razi] made the intention to inquire intended secondarily, thus it is known that it is within power, for that which is not within power cannot be attached to will (irāda).
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a statement by Imam al-Razi, explaining the nature of the disagreement about the first obligation (awwal wājib). The commentator first notes that al-Razi's statement demonstrates that the dispute between the three schools (Ash‘ariyya, Mu‘tazila, and others) is merely verbal (lafẓī). The key point is whether the obligatory act must be within one's power (maqdūr). The author of the text (al-Ījī) had considered intention (qaṣd) as not within power; al-Razi refutes this. The commentator then explains phrases:

- 'Intended by the first intention' (al-maqṣūda bi’l-qaṣd al-awwal): that which is intended primarily, not secondarily, whether it is a means (like inquiry) or an end (like knowledge).
- 'According to the one who deems it (knowledge) within power' (man yaj‘alihā maqdūra): i.e., the Mu‘tazila who hold that knowledge is an act of the servant. For them, 'within power' means being able to do or leave an action directly or indirectly.
- 'According to the one who does not deem it within power' (man lā yaj‘al): i.e., the Ash‘ariyya, for whom 'within power' means doing or leaving without any medium. Since knowledge is impossible before inquiry and necessary after it, it is not within power.
- 'Howsoever it may be' (kayfa kānat): whether essential or secondary. Al-Razi holds that intention to inquire is intended secondarily, and since will (irāda) attaches to it, it must be within power (non-maqdūr cannot be willed). Thus the disagreement is about definitions, not substance.

Key Terms
التحريف
النزاع اللفظي
الواجب
المقدور
القصد الأول
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the three schools fundamentally disagree on the first obligation, with one being correct and others wrong.
Correction: The passage shows the disagreement is merely verbal: it hinges on definitions of 'first intention' and 'within power'. Understanding this resolves the apparent conflict.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the dispute among the three schools is verbal?
A) Because they all agree on the same obligation.
B) Because the disagreement is about definitions (what 'first' and 'within power' mean).
C) Because none of them provide evidence.
D) Because the disagreement is only about knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The dispute about the first obligation is resolved by understanding that intention is within power, making it a verbal disagreement about definitions of 'first' and 'within power'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intend an action, do you truly feel that your intention is within your power, or do you sometimes feel it arises beyond your control? How does al-Razi's argument that will attaches to intention challenge you to take responsibility for your intentions?
?
The passage defines 'within power' in two ways. Which definition resonates more with your own experience of making choices? Do you consider the means as part of what you can do, or only the direct act? How does this affect your understanding of what Allah expects of you?
?
The dispute among scholars is revealed to be verbal. Are there areas in your own life where you elevate disagreements over definitions into fundamental conflicts? How can you apply this lesson to seek unity in understanding the essence of obligations?

Delaying Obligatory Reasoning Without Excuse
Al-Jurjani rules on delaying obligatory reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that delaying obligatory reasoning without a valid excuse incurs sin even if the obligation later becomes impossible to fulfill.

Core Idea
Negligence in delaying is sinful, regardless of outcome.

Al-Jurjani addresses a subtle point: what if someone delays obligatory reasoning without excuse and then dies before fulfilling it? He uses a powerful analogy to show that the sin lies in the negligence, not the outcome. This lesson unpacks that ruling and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic; they are from the original text.
2
Understand the ruling and the analogy
Identify the main ruling: delaying obligatory reasoning without excuse makes one disobedient. Then examine the analogy of the woman who breaks her fast after becoming pure, only to menstruate again. Ask why she is still sinful despite the impossibility.
The analogy illustrates that the sin is for the delay itself, not the outcome.
3
Consider the discussion on intention and knowledge
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between reasoning (requires time) and intention (not time-bound). He suggests that the intention to reason is part of the act of reasoning itself, not a separate obligation. Reflect on why this matters.
This addresses a potential objection: if intention were a separate obligation, it would lead to an infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطعاً، وأما إذا لم يشرع فيه بل أخره بلا عذر ومات ( ففيه احتمال والأظهر عصيانه ) لتقصيره ،بالتأخير وإن تبين عدم اتساع الزمان لتحصيل الواجب ( كالمرأة تصبح طاهرة فتفطر ثم تحيض) في ذلك اليوم ) فإنها عاصية وإن ظهر أنها لم يمكنها إتمام الصوم وإنما خص الفرع بالنظر لاقتضائه زماناً يتأتى فيه التفصيل الذي ذكره بخلاف القصد، وأما المعرفة فالشروع فيها راجع إلى الشروع في النظر، وقد يقال : في هذا التخصيص إيماء إلى أنه المختار فإن القصد إلى النظر من تتمته كيف ولو جعل واجباً برأسه وجب أن يقصد إلى تحصيله، ولزم أن يكون القصد مسبوقاً بقصد آخر.
Absolutely, and if he does not begin it but delays it without excuse and dies, then there is a possibility, and the apparent view is that he is disobedient due to his negligence in delaying, even if it becomes clear that the time was insufficient to fulfill the obligation. Like a woman who becomes pure (from menses) in the morning, then breaks her fast, then menstruates that day: she is disobedient even if it becomes apparent that she could not have completed the fast. The subsidiary case is specified to reasoning because it requires a time in which the mentioned detail can be realized, unlike the intention. As for knowledge, beginning it is referable to beginning reasoning. And it is said: in this specification there is an indication that it is the chosen view, for the intention to reason is part of its completion; how? If it were made an independent obligation, it would be necessary to intend to obtain it, and it would follow that the intention is preceded by another intention.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling on delaying obligatory reasoning (al-nazar) without a valid excuse, even if it later becomes apparent that the obligation could not have been fulfilled in time. The author uses the analogy of a woman who becomes pure from menstruation in the morning, breaks her fast (i.e., intends to fast that day), but then menstruates again, making fasting impossible. Despite the impossibility, she is considered disobedient because of her negligence. The passage then distinguishes between the obligation of reasoning (which requires time) and the obligation of intention (which is not time-bound). It also discusses whether the intention to reason is part of the act of reasoning itself or a separate obligation, suggesting that specification indicates the chosen view that the intention is part of the act.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar)
القصد (al-qasd)
المعرفة (al-ma'rifah)
التأخير (al-ta'khīr)
الواجب (al-wājib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an obligation becomes impossible to fulfill, there is no sin for delaying it.
Correction: The passage shows that the sin is for the negligent delay itself, regardless of whether the obligation later becomes impossible. The analogy of the woman who breaks her fast and then menstruates demonstrates that she is sinful even though she could not have completed the fast.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who delays obligatory reasoning without excuse and then dies?
A) He is not sinful because the obligation may have been impossible.
B) He is disobedient due to negligence.
C) It depends on whether he had enough time.
D) He must repent but is not sinful.

Key Takeaway
Delaying obligatory reasoning without excuse incurs sin due to negligence, even if the obligation becomes impossible to fulfill; the sin lies in the delay itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you delayed a religious obligation thinking you would make up for it later, only to find the opportunity lost? What does this passage say about the sin of that delay, even if you couldn't have fulfilled it? How will you change your attitude toward urgent duties today?
?
The woman broke her fast knowingly, assuming the outcome wouldn't matter. Reflect on a time you violated a command of Allah, rationalizing that it was inevitable or insignificant. According to al-Jurjani, what does your negligence reveal about your heart? Will you sincerely repent and seek to never repeat that negligence?
?
The passage insists that intending to reason is part of the act itself. Are you truly present and intentional when you seek knowledge, or do you go through the motions? What specific change can you make in your next study session to ensure your intention is genuine and integral, not a separate step?
