The Condition of Attentiveness in Reasoning
Al-Jurjani defines attentiveness and presents al-Razi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the condition of attentiveness (tafattun) is, why it is necessary for reasoning to yield knowledge, and how al-Razi's objection challenges it.

Core Idea
Attentiveness to subsumption is the hidden key that connects premises to conclusion.

Al-Jurjani introduces a subtle but essential condition for valid reasoning: the thinker must be attentive to how the particular subsumes under the general. The famous mule example illustrates how even correct premises can lead to error without this attentiveness. This lesson unpacks the condition and al-Razi's initial objection, setting the stage for the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition attentively
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning through the statement of the condition. Notice the key term 'tafattun' and how it relates to subsumption (indiraj).
The example of the mule is meant to show the practical consequence of heedlessness.
2
Analyze the mule example
Identify the two premises and the conclusion. Then consider why the observer mistakenly thought the mule was pregnant. Trace the failure to the lack of attentiveness to subsumption.
3
Study al-Razi's objection
Read al-Razi's objection carefully. He argues that requiring knowledge of subsumption leads to an infinite regress. Understand the reasoning behind his objection.
This objection is a common challenge to the condition.
4
Reflect on the infinite regress problem
Ask yourself: if every cognitive step required another premise, how could reasoning ever conclude? This is the core of the objection.
The next lesson will provide the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد التاسع : شرط إفادة النظر للعلم التفطن لكيفية الاندراج) والارتباط بين المقدمتين ( فإن من يعلم أن هذه بغلة وكل بغلة عاقر قد يراها منتفخة البطن، فيظن أنها حامل وما هو أي ظنه كونها حاملا (إلا لذهوله عن ارتباط الصغرى بالكبرى واندراج هذا الجزئي) الذي هو هذه البغلة ( تحت ذلك الكلي ) الذي هو كل بغلة عاقر ، إذ لولا هذا الذهول لجزم بكونها عاقراً، ولم يظن أنها حامل ) ومنعه الإمام الرازي ( فقال : ليس ذلك التفطن شرطاً لإفادة النظر للعلم ( لأن العلم بأن هذا مندرج في ذلك ) وبأن إحدى المقدمتين مرتبطة بالأخرى ( تصديق آخر مغاير للتصديق بالصغرى والكبرى ( فلو وجب العلم به ) أي بأن هذا مندرج في ذاك وبأن هذه مرتبطة بتلك ( كانت ) هذه القضية التي وجب العلم بها ( مقدمة أخرى منضمة إليها ) أي إلى المقدمات الأخر مرتبة معها ( ويجب ملاحظة الترتيب ) وكيفية الاندراج مرة أخرى ويلزم التسلسل فيمتنع حصول العلم بالمطلوب ) والجواب لا نسلم أن ذلك الذي وجب العلم به مقدمة أخرى بل ذلك ) التفطن الذي اعتبره ابن سينا (هو) ملاحظة نسبة المقدمتين إلى النتيجة ( فإنه من الشرائط المتفق عليها إلا أنه أخره ليكون ذكر الأمور المختلفة في سلك واحد، مع ما فيه من الاهتمام بما قدمه عليه قوله : (التفطن أي التفهم لكيفية الاندراج أي اندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط إيجاباً أو سلباً كلياً، أو جزئياً مثلاً في قولنا الجسم مركب وكل مركب ممكن بعد التصديق بالمقدمتين لا بد من ملاحظة اندراج الجسم بخصوصه في المركب ليستفاد الحكم عليه بكونه ممكناً، ولولا ذلك بل لوحظ ما صدق عليه الأوسط في الكبرى بعنوان مفهومه إجمالاً، ولم يلاحظ اندراج الأصغر فيه بخصوصه لربما غفل عن النتيجة خصوصاً إذا توهم أمراً مانعاً منها كما نبه عليه الشيخ بالمثال الجزئي المذكور، ثم إن اتصاف ذات الموضوع بمفهومه في القضية تقييد، فيكون ملاحظة الاندراج المذكور تصوراً لا تصديقاً كأنه قيل وكل مركب أي الجسم وغيره المتصف بالتركيب ممكن هذا ملخص كلام الشيخ، وهو حق لا شبهة فيه للمنصف، وبما حررنا لك ظهر اندفاع ما قيل على قوله وهي من قبيل التصور دون التصديق من أن مجرد ملاحظة نسبة المقدمتين إلى النتيجة غير كافية في حصول المطلوب، بل لا بد فيه من الجزم بها والجزم حكم خبري، نعم إن هذا التصديق الحاصل من الهيئة الاجتماعية للمقدمتين، وإن كان تصديقاً آخر مغايراً للمقدمتين لكن لا يلزم وجوب ترتيبه معهما كإيجاب الصغرى وكلية الكبرى. قوله : (هو ملاحظة لنسبة المقدمتين إلخ) أي كيفية اشتمالها عليهما وهي التفطن لكيفية الاندراج.
The fifth observation - the ninth aim: the condition for reasoning to yield knowledge is attentiveness to the manner of subsumption and the connection between the two premises. For one who knows that this is a mule and that every mule is barren might see it with a swollen belly and think it is pregnant, and his thinking it is pregnant is only due to his heedlessness of the connection of the minor premise to the major premise and the subsumption of this particular (which is this mule) under that universal (which is every mule). For if not for this heedlessness, he would have judged it to be barren and would not have thought it pregnant. And Imam al-Razi objected, saying: This attentiveness is not a condition for reasoning to yield knowledge, because the knowledge that this is subsumed under that and that one premise is connected to the other is another assent different from the assent to the minor and major premises. So if knowledge of it were required, then this proposition which is required to be known would be another premise added to the other premises and arranged with them, and the observation of the arrangement and manner of subsumption would be required again, leading to an infinite regress, thus preventing the attainment of knowledge of the desired conclusion. The answer: We do not grant that what is required to be known is another premise; rather, that attentiveness which Ibn Sina considered is the observation of the relation of the two premises to the conclusion. For it is among the agreed-upon conditions, except that he delayed it to mention the different matters in one sequence, along with what it contains of attention to what preceded it. His statement: (al-tafattun) i.e., the understanding of the manner of subsumption, i.e., the subsumption of the minor under the middle term positively or negatively, universally or particularly. For example, in our statement: 'The body is composite, and every composite is contingent,' after assenting to the two premises, there must be an observation of the subsumption of the body specifically under the composite so that the judgment of its being contingent is derived. If not, but rather what the middle term applies to in the major premise is observed under its conceptual description generally, and the subsumption of the minor under it specifically is not observed, one might neglect the conclusion, especially if something preventing it is imagined, as the shaykh pointed out with the particular example mentioned. Moreover, the description of the subject's essence by its concept in the proposition is a restriction, so the observation of the mentioned subsumption is a conception (tasawwur), not an assent (tasdiq), as if it were said: 'Every composite, i.e., a body and other things described by composition, is contingent.' This is the summary of the shaykh's words, and it is true without any doubt for the fair-minded. And by what we have written for you, the refutation of what was said about his statement that it is of the type of conception without assent—that mere observation of the relation of the two premises to the conclusion is insufficient for obtaining the desired conclusion, rather it requires certainty about it, and certainty is a declarative judgment—appears. Yes, this assent resulting from the collective configuration of the two premises, even if it is another assent different from the two premises, does not necessitate its arrangement with them as the affirmation of the minor and the universality of the major. His statement: (it is an observation of the relation of the two premises, etc.) i.e., the manner in which they encompass them, and that is the attentiveness to the manner of subsumption.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof-against-objection. It begins by stating the ninth aim of the fifth observation: the condition for reasoning to yield knowledge is attentiveness (tafattun) to how the minor term is subsumed under the major and how the premises are connected. An example is given: someone who knows a particular mule is a mule and that all mules are barren might see it with a swollen belly and think it is pregnant, but that is only due to his failure to notice that this particular mule is subsumed under the universal 'all mules are barren'. Without that heedlessness, he would have judged it barren. Then Imam al-Razi's objection is presented: the knowledge of subsumption and connection is itself another assent (tasdiq), so requiring it would lead to an infinite regress of premises. The answer follows: the attentiveness is not an additional premise but an observation of the relation between the premises and the conclusion; it is from the agreed-upon conditions but delayed. The commentary then elaborates on the nature of this observation: it is a conception (tasawwur) of the subsumption, not a separate assent. It explains with the example of 'body is composite, every composite is contingent' that one must observe the subsumption of the body specifically under 'composite' to derive the conclusion; if one only observes the general concept, one might neglect the conclusion. The passage concludes that this observation is a conception, and the objection that it must be an assent is refuted by noting that the assent that arises from the collective configuration of the premises is not a separate premise requiring its own arrangement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Correct premises always guarantee correct conclusions, regardless of the thinker's attentiveness.
Correction: The mule example shows that without attentiveness to subsumption, even true premises can lead to a false conclusion.

Checkpoint
In the mule example, why does the observer mistakenly think the mule is pregnant?
A) He does not know that all mules are barren.
B) He is not attentive to the subsumption of this mule under 'all mules are barren'.
C) The premises are false.
D) He lacks knowledge of the minor premise.

Key Takeaway
Attentiveness to how the minor term subsumes under the major is a necessary condition for reasoning to produce knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you jumped to a wrong conclusion because you were heedless of how a general truth applied to your specific situation? Can you recall the exact reasoning you missed?
?
You know that Allah is Just and that every sin has consequences. Yet you sometimes excuse a small sin in yourself. What is the subsumption you are ignoring between the universal 'Allah punishes injustice' and the particular sin you tolerate?
?
Al-Razi’s objection shows that attentiveness cannot be a separate step. In your own life, do you treat spiritual attentiveness (taqwa) as a separate 'premise' you add when convenient, or as the very act of connecting your knowledge to your actions? How would your next decision change if you truly made it the latter?

Refuting Infinite Regress: Attentiveness as Conception
Al-Jurjani answers al-Razi and elaborates the nature of attentiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes al-Razi's infinite regress objection by showing that attentiveness is a conception (tasawwur) of the relation, not a separate premise.

Core Idea
Attentiveness is a conception of relation, not a premise — thus no infinite regress.

Al-Razi's objection threatens to undermine the very possibility of reasoning by requiring an infinite chain of premises. Al-Jurjani's answer is subtle: the required attentiveness is not a propositional assent but a conception (tasawwur) of how the premises relate to the conclusion. This lesson unpacks that answer, clarifies the distinction between conception and assent, and shows why no regress follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer from the passage
Start from 'Wal-jawab la nusallimu' and read through to the end. Notice the key phrase: 'bal dhalika al-tafattun... howa mulahazat nisbat al-muqaddimatayn ila al-natija'.
The answer distinguishes between a premise and an observation of relation.
2
Understand the distinction between conception and assent
Al-Jurjani explains that the observation of subsumption is a conception (tasawwur), not an assent (tasdiq). Grasp why this difference matters for avoiding regress.
A conception is a mental grasp, not a proposition that needs proof.
3
Apply the distinction to the body/composite example
Follow the detailed example of 'The body is composite; every composite is contingent'. See how the observation of subsumption is a conception of the relation, not a third premise.
The example shows that the subsumption is understood, not asserted as a separate statement.
4
Reflect on the final point about the collective configuration
Al-Jurjani concedes that the resulting assent from the premises is a new act but argues it does not require its own arrangement as a premise.
This resolves the regress: the new assent arises from the premises without needing a separate step.

Source Text

المرصد الخامس - المقصد التاسع : شرط إفادة النظر للعلم التفطن لكيفية الاندراج) والارتباط بين المقدمتين ( فإن من يعلم أن هذه بغلة وكل بغلة عاقر قد يراها منتفخة البطن، فيظن أنها حامل وما هو أي ظنه كونها حاملا (إلا لذهوله عن ارتباط الصغرى بالكبرى واندراج هذا الجزئي) الذي هو هذه البغلة ( تحت ذلك الكلي ) الذي هو كل بغلة عاقر ، إذ لولا هذا الذهول لجزم بكونها عاقراً، ولم يظن أنها حامل ) ومنعه الإمام Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any cognitive step required in reasoning must be a separate premise, leading to infinite regress.

According to al-Jurjani's answer, why does attentiveness not lead to infinite regress?
A) Because it is a conception, not a separate premise.
B) Because it is an optional step that can be skipped.
C) Because al-Razi's objection is invalid for other reasons.
D) Because the premises themselves are sufficient without any observation.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you knew two facts but failed to connect them to an obvious conclusion. What was missing? Was it a lack of attentiveness to the specific relationship between them? How can you train yourself to observe that subsumption in your own reasoning?
?
The passage teaches that attentiveness is a conception, not an assent. Do you sometimes confuse having a mental image of a relationship with actually judging it to be true? How does distinguishing conception from assent help you avoid errors in your beliefs?
?
Al-Jurjani argues that the certainty from premises does not require its own premise. Are there areas in your life where you delay acting because you feel you need 'one more proof'? How does this lesson challenge you to trust the reasoning you already have and act upon it?

The Noticing as Conception: Avoiding Infinite Regress
Al-Jurjani asserts the noticing is a conception and blocks infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani considers the noticing (al-tafattun) to be a conception (tasawwur) rather than an assent, and how this prevents infinite regress in the process of acquiring knowledge.

Core Idea
The noticing is a conception, not an assent, thus no infinite regress.

Al-Jurjani begins by establishing that the only path to unknown knowledge is through attentiveness to how known facts lead to the sought conclusion. He clarifies that this attentiveness is a conception of the relation, not an act of assent, thereby blocking any charge of infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Note the key terms: تفطن (attentiveness), تصور (conception), تصديق (assent).
The passage is the foundational statement for the rest of the discussion.
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Jurjani claims that the noticing is a conception, not an assent. Why is this distinction important? It prevents infinite regress.
Infinite regress would occur if each step required another step of the same kind.
3
Understand the regress problem
If the noticing were an assent, then to assent to the relation would require another noticing, leading to an endless chain. By making it a conception, the chain stops.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
This section sets up the objection from al-Baydawi and the subsequent refutation. Remember that the noticing is a simple awareness, not a judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : هكذا فلا سبيل إلى درك مطلوب مجهول إلا من قبل حاصل معلوم، ولا سبيل أيضاً إلى ذلك إلا بالتفطن للجهة التي لأجلها صار مؤدياً إلى المطلوب، فأشار بالتفطن للجهة المذكورة إلى تلك الملاحظة وهي من قبيل التصور دون التصديق فلا تسلسل ( وقد احتج البعض) يعني القاضي البيضاوي ) على رأي ابن سينا) وكون التفطن شرطاً للانتاج باختلاف الأشكال في الجلاء والخفاء) فإنا نجد شكلين يتركب كل منهما من مقدمتين بديهتين، مع أن إنتاج أحدهما لنتيجته بين جلي وإنتاج الآخر خفي، محتاج إلى بيان وما ذاك إلا لأن هيئة الأول قريبة من الطبع يتفطن لها بالبديهة وهيئة الثاني بعيدة منه فلا يتفطن لها إلا بدليل أو تنبيه ( وفيه نظر لاختلاف اللوازم ( في الأشكال ( فقد يكون إنتاجها لبعض ) من تلك اللوازم ( أظهر) من إنتاجها لبعض آخر منها، وتفصيل الكلام أن الأشكال مختلفة على سبيل منع الخلو إما في المقدمات وإما في النتائج فإذا فرض الاتحاد في المقدمتين في الأول والرابع كان اللازم من أحدهما عكس اللازم من الآخر، وإذا كان أحد الاختلافين لازماً، وقد يجتمعان أيضاً جاز أن يكون الاختلاف في الجلاء والخفاء لاختلاف اللوازم، أو لاختلاف الملزومات، أو لاختلافهما معاً، فإن اللزوم بين أمرين قد يكون بينا، ولا يكون بين أمرين آخرين، أو بين أحدهما وأمر آخر بيناً ( والحق أنه إن أراد ) ابن سينا بما ذكره وجعله شرطاً للانتاج ( اجتماع المقدمتين معاً في الذهن) مرتبتين على ما ينبغي (فمسلم) لأنه لو كان حصول المبادي وحدها بلا ترتيب معتبر بينها، كافياً في حصول المطلوب لكان العالم بالقضايا الواجب قبولها عالماً

قوله : (يعني القاضي البيضاوي حيث قال : في الطوالع والأشبه أنه لا بد الترتيب والهيئة وإلا لما تفاوتت الإشكال في الجلاء والخفاء. قوله : ( فلا يتفطن لها أي للاندراج المستفاد منها . كما
ملاحظة من
قوله: (وهي من قبيل التصور دون التصديق أورد عليه أن تصور النسبة وملاحظتها غير كافية في حصول المطلوب، بل لا بد فيه من الجزم بها والجزم حكم خبري، نعم هذا الجزم حاصل من الهيئة الجمعية للمقدمتين أعني صورة القياس، ولا يلزم من كون الجزم بها تصديقاً آخر مغايراً للمقدمتين، وجوب ترتيب مخصوص مستدع لتفطن ،آخر، وذلك لأن هذا التصديق إنما هو لصحة ترتيب المقدمين لا لأجل أن ذلك مقدمة أخرى.
قوله : ( فإذا فرض الاتحاد إلخ) كقولنا : كل أب وكل ب ج ينتج من الأول كل آ ج وإذا عكس الترتيب ينتج من الرابع بعض ج أ ثم لا يخفي أن للترتيب دخلا في الاستلزام، فاختلاف الملزوم لازم البتة .
He said: Thus, there is no way to grasp an unknown sought except from an known obtained, and there is no way to that except by attentiveness to the aspect for which it became a means to the sought. So he indicated by attentiveness to the mentioned aspect that observation, and it is of the type of conception (tasawwur) without assent (tasdiq), so there is no infinite regress. And some argued – meaning al-Qadi al-Baydawi – against the opinion of Ibn Sina and that attentiveness is a condition for entailment, by the difference of the figures in clarity and obscurity. For we find two figures each composed of two evident premises, yet the entailment of one for its conclusion is clear and obvious, while the entailment of the other is obscure, needing exposition. That is only because the form of the first is close to nature, so one is attentively aware of it by intuition, and the form of the second is far from it, so one is not attentively aware of it except by evidence or prompting. But this is open to consideration because of the difference of the consequences in the figures; for their entailment of some of those consequences may be more apparent than their entailment of some others of them. The detailed discussion is that the figures differ, either in the premises or in the conclusions, necessarily. So if identity is assumed in the two premises in the first and fourth, the consequent of one would be the converse of the consequent of the other. And if one of the two differences is necessary, and they may also combine, then the difference in clarity and obscurity may be due to the difference of the consequences, or the difference of the premises, or both. For the entailment between two things may be evident, and not evident between two other things, or between one of them and another thing. And the truth is that if Ibn Sina intended by what he mentioned and made a condition for entailment the gathering of both premises together in the mind, arranged as they should be, then it is granted. Because if the attainment of the principles alone without a considered arrangement between them were sufficient for obtaining the sought, then one who knows the propositions that must be accepted would be knowing [everything].

His statement: (meaning al-Qadi al-Baydawi where he said in al-Tawaliʿ: 'The more likely is that arrangement and form are necessary, otherwise the figures would not differ in clarity and obscurity.') His statement: (so he is not attentively aware of it) i.e., the subsumption derived from it.

From the observation:
His statement: (it is of the type of conception without assent) an objection was raised against it that the conception of the relation and its observation is insufficient for obtaining the sought; rather, it requires certainty about it, and certainty is a declarative judgment. Indeed, this certainty is obtained from the collective configuration of the two premises, namely the form of the syllogism. And the fact that certainty about it is another assent different from the two premises does not necessitate a particular arrangement requiring another attentiveness, because this assent is only for the validity of the arrangement of the premises, not because that is another premise.

His statement: (So if identity is assumed, etc.) As in our saying: 'Every A is B and every B is C' yields from the first 'every A is C'; and if the order is reversed, it yields from the fourth 'some C is A'. Then it is not hidden that arrangement has an effect on entailment, so the difference of the entailed is necessary.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a proof and an objection-and-answer structure. It begins with a quotation from the matn stating that the only way to grasp an unknown from a known is through attentiveness to how the known leads to the unknown, and that this attentiveness is a conception, not an assent, thus avoiding infinite regress. Then an objection from al-Baydawi is presented: he argues that if attentiveness were not a condition, then all syllogistic figures would be equally clear, but we observe that some figures with evident premises produce obvious conclusions while others produce obscure ones, requiring exposition. This difference, he claims, shows that attentiveness to the form is necessary. Al-Jurjani then critiques this: the difference in clarity could be due to differences in the consequences themselves, not necessarily due to a need for attentiveness. He elaborates that figures differ in premises or conclusions, and the clarity depends on the entailment relation, which may be evident in some cases and not others. The truth, he says, is that if Ibn Sina meant that both premises must be present together in the mind arranged properly, then that is accepted. The commentary then addresses an objection against the claim that the noticing is a conception: mere conception of the relation is insufficient; certainty (jazm) is needed. The response is that this certainty arises from the collective form of the syllogism, and it is not a separate premise requiring its own arrangement. Finally, an example illustrates how arrangement affects entailment: in the first figure 'every A is B, every B is C' yields 'every A is C', while reversing the order (fourth figure) yields 'some C is A', showing arrangement matters.

Key Terms
تفطن
تصور
تصديق
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does al-Jurjani say the noticing (al-tafattun) is?
A) An assent (tasdiq) that requires further justification.
B) A conception (tasawwur) that prevents infinite regress.
C) A combination of conception and assent.
D) An intuitive knowledge without any relation.

Key Takeaway
The noticing is a conception, not an assent, thus avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter something you do not understand, do you first acknowledge what you already know and then attentively look for the aspect that connects it to the unknown? Or do you rush to ask others or search for answers without this inner attentiveness? How can you train yourself to practice this essential noticing?
?
This lesson teaches that the noticing is a simple conception, not a heavy judgment. In your own learning, do you sometimes treat new insights as burdensome judgments instead of simple awarenesses? How might embracing the simplicity of conception ease your pursuit of knowledge?
?
Reflect on a specific time when you felt stuck in a cycle of confusion, perhaps going around in circles. Could it be that you were treating each step as a judgment requiring further judgments, rather than seeing the clear connection as a simple awareness? What would change if you stopped the regress by accepting the noticing as enough?

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
Al-Baydawi's Objection: The Difference in Clarity of Syllogistic Figures
Al-Baydawi's objection and al-Jurjani's initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baydawi's objection that differences in clarity between syllogistic figures prove that attentiveness is a condition for entailment, and al-Jurjani's initial response that the difference may be due to differences in consequences.

Core Idea
The variation in clarity among figures does not necessarily prove a separate condition of attentiveness.

Al-Baydawi raises an objection against Ibn Sina's view that attentiveness is a condition for syllogistic entailment. He points out that different figures with equally evident premises produce conclusions of varying clarity, suggesting that attentiveness to the form is needed. Al-Jurjani begins his refutation by noting that the difference might stem from the consequences themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Baydawi's objection
Read the passage of the objection carefully. Note the example of two figures with evident premises but different clarity of conclusions.
The key is the phrase: 'because the form of the first is close to nature...'
2
Identify the implicit assumption
Al-Baydawi assumes that the only explanation for the difference in clarity is the need for attentiveness to the form. He uses this to argue that attentiveness is a condition.
3
Read al-Jurjani's initial critique
Al-Jurjani responds briefly: 'This is open to consideration because of the difference of the consequences in the figures.' He suggests that the difference might be due to the consequences themselves, not the need for attentiveness.
This is a preliminary point; the full discussion follows in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the dialectic
Notice how the objection and response set up a deeper analysis of logical forms. The disagreement is not about the existence of clarity differences, but about what explains them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : هكذا فلا سبيل إلى درك مطلوب مجهول إلا من قبل حاصل معلوم، ولا سبيل أيضاً إلى ذلك إلا بالتفطن للجهة التي لأجلها صار مؤدياً إلى المطلوب، فأشار بالتفطن للجهة المذكورة إلى تلك الملاحظة وهي من قبيل التصور دون التصديق فلا تسلسل ( وقد احتج البعض) يعني القاضي البيضاوي ) على رأي ابن سينا) وكون التفطن شرطاً للانتاج باختلاف الأشكال في الجلاء والخفاء) فإنا نجد شكلين يتركب كل منهما من مقدمتين بديهتين، مع أن إنتاج أحدهما لنتيجته بين جلي وإنتاج الآخر خفي، محتاج إلى بيان وما ذاك إلا لأن هيئة الأول قريبة من الطبع يتفطن لها بالبديهة وهيئة الثاني بعيدة منه فلا يتفطن لها إلا بدليل أو تنبيه ( وفيه نظر لاختلاف اللوازم ( في الأشكال ( فقد يكون إنتاجها لبعض ) من تلك اللوازم ( أظهر) من إنتاجها لبعض آخر منها، وتفصيل الكلام أن الأشكال مختلفة على سبيل منع الخلو إما في المقدمات وإما في النتائج فإذا فرض الاتحاد في المقدمتين في الأول والرابع كان اللازم من أحدهما عكس اللازم من الآخر، وإذا كان أحد الاختلافين لازماً، وقد يجتمعان أيضاً جاز أن يكون الاختلاف في الجلاء والخفاء لاختلاف اللوازم، أو لاختلاف الملزومات، أو لاختلافهما معاً، فإن اللزوم بين أمرين قد يكون بينا، ولا يكون بين أمرين آخرين، أو بين أحدهما وأمر آخر بيناً ( والحق أنه إن أراد ) ابن سينا بما ذكره وجعله شرطاً للانتاج ( اجتماع المقدمتين معاً في الذهن) مرتبتين على ما ينبغي (فمسلم) لأنه لو كان حصول المبادي وحدها بلا ترتيب معتبر بينها، كافياً في حصول المطلوب لكان العالم بالقضايا الواجب قبولها عالماً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference in clarity between syllogistic figures must be due to a separate condition of attentiveness
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that it may be due to differences in the consequences (lawāzim) or the premises themselves.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani's initial response, what might explain the difference in clarity between syllogistic figures?
A) The need for attentiveness to the form.
B) The difference in the consequences (al-lawāzim).
C) The weakness of the premises.
D) The lack of intuition in the reasoner.

Key Takeaway
The difference in clarity among figures may be due to differences in consequences, not a separate condition of attentiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult logical argument, do you immediately assume the obscurity lies in your own mind, or do you consider that the inherent relationship between ideas might itself be unclear? How would your approach to learning change if you paused to examine the consequences before doubting your ability?
?
Al-Jurjani invites us to look at the content itself rather than blame our attentiveness. In your study of aqeedah, can you recall a time when you dismissed a proof as 'unclear' when the real issue was the depth of the concept? What would it look like to trust the clarity of truth and patiently work through obscurity?
?
If the clarity of a syllogism depends on the conclusion itself, then your lack of immediate understanding does not prove a deficiency in you. How might this free you from the arrogance of thinking you should grasp everything instantly, and instead humble you to seek understanding through careful reflection?

Method of Learning

Detailed Discussion: Arrangement, Entailment, and the Need for Certainty
Al-Jurjani's detailed discussion of figures, the truth condition, and the resolution of the certainty objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed argument that arrangement affects entailment, the truth that both premises must be present together in the mind, and the resolution that the certainty needed for knowledge arises from the form of the syllogism, not from an additional premise.

Core Idea
The certainty in syllogistic knowledge comes from the collective form, not an extra premise.

Al-Jurjani expands his analysis by explaining that the figures differ in premises or conclusions, and the entailment relation may be more or less evident depending on the terms. He then affirms that if Ibn Sina meant that both premises must be present together in the mind arranged properly, that is acceptable. Finally, he addresses an objection that mere conception is insufficient, showing that the required certainty comes from the syllogistic form itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed discussion on figures
Al-Jurjani explains that figures differ necessarily, and gives an example with the first and fourth figures. Notice how arrangement affects the conclusion.
The example: 'Every A is B, every B is C' yields 'every A is C' in the first figure, but reversed yields 'some C is A' in the fourth.
2
Understand the truth condition
Al-Jurjani says that if Ibn Sina intended that both premises must be gathered in the mind arranged properly, that is granted. This clarifies the intended meaning.
3
Read the objection and its resolution
An objection argues that mere conception of the relation is not enough; certainty is needed. Al-Jurjani responds that this certainty comes from the collective configuration of the premises (the syllogistic form) and does not require a separate premise.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Notice how al-Jurjani defends the position that the noticing is a conception while still accounting for the need for certainty. The key is that the form itself produces certainty.

For the entailment between two things may be evident, and not evident between two other things, or between one of them and another thing. And the truth is that if Ibn Sina intended by what he mentioned and made a condition for entailment the gathering of both premises together in the mind, arranged as they should be, then it is granted. Because if the attainment of the principles alone without a considered arrangement between them were sufficient for obtaining the sought, then one who knows the propositions that must be accepted would be knowing [everything].

His statement: (meaning al-Qadi al-Baydawi where he said in al-Tawaliʿ: 'The more likely is that arrangement and form are necessary, otherwise the figures would not differ in clarity and obscurity.') His statement: (so he is not attentively aware of it) i.e., the subsumption derived from it.

From the observation:
His statement: (it is of the type of conception without assent) an objection was raised against it that the conception of the relation and its observation is insufficient for obtaining the sought; rather, it requires certainty about it, and certainty is a declarative judgment. Indeed, this certainty is obtained from the collective configuration of the two premises, namely the form of the syllogism. And the fact that certainty about it is another assent different from the two premises does not necessitate a particular arrangement requiring another attentiveness, because this assent is only for the validity of the arrangement of the premises, not because that is another premise.

His statement: (So if identity is assumed, etc.) As in our saying: 'Every A is B and every B is C' yields from the first 'every A is C'; and if the order is reversed, it yields from the fourth 'some C is A'. Then it is not hidden that arrangement has an effect on entailment, so the difference of the entailed is necessary.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a proof and an objection-and-answer structure. It begins with a quotation from the matn stating that the only way to grasp an unknown from a known is through attentiveness to how the known leads to the unknown, and that this attentiveness is a conception, not an assent, thus avoiding infinite regress. Then an objection from al-Baydawi is presented: he argues that if attentiveness were not a condition, then all syllogistic figures would be equally clear, but we observe that some figures with evident premises produce obvious conclusions while others produce obscure ones, requiring exposition. This difference, he claims, shows that attentiveness to the form is necessary. Al-Jurjani then critiques this: the difference in clarity could be due to differences in the consequences themselves, not necessarily due to a need for attentiveness. He elaborates that figures differ in premises or conclusions, and the clarity depends on the entailment relation, which may be evident in some cases and not others. The truth, he says, is that if Ibn Sina meant that both premises must be present together in the mind arranged properly, then that is accepted. The commentary then addresses an objection against the claim that the noticing is a conception: mere conception of the relation is insufficient; certainty (jazm) is needed. The response is that this certainty arises from the collective form of the syllogism, and it is not a separate premise requiring its own arrangement. Finally, an example illustrates how arrangement affects entailment: in the first figure 'every A is B, every B is C' yields 'every A is C', while reversing the order (fourth figure) yields 'some C is A', showing arrangement matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Certainty in syllogistic reasoning requires an additional premise or a separate act of assent beyond the premises.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explains that the certainty comes from the collective form of the premises (the syllogistic figure itself), not from an extra premise.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, where does the certainty (al-jazm) necessary for syllogistic knowledge come from?
A) From a separate premise that must be added.
B) From the intuitive nature of the first figure.
C) From the collective configuration of the two premises (the syllogistic form).
D) From the attentiveness of the reasoner.

Key Takeaway
The certainty in syllogistic knowledge arises from the form of the syllogism, not from an extra premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a firm belief, do you reflect on the structure of the evidence that led you to it, or do you rely on the mere accumulation of true statements? How might your certainty be affected if you examined the arrangement of your reasoning?
?
Consider a time you were absolutely certain about a conclusion. Was that certainty based on the logical form of your reasoning or on external factors? What would change if you scrutinized the arrangement of your premises the way al-Jurjani examines syllogisms?
?
Are you willing to reorder your assumptions if the structure of your argument fails to produce the expected conclusion? What is at stake if you refuse to do so?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
The Essential Condition for Valid Reasoning: Subsumption, Not Just Form
Commentary on the condition of syllogistic form and answer to an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the real condition for valid syllogistic reasoning is the subsumption of the minor under the middle, not merely the arrangement of premises in the first figure.

Core Idea
Valid reasoning requires actual subsumption, not just form.

This passage from a commentary on logic addresses a crucial nuance: after arranging premises in the first figure, is any further condition needed? The commentator argues that while a specific form is necessary, the mere observation of arrangement is insufficient. The true condition is the subsumption of the minor term under the middle term. He also answers the objection that a dull person might still miss the conclusion despite correct arrangement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the commentator's dialectical style: he presents a position, an objection, and a clarification.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two key concepts
Find where 'hay'a makhsusa' (specific form) and 'indiraj al-asghar taht al-awsat' (subsumption of minor under middle) are discussed.
These are the main technical terms.
3
Understand the objection and reply
The objection from al-Bulgha claims that ordering premises suffices. The commentator replies that the real issue is heedlessness of one premise, and that subsumption is the true condition.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The commentator reiterates that observing subsumption is a condition, while mere observation of arrangement is not.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلوم لانتهاء الكسبيات إلى الضروريات، وليس كذلك فوجب أن تكون مع بجميع المبادئ هيئة مخصوصة عارضة لها هي صورة للنظر كما مر ( وإن أراد أمراً) آخر ) وراءه ) أي وراء الاجتماع المذكور (فممنوع) إذ لا حاجة بنا بعد ترتيب المقدمتين على هيئة الشكل الأول إلى أمر آخر، والحاصل أنه لا بد مع المقدمتين من الترتيب والهيئة ومن أن تكون لهما نسبة مخصوصة مع النتيجة، وأما ملاحظة الترتيب والهيئة والنسبة المخصوصة فلا دليل على كونها شرطاً سوى قضية جلاء الأشكال وخفائها وقد عرفت ما فيها ( وما ذكره من المثال ( في البلغة (إنما يصح عند الذهول عن إحدى المقدمتين وأما عند ملاحظتهما على الترتيب اللائق ( فلا ) ذلك المثال، نعم إذا لوحظ الكبرى قبل الصغرى كان الترتيب مفقوداً، وأمكن يصح ذلك الظن . قوله: (فممنوع) قد عرفت مما حررنا لك سقوط هذا المنع. قوله: (وأما ملاحظة الترتيب إلخ) وقد عرفت أنه عبارة عن ملاحظة اندراج الأصغر بخصوصه تحت الأوسط وأنه لا شبهة في كونه شرطاً، فلا يلزم من عدم كون ملاحظة الترتيب شرطاً عدم كون ملاحظة الاندراج شرطاً ، نعم إنه يصح رداً على ما قاله القاضي البيضاوي . قوله : (إذ لا حاجة بنا إلخ ) فإن قلت المتناهي في البلادة ربما يرتب المقدمتين على هيئة الشكل الأول، ومع ذلك تخفى عليه النتيجة ويغفل عن لزومها بسبب غفلته عن أن الأصغر يندرج تحت الأوسط، قلت : الظاهر أن الغفلة بسبب عدم قدرته على جمع المقدمتين .
The sciences because the acquired (kashiyyat) end at the necessary (daruriyyat), and it is not so, so it must be that along with all the premises there is a specific form (haya makhsusa) accidental to them which is the image (sura) of the consideration (nazar) as previously mentioned. (And if he intends another matter) beyond it, i.e., beyond the mentioned collection, (then it is denied) because there is no need for us after arranging the two premises in the form of the first figure (shakl al-awwal) for another matter. The conclusion is that there must be, along with the two premises, arrangement (tartib) and form (hay'a), and that there be between them and the conclusion a specific relation (nisa makhsusa). As for observing (mulahaza) the arrangement, form, and specific relation, there is no proof that it is a condition except the matter of the clarity of the figures and their obscurity, and you have known what is in it. (And the example he mentioned) in al-Bulgha (is only valid upon heedlessness (dhuhul) of one of the two premises; but when observing them in the appropriate order (tartib la'iq), then that example does not hold). Indeed, if the major premise is observed before the minor, the arrangement is missing, and that suspicion (zann) may occur. He said: (Denied) — you have known from what we have written for you the fall of this denial. He said: (And as for observing the arrangement, etc.) — and you have known that it is an expression of observing the subsumption (indiraj) of the particular minor under the middle (awsat), and there is no doubt that it is a condition; so it does not follow from the fact that observing the arrangement is not a condition that observing the subsumption is not a condition. Yes, it is valid as a reply to what al-Qadi al-Baydawi said. He said: (Since there is no need for us, etc.) — then you might say: The extremely dull person (mutanahi fi al-balada) may arrange the two premises in the form of the first figure, yet the conclusion remains hidden from him and he becomes heedless of its implication due to his heedlessness of the fact that the minor is subsumed under the middle. I say: It is apparent that the heedlessness is due to his inability to combine the two premises.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the condition of arrangement in reasoning. It begins by stating that the acquired sciences terminate at necessary ones, and therefore the premises must have a specific form. The author argues that after arranging the two premises in the first figure, no further matter is needed. However, the mere observation of the arrangement and form is not a condition; what matters is the subsumption of the minor under the middle. An example from al-Bulgha is given to illustrate that heedlessness of one premise can lead to a false conclusion. The commentator then explains the phrase 'denied' and clarifies that the denial has been refuted. He further elaborates on 'observing the arrangement', explaining that it means observing the subsumption of the particular minor under the middle, which is indeed a condition. The objection about the dull person who arranges premises correctly but still misses the conclusion is answered: his heedlessness is due to his inability to combine the two premises mentally.

Key Terms
هيئة الشكل الأول
ترتيب المقدمتين
اندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط
ملاحظة الترتيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simply arranging premises in the first figure guarantees a valid inference.
Correction: The true condition is the actual subsumption of the minor under the middle; mere order is insufficient, as shown by the heedlessness example.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator identify as the essential condition for reasoning, beyond mere arrangement of premises?
A) Observing the specific form (hay'a makhsusa) of the premises.
B) Observing the subsumption (indiraj) of the minor under the middle term.
C) Having the major premise before the minor premise.
D) Relying on innate human intelligence.

Key Takeaway
The essential condition for a valid syllogism is the actual subsumption of the minor under the middle, not just the arrangement of premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your religious practice do you have the correct external form (like prayer or fasting) but your heart has not truly subsumed its meaning? What is one concrete step you will take today to combine the premises of your action with inner conviction?
?
The extremely dull person arranged the premises correctly but missed the conclusion. Where in your pursuit of knowledge might you be content with form over substance—memorizing but not internalizing? What will you do this week to move from mere arrangement to real understanding?
?
If the essential condition for a valid syllogism is the mental act of subsumption, what does that teach you about the condition for accepted deeds? Is there an act of worship you perform mechanically without truly combining its outward form with heartfelt presence? How will you change that starting tomorrow?
